As late as the thirteenth century, vice trees still appeared in illuminated psalters, a testimony to the power of this image of sin.39 Cassian uses both metaphors: “For a tall and spreading tree of a noxious kind will the more easily be made to wither if the roots on which it depends have first been laid bare or cut; and a pond of water which is dangerous will be dried up at once if the spring and flowing channel which produce it are carefully stopped up.”40 The capital vices name the set of vices that grow out of pride and tend to proliferate additional sin. Thus, in the works of church fathers like Cassian and Gregory the Great, as well as medieval manuals for confession based on them, each vice would be listed along with its “offspring” vices, sprouting out from the main branches.

The tree metaphor yields a picture of moral education in which the goal is to dig down to the problem’s source and to root it out, thereby eliminating the whole host of related vices it nourishes. Moreover, the tree picture suggests that merely pruning the new shoots and twigs at the end of the branches will treat only surface symptoms without addressing the underlying disease that causes them. You can’t successfully treat a polluted area without addressing the sources of pollution continually flowing in from its feeder streams. And you can pick all the fruit you like from a tree: it will just grow more. The “roots and offshoots” picture shows that sin is organic and dynamic. Vice and sin don’t just sit there in an isolated corner of your heart, inert and lifeless. They grow and produce fruit (just as a tree of virtues, rooted in Christ, produces the fruit of the Spirit). If you really want to deal with sin in your life, you will have to cut off its main branches, put an axe to the stump, and grind the root. The tree metaphor therefore encourages people to see certain sins as likely indicators of deeper moral problems and to see their connection to that great original sin, pride.41

The vices are less like a broken arm, which is a local injury that heals in a few weeks, and more like cancer—pervasive, hidden within, hard to treat and eradicate, and for which treatment is difficult and long-term. The desert tradition treats a vice diagnosis seriously, knowing that temporary symptom-relief will not cure what truly ails us.

Deadly Sins or Capital Vices?

As such metaphors imply, the label “capital vices” has a significantly different meaning than its alternative, “the seven deadly sins.” Notice first the difference between “sin” and “vice.” “Sin” has a broader scope, since it can include patterns of sinfulness in our behavior, our fallen condition or nature in general, or, perhaps most commonly, a single act of disobedience—as in, “Did she commit a sin?” The term “vice,” on the other hand, targets something more specific: vices concern deeply ingrained patterns in our character, patterns broader than a single act but narrower than our sinful human condition in general. The category of vice also makes better sense of the link between poisoned fruits, offspring vices, and their seven sources. For example, sixteen vices growing from sloth might appear too overblown an estimate of the damage from a single act of sin (although in certain cases, perhaps not!).42 Such an array of offshoot vices better describes the typical impact on our character made over time by a deeply rooted compulsion at the center of our lives, or a strong desire that drives our ongoing search for happiness.

An even more important difference between the two labels “capital vices” and “deadly sins” lies in the meaning of “deadly.” The “deadliness” of the seven deadly sins refers to the distinction between mortal and venial sins. In Catholic moral theology, mortal sins are so called because they cause spiritual death. They cut us off from God’s grace when we reject the source of our spiritual life, the indwelling of the Holy Spirit. Aquinas calls this gift of the Holy Spirit’s indwelling the virtue of charity: the love of God and neighbor that is the perfect fulfillment of the law (Matt. 22:37–40; Rom. 13:10). So he defines mortal sins as sins against charity. In this picture, to commit a deadly or mortal sin is to forfeit the grace of the Holy Spirit by rejecting God as our highest good and willfully refusing to put love of God and neighbor first in our actions. Our decision effectively shows that we prefer committing the sin—obtaining whatever good it promises—to having a relationship with God. Venial sins, on the other hand, may dispose us to mortal sin but do not themselves sever our union with God.

Some strands of Protestant theology deny the possibility that anyone can commit mortal sin after receiving saving (justifying) grace. According to the doctrine of the perseverance of the saints, once we are justified, nothing can separate us from the love of God (Rom. 8:38–39). Furthermore, if “the wages” of all sin is death (Rom. 6:23), because every sin separates us from God, then the distinction between mortal and venial sin breaks down. Those who hold such beliefs, along with those who deny the traditional Catholic distinction between mortal and venial sin on other grounds, would be more faithful to their own theological commitments by using the label “capital vices.” Ironically, most Protestants (likely out of ignorance of the vices’ history) tend to call them “the seven deadly sins.”

We might also consider other important reasons to prefer the title “capital vices” to “deadly sins.” Aquinas argues that not all acts of these vices are necessarily (or even usually) mortal sins. Many instances of lust and gluttony, for example, seem better explained by weakness or impulsiveness than by an entrenched or willful preference for pleasure over a relationship with God, although they might well tend in that direction over time. Moreover, the conception of the sins as “deadly” does not explain their association with specific offspring vices. From the beginning of the history of the list, the vices were named principia vitia (“principal vices”) and enumerated together with predictably characteristic progeny. Again, here is Cassian:

And though these [offspring vices] are far more numerous than the virtues are, yet if those eight principal sins, from which we know that these naturally proceed, are first overcome, all these at once sink down, and are destroyed together with them with a lasting destruction. For from gluttony proceed surfeiting and drunkenness. From fornication filthy conversation, scurrility, buffoonery and foolish talking. From covetousness, lying, deceit, theft, perjury, the desire of filthy lucre, false witness, violence, inhumanity, and greed. From anger, murders, clamour and indignation. From dejection, rancor, cowardice, bitterness, despair. From accidie, laziness, sleepiness, rudeness, restlessness, wandering about, instability both of mind and body, chattering, inquisitiveness. From vainglory, contention, heresies, boasting and confidence in novelties. From pride, contempt, envy, disobedience, blasphemy, murmuring, backbiting.43

Aquinas, in turn, offers a detailed explanation of the name “capital vices,” the only label he uses to describe the list of seven. The list guided practice for centuries before medieval theology articulated the distinction between mortal and venial sin and applied the label “deadly.” For all these reasons, then, regardless of whether we accept the category of mortal sin, “capital vices” counts as the best label for the great list of seven.

The List of Seven

What are the individual capital vices? As we know from their history, even early sources disagreed about this (as well as their proper order). The original Evagrian list consisted of eight members. “All the generic types of [evil] thoughts fall into eight categories in which every sort of thought is included. First is that of gluttony, then fornication [or lust], third avarice, fourth sadness, fifth anger, sixth acedia [or sloth], seventh vainglory, eighth pride.”44 In his systematization of the wisdom of the desert fathers, Cassian says, “There are eight principal faults which attack [human]kind; viz., first gastrimargia, which means gluttony, secondly fornication, thirdly philargyria, i.e., avarice or the love of money, fourthly anger, fifthly dejection, sixthly acedia, i.e., listlessness or low spirits, seventhly cenodoxia, i.e., boasting or vain glory, and eighthly pride.”45 (Envy, the only vice missing from these early lists, is added later.)

We have seen that Gregory pared down the list to seven by designating pride, traditionally understood as the original sin, as the originator or root of all other vices.46 Even Gregory’s authority, however, did not finally settle the issue of whether pride was the root of the seven other sins or itself a member of the list. Most recent versions of the seven, for example, include pride rather than vainglory. Perhaps this is because the difference between them is not easy to see, on first glance, and vainglory is the less familiar term of the two. This book will follow the Gregorian tradition and give pride a role as the source of the seven vices. In one sense this includes the best of both traditions, since making pride the root of the seven “source” vices implicitly counts it as a source vice itself, just a more ultimate one. I will also, however, replace Gregorian sadness with sloth, following Aquinas, who casts sloth as a specific kind of sorrow.47 The list of seven used here thus includes vainglory, along with envy, sloth, avarice, wrath, gluttony, and lust, as well as pride, which is their root.

Since the following chapters of the book give each vice an extended treatment, I will not describe each of them here. For now, we should keep in mind that the list of seven vices does not directly correspond to the seven principal virtues—faith, hope, charity, practical wisdom, justice, courage, and temperance. Historically, we nonetheless observe attempts to pair the vices with a list of opposing virtues. For example, in Prudentius’s Psychomachia, anger is overcome by patience, pride by humility, lust by chastity, and so on.48 It’s easy to see what motivated the shift to seven vices, given the widespread standardization of a larger system of “sevens,” which includes the seven virtues, the seven petitions of the Lord’s Prayer, the seven beatitudes, the seven corporal and spiritual works of mercy,49 the seven gifts of the Holy Spirit (Isa. 11:1–3), and so on.

In his Summa theologiae, by contrast, Aquinas follows the Aristotelian pattern of opposing each virtue to its own pair of vices. Every virtue, in his account, lies on a continuum between two opposing vices, one on each extreme.50 Generosity, for example, is a virtue characterized by a love of giving and an appropriate freedom from attachment to money. Avarice or stinginess, on the one extreme, names an excessive attachment to money, so that we hoard money for ourselves rather than giving it away when we ought. On the other extreme is the vice of deficiency—prodigality—a habit of not being attached enough to money, to the point of carelessness and wastefulness. The virtue, therefore, lies somewhere between the two extremes, in what philosophers (following Aristotle) call “the golden mean.” Because Aquinas organizes his Summa theologiae around the seven virtues and his structure requires more than seven opposing vices, it is difficult for him to retain much sense of the order or importance of the traditional seven. In the Disputed Questions on Evil, however, he adopts both the Gregorian list and order and does not oppose each vice to a particular virtue.51 So, for example, avarice in this work is not opposed to the virtue of generosity (as just described) but instead is considered an offense against justice.

We should not be surprised that the vices list (and the virtues paired with it) isn’t more systematically ordered or standardized, given its roots in the practical experience of spiritual counsel and formation. This history explains why later attempts at formulas or systematic approaches to the list of seven sometimes appear discordant with each other or come across as creative reasons for already existing practices. What still needs explaining, given this backstory, is why and how the list had the extraordinary staying power it did.

Glittering Vices: The Misguided Pursuit of Happiness

Aquinas’s formulation in the Summa clearly reveals the goods at stake in the case of each vice. A glance at the virtues with which he associates the seven vices yields the following conclusions: charity is about love of God and neighbor, while sloth and envy oppose and undercut this; justice and generosity concern money, possessions, and security, while avarice craves and pursues them excessively; magnanimity regards honor and respect, while vainglory is the twisted pursuit of them; and temperance targets pleasures associated with eating, drinking, and sexual activity, while lust and gluttony, each in their own way, seek these goods in increasing measure and for immediate self-gratification. In other words, Richard Foster’s book on three great temptations—Money, Sex, and Power—about sums up sin for most of us.52

Why count these seven as the main sources and most fruitful of the vices? Aquinas explains that they aim at things that most attract human beings, the goods we most long to possess.53 Because each good on the list above holds a close affinity to human fulfillment, we find ourselves perennially tempted to substitute them for true fulfillment as the goal of our lives. The vices offer subtle and deceptive imitations of the fullness of the human good, which we often simply call “happiness.” Lust offers pleasure; avarice promises self-sufficiency; envy and vainglory offer to secure our worth and approval; sloth beckons with comfort; wrath clings to control; pride prizes status, superiority, and power. In the words of one contemporary author, “The simplest definition of an addiction is anything we use to fill the empty place inside of us that belongs to God alone.”54 Augustine too refers to this substitute-fulfillment pattern of sin in his Confessions: “My sin was this, that I looked for pleasure, beauty, and truth not in [God] but in myself and in his other creatures, and the search led me instead to pain, confusion, and error.”55

The vices exercise such attractive power because they parody happiness so well. The goods they promise look so much like true human perfection to us. As Augustine says, “Specious vices have a flawed reflection of beauty.”56 They offer an easy shortcut and a recipe for self-made satisfaction. In a twisted way, our pursuit of the vices shows us attempting to attain goods like love and friendship, provision and security, recognition and approval, comfort and pleasure, status and worth, all by ourselves (and, often, all for ourselves).

When vices distort our character, we seek these goods—and they are genuinely good things—in a misguided or even idolatrous manner: in the wrong way, at the wrong times and wrong places, too intensely, or at the expense of other things of greater value. That’s what makes the vices evil. Our values are misaligned—or, to use Aquinas’s Augustinian terms, our loves are “disordered.” Our desire for and pursuit of these goods does not respect the right ordering of real value. When children grab fistfuls of cookies off the serving plate at a party, their desire for the pleasure of sweets trumps consideration of others (or their own health). Love of pleasure trumps love of neighbor. Yet most of us would concede that food and pleasure are genuine goods, in their due place and appropriately sought. When an employee exaggerates his achievements to make himself look good to his coworkers, he values the approval of others more than truth or trust. The respect and acceptance of others is a genuine good within human social relationships. His pursuit of it goes awry, though, when he is willing to deceive others and subvert other goods in order to achieve it (or a semblance of it).

Ultimately, Augustine and Aquinas note, the vices name our pursuit of finite, created things in place of the goodness of God, our Creator. We were made for union with an infinitely good God. No wonder lesser goods fail to satisfy. Such a dynamic explains why the vices most often lead us to excessive desire: we keep going back for more when created things don’t fulfill us. But too much of a good thing isn’t good for us. When good things are wrongly pursued, sin happens. And when sin accumulates, our character becomes warped and misshapen as well.

A New Look at the Seven Deadly Sins

After all the twists and turns of history, all the disputes over which vices are on the list and why, and all the wrangling between religious and nonreligious conceptions of the vices, the list of seven is still with us—in popular culture as well as in academic and pastoral contexts. What explains the vices’ abiding power to captivate the human imagination? Why do people continue to be fascinated by them?

My answer—based on my own experience of pusillanimity years ago and confirmed by the reactions of those I teach today—is simple: the list maintains its appeal because the vices powerfully articulate distortions of deeply human desires. We see them in ourselves, in our own hearts, and in our own broken lives. As the ancient philosophical schools understood, and as our contemporary therapeutic culture also knows, humans seem to yearn to understand themselves better. We want to understand what makes us tick so that we can live more wisely, more worthily, and with more peace. Ancient and medieval philosophers sought this sort of self-knowledge as part of the ethical life, as the inscription at Delphi—“know thyself”—tells us. The quest for moral and spiritual self-understanding, likewise, powered the mission of Christian thinkers from Evagrius to Aquinas, all of whom dedicated their lives to the “care of souls.”57 Contemporary fascinations with the self might at times be more shallow or self-absorbed, but they strain to illuminate something about ourselves we did not see before, sometimes prompted by a vague sense that something deeper is wrong with us. And they are still ostensibly oriented toward self-improvement of some sort or another. Given the ubiquity of this basic human instinct, the tradition of vices serves as a rich and still relevant frame for better understanding ourselves and our motivations today.

A profounder understanding of the disorder within us yields one further reason to retrieve the vices’ tradition and history: the list developed in a community of Christians whose daily practices and spiritual progress depended on the grace of God. They teach us that identifying and struggling against vice cannot work as a purely human endeavor. Neither will it be successful as an individualistic self-help program. Instead, our pursuit of self-knowledge through studying the vices needs the graced and disciplined formation of the body of believers who together seek to become more and more like Jesus Christ. Like Cassian’s monks, self-knowledge is best sought in this way and for this end:

Looking at [their struggles] as in a mirror and having been taught the causes of and the remedies for the vices by which they are troubled, they will also learn about future contests before they occur, and they will be instructed as to how they should watch out for them, meet them, and fight against them.

As is the case with the most skilled physicians, who not only heal present ills but also confront future ones with shrewd expertise and forestall them with prescriptions and salutary potions, so also these true physicians of souls destroy, with a spiritual conference as with some heavenly medicine, maladies of the heart just as they are about to emerge, not allowing them to grow in the minds of young men but disclosing to them both the causes of the passions that threaten them and the means of acquiring health.58

For the next seven chapters, we will examine each of the capital vices in turn, expecting that as we learn about them, we will also learn about ourselves. Ideally, looking at our character in the mirror offered by the Christian tradition will better enable us, as Cassian intends, to make progress in spiritual formation and, by grace, to turn from vice to greater virtue.

FURTHER REFLECTION

Reflecting on our hopes and dreams as well as our strongest emotions (especially intensely negative ones, such as fear, disappointment, grief, anger, and despair) is a good way to identify excessive attachments. Try using that test as a diagnostic tool for your own patterns of vice and pride. Which good things do you turn to as happiness substitutes?

Which vice (or vices) do you think your community or culture fosters most? In these cases, does that make your personal struggle with this vice more difficult? Do you have other role models who help you practice ways to resist it?

Find a film, novel, TV show, advertisement, or song that exemplifies each vice. Which one was the easiest to find examples of? Spend a day or a week being alert to the prevalence of this pattern of disordered love. What fruit is it bearing?

FURTHER READING

Henri Nouwen, The Way of the Heart: Connecting with God through Prayer, Wisdom, and Silence (New York: Seabury, 1981; repr., New York: Ballantine, 2003).

The Sayings of the Fathers: The Alphabetical Collection, trans. Benedicta Ward, SLG (Kalamazoo, MI: Cistercian Publications/Liturgical Press, 1975).

cCP

3 
Vainglory

Image Is Everything

I lust after recognition, I am desperate to win all the little merit badges and trinkets of my profession, and I am of less real use in this world than any good cleaning lady.

—Garrison Keillor, Christian Century

Glory is fleeting, but obscurity is forever.

—Napoleon Bonaparte

Aptly enough, the vice of vainglory enjoys a good reputation in contemporary culture. Every waiting room in America stocks a shelf of magazines dedicated to the cult of “beautiful people.” They captivate our imagination, and in return we offer them our adulation, striving to emulate their looks and lifestyles. Advertising and social media feed on and foster vainglorious impulses in us from an early age: we learn about cool fashion and foods by watching TV, teens dream of having YouTube channels with enough views to garner fame (and with it, corporate sponsors), and adults project their successful professional selves and profile pictures on LinkedIn and Facebook. But vainglory also shows up in church. Worship leaders and pastors stand in the spotlight, worried that their culturally formed congregations will come expecting a polished performance. For people in the public eye, and the rest of us too, maintaining an image with the right approval ratings is a way of life, even a respectable profession. Even if we don’t take too many selfies, we can find ourselves wanting to be (or to be seen as) what the band R.E.M. once called “Shiny Happy People.”

When I ask my students to name a celebrity, they can instantly rattle off a list of twenty. When I ask them to say who their personal heroes are, they respond with puzzled silence and furrowed brows. After they think about it awhile, a few name their grandparents (or parents, or a youth pastor or mentor) as the people they admire or want to be like. But even in the next town over, nobody knows the names of these heroes; none of them have made a splash in the national news. When we compare what celebrities are renowned for and what our heroes are admired for, we typically find a chasm between the two—people whose glory far outstrips the value of the goods for which they receive it and people whose worth far outstrips any glory they will ever receive. Pondering that gap shines a spotlight on the vice of vainglory.

We don’t have to be famous to embrace the goal of being well known and well liked, publicly approved of and applauded. To wrestle with the vice of vainglory therefore requires reflection on how much the desire for attention and acclaim can dominate—and damage—our lives.

Vainglory by Any Other Name . . .

Most of us will immediately recognize this vice. Perhaps we will even find it painfully familiar, despite having never heard its rather archaic name before. Vainglory is the excessive and disordered desire for recognition and approval from others. Whether or not we are successful in gaining this approval, we all know what it is like to desire recognition for ourselves or our accomplishments. A deep part of us longs to be known and approved of, and to have this publicly acknowledged. Take, for example, a stay-at-home parent’s mundane toil, or a missionary’s lonely years of faithfulness abroad, or the support staff of a prominent business or political leader. Most of their labor is invisible to others. Sometimes it doesn’t even get counted as work—as in, “Do you work or do you just stay home with the kids?” They may find their efforts significant and their commitments worthwhile but still feel like they are the only ones who ever acknowledge that. That being invisible and taken for granted is painful to them bears witness to the human need to be seen and affirmed. A child on the playground shouts to his parent, “Look at me!”; a quiet hug from a friend says she’s glad that you’ve remembered her birthday; an inmate longs to be remembered and called by his first name; a nursing-home resident lights up when she has a visitor to listen to her stories. In view of such examples, Aquinas observes: “It seems to belong to a natural appetite that one wish one’s goodness to become known.” Moreover, he argues, “we can rightly . . . desire to please other human beings.”1 But this desire so easily goes askew. The flattery of others, writes William Ian Miller, is “narcotic and addicting. It preys on two desperate and inescapable desires: to be thought well of by others and to think well of ourselves. . . . We desire and need approbation so badly that we seem more than willing to accept counterfeit coinage as real.”2

When caught in the vice of vainglory, we want acclaim too much—so much, in fact, that we will accept it whether or not we deserve it. Home, school, the athletic field, the workplace, even church: there seems to be no shortage of places offering abundant temptations to vainglory. On the other hand, as the early church fathers pointed out, we are vulnerable to vainglory perhaps especially when we have virtue and good character, for this vice continues to haunt us whether our virtue goes viral or our well-deserved worthiness is overlooked by others.

In his Confessions, Augustine tells a famous story about his teenage years. One night he and some friends were hanging out with not enough to do. They decided to steal pears from a neighbor. Since they neither needed nor even wanted the pears—they ended up throwing them all to the pigs—they stole them just for the fun of it. Reflecting later on his crime, Augustine ponders what fueled his desire to sin. In a crucial part of his confession, he notes that he would not have done it alone. He wanted to impress his friends. “As soon as the words are spoken, ‘Let’s go and do it,’” he confesses, “one is ashamed not to be shameless.”3 The same desire for approval and the corresponding fear of shame drove Augustine to excel in school and become an accomplished rhetorician. Even then, however, he cared more that his speeches in the law courts had the proper style and erudition to win him applause than that they conveyed truth or brought justice for the human lives that hung in the balance.4

Vainglory has similar forms today.5 For example, we find it difficult to imagine someone who has never exaggerated something she has done or made up something about herself to impress those listening. Or perhaps we tell a witty tale, sharing something bad or false about another person, to get our friends to see how funny or entertaining we are. Or we do something good and anticipate that others will notice it (perhaps even because we anticipate that others will notice). Or on the flip side, we do something good that others did not notice, and we feel disappointed. We may even do something wrong, illegal, or foolish because we want attention from a certain crowd. Many of us habitually stretch or streamline the truth to seem better than we are or to avoid criticism. Others of us worry about a performance or task because we feel overly concerned about gaining the approval of those judging it. We invest ourselves in building a reputation based on shallow, trivial things. Or we do something good, get attention for it, and then take all the credit for ourselves, without the slightest nod to mentors, supporters, or God, the Giver of all good and perfect gifts. We may not know these things under the name “vainglory,” but our lives remain a glaring illustration of this vice.

Vainglory and Its Vicious Cousins

As familiar as these examples of vainglory may be, we can’t properly diagnose our struggle with vainglory unless we understand how it names a reality distinct from a host of other related vices. So let’s first do a bit of sorting.

Vainglory is easily confused with pride, the root of all the vices. Especially because they often compete for a single place on the list of the seven deadly sins, these two vices tend to be conflated. Evagrius and Cassian considered pride and vainglory major vices on their lists of eight. Later, Gregory the Great and Thomas Aquinas included vainglory in their canonical lists of seven but rooted those seven branches in pride as the ultimate source of them all. Contemporary lists, however, typically include pride among the seven and fail even to mention vainglory. A 20/20 television series on the seven deadly sins counted pride as a member of the list but defined it as “vanity,” confusing the desire for preeminence with the desire to have that preeminence displayed to others. The Seven Deadly Sins board game names “vanity” as a member of the list but associates it, somewhat confusingly, with both the recent cosmetic surgery boom (which fits vainglory) and the color purple (which is traditionally related to pride).6 We will follow the early Christian tradition and leave vainglory on the list, because it seems right to include the desire for honor and glory, recognition and approval, among the great temptations of the human heart.

We might also easily confuse vainglory and pride because they both count as “spiritual” vices—that is, vices with a spiritual rather than a bodily good as the object of their disordered love. Their objects of love further indicate their close connection. Pride excessively concerns excellence itself (i.e., my excelling others); vainglory, by contrast, concerns primarily the display or manifestation of my excellence.7 If that weren’t enough to complicate their relationship, pride-vainglory “hybrid” cases may be the most common way these vices show up in our lives. Like Gaston in Beauty and the Beast, even if we think we are the best or most deserving guy in town, we also want our excellence acknowledged (in a rousing song by townspeople in the local pub, perhaps). Contrast that with cases of each vice in isolation. A solely prideful person, who is not vainglorious, thinks himself so superior to others that seeking their good opinion of him lies beneath him. Think of the highbrow Mr. Darcy in the early chapters of Pride and Prejudice. A solely vainglorious person, who is not prideful, would do anything for a little limelight, even if it meant she had to stoop a little lower to get her share of sensational notice. Think of the flashy but fabricated publications of Professor Gilderoy Lockhart in Harry Potter and the Chamber of Secrets. For most of us, most of the time, however, the two vices come as a package. Our life’s adventures are more satisfying when filmed and shared, just as our child’s spot on an elite sports team or our own completion of a road race warrants a sticker on the back window of our SUV. More mundanely, we often feel a bit flat if we have a really productive day at work but no one to tell about it.

What makes vainglory distinct from pride, then, is love of “the show.” Prideful people want superior status more than anything else. They value being “number one,” to the point of seeking greatness in ways that appropriately belong only to God. The vainglorious, on the other hand, seek whatever will bring in the most attention and applause, whether it is excellent and deserving or not. Pride names the desire for genuinely exalted status; vainglory, a desire for recognition, publicity, and acclaim. If we had a chance to possess some outstanding quality, say, but knew that it would remain hidden, the prideful would be tempted, but the glory-lovers would walk away. The goal of the vainglorious is to win others’ notice, to have people nod and smile approvingly when they walk by. Pushed to its limits, vainglory disposes us to be more concerned with our reputation (what others think about us) than with what we really are. For the vainglorious, image is everything.

“Glory” can be a tricky word for us. Some Christians understand it as something that can rightly be given only to God. In one sense of the word, they are correct, because all goodness ultimately has its source in him. In the Christian tradition, however, the term “glory” more broadly marks anything good that’s shown and made known. Glory, according to Aquinas, is simply goodness made manifest.8 For example, Psalm 8 uses “glory” in both senses—the glory due to God and the glory that human beings have. Common usage attributes glory to other created things as well. We aptly call a beautiful sunset over Lake Michigan or a breathtaking mountain view “glorious.” Human feats and qualities can also be glorious, like an athletic achievement or a virtuosic musical performance. Used broadly, glory means any goodness that, when displayed, garners our notice and appreciation.

In Aquinas’s account, glory also differs from honor.9 The prideful person’s excessive desire for excellence can initially be difficult to distinguish from an excessive desire for honor or glory, because our ordinary uses of the terms overlap. Aquinas distinguishes them this way: excellence (having some superior quality) makes someone worthy of and apt to receive honor (respect and esteem from good judges), and the natural result of being thus honored is glory, “for the reason a person loves to be honored and praised is that [s]he thinks thereby to acquire a certain renown in the knowledge of others.”10 Imagine your excellent athletic win on the track garnering all-conference honors: that’s the reason the news publicizes the story and publishes your picture with it, so now everyone knows and lauds you. Vice creeps in when we take innocent cases of well-earned worthiness and push them to extremes.

This trio of desirable goods can also be teased apart, though. Unlike the vainglorious, those craving honor seek it not from just anyone, but rather from those with the requisite taste or expertise. Like the prideful, who often have a good eye for genuine excellence, the honor-seeker often has better taste in friends than those with vainglory—she wants the honor and esteem of the “right people.” When excessive, this desire inclines us to seek honor over other, more deserving goods. The high-achieving student values and seeks to win the esteem of her teachers, of the National Honor Society, or of the college board administering the SAT. But she must avoid making that honor more important than the education for which she earned it; she must not seek high marks purely for prestige. Vainglory, by contrast, merely inclines us to seek a big round of applause from anyone willing to watch. Vainglory also contrasts with pride: the proud person wants to be the director of the best show ever produced, and the honor-seeker desires rave reviews crediting her work as director from a certain circle of critics, but the glory-lover will happily sink to new depths of shocking spectacle as long as ratings will be high. Neither excellence nor honor nor glory is itself a bad thing. Their connections, however, illuminate the signature spiritual dangers afoot: our initial pursuit of something genuinely good for the right reasons can spin, spiral, and slide into vice. Like excellent athletes who turn to steroid use, or gifted scholars or pastors who plagiarize as their career advances, those who initially love and achieve good things through genuine effort can edge toward vainglory as the pressure mounts. As they find it harder and harder to meet expectations of continued greatness from their admiring audiences, and as they get attached to regular recognition from their successes, they also find they can’t let go of the acclaim they’ve won. Vainglory now tempts them to maintain their good name through falsity.

Rather than wanting excessively to be superior, like the prideful, or seeking excessively to be honored for our notable achievements, in vainglory we seek mainly the “manifestation of excellence.” That is, we want more than anything to be well and widely known. The name of this vice aptly includes the term “glory,” for glorious display is the end sought by those with this vice.11 It’s all about the show. The vainglorious primarily desire attention, approval, and applause—preferably for all to see.

What Is Worthy of Glory?

Vainglory pinpoints not just any glory-seeking, of course, but rather its disordered form. Glory-seeking goes awry in many ways, but the name “vainglory” spotlights ways that are vain or empty. Lady Gaga—herself a self-proclaimed “master of the art of fame”—parodies stardom and its baggage in songs like “Paparazzi” and “Starstruck” on her early album The Fame, even as she illustrates its contemporary excesses in her own elaborately costumed public appearances. Celebrities seek and receive glory not (or at least not only) for genuine talent but also for red-carpet appearances at award ceremonies, covered in excruciating detail by the media. Stadiums herald athletes with fireworks and flag-waving at the start of a game. Closer to home, people glory in more mundane matters: the greenest lawn, the flattering cut of a new dress or suit, the cleanliness of the kitchen, a successful diet, possessions that signal social status, or (in my community, which touts frugality as a virtue) the most effective use of cost-saving coupons. Do we choose our wardrobe for comfort and function, or for styles that will elicit attention and affirmation from those who see us—often at the cost of comfort and function? Worse yet, are we sometimes willing to “fake it till we make it”? Celebrity status aside, faking it might mean having a house we can’t afford, an embellished résumé, or the hair color of someone half our age.

Swept away by a desire to be thought well of, the vainglorious neglect questions like these: How much do these things really matter? To what lengths should we go to impress? Whose approval should we desire most, and why?

In the film The Greatest Showman, P. T. Barnum poignantly considers what remains in the rubble of his life when the allure of fame fades away. After spending too many years chasing glory, he acknowledges the dark side of this pursuit. Returning to his senses and to his family, he promises them that “from now on / These eyes will not be blinded by the lights.”12

In contemporary Western life, the cosmetic and fashion industries make billions in profits every year capitalizing on our weakness for vainglory. But make no mistake: this vice does not plague only women. It’s also behind the tendency to define ourselves by the expensive sports cars we own, to enjoy the reputation earned by lucrative jobs with important titles, and to bolster our masculine credibility with swaggering locker-room talk that echoes Augustine’s confession:

Among my peer group, I was ashamed not to be equally guilty of shameful behavior when I heard them boasting of their sexual exploits. Their pride was the more aggressive, the more debauched their acts were; they derived pleasure not merely from the lust of the act but also from the admiration it evoked. . . . I went deeper into vice to avoid being despised, and when there was no act by admitting to which I could rival my depraved companions, I used to pretend that I had done things I had not done at all, so that my innocence should not lead my companions to scorn my lack of courage, and lest my chastity be taken as a mark of inferiority.13

Why does the typical description of our past athletic exploits smack more of fiction than fact? Empty of real substance, we vaingloriously revel in half-truths and the latest fleeting fad. Good looks and athleticism fade quickly with age, the fashions of the day have an arbitrariness that defies reason, career titles are pretentiously euphemistic, and a perfectly decorated, perfectly clean house does not make a home. Yet the vainglorious pursue these things as if their personal worth and happiness depended on them. Saturated with savvy marketing, the present advertising culture runs on glory-driven fuel and encourages us to run on its fumes. If we step back for a moment, we might find it disconcerting to think how much of our lives we spend keeping up appearances to impress lots of other people for qualities that we don’t have or that don’t really matter.

Soli Deo Gloria

Aquinas opposes vainglory to the virtue he calls “magnanimity”—a virtue that, like vainglory, is unfamiliar to most contemporary people.14 Magnanimous people stretch themselves to inspiring acts of virtue as something to which God has called them. Their achievements genuinely deserve honor and turn our thoughts to the glory of God because they obviously aren’t something anyone could have done without grace. Magnanimous people are luminous, alight with God’s beauty and goodness.15 Their works draw attention to that glory, a glory that outshines the person and his or her act. When we witness such transcendent greatness, we are ushered with wordless gratitude into the cathedral of God’s presence in human action. Victor Hugo’s Les Misérables shows us two characters whose lives are marked by magnanimity: first the bishop, who expectedly and graciously pardons Jean Valjean, and then from that moment of grace onward, Jean, who sacrificially cares for others. Acts of this virtue, whether public or private, large or small, inspire not empty glory but genuine awe. A magnanimous action may be something great in the world’s eyes (e.g., a heroic deed, a life of philanthropy, a gift of compassion), or it may be something that would puzzle the paparazzi (e.g., an act of grace, sacrifice, or forgiveness born of great suffering; or Mary’s quiet fiat at the annunciation [see Luke 1:26–38]; or Mother Teresa’s famous counsel to “do little things with great love”). Magnanimous virtue stretches human power to its limits and beyond. It gives us “city shining on a hill” moments, in which we glimpse graced obedience and shalom breaking through darkness like the dawn. The vainglorious, by comparison, labor for the artificial illumination of the limelight and the canned applause of a sitcom audience. They achieve only the shallow veneer of magnanimity’s true substance. Vainglory gives us an ephemeral high instead of the substantial reward of virtue.

The contrast with magnanimity makes clearer what’s wrong with vainglory: not the goodness of glory itself nor the goods of excellence and honor themselves. Every year at our college’s graduation ceremony, after the last student sits down with diploma in hand, the president invites the audience to congratulate the graduates. Parents and friends surrounding the graduates respond by celebrating with a thunderously joyful noise—including cheers, bleacher-pounding, and applause with a standing ovation. The graduates have worked hard to achieve excellence; they have received high marks and earned respect from the faculty, and at the end of their four years, the faculty and family who love them and encouraged them along the way take delight in publicly recognizing their accomplishments with great pomp and circumstance, much clapping and cheering, heartfelt joy, and even tears. We call pride and vainglory vices because they disorder our pursuits of good things. We veer off course in vainglory not because we have the human need for recognition and approval but because of the excessive and empty ways we seek to satisfy this desire. The glory itself isn’t the main problem; it’s the vanity of how we seek it and what we seek to find it in.

In a pattern fitting many vices, vainglory’s disorder consists either in desiring glory for the wrong objects or in the wrong manner or for the wrong end. Worse yet, we can seek glory in several disordered ways all at once.

In the first category of cases—desiring glory for an inappropriate object—our glory-seeking is focused on “vain things,” where “vain” literally means “empty of glory.” The vainglorious can seek recognition and approval for evanescent or superficial goods—things not worth much glory in general (e.g., a fancy manicure or cool haircut) or things that garner acclaim now but aren’t worth much from an eternal perspective (e.g., socially respectable worldly goods, such as a prestigious job or an Ivy League degree). They might even seek glory for qualities they do not actually have. Fakery must be the emptiest sort of glory-seeking possible, for in it we glory in nothing but the appearance or outward pretense of goodness. Sometimes we even seek glory for evil things—as rebels, revered mafiosi, or extreme risk-takers. Like adolescents building credibility with peers by defying authority, or Augustine bragging about his sexual exploits (real or invented) among his friends, this type of vainglory might well earn us the label “notorious vainglorious.” When we seek empty glory, even evil things can win us the right kind of credibility with the wrong kind of crowd.

In the second category of cases—seeking admiration in inappropriate ways—the desire for glory turns excessive because we want approval and applause so badly that we will do whatever it takes to get it, even if that means misusing otherwise good things or misleading others. For instance, we might gain glory through illicit means or deceive others into giving us undeserved approval. Whether or not the object of our desire is worth the glory we receive for it, the manner of our pursuit or desire for it is excessive, inappropriate, and disordered. This sort of glory-seeker may be an employee who blames her boss for her own incompetence in hopes of gaining the acceptance of her coworkers. Or it could be a student who cheats on a test to get a better grade and win the approval of his parents or teachers. Dating and romance often deal cheaply with the truth in exchange for affirmation and enhanced attractive power. Even a polished musical performance in a religious service could provide “cover” for compliment-seeking or reputation-building motives when disguised as worship. When it’s not what we are offering but how we are “offering” it that’s problematic, vainglory can toxically mix our motives, tempting us away from sincerity, in church and anywhere else.16

Glory-seeking also gets twisted when problems arise with the audience from whom we desire glory, although this symptom often tends to signal (and overlap with) problems covered by the first two types. Which audiences we seek approval and applause from yields telltale clues about our motives. For example, it might be perfectly appropriate to share a compliment you received for a good day’s work with your spouse, but the same sharing would be suspect if you frequently post lists of your accomplishments or accolades on social media for a thousand other friends. Cassian notes that we can even play to a fictional audience envisioned in our own minds.17 Retreating to the desert away from the public eye could therefore offer no guarantee that you could beat vainglory. If real applause is not available, why not manufacture some (and rehearse and replay it) in your imagination?

The worst sort of vainglory, however, according to Aquinas and others, occurs when we fail to give due glory to God as the source of our good.18 We might call this glorying “in vain,” because in such cases we seek glory for the wrong end, aim, or purpose. Glory-seekers of this type fail to recognize their role as recipients and stewards of the good things they have rather than as their ultimate source. Their glory is not exclusively their own but must be directed beyond themselves to God.

It’s worth exploring this last form of vainglory further, since it tends to arise when people receive glory for things that genuinely deserve glory and still find themselves entangled in disordered desire. Moreover, everyone from Cassian to Aquinas tags this form of vainglory as the most morally serious kind. When they receive renown and praise from others for some good gift from God, the vainglorious either glory in the gift while forgetting its Giver and source, or they glory in the affirmation and applause of others over (or against) God’s approval. Augustine, for example, possessed a truly impressive intellectual acumen and praiseworthy rhetorical skill, but the way he used these talents revealed his desire to win recognition from others (parents, friends, colleagues, and audiences) at the expense of God’s commendation.19 Like Augustine, we can have such a strong desire to boost our worldly approval ratings that in our choices and actions we end up either ignoring or spurning the gift-giver who makes them and their use possible.

Later in his life, while writing The City of God, Augustine uses vainglory to name the ultimate disorder behind the otherwise genuinely admirable acts of the Romans. “Glory they most ardently loved: for it they wished to live, for it they did not hesitate to die,” he writes.20 Even the Romans’ noble pursuit of virtue fell prey to vainglory. They cultivated virtue ultimately to enhance and secure the greatness of their own reputations. They sought after goodness for their own glory, rather than for God’s glory.21 Like much of our people-pleasing good behavior, the Romans’ vainglory drove people to strive for greater virtue. But whether we neglect to acknowledge God and arrogate his glory for ourselves, or whether we value human over divine approval, the end for which we seek such glory is vain.

All expressions of vainglory, however implicitly, include the desire for our own glory, by whatever means we manage to get it. When we fail to refer glory to God for the true goods he has given us, we therefore encounter not merely an additional type of vainglory but the disorder behind all forms of vainglory. Our failure shows us to be both unjust and ungrateful, because we are so busy seeking to manifest our own goodness that we deny or ignore its fundamental cause and source. Praise for the gift fittingly redounds to the glory of the Giver. Augustine famously divides those who seek to suppress this truth from those who live acknowledging the ways all goodness connects them to God:

Accordingly, two cities have been formed by two loves: the earthly by the love of self, even to the contempt of God; the heavenly by the love of God, even to the contempt of self. The former, in a word, glories in itself; the latter in the Lord. For the one seeks glory from [human beings]; but the greatest glory of the other is God, the witness of conscience. The one lifts up its head in its own glory; the other says to its God, “Thou art my glory, and the lifter up of mine head” [Ps. 3:3].22

To give up vainglory thus requires us to relinquish our place as the center of attention—in our pursuit of goodness, in our religious practice, in our everyday accomplishments at work and play. We must admit that from beginning to end, “it’s not about me.” Johann Sebastian Bach is remembered not only for writing beautiful music but also for writing on every manuscript soli deo gloria (glory to God alone). Affirming our gifts and the gifts of others, appreciating genuine goodness wherever we find it, and marveling at beauty and magnificence, whether large or small—these ways of celebrating goods with genuine value should all ultimately point us back to God.

Our typical reaction when a child is born gives us another example of a virtuous response. In the moments after birth, parents find themselves simply awash in waves of profoundest gratitude and overwhelmed by the miraculous wonder of the tiny bundle laying in their arms. What an amazing gift! What a wonder and a blessing. For that moment, at least, we might forget ourselves entirely, breathing our glorias in tearfully grateful, hushed, and awestruck tones. That frame of mind contrasts sharply with the parade of occasions in years to follow where vainglory whispers boastful suggestions in our ears and tempts us to show off pictures of our nice-looking children (but only when they are looking nice), as if that would be to our credit anyway. The amazing goodness of a child directs our attention to the Giver of all perfect gifts in the first case, while in the second, showing off the gift strategically places us in the spotlight. This example reveals how vainglory centers our gaze on ourselves and narrows our vision of goodness, rather than allowing created goodness to be fully embraced as a gift from a God from whom all blessings flow.

Hypocrisy and Onions

Jesus directly confronts vainglory in his Sermon on the Mount. Rather than picking on easy targets like celebrities, he brings this vice uncomfortably close to home, warning especially against the religious form of vainglory. To put it bluntly, he is addressing us. Aquinas quotes John Chrysostom to make the same point: “While other vices find their abode even in the servants of the devil, vainglory finds a place even in the servants of Christ.”23 Who among us hasn’t been tempted to try to look like a better Christian than we really are to win approval and acclaim from others? Christ’s sermon targets the cherished, self-serving fantasies of the vainglorious churchgoer: “Beware of practicing your piety before others in order to be seen by them . . . so that [you] may be praised by others . . . [and] seen by others. . . . Do not heap up empty phrases . . . [in order to] be heard because of [your] many words” (Matt. 6:1–2, 5, 7). As Christ makes clear in that passage, the applause of others is the sole reward such religious appearances will ever earn. A life spent craving human awards of glory and praise, full of empty-hearted acts designed to curate the right sort of attention from the right sort of audience, will bring only the fleeting applause the world can give. Its approval lasts only as long as you can put on a good show.24 The desert father Evagrius offers this counsel:

Do not sell your labours for people’s esteem, nor hand over the future glory for the sake of paltry fame, for human esteem settles in the dust (cf. Ps. 7:6) and its reputation is extinguished on earth, but the glory of virtue abides for eternity.25

Set the seal of silence on the spices of your ascetic labours, lest unfastened by your tongue they be stolen by esteem.26

Especially in our religious practice and devotion, hypocrisy—traditionally marked as a vice that springs from vainglory—trades truthfulness for a false front or respectable reputation, all in God’s holy name. Jesus reserves some of his harshest words for the Pharisees because they cared more about the outward observances of piety than a heart truly dedicated to worshiping God: “Woe to you, scribes and Pharisees, hypocrites! For you are like whitewashed tombs, which on the outside look beautiful, but inside they are full of the bones of the dead and of all kinds of filth. So you also on the outside look righteous to others, but inside you are full of hypocrisy and lawlessness” (Matt. 23:27–28).

Hypocrisy naturally grows from a heart captivated by vainglory. We come to church each week to worship in clothing intended more to impress others than to fit us for worship. When we serve, we find false modesty easier than genuine hiddenness. We cherish our reputation as pastors who change lives with our powerful preaching. Or maybe we simply lament that we can’t pull off such a “witness” but still secretly wish we could.

Like you, I prayed for my family to look perfect in the pew, but my vainglorious dreams of model parenthood were easily shattered—and not just in church. Our four young (sometimes unruly and stain-covered) children kept blowing my cover—in the supermarket checkout line, during family portrait sessions, or worse, as we shuffled loudly into worship services (noticeably late, after I had just angrily shouted “Behave!” at everyone in the car). Such is the stuff of ordinary life; however, such public humiliations stung because of the vainglorious way my ego hung on my “Christian” image and reputation as a decent mother. But perhaps there is strange grace in having our masks stripped off. (At the very least, it made me more gracious toward other struggling parents.)

In our professional lives, we can identify parallel attempts at hypocrisy—some more successful than others. One cartoon shows a manager with a pie chart at an office meeting, admitting, “At this juncture in my presentation, I’d like to dispense with the illusion of coherence.”27 Sometimes God himself calls us out. Evagrius describes God’s discipline for the desert dweller who, after attaining some small spiritual gain, “acquires the horse of vainglory and rushes into the cities, getting its fill of the lavish praise accorded to its repute.” He faces the humiliation of falling into some previously vanquished and shameful sin (usually lust) for all to see. Doesn’t that sound eerily familiar? The lesson for all Christian leaders in the limelight, says this desert father, is that we should be careful how we share our virtue until we have learned to value humility over the opinions of others.28 In the end, whether your reputation is measured by worldly success or religious standards, and whether your self-image hangs on being a Hollywood celebrity or a holy Christian saint, Carly Simon might sing of all of us: “You’re so vain, I bet you think this [vice] is about you, don’t you?”29

Besides hypocrisy, the tradition also names boastfulness and the “love of novelties” as offspring vices of vainglory. Both are typical means to attract favorable attention. Boastfulness directly draws others’ attention and approval by verbally showcasing our good qualities. Bragging, talking ourselves up, and dominating conversation with stories about ourselves manifests our excellence in obvious ways to others. Showing off visually displays with action what big talk displays with words. Instagram, Facebook, and the like have merely created the newest venues for displaying this ancient vice and creating publicity for our private lives. Our culture loves its own self-made, self-starring spectacles.

Another of vainglory’s signature offspring vices, “love of novelties,” sports an archaic medieval name for the familiar phenomenon of having or doing the latest, greatest thing to win recognition and applause. Trending YouTube videos, stunts from the newest extreme sport, and everything ever documented in Ripley’s Believe It or Not! all count, as does any advertisement that catches our eye with the tagline “new and improved!” Love of novelties also covers being the kid on the bus with the brand-new toy based on a just-released hit movie or the first among our friends to vacation in the most recently popularized exotic location. In fact, it’s a testimony to our love of novelties that I find it nearly impossible to list examples of this phenomenon that won’t already be out-of-date by the time the book is published. (It’s as challenging as trying to keep references to the latest iPhone up-to-date!) In old and new forms, the driving desire to use novelty and newness to captivate others’ attention plays the same tired old vainglory game that Gregory the Great diagnosed more than a millennium ago.

Novelty-seeking typically falls on the shallow end of vainglory’s common symptoms, but more serious forms also regularly threaten. Vainglory, according to Cassian and other early teachers in the church, presents a special problem for Christians who are making progress in the spiritual life—that is, especially for the virtuous and holy. The more progress we make, and the more virtue we attain, the more goodness we have for others to notice and admire. And if we lack an audience, we are often happy to supply the part ourselves. Cassian tells an entertaining story of two monks, both living in solitude in the desert. The elder monk went to visit the other and heard him speaking aloud in his cell. He listened in for a moment, wondering which psalm his brother was reciting with such passion. It turned out, however, that the younger monk was absorbed in a vainglorious fantasy, playing aloud the part of a great preacher exhorting his imaginary congregation with great flourish, while relishing their rapt attention and admiration of his wisdom. The visiting monk knocked on the door, and when the “great preacher” stopped his “sermon” to answer it, he sheepishly asked how long ago his visitor had arrived. Bemused, the elder monk replied, “Just in time for the benediction.” Evagrius likewise warns against vainglorious self-congratulation in our daydreams about our great virtue: “One gets a fantasy of the priesthood and then spends the entire day thinking through the things that that involves; or as if the charism of healing were about to be granted, one sees in advance the miracles that happen and fantasizes about the people who will be healed, the honors coming from the brothers, and the gifts brought by outsiders, all those that come from Egypt and also from abroad, drawn by our renown.”30

Vainglory does not attack only when our virtues are mere fantasies. We also face it when our gains in virtue are real. Cassian warns that even when “we could be not overcome in direct combat with our adversary [the devil], we are conquered by the loftiness of our triumph” against him.31 Such vainglory is like an onion, the elders warn. You strip away one layer of vainglory only to find that that very achievement provides fodder for a further temptation to the same vice.32 “It is difficult to escape vainglory,” writes Evagrius, “for what you do to rid yourself of it becomes for you a new source of vainglory.”33 Perhaps our only consolation is confronting a smaller onion, which we must continue to peel with tears in our eyes.

A Living Doxology

The apostle Paul quotes Jeremiah, saying, “‘Let the one who boasts, boast in the Lord.’ For it is not those who commend themselves that are approved, but those whom the Lord commends” (2 Cor. 10:17–18). Real disciples bear fruit, says Jesus, fruit that glorifies God (John 15:8). What would it look like to seek God’s approval and praise first and foremost, instead of human glory? This may be more complicated than it first appears.

In his Sermon on the Mount, Jesus calls his followers to be witnesses for him, saying, “You are the light of the world. A city built on a hill cannot be hid. No one after lighting a lamp puts it under the bushel basket, but on the lampstand, and it gives light to all in the house. In the same way, let your light shine before others, so that they may see your good works and give glory to your Father in heaven” (Matt. 5:14–16). But only a few verses later, he cautions,

Beware of practicing your piety before others in order to be seen by them; for then you have no reward from your Father in heaven. So whenever you give alms, do not sound a trumpet before you, as the hypocrites do in the synagogues and in the streets, so that they may be praised by others. Truly I tell you, they have received their reward. But when you give alms, do not let your left hand know what your right hand is doing, so that your alms may be done in secret; and your Father who sees in secret will reward you. (6:1–4)

As in Augustine’s discussion of the virtues of the Romans, we must ultimately direct any acclaim for a virtuous act beyond ourselves, whether implicitly or explicitly. When we show our “light” to the world, we are not shining a spotlight on ourselves but illuminating the source of our goodness for others to see.

What motivates our disordered glory-seeking? Looking at vainglory’s roots provides clues about why we feel driven by this vice. Perhaps you don’t think vainglory is a spiritual problem for you because you would do anything to avoid being in the limelight. But the allure of vainglory likely entices all of us one way or another—even those of us who seem to be avoiding the public eye. For example, many of us are convinced deep down we will never really be good enough to shine without artificial lighting and a little extra polish. Maybe we are faking it because we fear we will never really make it. Vainglory grips us, but in a fearful form. In Miller’s words, we live in “a world of posing and shams, anxieties of exposure, and a fear that the genuine may be just another sham whose cover is too tough to be blown.”34 Our fear of exposure can lead us to clutch at more control than we ought, making sure our picture-perfect masks protect us from publicly revealing our obvious flaws.

Vainglory also shows itself in an arrogant form. Pursuing virtue often requires us to give up what the world calls success and happiness in order to achieve real peace. Being liked, respectable, and popular don’t always coincide with simplicity, integrity, and goodness.35 Vainglory, in its religious or nonreligious forms, serves those who prefer to take a shortcut to these real goods. Why work at actually being pious, or face difficulty and the possibility of failure, if we can pull off the mere reputation more easily? Or perhaps reputation-building does take significant effort, but not effort that is painful, since it allows us to remain masters of our own lives, comfortably surrounded by self-made means of affirmation. Devoting ourselves to a life that glorifies God and enjoys him forever36 takes humility and the yielding of control because we must receive our own good and others’ good as a gift. As the fantasies exposed by the desert fathers show, we would rather create and manage our own image than try to live up to being image-bearers of God—never a project known for its popularity. We should not be surprised, then, to find pride—the desire to control our own happiness, whether from fear or overconfidence—at the root of vainglory after all. In fact, Gregory calls vainglory pride’s “immediate offspring.”37

Tragically, vainglory wins applause and approval at the price of distancing us from others. Relationships cannot flourish when we dupe others or use them as sources of flattery, as a dull background against which we can shine, or as useful props in our reputation-building program. Human as well as divine relationships flourish in the context of truthfulness and sincerity. The publican, not the pridefully polished Pharisee, receives forgiveness because he exposes what he truly is to God. So too the deepest love flourishes in the openness of marriage and friendship, not the best-face-forward games of dating and partying. Communion with others, costly though it is, counts as vainglory’s greatest casualty. Like other sins, this vice isolates us, rather than bringing us into loving fellowship with God and other people. Vainglory’s contrasting virtues include integrity, humility, and gratitude. Pursuing these virtues keeps us real. Relinquishing our reputation-seeking yields a significant reward: we have a chance to be truly known and loved.

It’s ironic that the art of impressing others and gaining applause usually requires carefully hiding ourselves as much as it involves showing ourselves off to advantage. To be lauded by others, we cannot let them see our flaws, our failures, and our dark sides. Winning their approval and praise requires not only that we keep up a false facade but also that we carefully conceal the ugly truth about ourselves. Before we leave the house in the morning, we need to put on our game face and superhero costume and keep up the charade all day, without letting the cracks show. The sort of acceptance and approval this strategy wins us rightly feels fragile and hollow, not to mention exhausting. When others give us affirmation, then, they are affirming only the mask we let them see. Our deep human need for full and unconditional welcome remains unfulfilled and unsatisfied.

The great vices all make the list of seven because they pretend to fill the deepest and most significant of human desires. Vainglory provides a cheap substitute for true fulfillment of the human desire to be profoundly known by another person—to be known by name, for who we truly are—and to be loved just that way. Scripture itself acknowledges this need.

But now thus says the LORD,

he who created you, O Jacob,

he who formed you, O Israel:

Do not fear, for I have redeemed you;

I have called you by name, you are mine.

(Isa. 43:1)

O LORD, you have searched me and known me. . . .

For it was you who formed my inward parts;

you knit me together in my mother’s womb.

(Ps. 139:1, 13)

For the people the world renders invisible, whose good has been overlooked and neglected,38 as for all of us, this word from God is a comfort and balm. God promises to fulfill our deep desire to be known, to be acknowledged, and to be accepted with unconditional love. Before we make any effort to bolster approval ratings for ourselves, God has already freely given us what we need. Who could refuse such a welcome? From where, then, does the inclination come to resist that gift? From the knowledge that to receive that gift means we live by God’s standards of honor and glory and that we must relinquish our own picture of what makes us okay in the world’s eyes?

Augustine, in his Confessions, makes a further claim. Not only are we made to be known and loved, but we are made for praising our Creator—for knowing and loving God. A life spent turned inward, praising ourselves or seeking our own praise, will thereby stunt our growth and flourishing as human beings. To see truly who God is and how deeply he loves us evokes our praise and worship. To love God moves us to give him glory. Augustine says,

“You are great, Lord, and highly to be praised” (Ps. 47:2); “great is your power and your wisdom is unmeasurable” (Ps. 146:5). [Human beings], a little piece of your creation, desire to praise you, human beings “bearing their mortality with them” (2 Cor. 4:10), carrying with them the witness of their sin and the witness that you “resist the proud” (1 Pet. 5:5). Nevertheless, to praise you is our desire, a little piece of your creation. You stir us to take pleasure in praising you, because you have made us for yourself, and our heart is restless until it rests in you.39

A clear-eyed look at ourselves—we who are “a little piece of [God’s] creation”—removes attention from our worldly reputations and redirects it to God, who has made us what we are. We are good and worthy of honor because we are a reflection of him.40

From “Spin” to Silence

It may be very difficult to imagine living a life in which we care more about what God thinks of us than what others think. Vainglory wields significant power over us. What practices reveal and counter its power?

In what may be the strangest piece of pastoral advice ever dispensed, the desert father Macarius (Evagrius’s spiritual mentor) once counseled a young monk to go to the cemetery to shout both curses and praises at the dead. The monk returned, reporting that he had dutifully done so and that in both cases (not surprisingly) they had made no reply. Macarius then delivered the punch line of this living parable, soberly informing the monk that he too must become like the dead, deaf to both the abuse and the accolades of others. It’s a story meant to shock us, but by it Macarius means to teach that our excessive attachment to our own glory and our craving for human approval may be extreme enough to require extreme measures of detachment. Of course, the young monk still had to listen to his mentor Macarius, and to God, so the flip side of the discipline entails learning whose voice to pay attention to and what sorts of goodness to remain attached to.

If you are not too eager to go shouting at gravestones but are serious about learning the same lesson about resisting vainglory, two spiritual disciplines offer a path into new life—silence and solitude. Following desert counsel, both disciplines school us in detachment. We can practice each of them in many ways. We can be silent with others or before God or both. Richard Foster suggests letting our lives and actions speak for themselves by shutting down our self-made spin.41 Instead of elbowing into the conversation to justify ourselves or selectively describing a situation to make ourselves look better, he advises, be still. In silence we can recognize our anxious affirmation-seeking for what it is: a drive to groom an appreciative audience and put ourselves in the best light. Regular practice of the discipline of silence quietly weans us off our overdependence on human approval ratings. It also provides a break from voices clamoring with disapproval, including our own. When was the last time you spent time in a quiet place, with no role to play and no reputation to uphold, simply resting in God’s loving presence and listening attentively to him?42

Likewise, the discipline of solitude, writes Foster, “frees us from the panicked need for acclaim and approval” by removing our audience.43 Alone, we are cut off from opportunities to seek recognition and stripped of the need to maintain a respectable self-image. We no longer have to put our best face forward or keep up a convincing performance, even for ourselves. Foster writes, “I am free from desiring public gaze when I need hiddenness.”44 Jesus modeled a spiritually healthy rhythm of sociability and solitude: he ministered among the crowds, teaching and healing, but balanced that work by withdrawing often to a solitary place to pray. If your job regularly places you up front or in the public eye, Jesus’s example may be especially instructive. Silence and solitude yield a centered heart capable of attentiveness to others and an ear attuned to God’s voice. Such positive practices serve as a school of resistance against a world that tempts us to turn others’ “likes” of our successful, happy selves into homemade idols.

It may take time before this feels like freedom. The work of putting the right spin on our lives is exhausting, but it is also addictive, as our social media use attests. We need regular rhythms of tuning out and turning off the insistent voice-overs of vainglory. In our current context, relearning how to be silent and still so that we can listen may be the most countercultural thing we do. Stepping out of the spotlight and cultivating habits of silence and solitude may at first make us feel lonely, or even bereft. With persistent practice, though, these disciplines offer relief from the incessant work of curating our carefully crafted self-image and striving to win the world’s attention and approval. They invite us, in stillness, to receive God’s word of love and let it take root.

Start small. For one day or a week, let your actions speak for themselves, without defending yourself when you suspect others are being critical. How hard would it be not to look in the mirror at all, or to refrain from asking others about our appearance? Could we cease conversation about ourselves—not telling stories about our day or recounting our own version of events or rehearsing our feelings? In our work and elsewhere, could we choose to serve others in anonymous ways? Even such modest endeavors can reveal the surprising amount of mental effort and energy we devote daily to enhancing our image in the eyes of others. Over time, silence and solitude help us discern whether we are engaging in conversations and activities because of a desire to win recognition and renown or because we have something genuinely helpful and worthwhile to share.

Living with excessive desires for the approval of others can be crippling. Novelist Anne Lamott recounts the rather unconventional and slightly irreverent ritual with which she begins her writing. She imagines all the voices in her head—both positive and negative, constantly reciting their high expectations and devastating criticisms of her writing—as little mice. She drops them one by one by their tails in a big glass jar and screws the lid on tight. She can see the mice pawing at the sides and squeaking away furiously, but she cannot hear them. She sets the jar aside. Only when the voices are stilled and moved out of sight can she hear the characters she is writing about.45 Would you, too, find it easier to live, with inner and outer voices silenced, before an audience of One?

Be ready to find silence and solitude challenging, and not simply because you don’t want to take the time. Most of us are deeply invested in maintaining a certain image of ourselves. The discipline of silence also involves laying our own cherished self-image before God, including its elaborate rationalizations and sense of hard-earned respectability. Our ability to reflect on our own behavior as self-conscious beings means that vainglory’s pressure comes from within as well as without. As the desert fathers warn, we should be aware of the lingering temptation to create an imaginary internal audience that approves our fantasy-selves, long after we have withdrawn from public view. God asks us to relinquish our own favorite self-images too. Even when we are stripped of everything we think makes us worthy of approval, God wants us to know how dearly beloved we still are.

Sometimes we don’t realize how much our reputation means to us until it is damaged or lost. You might not realize how much you value being known as a competent professional or organized parent until you are sidelined during cancer treatment. You might chafe against an injury because it damages your picture of yourself as a high-achieving athlete. You might do anything to avoid chitchat with new people if you are unemployed, because the question “What do you do for a living?” makes you ache with embarrassment. What if your dream of being a perfect family is crushed by wayward children whose exploits become painfully public? What if others will always identify you first and foremost as a convict because of a crime you committed forty years ago?

For all these reasons and more, we need to lay down both our beautiful and our broken images of ourselves. Behind every discipline is grace. With enough regular time in God’s presence, listening to his word about us, our thirst to be well known for certain superficial qualities dies down or fades. In time, we also discover how easily and peacefully we can enjoy life without constantly thinking about refashioning ourselves or upgrading our lives. With practice, it becomes easier to recognize the emptiness and ephemerality of most of the goods vainglory inclines us to pursue.

Ultimately, however, vainglory needs to be pulled out at the root, which means cutting it off from pride, the source that feeds and nourishes it. Cassian notes that “we wrong . . .
