God by preferring to do for the sake of human beings what we should have done for his sake, [and we are] convicted by him who knows what is hidden of having preferred human beings to God and the glory of the world to the glory of the Lord.”46

Both pride and vainglory privilege our own conception of happiness and our own power to achieve it. Both prideful and fearfully controlling vainglory put the self at the center, demanding that the spotlight shine on only our most attractive side. Captivated by vainglory, we direct our best energy toward enhancing and controlling our public image. In the end, however, trying to manage our own reputation and manufacture approval for ourselves yields a life of isolation, falsity, shallowness, and self-preoccupation. What a contrast with a life rooted in God’s unconditional approval, a life in which we “lay down the crushing burden of the opinions of others” and “give up on all the little human systems of self-aggrandizement” and self-promotion that lead to exhaustion and emptiness.47 Like Garrison Keillor’s cleaning lady in this chapter’s epigraph, perhaps that kind of life—whether or not it earns worldly attention and applause—will be of some real use to the world.

Weaning ourselves from disordered desire for glory leads to the freedom to enjoy good things, wherever we find them, with celebration and gratitude. Call this creating a culture of good glory. We can bless the world and build up the body of Christ by appreciating good things as gifts from God and rejoicing in others’ goodness. Instead of peeling the onion of vainglory, only to find more layers of vice spiraling all the way down, Augustine exhorted his congregation to create a virtuous circle of glory-giving instead. Augustine compared his preaching (and, by extension, the use of any of our gifts) to serving as a waiter in the house of the Lord. None of us is master of the house. We serve only as the stewards who are fed by the same bread we share with everyone else.48 God is glorified when we acknowledge our goodness as a gift, when we gratefully offer such gifts and receive others’ in return, and when all give thanks for gifts well shared. Such a virtuous community of proper enjoyment and encouragement itself glorifies God by being a beautiful witness. Practicing good glory-giving also means welcoming, celebrating, encouraging, and blessing others because we see them as gifts from God. A mother’s loving embrace; a grandfather’s blessing; a coach who believes in the best we have to offer; a good word from a mentor, caregiver, or friend who reminds us how beloved we are as God’s own children—all these are pictures of good glory-giving. When our affirmations echo God’s, they testify to the goodness in us that is genuinely worth celebrating.49

Once we recognize vainglory’s hold on us, we can begin to trade practices of self-glorification for training in a better way. Wresting free from vainglory’s empty grasping, we can turn to God for the recognition and acceptance human beings most deeply need. When we are known and loved by the God whose approval we cannot live without, our eyes will not be so blinded by flash and dazzle. Then we can rightly hear and heed God’s call to let our light shine.

FURTHER REFLECTION

Try to think of a good you have sought glory in that fits the following categories of vainglory: 

a. A quality, appearance, or action that you have faked having

b. A quality, appearance, or action that isn’t worth much in the eternal scheme of things

c. A quality, appearance, or action that is unkind, unjust, unethical, or illegal but that garners valuable social acceptance for you

d. A truly good quality that you have taken too much credit for or have used to enhance your own reputation without showing gratitude to God for his gifts to you

Which form of vainglory are you most susceptible to? Are you motivated more by prideful vainglory (the need to display your goodness) or fearful vainglory (the need to cover up your defects)?

“It was said of Abba Agatho that for three years he kept a small pebble in his mouth, to teach himself silence.”50 Try silencing your self-talk for a day or a week. Avoid sharing your opinions or feelings, and don’t respond to criticism. A fast from social media might be a useful part of this exercise. Focus on listening attentively and lovingly to others instead.
Journal each day on your experience. What have you learned about how you use conversation for self-aggrandizement, image-enhancement, and reputation-building? In what ways is your communication about yourself healthy and loving?

Think of a time when you have been graced with pure joy and celebration over God’s gifts (to you or another person, or even in creation). What about this experience made it an occasion of good glory? Who has been your greatest encourager or someone who named gifts or goodness in you?

FURTHER READING

Ruth Haley Barton, Invitation to Solitude and Silence (Downers Grove, IL: InterVarsity, 2010).

Rebecca K. DeYoung, Vainglory: The Forgotten Vice (Grand Rapids: Eerdmans, 2014).

cGN

4 
Envy

Feeling Bitter When Others Have It Better

Of all the deadly sins, only envy is no fun at all.

—Joseph Epstein, Envy

The film Amadeus, set in eighteenth-century Vienna, chronicles the relationship between court composer Antonio Salieri and his famous rival, Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart.1 As a young man, Salieri prayed for musical success, promising God his devotion and chastity if only God would make him a great composer. When Mozart burst on the scene with astounding talent, overshadowing all other contenders and destroying Salieri’s dreams of greatness, Salieri was enraged. How could God lavish such amazing musical gifts on such an arrogant, shallow buffoon? From their first meeting, Salieri envied Mozart, and he nurtured his envy long after Mozart was dead. As an old man, Salieri makes a fascinating character study—still yearning for greatness, but also knowing that Mozart’s music will outshine his own forever.

In the film’s opening scene, Salieri tries to commit suicide, guilt-stricken over his complicity in hastening Mozart’s death. A priest visits Salieri to hear his confession, a confession the rest of the film recounts. When the priest arrives, the aged Salieri is playing the piano from his wheelchair. Salieri identifies himself as a composer and asks his confessor if he has any musical training. The priest admits to learning a little as a young boy in Vienna, and Salieri seizes the opportunity to test his reputation. He plays tune after tune he has written, expecting his listener to find one of them familiar. “This one was very popular in its day,” he insists. The priest shakes his head, embarrassed that he does not recognize any of them and exasperated that Salieri persists, exposing his ignorance. Then Salieri pauses. “Wait!” he says, with a gleam in his eye. “What about this one?” He plays the opening measures of a sparkling little tune. The priest lights up almost immediately—“Yes, yes, I know it!”—and he continues humming it even after Salieri lifts his hands from the keyboard. He smiles at Salieri, visibly relieved. “Oh, that’s nice! That’s very nice. I’m sorry, I didn’t know you wrote that.” Salieri’s face darkens with malice and his eyes narrow.

“I didn’t,” he replies. “That was Mozart.”

His experience with the priest only confirms the truth that Salieri cannot bear to admit to himself but also cannot escape: Mozart’s God-given gifts will always eclipse his own. Compared to Mozart, his musical talent will always be second-rate and second-best. The priest tries to console him: “All men are equal in God’s eyes.” Salieri arches his eyebrows skeptically. “Are they?” After his rivalry with Mozart, he cannot, or will not, let himself believe it.

Amadeus shows us a portrait of the vice of envy and its destructive power. Why are we envious? Whom do we envy? And why does envy lead to such destructive impulses? While envy typically breeds malice—Gregory the Great lists hatred among its offspring vices—envy has an obviously self-destructive, self-despising side too. A poem by Victor Hugo recounts an opportunity granted to Envy and Avarice to receive whatever they wished, on the condition that the other receive a double portion. Envy replied, “I wish to be blind in one eye.”2 The envious person resents another person’s good gifts because they are superior to his or her own. The envier feels bitter not just because the other person is better; rather, she chafes at the way the other’s superiority makes her feel her own lack and inferiority more acutely.

Envy and Its Ugly Cousins

First we need to distinguish between envy in the technical sense we are speaking of here and envy in the everyday ways we use the word, especially since in ordinary language “envy” is treated as synonymous with terms like “jealousy,” “covetousness,” and “greed.” For example: “I’m so jealous that you got to go on such a nice vacation!” or “I’m so envious of that great new car you are driving. Where did you get it? I want to buy one too.”

Both envy and covetousness focus on something desirable that belongs to another, something we ourselves lack. The envious and the covetous are “have-nots.” If they were greedy, they would want something like what the other has, or perhaps want more of it than another, and they could say, “I want one of those too.” But the envious and the covetous don’t want to “have one too.” They want the very thing the rival has—“I want that one, the one she has.” While the covetous person’s desires focus on having an object for herself, the envious person is at least as concerned with the rival’s status or good standing as a result of having that object. The covetous person delights in acquiring the thing, while the envier delights in the way the redistribution of goods affects her and her rival’s respective positions. Thus, it often gives the envier pangs to see the rival have something she does not, or it gives her perverse satisfaction to see her rival’s good taken away, even if she herself does not acquire it as a result.

Covetousness, like greed, tends to be more focused on possessions—things we have or own—than envy is. In Scripture, King Ahab covets Naboth’s vineyard (1 Kings 21).3 Envy, on the other hand, typically concentrates its concern on who we are. In envy, we eye the internal qualities of another person, qualities that give a person worth, honor, standing, or status. If the envious do desire an external thing, it is because that object symbolizes or signifies its owner’s high position or greatness. Consider the difference, for example, between a car aficionado wanting a BMW 750i because she loves the driving performance of that particular model, and a neighbor relishing the purchase of a BMW because it makes him feel superior to his brother-in-law, who just bought a new Toyota Camry. It’s not the car that makes us green with envy but what being the owner of such a car says about who we are, as demonstrated by the personal respect and admiration that we command when we pull up to the curb in it.4 Getting the right car (or clothes or house or job) serves as a means to an end: it signals something significant about our personal worth. Not having the car means more than simply lacking that possession; more importantly, it means being deficient or defective as a person. Because of what they lack, enviers feel less admirable and less worthy of love.

In this way, envy, like jealousy, has to do with love—both love between persons and love for ourselves. Although we commonly use “jealousy” and “envy” synonymously, jealousy differs in that it refers to the condition of loving something and possessing it, and then feeling threatened because the loved thing or person might be taken away. God is described as a jealous God in Deuteronomy 5, for example, because the love he commands from his people can be lost when they run after idols instead. As we see in the most common examples of jealousy—the love triangle and the jealous lover—jealousy paradigmatically arises in a love relationship, and specifically, one in which a person considers another person his or her own. Because God describes himself as jealous in Scripture, we cannot label every instance of jealousy as sinful in the way every instance of envy is, according to the tradition. Perhaps sometimes jealousy is properly protective and therefore good. But we typically use the term pejoratively, to indicate a twisted or disordered claim to possession, not a righteous claim to what belongs to us. Human jealousy, in the bad sense, stems from a selfish or inappropriate claim to possess another, as when a jealous husband treats his wife as his property or a jealous wife treats her husband as under her control. Since only God can make a fully possessive claim toward another person—“You are mine!”—human claims to jealousy toward another remain morally dubious at best.

Jealousy counts as envy’s close cousin because both are personal and related to love. The jealous “have” something or someone they love but might lose. The envious, by contrast, are “have-nots”—they do not have the good their rival does, and they do not have self-love either. Thus, they have nothing to lose and everything to gain from another’s loss.

In Thomas Aquinas’s analysis, envy also counts as a type of sorrow. We might translate that term today as a pang of resentment or an inward cringe. He uses “sorrow” in a technical sense, as a type of aversion, which includes everything from not being entirely happy for another’s goodness to more obvious chagrin, Schadenfreude, antipathy, and deep resentment.5 Envy does not wholeheartedly rejoice with those who rejoice. Sorrow over another person’s good, however, doesn’t always count as envy. We might feel threatened by someone else’s good in a nonenvious sense if we fear that another’s good will cause us harm (e.g., an enemy acquires strength to be used against us). Or we might rightly be dismayed because someone else’s good is undeserved (e.g., someone unethically or illegally profits at others’ expense). Envy’s sorrow, by contrast, is primarily about our relative status and superiority where ego is on the line. Envious sorrow might be directed inwardly or outwardly: we can express it in disappointment or discontent with ourselves, or we can catch hints of it in our unnecessary criticism or ridicule of others, our keen eye for others’ flaws, or our feelings of defensiveness around superiors. Sometimes we see the traces of envy when we simply feel better about ourselves because the playing field is a bit more level. In envy, we chafe at another person’s good, simply because their goodness makes ours pale in comparison.

The Comparison Game

The envious care how they stack up against others because they measure their self-worth comparatively. In Aquinas’s terms, the envious grieve over another’s good because it is greater than their own,6 and because the comparison reveals not only their lack of that particular good but also their consequent lack of worth. Unlike the possession of goodness (“I am good” or “this is good”), to excel in goodness entails a comparative ranking between two persons or things: “I excel you” or “this excels that.” All of us need to be loved and found worthy, but the envier makes attaining this love and worth a competitive game. As illustrated in the stories of Jacob favoring Joseph over his brothers and favoring Rachel over Leah, the envious do not believe that all are loved fully, equally, or unconditionally. Their love game admits winners and losers, superior and inferior, more and less worthy. As Francis Bacon put it, “Envy is ever joined to the comparing of a man’s self; and where there is no comparison, no envy.”7 Thus, envy speaks at least as much about the lack of worth that the comparative ranking exposes as it does about any particular good the envier has or lacks. Envy depends on a sense of inferiority and a lack of self-love.

In Amadeus, Salieri envies Mozart’s musical genius. Salieri cannot buy something comparable or attain that level of talent with more hard work. Mozart’s musicianship is a special gift, a personal quality, something unique and internal to who he is. Salieri wishes such giftedness had been his but realizes it lies impossibly out of reach. (Since Salieri’s envy targets another’s personal quality, however, he can’t destroy the talent Mozart has without in some sense destroying Mozart himself.) Consequently, Salieri will never rest content to become more like Mozart. In envy’s comparison game, being superior to Mozart is the only way he thinks he can avoid being inferior. With a malignant gleam in its eye, envy takes others’ excellence as a personal insult. Salieri’s strategy, therefore—typical of those consumed by envy—is to take down his rival and ruin him, rather than emulate him.

When circumstances force the envious to confront what they judge to be their own lack of worth, their unhappiness and grief can be unbearable. They feel compelled to do something—anything—to get themselves out from under it. In extreme cases, this means sabotaging the rival, but even this stratagem cannot fully rescue the envier from stewing in her or his own self-made resentments. In Disney’s The Lion King, Scar illustrates this attitude. He disguises his own sense of inferiority by bullying his obviously inferior sidekicks (the hyenas) to prop up his own ego, while planning the demise of Mufasa, his actual superior and rightful king, with a bitter spirit. Similarly, in The Incredibles, archvillain Syndrome vows to destroy all superheroes, whose defining feature is to outrank him in power. Yet even with all the technological props in the world, he can never be what they are—real superheroes with real powers. Both fictional characters take envy to extremes, but in less malicious and more mundane cases too, envy’s “evil eye” surveys the world with hostility, aggrieved at others’ goodness and dissatisfied with its own.

According to one confessional manual, envy betrays itself with a variety of symptoms: feeling offended at the talents, successes, or good fortune of others; selfish or unnecessary rivalry and competition; pleasure at others’ difficulties or distress; ill will; reading false motives into others’ behavior; belittling others; false accusations; backbiting (saying something bad, even if true, behind another’s back); slander (saying something bad, even if true, in the open about another); the initiation, collection, or retelling of gossip; arousing, fostering, or organizing antagonism against others; scorn of another’s abilities or failures; teasing or bullying; ridicule of persons, institutions, or ideals; and prejudice against those we consider inferior, who consider us inferior, or who seem to threaten our security or position.8 Even if you don’t remember middle school and haven’t watched Mean Girls, such a list may make envy feel achingly familiar.9

Evidence of envy may escalate in a twisted progression from thoughts and feelings to words to deeds, and from secret and underhanded tactics to open and forceful animosity. We can see this in the catalog of envy’s offspring vices. According to Aquinas, envy typically exposes itself outwardly with “detraction,” more commonly known as backstabbing—for instance, a writer who murmurs behind her hand about her rival’s book’s “unfortunate” stylistic weaknesses and the author’s embarrassingly shoddy research.10 The envier’s spiteful spirit may occasionally show openly as reviling, but even direct sabotage often looks like subterfuge, like the time-honored ploy of “damn[ing] . . . with faint praise.”11 If these methods of trying to detract from the rival’s excellence successfully damage the rival’s reputation, the envious rejoice at the other’s downfall (Schadenfreude). As François La Rochefoucauld notes, “In the misfortunes of our best friends we always find something not wholly displeasing to us.”12 Of course, they can also revel in their rival’s misfortune even if it happened by accident or by another’s agency. Anything that closes the comparative gap assuages the envier’s discontent.

Walker Percy offers this insightful commentary on the invidious heart:

The Envious Self (in the root sense of envy: invidere, to look at with malice): Why it is that the Self—though it Professes to be Loving, Caring, to Prefer Peace to War, Concord to Discord, Life to Death; to Wish Other Selves Well, not Ill—in fact Secretly Relishes Wars and Rumors of War, News of Plane Crashes, Assassinations, Mass Murders, Obituaries, to say nothing of Local News about Acquaintances Dropping Dead in the Street, Gossip about Neighbors getting in Fights or being Detected in Sexual Scandals, Embezzlements, and other Disgraces?13

Envy can escalate. If the envious try to undermine their rival and are not successful, then their bitterness intensifies and they resent the other person’s good fortune even more. If unassuaged, envy can even lead to full-scale hatred of a rival. Hatred’s object is whatever blocks our happiness. Naturally, we have a strong aversion to such things as evil, because we see them as harmful or damaging to our own good. For the envious, however, the hated obstacle to their own good is the rival’s possession of excellence, or more precisely, the rival himself who is superior because of the good he possesses. Envy tinges her view of the world with enmity, or at least with antagonism: it’s me-versus-you, my good or your good—never both. Even if she does not undermine the rival’s good, the envier finds herself pleased with its despoilment.

Finally, if the envy that fuels these strategies becomes entrenched over time, it edges closer to open malice toward the rival. At this point, the envier’s hatred of her neighbor spills over into hatred of the one who stacked the deck against her. God (or fate, or whatever implacable force allocated the goods at stake) gets the ultimate blame for what the envier perceives to be the unjust distribution of excellence those goods bring. We see in Salieri’s case that he holds God responsible for shortchanging some while helping others flourish. In a chilling scene, Salieri takes down his crucifix—before which he earlier prayed for the talent to become a great composer—and burns it in the fire. He vows revenge, telling God, “I will destroy Your incarnation” in Mozart.

Strikingly, the envier does not typically advertise his envy out in the open; he tends toward passive-aggressive stabs in the back. In William Langland’s Piers Plowman, the character Envy disguises his invidious hatred for his neighbor with well-mannered words in public, knowing that he is not in a position to pull off a public takedown. Instead, he sows the seeds of malicious gossip in whispers instead.

There’s a nearby neighbour I have often annoyed;

I’ve denounced him to nobles, who’ve seized his possessions,

And by false accusations made foes of his friends,

For I’m sorry to see him succeed or in luck.

I sow such dissension between estates

That it leads to much loss both of life and of limb.

When I meet at market the man I most hate

I hail him heartily as an honoured friend—

I daren’t do differently as he’s definitely stronger—

But heaven help him if I have him in my power. . . .

Thus I live without love like a dog running loose,

My body all bloated with bitter gall.14

Why do the envious shun open warfare? Because they typically stand in a position of inferiority, the envious often prefer deceitful or underhanded strategies. Their sense of being less (worthy, talented, successful, etc.), so rooted in their identity, generates feelings of powerlessness. After all, if they really were worth something or had the power to be greater, then they would already be ranked as high as their rivals. To come out in the open and declare their envy amounts to a public admission and humiliating display of inadequacy. Even though envy essentially requires the envier’s acknowledgment (at some level) that her rival excels her, the envier is pained to admit this to herself and even more pained to admit it to others.15 So her envy silently eats her up from the inside. In the words of John Chrysostom, “As a moth gnaws a garment, so doth envy consume a [person].”16

But the envier’s sense of powerlessness also comes from a second source. Compounding their inferior self-worth, the envious also typically succumb to a sort of fatalism. In their view, the world, God, fate, society, or some other force external to their own sense of control has dealt them a bad hand. They feel cheated, but their own efforts against such a great, distant, and alien force seem doomed. Hence, their passivity. “The deck has been stacked against me,” thinks the envious person. Salieri prays for musical talent and acclaim, vowing to dedicate himself to God in return for his answered prayer. God does not answer his prayer. Instead he doles out talent in spades to Salieri’s undeserving rival. Who can win against God? When even an appeal to divine intervention fails him, Salieri’s only remaining option is to strike out at Mozart himself. Even so, he reverts to the usual stratagems of deceit that are characteristic of those who perceive themselves as weaker or inferior, or the guerrilla-warfare-and-retreat tactics of those who know they cannot win an open battle.

Joseph Epstein tells a joke that illustrates envy’s malicious and impotent stance well. The characters are an Englishwoman, a Frenchman, and a Russian:

Each [was] given a single wish by one of those genies whose almost relentless habit is to pop out of bottles. The Englishwoman says that a friend of hers has a cottage in the Cotswolds, and that she would like a similar cottage, with the addition of two extra bedrooms and a second bath and a brook running in front of it. The Frenchman says that his best friend has a beautiful blonde mistress, and he would like such a mistress himself, but a redhead instead of a blonde and with longer legs and a bit more in the way of culture and chic. The Russian, when asked what he would like, tells of a neighbor who has a cow that gives a vast quantity of the richest milk, which yields the heaviest cream and the purest butter. “I vant dat cow,” the Russian tells the genie, “dead.”17

As Buechner says, envy’s trademarked desire is for “everyone else [to be] as unsuccessful as you are.”18 If you cannot rise, then you work to level the playing field.

The story of Syndrome in The Incredibles gives us a picture of envy’s futility and the envier’s inferiority. Together, they secure what the envier feels doomed to: inevitable failure in besting his rival.19 Early in the film, Incrediboy is an ardent but annoying admirer of the superhero Mr. Incredible. After being spurned by his hero, he changes his name to Syndrome and launches upon a lifetime’s work of killing off superheroes. At the same time, he spends all his energy trying to make himself into an imitation superhero, creating a machine that only he, with his superior technological gadgetry, can defeat. His plan, as revealed to Mr. Incredible, is simple: perfect the machine, defeat it in a mock battle to win acclaim, and then sell his technology to the masses. His rationale? As we saw in this book’s introduction, Syndrome believes that if everyone can be “super,” then no one will be. His efforts are exposed as a pathetic failure when he sends his machine to wreak havoc in the city and his charade of “defeating” it with pseudo-superpowers is exploded for the second-rate fakery it is. No technological imitation counts as a real superpower. Syndrome’s ill-fated position of inferiority cannot be changed, despite his best efforts. His inferiority only gets confirmed even more painfully for all to see.

The Rivalry

When analyzing envy, we will also find it instructive to think about why Salieri envies Mozart and not someone else. If we think about the people we envy, and why we envy them in particular, a pattern emerges. Enviers don’t usually envy those who are far removed from their interests and lifestyles or who are vastly more talented or successful than they are. They tend to envy people to whom they might actually be compared unfavorably—that is, those who are just like them, only better. A writer couldn’t care less if an Olympic athlete wins a gold medal in the five-thousand-meter run while she can barely finish a local 5K road race. But she burns when her colleague gets a rave review on his new novel while her own published work gets only half-hearted notice. For the person who defines himself by his career status and earning power, success is not defined in comparison to Bill Gates or Warren Buffet but rather by “making $10 a month more than [his] brother-in-law.”20 As Aquinas puts it, we envy only those whom we wish to rival or surpass in reputation.21 As an old song says bluntly, “We hate it when our friends become successful” because we think “it should’ve been me / it could’ve been me.”22 The envious characteristically resent the success only of those in whose place they can picture themselves—those whom they “might have been” were it not for the cruel twists of fate. Yet at the same time they find themselves feeling as though that possibility is so far removed from them as to be impossible. The target of our envy paradoxically stands both too close for comfort and completely out of reach.

The envious long to be superior, for their self-esteem depends on outranking others in the relevant field of comparison. Their own identity depends on excelling others, but only those others who threaten that identity—that is, those close enough to be compared as rivals.23 If we reflect on those we envy, we are likely to discover how we define our own identity and where we see that identity as most vulnerable. Envy generally emerges when another’s superiority seems to threaten or lessen our own excellence and where that comparison leaves us feeling inferior. If those areas encroach closely on our sense of who we are and why we are valued, envy’s bitter gall can turn to pure venom.

Reflecting on the different ways we can experience competition and rivalry gives us some insight into the envier’s perspective. How and why do we strive for excellence, and how do we distinguish envious striving from nonenvious aspiration in competitive venues? The film Chariots of Fire gives us a character portrait of two different men with diametrically opposed views of competition.24 Eric Liddell and Harold Abrahams contend against each other in the hundred-yard dash. Harold drives himself to excel and devotes himself to winning—because he fears losing. He displays the envier’s mentality. After the first race he fails to win, Harold sits in the stands with his head in his hands, his self-image dashed to pieces. He cannot see himself as someone who runs and sometimes wins; rather, he must be a winner. “If I can’t win, I won’t run!” he says vehemently to the woman he loves. Exasperated, she responds, “Well, if you won’t run, you can’t win.” Harold has an envier’s attitude—defensive, afraid of being shown inferior, and happy with himself only when he outranks his competition in excellence. Being good enough means being better than another. His envy makes his competitors antagonists. He cannot fully celebrate their excellence.

Eric Liddell, in turn, tries to explain to his sister that his desire to run comes not from a restless desire to prove what he is but from an already-possessed sense of rest in God. “When I run,” he tells her passionately, “I feel God’s pleasure.” When I run, that is—not when I win. Eric already feels sure of God’s love. He strives to run well and to win, not in order to earn favor but in order to revel in excellent activity with joy. He loves competition, not because he needs to win but because he loves to run well. He values his competitors as his partners in seeking excellence rather than as rivals who would strip him of excellence should they best him in the race.

Each man commits to running well, with intensity and fervor. But Harold has the anxious edge of the envier, someone who can never be good or good enough unless he triumphs. Harold’s love for himself is contingent on his comparatively successful performance. By contrast, Eric competes with the calm confidence of someone who knows he is worth something, whatever place he finishes. He therefore runs with joy and freedom, not fear of failure.

Philosopher Thomas Williams has argued that the envious astutely judge both the excellence at stake and their rank compared to others.25 The distorting effects of the vice show instead in how and why they seek that excellence—namely, to shore up their precariously contingent and conditional sense of worth. Their perception of inferiority—based on their lack of some quality they value highly, something in terms of which they measure their value—leaves the envious feeling bad about themselves. When their rivals outshine them, therefore, it is not over something negligible or trivial. Rather, their rivals’ success threatens the best part of themselves—the part they take pride in, something on which their self-respect depends.26 Their judgment of who is “better” or “worse” might be accurate enough, but they take this judgment further, internally chafing against such an evaluation because, to them, it implies they are worth less than someone else who is better. And who could love a worthless self? When we envy, our love for ourselves depends on excelling our rival. That explains why Aquinas locates the vice of envy in the will—the same place he locates the virtue of love.

Envy: The Enemy of Love

I hope it’s obvious by now how the fundamental disposition of the envious directly opposes love. When you love, you rejoice in others’ good.27 When you envy, you sorrow over it. The “greatest commandment” instructs us to love God above all and our neighbors as ourselves (Matt. 22:37–39). Envy directly undercuts love of our neighbor. Fictional examples such as Amadeus, The Lion King, and Snow White show the envious planning their superior rivals’ downfall and, ultimately, plotting their death, while The Incredibles gives us a slightly more comic take on the same theme. In these extreme cases, the chain reaction begins with the capital vice of envy, which eventually gives rise to hatred, which incentivizes murder, hatred’s bitter fruit. The same themes play out in the story of Joseph and his brothers in Genesis: “Now [Jacob] loved Joseph more than any other of his children, because he was the son of his old age; and he had made him a long robe with sleeves. But when his brothers saw that their father loved him more than all his brothers, they hated him, and could not speak peaceably to him” (Gen. 37:3–4, emphasis added). Soon after, they devise a plan to kill him. This chapter’s analysis of envy so far suggests, however, that spite at the unworthiness of the rival also works to distract from and disguise the envier’s own sense of unworthiness. The commandment is to love your neighbor as you love yourself. The envier can do neither.

Amadeus further links Salieri’s hatred of Mozart to hatred of God. The film’s title plays on the idea that God, like Jacob, plays favorites: Amadeus means “loved by God”—as in, if Mozart is loved, then Salieri is hated. A contemporary confessional manual picks up on this idea, defining envy as “dissatisfaction with our place in God’s order of creation, manifested in begrudging his gifts to others.”28 Salieri’s ultimate gripe is not with Mozart but with God, for giving Mozart greater talent than he gave Salieri. Such extreme cases show that the envier cannot rejoice in what God gives others, because it makes her feel worse about herself. Aquinas argues that envy’s opposition to love of God and neighbor (the virtue of caritas) makes it a mortal or deadly sin.29 In short, the envious resent God, feel bitter toward others, and condemn themselves to a hell of their own making already on earth.

Sometimes the envier will try to resolve her predicament by rearranging the rankings so that she comes out excelling her rival. Even if she could be successful in bringing her rival down and establishing her own superiority, however, the ground of her self-worth remains fragile and temporary. That’s why those who are prone to envy—like Harold Abrahams—feel fearful even when they win. Their hold on their higher position is tenuous and easily lost. And when their position of excellence gets undermined, so does their sense of self-worth. Like Uncle Frank, who fails to retain his place as the “number one Proust scholar” in the film Little Miss Sunshine, when we lose our top place in the ranking, we lose everything.30 (In response to Frank’s depressed outlook, his antagonistic brother-in-law remarks, “Sarcasm is the sour grapes of losers trying to bring winners down to their level.”) Even when the envier succeeds in outdoing her rival, therefore, she still does not have what she really desires or needs—namely, a secure, noncontingent, unconditional sense of her own worth. Some secular philosophers declare envy to be naturally human and incurable, and therefore recommend trying to channel envy’s discontent into self-improving strategies.31 Our analysis suggests, however, that the cure for envy requires getting out of the comparative game of engineering self-worth altogether.

The vice of envy has roots sunk deep in pride. The envier claims the prerogative and responsibility to make her own place in the ranking and to manufacture her own worth by excelling the relevant rivals. The envious thus expend all their efforts usurping God’s role: founding their self-worth on their own claims to excellence, resenting their inferior status or engineering the downfall of their competitors, and deciding for themselves who is and is not worthy of the greater gifts. As with all forms of pride, however, the best the envious can do is play God, a strategy that ultimately collapses into one big charade. The envious cannot give themselves talent or success; they cannot make themselves excellent or give themselves worth. Nor can they make others less than who they are, or of less value. Years later, the only tune the priest recognizes is Mozart’s. Salieri’s greatest efforts ended in failure.

Envy is a loser’s game. In more ways than one, the envy game dooms us to lose even if we win. “Winning” at envy means destroying the possibility of love between ourselves and others, and between ourselves and God. The envious insist on living in a way that precludes gratitude and contentment, love and happiness. But only relationships of love will truly make us happy. The envious thus pursue happiness in a way that necessarily undermines their own chances of having it.

To escape from this vice, we must therefore find a completely different foundation for our self-worth. We’ve already noted that envy depends on a comparative self-value. From the enviers’ perspective, their worth is conditional. If they excel their competitors, then they have value. Moving out of envy into love is analogous to making the transition from dating to marriage. The premise of dating includes needing to win your beloved’s conditional affection in order to secure the relationship, while in marriage, you both live from an already secure relationship of unconditional love.

What would it be like to live anchored in such deep, secure love? What would it be like to believe that you yourself have unconditional and noncomparative value? As a spiritual problem, envy requires a spiritual solution. To overcome envy, we need to work from a new vision of who we are, as unconditionally beloved children of God. God tells us in Isaiah 43:1–4, “But now thus says the Lord, he who created you, O Jacob, he who formed you, O Israel: Do not fear, for I have redeemed you; I have called you by name; you are mine. . . . You are precious in my sight, and honored, and I love you.” God loves us already and without any qualifications—not because of our moral worthiness, our attractiveness, or our achievements—but simply as God’s own. “A woman has two smiles that an angel might envy,” wrote Thomas Haliburton, “the smile that accepts a lover before words are uttered, and the smile that lights on the first born baby, and assures it of a mother’s love.”32 We all need that kind of love. And that is exactly how God loves us.

Jesus in the Gospels—especially in Luke’s account—makes a point of affirming and lifting up those who lack status or worth, those who count little or not at all in the social rankings of the day, those who are the smallest and least. His love of them does not rest on their performance, excellence, or special qualities that make them better or more worthy than others. He offers love as a sheer, undeserved gift. He gives it to them just as they are. In offering love, acceptance, and value especially to these people, Jesus seems to be deliberately defying envy’s competitive view of love and worth, trying to help us see beyond it. Perhaps like them, we need to see him face-to-face to realize what such love looks like. No effort we can make could earn such love—nor can we manufacture a satisfying substitute for it.

Robert Roberts explains how God’s unconditional love stands as the antithesis of envy:

The Christian’s self-understanding is that she is precious before God—however much a sinner, however much a failure (or success) she may be by the standards of worldly comparisons—and that every other person she meets has the same status. . . . This vision is not only one that levels every distinction by which egos seek . . . glory. . . . This vision, when appropriated, is also the ultimate ground of self-confidence. For the message is that God loves me for myself—not for anything I have achieved, not for my beauty or intelligence or righteousness or for any other “qualification,” but simply in the way that a good mother loves the fruit of her womb. If I can get that into my head—or better, into my heart—then I won’t be grasping desperately for self-esteem at the expense of others, and cutting myself off from my proper destiny, which is spiritual fellowship with them.33

If we know ourselves securely affirmed as having unconditional worth, a worth based on God’s love, we can freely affirm others’ gifts without feeling threatened or inferior. God gifts us with a self that is free to love without anxiety that its own contributions will be compared to another’s and found wanting. With such a gift, we can take joy in our own good and in the good of others. As W. H. Auden wrote, the key, “since all self-knowledge tempts man into envy,” is to “love without desiring all that you are not.”34

The Evil Eye

Envy’s deep link to love and acceptance is the key to understanding its importance, its rank among the capital vices, and its cure. Aquinas says that two capital vices oppose love: sloth undermines our love for God (the first and greatest commandment) and envy undermines our love for our neighbor, whom we should love as ourselves (the second commandment—second, in part, because love of God enables us to love ourselves and others as God does). Entrenched envy causes us serious damage, because love figures so centrally in human fulfillment. Overcoming envy requires acknowledging a deeply human need for unconditional love. It likewise requires that we acknowledge the divine source of such love.

“Envy comes from people’s ignorance of, or lack of belief in, their own gifts.”35 Because this vice powerfully distorts our view of our value and self-worth—that is, how we see ourselves and the place from which we view everything else—and does so at such a fundamental level, we might find it difficult to imagine how envy could ever be remedied. Envy’s distorted vision earns this vice the nickname “the evil eye.” Thus its remedy requires a regimen that will reframe our vision of the world, ourselves, and other people. But reframing what we see begins with a change of heart. How do we learn to comprehend and receive unconditional love? And how does that help us exchange fear and resentment for gratitude and contentment?

Because the envious essentially see the world as a competition—a world where some outrank others—part of unlearning envy (or better still, preventing it) involves investing ourselves deliberately and deeply in activities with shared or common goods. Goods count as common when one person’s having or participating in them does not diminish others’ share. For example, common goods include enjoying music or a quiet walk together, appreciating a beautiful piece of art, being part of a good friendship, sharing conversation over a cup of coffee, learning something or gaining wisdom, or hiking to discover a breathtaking view. We benefit from investing ourselves in activities that involve others without the threat of one person having a greater share of goodness. Such experiences help us learn to appreciate goods outside a competitive frame of mind. Further, they can teach us how it feels to rejoice in something good and to rejoice in that good as shared with others.36 If all goes well, sharing in the pleasure of this experience will provide the envier with a taste of something better. From that perspective, envy’s characteristic displeasure over the good because someone else has it will seem petty and lackluster in comparison. A significant recurrent theme we will find when addressing all the vices (as with spiritual formation in general) is that attraction and attachment to genuine goods incentivizes detachment from their empty, false alternatives.37

Envy often gets entangled with the vice of vainglory, a vice defined by the excessive love of praise. Aquinas and Gregory believe that vainglory often gives rise to envy. Why? As we have seen in the previous chapter, when we are afflicted by vainglory, we seek the spotlight to win others’ approval. That acclamation is a conditional good, which grounds our fragile ego; in most cases, it is also comparatively and competitively sought. Vainglory intensifies our envy when our inferiority or others’ superiority in social comparisons gets displayed or widely publicized. Social media use only exacerbates this damaging dynamic. Both the envious and the vainglorious have a self-worth dependent on outdoing and outshining others, even if envy can be hidden in a way that vainglory usually is not. If others notice unfavorable comparisons with a rival, and offer judgments that reinforce our own—as first the Vienna court and later the priest hearing his confession did in Salieri’s case—envy and vainglory can feed off each other with doubly toxic effect.

If the tradition rightly makes this link between vices, a remedy for vainglory may help diminish envy as well. In this case, the envious can practice seeking others’ good, countering the vice’s malicious inclinations, in ways that no one else notices. The hiddenness of an act of love helps eliminate any competitiveness or temptation to have others measure our self-worth by comparing our benevolent efforts to anyone else’s. The focus of this discipline remains on doing loving things that would likely not catch others’ attention.38 Why? Because the envier needs to learn what it feels like to do something good for another, without her usual frame of reference in which her performance will be noted, tallied up, and made the basis of comparisons of worth between persons. Quiet benevolence also develops a habit of acting for others’ sake in ways that are not instrumental to engineering superiority or status for ourselves. Mother Teresa famously said, “If we could only remember that God loves [us], and [we] have an opportunity to love others as he loves [us], not in big things, but in small things with great love.”39 This seems like as good a description as any for actions designed to counter vainglory-exacerbated envy. I personally learned to recognize and gratefully receive this kind of love from my children when they were very young. Children simply take you as you are, having not yet learned the calculating acceptance that comes from having experienced rejection or needing to perform or outperform another to earn affirmation. The experience of giving and receiving such love can build a beachhead in the soul, creating an envy-free zone that gives us room to live and breathe again. Such encounters help us see through and resist envy’s temptation to perceive all goods possessed by others and all relationships to others as threatening our own worth.

We might worry that this acceptance of ourselves as unconditionally loved will make us complacent. Without competition and comparison, won’t we get soft and fail to strive for better things? The opposite may well be true. Like Eric Liddell’s love of running, living without envy removes the fear of failure and public exposure, which better allows us to stretch ourselves and take risks. Aquinas distinguishes envy from something he calls “zeal.” Like envy, zeal requires acknowledging our own lack or inferiority with respect to attaining some good. A zealous person can see clearly that another excels her. Because, however, the zealous person stands secure in God’s love for her—a love not contingent upon her performance or ranking of worthiness—she remains free to look at herself with honest humility and desire to be better. Because she operates under the conviction that the church is a body made up of many members, she does not see others as competitors for God’s esteem and favor (since his love and fellowship count as common goods, enjoyable by all). Rather, others are saints and fellow travelers who encourage each other along the way. With zeal, we see in our superiors and those we admire an attractive model to imitate with gratitude. Envy seeks to bring another down; zeal seeks to be lifted up and made better. Whereas envy distances others, zeal brings us into mentoring relationships within the communion of saints. Such mentors can help us get started, push us when our enthusiasm flags, rejoice with us when we make strides, and lift us up when we fall.40

As always, grace and the work of the Spirit empower our progress in virtue. We begin by learning to receive God’s unconditional love—a good that’s already our own—with gratitude. From that foundation, we can earnestly aspire to be all that God wants us to be, at his invitation, not out of our desperation. The sheer giftedness of grace and God’s unconditional love replaces envy’s bitterness with a sense of unshakeable blessedness. As Roberts notes, the envious prefer to view the world through the lens of what they are owed, or have accomplished themselves, rather than view what they have received as a gift. “I deserve better,” the envious say. Or “why isn’t my life as good as hers?” Or “I’ve been cheated if I don’t get that (and someone else does).” All sound like echoes of envy. But our God is a God of abundant love and goodness, not scarcity. The envious must unlearn their narrative of viewing themselves and what they have been given as “not good enough.” The practice of gratitude—naming specific quotidian gifts and blessings, large and small, and thanking God for the gifts of others that blessed us that day—can also give us fresh eyes to see and hearts to celebrate the goodness that has already been lavished on us as the beloved of God.41

The security of knowing we are already well-beloved grounds both our gratitude for all good gifts and our zealous strivings to be better. We have no reason to fear that we will not be lovable unless we measure up or excel each other. Envy’s evil eye shrinks our view of the good; a heart of gratitude enlarges it. The saints and those we admire as excellent do not threaten us as rivals but serve as beautiful inspirations. As their apprentices, we can see them not as obstacles or competitors but as experienced travelers leading the way. With their example and encouragement, we can reach beyond anything we would have imagined for ourselves. Buechner writes, “The Holy Spirit has been called, ‘the Lord, the giver of life,’ and drawing their power from that source, saints are essentially life-givers. To be with them is to become more alive.”42 They invite us to live joyfully from a grateful sense of God’s love and life-giving power. Unspoiled by the envier’s comparative lens, we can embrace our goodness simply as a blessing and a gift from a good Father. And from the fullness of that grace, we can both rest content in his love and eagerly follow the call to grow.

FURTHER REFLECTION

Whom do you envy? What does this say about what you value in yourself?

Can you think of a rivalry that damaged the people in it? Can you think of a rivalry that raised the competitors to a new level of excellence? What made the difference between the two cases? Which of your relationships are built on competition and comparison—and which are not?

Think of a story about the way someone has helped you develop your gifts and virtues. Did they lead by example, instruction, encouragement? Why is your attitude toward them admiration and gratitude rather than envy? How did they make you want to be better, and why didn’t you feel threatened by their obvious superiority?

FURTHER READING

Timothy Perrine and Kevin Timpe, “Envy and Its Discontents,” in Virtues and Their Vices, ed. Kevin Timpe and Craig Boyd (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2014), 225–44.

Robert C. Roberts, “Humility as a Moral Project” and “Gratitude,” in Spiritual Emotions: A Psychology of Christian Virtues (Grand Rapids: Eerdmans, 2007), 78–93, 130–47.

cKG

5 
Sloth (Acedia)

Resistance to the Demands of Love

The secret is that God loves us exactly the way we are and that he loves us too much to let us stay like this.

—Anne Lamott, Operating Instructions

Grace is costly because it compels a person to submit to the yoke of Christ and follow him; it is grace because Jesus says: “My yoke is easy and my burden is light.”

—Dietrich Bonhoeffer, The Cost of Discipleship

Laziness and Diligence

Picture a hairy, long-toed sloth hanging from a branch, headphones on, listening to “sloth motivational tapes.” A soothing voice murmurs, “Relax, take your time, what’s the hurry? Life goes on whether you’re asleep or not.” One of my favorite cartoons of sloth depicts this supposed vice in just this way.1 I say “supposed” because, on first glance, hardly anyone would think of sloth as a serious, much less deadly, sin. But why is this?

Perhaps it’s because we, like the cartoonist, typically think of sloth as laziness. Online shoppers can buy a sloth sleep mask, sloth onesie pajamas, more sloth T-shirts to avoid doing laundry, and a sloth yoga calendar (move slowly!) with a single click. Does laziness really rank with sins like envy and lust in its evil and destructive power? Since when was sitting on the couch watching reruns of The Office and munching on a bag of chips a moral and spiritual failure of the first order?

Our initial reaction might be to say that sloth doesn’t belong on the list of the seven capital vices. If putting it on the list in the first place didn’t count as an outright mistake, keeping it there now is certainly outmoded. We might agree with Evelyn Waugh’s twentieth-century assessment of sloth: “[‘Sloth’] is a mildly facetious variant of ‘indolence,’ and indolence, surely, so far from being a deadly sin, is one of the world’s most amiable of weaknesses. Most of the world’s troubles seem to come from people who are too busy. If only politicians and scientists were lazier, how much happier we should all be. The lazy [person] is preserved from the commission of almost all the nastier crimes.”2

Likewise, Wendy Wasserstein uses a conception of sloth as laziness and sheer inertia to construct a delightful parody of self-help literature in her 2005 book. On its jacket we read,

With tongue in cheek, Sloth guides readers step-by-step toward a life of non-committal inertia. “You have the right to be lazy,” writes Wasserstein. . . . Readers will find out the importance of Lethargiosis—the process of eliminating energy and drive, the vital first step in becoming a sloth. To help you attain the perfect state of indolent bliss, the book offers a wealth of self-help aids. Readers will find the sloth songbook, sloth breakfast bars (packed with sugar, additives, and a delicious touch of Ambien), sloth documentaries (such as the author’s 12-hour epic on Thomas Aquinas), and the sloth network, channel 823, programming designed not to stimulate or challenge in any way.3

In the 1987 Harper’s spoof of the deadly sins, the caption of the ad for sloth reads, “If the original sin had been sloth, we’d still be in paradise.” From scholarly to popular accounts of the vice, then, contemporary culture seems often to equate sloth with laziness, inactivity, and inertia.

On the other hand, we could accept the same description of sloth and conclude that it does deserve to be called a sin—even a serious one. Both sacred and secular audiences offer a version of this answer.

The sacred version goes like this: Sloth opposes the great Christian virtue of diligence—that powerful sense of responsibility, dedication to hard work, and conscientious completion of our God-given duties. What is hard work and dedication at its best, after all, but an expression of love and devotion? The telltale root of our word “diligence” is the Latin diligere, which means “to love.” Sloth, on this view, expresses pernicious apathy—comfortable indifference to duty and neglect of others’ needs. If you won’t work hard enough, then you don’t care enough. On this view, we should label sloth a sin not merely because it makes us lazy but because of the lack of love that lies behind that laziness.

Communities that value diligence in this way point to proverbial warnings such as, “Go to the ant, you lazybones” (Prov. 6:6), and the apostle Paul’s admonition to do useful work with your hands (1 Thess. 4:11–12), echoed a few centuries later by John Cassian’s call to manual labor in the monastery as a remedy for this vice. Especially if our work counts as a divinely appointed vocation, sitting around is not only useless; it’s thumbing our noses at God’s call. Hence the (in)famous Protestant work ethic. Notably, those who hold this view today do not seem nearly as concerned about the dangers of workaholism, often excusing or praising it under euphemisms such as “devoted service to the kingdom” and “commitment to ministry.” How can you say no to God?

Even outside religious circles, however, secular views serve to glorify the virtue of diligence and frown upon slothful “slacking off.” In the stirring words of Henry Ford, “Work is our sanity, our self-respect, our salvation. Through work and work alone may health, wealth, and happiness be secured.”4 Likewise, the Chronicle of Higher Education once put “discipline”—that is, how diligently we work—at the top of its list of the virtues necessary for success in graduate school.5 “In popular thought the ‘capital sin’ of sloth revolves around the proverb ‘An idle mind is the Devil’s workshop,’” writes Josef Pieper. “According to this concept, sloth is the opposite of diligence and industry; it is almost regarded as a synonym for laziness and idleness. Consequently, acedia has become, to all practical purposes, a concept of the middle class work ethic. The fact that it is numbered among the seven ‘capital sins’ seems, as it were, to confer the sanction and approval of religion on the absence of leisure in the capitalistic industrial order.”6

Since the modern, industrial era, diligence and “industriousness” have become pragmatic virtues aimed at profitability and professional success. When careers replace religion as a source of meaning, worth, and identity, laziness still carries a significant stigma. Our society measures personal worth in terms of productivity, efficiency, and the maximization of our potential. So we’d better get busy, or we’ll be good for nothing.

Love and the “Long Course of a Lifetime”

I should confess that when I started studying sloth, I felt fairly confident that it would be the one vice about which I would never have to worry. If anything, I reasoned, I’m too busy, hardworking to a fault, and something of a perfectionist besides. Carelessness, apathy, laziness, and lack of effort would definitely not be my problem! My fragile bubble of self-righteousness quickly burst, however, when I came across Pieper’s argument that busyness and workaholism were not virtuous but rather sloth’s classic symptoms. “Not only can acedia and ordinary diligence exist very well together; it is even true that the senselessly exaggerated workaholism of our age is directly traceable to acedia.”7 It turns out that both the apathetic inertia of the lazy person and the perpetual motion of the busy person can stem from a heart afflicted by this vice, according to the traditional conception. How can this be?

If sloth were simply laziness—a vice opposing the virtue of diligence—we would find that question impossible to answer. The full explanation requires returning to the original definition of this vice. Sloth—or “acedia,” as Cassian and other early Christians called it8—named a temptation that captured the experience of the desert fathers and mothers of the fourth century AD. Christian medieval theologians who followed in their footsteps further developed the idea. As we look back through acedia’s long history in the Christian tradition of moral and spiritual formation, the distance between our contemporary conception and acedia’s original spiritual roots might surprise us. This is why I prefer to use its older name.9 Retrieving the traditional definition will show us why we now tend to mistake acedia’s symptoms for ostensible virtues, and how originally acedia had to do more with being lazy about love than with being lazy about our work.

For the early Christians in the desert, acedia commanded much attention in the spiritual life. They universally categorized it as a spiritual vice, rather than as a vice with a “carnal” object, like lustful physical pleasures. Acedia could drive even a mature Christian to abandon his or her religious commitments altogether.10 Consider Evagrius of Pontus’s colorful fourth-century description of the vice:

The demon of acedia, also called the noonday demon (cf. Ps. 90:6), is the most oppressive of all the demons. He attacks the monk about the fourth hour [10 a.m.] and besieges his soul until the eighth hour [2 p.m.]. First of all, he makes it appear that the sun moves slowly or not at all, and that the day seems to be fifty hours long. Then he compels the monk to look constantly towards the windows, to jump out of the cell, to watch the sun to see how far it is from the ninth hour [3 p.m.], to look this way and that. . . . And further, he instills in him a dislike for the place and for his state of life itself, for manual labour, and also the idea that love has disappeared from among the brothers and there is no one to console him. And should there be someone during those days who has offended the monk, this too the demon uses to add further to his dislike (of the place). He leads him on to a desire for other places where he can easily find the wherewithal to meet his needs and pursue a trade that is easier and more productive; he adds that pleasing the Lord is not a question of being in a particular place: for scripture says that the divinity can be worshipped everywhere (cf. John 4:21–4). He joins to these suggestions the memory of his close relations and of his former life; he depicts for him the long course of his lifetime, while bringing the burdens of asceticism before his eyes; and, as the saying has it, he deploys every device in order to have the monk leave his cell and flee the stadium. No other demon follows immediately after this one: a state of peace and ineffable joy ensues in the soul after this struggle.11

Whereas the other temptations are like the sun which rises and sets, touching only one part of the soul, the noonday demon [akēdia] is accustomed to enveloping the whole soul and strangling the mind.12

Evagrius’s account sounds similar to Amma Theodora’s. Many credit her with giving the first description of the vice:

As soon as you intend to live in peace, at once evil comes and weighs down your soul through accidie, faint-heartedness, and evil thoughts. It also attacks your body through sickness, debility, weakening of the knees, and all the [bodily] members. It dissipates the strength of soul and body, so that one believes one is ill and no longer able to pray. But if we are vigilant, all these temptations fall away. There was in fact an ascetic who was seized by cold and fever every time prayers began, suffering from headaches, too. In this condition, the ascetic thought, “I am ill, and near to death; so now I will get up before I die and pray.” By reasoning in this way, the ascetic exercised self-discipline. When he finished prayer, the fever abated. So . . . the ascetic resisted, prayed, and was able to conquer unhelpful thoughts.13

What the desert fathers and mothers meant by “acedia” does imply a failure of effort, a failure linked to a lack of love—the Greek word they use (akēdia) literally means “lack of care.” Monks and nuns with this vice faced a grave spiritual malady, expressed in dejection or a feeling of oppressiveness or even disgust. A lack of physical effort, if anything, embodied symptoms of this deeper problem within.

Throughout Evagrius’s account (only briefly represented here), we should note two things: first, the desert community treated acedia as an extremely powerful and serious vice; and second, on their view, acedia threatens our fundamental commitment to our identity and vocation as Christians. Since it attacks the fundamental dedication of our life to God, acedia entangles us in a high-stakes spiritual struggle. We should not confuse it either with the comfortable pursuit of bodily rest or with the emotion of sorrow (and much less with clinical depression).14 Even though acedia sometimes drags us down with feelings of sadness, like all vices, it and its symptoms ultimately embody the cumulative effect of our own choices. Evagrius classifies acedia as a spiritual vice because it involves inner resistance and coldness toward our spiritual calling and its attendant practices. The desert fathers and mothers describe acedia as distaste, disgust, dejection, oppressiveness, and restlessness, because those with the vice experience staying committed to God as an intolerable burden. From the perspective of those with the vice, such a life of faithfulness offers nothing but daily drudgery and dreary discipline. They would much prefer to escape the claims of their religious identity and be free of its wearisome demands. In early desert spirituality, having this target for our aversion earned acedia a spot on the spiritual end of the chain of vices.

In the writings of Evagrius’s disciple, John Cassian, we see a shift in emphasis toward the external manifestation of sloth’s characteristic inner resistance. Cassian transplanted desert asceticism into the Latin West, establishing communal forms of monasticism more familiar to us today (e.g., religious orders that follow St. Benedict’s Rule). Religious orders expected each monk or nun to contribute to the spiritual and physical well-being of the community. Although the desert fathers and mothers also emphasized the spiritual importance of manual labor, they associated acedia more directly with “fleeing the cell” (the place and community in which you lived) than with evading work, as Cassian did. Cassian explicitly and extensively discusses the importance of manual labor as a remedy for this vice. Early on in its history, then, acedia picked up its association with physical inactivity and shirking manual labor. Cassian uses language like “laziness,” “sluggishness,” “sleepiness,” “inertia,” and “lack of effort” in his descriptions of acedia, such as this: “[Monks] overcome by slumbering idleness and acedia . . . [have] chosen to be clothed not by the effort of [their] own toil but in the rags of laziness . . . [and] have grown remiss as a result of sluggishness and . . . are unwilling to support themselves by manual labor.”15

Even for Cassian, however, idleness marks behaviors symptomatic of the inner condition of someone besieged by sloth. In this he echoes Evagrius’s description of the vice: “Once [acedia] has seized possession of a wretched mind, it makes a person horrified at where he is, disgusted with his cell. . . . Likewise it renders him slothful and immobile in the face of all the work to be done within the walls of his dwelling.”16 On Cassian’s account, physical inactivity or lack of effort is an effect or expression of our inner condition. “The work to be done within the walls of his dwelling” includes both spiritual practices and physical duties done on behalf of the religious community. Members who shirked this “work” in either form were distancing themselves from their identity and investment in that particular spiritual community bound together by love for God. Mere (physical) laziness would not necessarily indicate a case of vice. Rather, shirking our spiritual duty—whether this involves devotional practices or manual labor on behalf of others in the monastery—counts as acedia only if it signals inner discontent with and resistance to our spiritual calling.17

Both inner and outer manifestations of acedia link this vice to the daily demands of the spiritual life. Like Evagrius, Cassian takes acedia very seriously. It not only threatens to undermine a person’s fundamental identity as someone devoted to developing a lifelong relationship with God, but it also erodes the commitment to the religious community formed by that identity.18 Psalm 119 highlights the contrast between such devotion and acedia. In verse 28, the psalmist finds himself overcome with weariness and feels this oppression interfering with his daily meditation on God’s law, a signature spiritual practice expressing his love and faithfulness to God.

The Now and the Not Yet

The medieval theologian Thomas Aquinas gives an account of acedia that stands at the crossroads between the ancient ascetic tradition’s thoughts on acedia and modern conceptions of sloth as laziness. Aquinas explains why acedia makes the traditional list of great vices. He also explains how this vice reflects a lack of love that can be expressed both as laziness and as restless busyness. His definition needs some unpacking, though. He begins with the cryptic definition of acedia as “aversion to the divine good in us.”19 What does this mean?

An analogy might help. Imagine a typical husband and wife. In general, they have a relationship of genuine love and friendship. One evening, they quarrel at dinnertime and head off to opposite corners of the house for the rest of the night. They find it much easier to maintain that miserable distance and alienation from each other than to do the work of apologizing, forgiving, and reconciling. Learning to live together and love each other well after a rift requires giving up their anger, their desire to have their own way, their insistence on seeing the world only from their own individual perspectives. Saying “I’m sorry” takes effort, but by “effort” we do not mean simply that each resists the physical work of walking across the house and saying the words. Likely they see this most recent disagreement as just another wearisome rerun of the same thing they’ve been fighting about for years, and it doesn’t feel like they are getting any nearer to resolving it. What’s the point of going through the motions of apologizing one more time?

Do they want the relationship? Yes, they do—neither would renege on their commitment to each other (yet). But do they want to do whatever it takes to be “all-in”—do they want to honor the relationship’s real, sometimes tedious, daily claims on them? Do they want to learn genuine unselfishness in the ordinary daily task of living together? Sigh. Maybe tomorrow. For now at least, each spouse wants the night off to wallow in his or her own selfish loneliness. Their lackluster response is especially tempting when love takes unexciting effort, or when expressing it feels like a dull formality or an empty ritual. Sometimes they even shirk efforts that aren’t terribly taxing—like looking up from something diverting on their smartphone to offer a greeting when their spouse comes home from work. As time goes on, they progressively neglect the small ways of turning toward their spouse in love because such gestures become inconvenient or tiresome. They never seem to make much difference anyway.

Why do marriages and likewise long-term friendships make good pictures of what goes wrong in the vice of acedia? For all its joys, any intense friendship or marriage has aspects that can seem burdensome. We must invest not only our time but also ourselves to maintain and grow a relationship that flourishes rather than founders. Even more difficult than the physical accommodations are the accommodations of identity: from the perspective of individual “freedom,” cultivating a relationship of love will change us and cost us. It will push us to restructure our priorities. It may compromise our plans, small and large. It will demand sacrifice. It will alter the pattern of our thoughts and desires and transform our vision of the world. It’s not just “your life” or “my life” anymore—it’s “ours.” Seen in this light, we might judge that staying at arm’s length without engaging or investing would be easier and safer—even if ultimately unhappier—than risking openness to love’s transforming power and answering its claims on us.

Sometimes family life or long-term friendships feel euphoric and energizing; other times, they feel like unexciting routines or tedious work. To understand the dynamics of acedia, we need to see how staying committed to any love relationship takes daily nurturing, daily effort, and daily practices that build it up. Neglecting these will slowly break the relationship down. Nurturing grudges or selfish claims instead will erode it and make us resentful of a relationship that comes to feel like a suffocating trap. Kathleen Norris once commented that married love is “eternal, but it’s also daily, about as daily and unromantic as housekeeping.”20 Through consistent practices and disciplines, whether we feel like doing them or not, we renew and refresh our decision to love, keeping our commitment of love alive. The person with acedia resists the effort of doing day after day after day whatever it takes to keep the bonds of love strong and living and healthy, whether she feels particularly inspired about doing it or not.

If we think of our relationship to God as following the same pattern, we’ll be well on our way toward grasping Aquinas’s definition of acedia. Something must have gone awry in us if acedia’s signature move shrinks back in distaste from a divine good, instead of taking delight in it. What that good is, however, still requires a little more explanation.

“The divine good in us,” in Aquinas’s medieval-ish way of talking, means the indwelling of the Holy Spirit in our hearts—God’s life in us. Paul puts it this way: “I have been crucified with Christ; and it is no longer I who live, but it is Christ who lives in me” (Gal. 2:19–20). When God lives in us, the Spirit transforms our whole being: the old has gone, the new has come. By the power of the Spirit, we are to “clothe [our]selves with the new self, created according to the likeness of God in true righteousness and holiness” (Eph. 4:24; see also Col. 3:10). Aquinas calls this grace “a beginning of glory in us.”21 That means acedia’s resistance targets nothing less than the gift of the Holy Spirit and our new identity in Christ. But how?

Christians know that God lives in our hearts by his Holy Spirit, empowering us to become new people. Our new identity in Christ, however, is both “now” and “not yet,” a promise and a present reality. Acedia gets a foothold in that gap. For now, God’s presence is a promise and a beginning; our new selves are created but not yet mature and perfected. Why not yet? Because the Holy Spirit doesn’t jump in and wave a magic wand to conjure up perfection overnight. The project of growing into our new identity takes a lifetime—and a lifetime of cooperation on our part. We call this “sanctification,” the process of being made holy. So in one sense, we are Christians, and in another sense, we are still becoming Christians. God is both “already” and “not yet fully” present in us. Our love for him has the character both of longing and of the restfulness of delight. Thus, it makes sense for Paul to encourage Christians to grow in faith and become more and more like Christ. We can’t just say, “I’m saved! Praise the Lord!” and then sit back and assume God is done with us.

Nonetheless, we cannot lose sight of the role grace plays in any progress we make in holiness. Becoming Christlike doesn’t mean working like crazy to improve ourselves and merit a place in God’s favor. Sanctification requires effort—not earning. Second Peter 1:3–7 clears up any misunderstanding here:

His divine power has given us everything we need for a godly life through our knowledge of him who called us by his own glory and goodness. Through these he has given us his very great and precious promises, so that through them you may participate in the divine nature, having escaped the corruption in the world caused by evil desires.

For this very reason, make every effort to add to your faith goodness; and to goodness, knowledge; and to knowledge, self-control; and to self-control, perseverance; and to perseverance, godliness; and to godliness, mutual affection; and to mutual affection, love. For if you possess these qualities in increasing measure, they will keep you from being ineffective and unproductive in your knowledge of our Lord Jesus Christ. (NIV, emphasis added)

To revisit our earlier analogy, this picture of the Christian life parallels marriage: both involve accepting a new identity that needs to be lived out, day by day, for the rest of your life. A couple takes their vows on their wedding day, and from that moment on they are married. Yet being married—living out those vows by investing in and building their relationship—will take all their efforts, every day, for the rest of their lives. Their love and identity have both a now and a not-yet character. They experience love as both a gift and a life-transforming task. So, likewise, the person afflicted by acedia resists this transformation of identity by God’s love.

That transformation takes time. This explains why the tradition consistently places the vice of acedia in opposition to perseverance, faithfulness, and enduring commitment. Aquinas once argued that the demons can’t have acedia. On the mistaken “sloth-as-laziness” interpretation, we might think it’s because the demons are spiritual creatures who can’t be physically weary or get tired of making physical effort. On Aquinas’s view, however, it’s because their wills can fully love or reject God in a single moment of choice, unlike the (less perfect) human will, which grows to love or reject God through many choices and actions over the long course of a lifetime. Our love for God, our choice to be like him, must be lived out over and over, day after day. And the need to persevere in that commitment over time yields an opportunity for acedia to arise.
