In summary, when we make acedia a habit, we turn our backs on the joy we should have over being united with God and committed to him in love. Instead of rejoicing at God’s presence in us, we chafe at it and resent the claims that God’s love makes on us. Rather than being willing to dedicate ourselves to developing and deepening the relationship, we resist its demands. Although acedia can appear symptomatically similar to chronic depression, it is not a matter of brain chemistry or psychological symptoms; it is a habit of the heart. Even though acedia typically weighs us down with feelings of sadness, like all vices it and its symptoms ultimately express the cumulative effect of our own decisions. Feelings don’t primarily define acedia, even if they arise from it and also reinforce it. Instead, the vice consists in well-entrenched and willful habits of resistance, even as love is fundamentally a series of choices that add up to a commitment.22

Aquinas’s take on acedia explains its traditional reputation as a serious vice. When acedia shapes our hearts, we resist our identity in Christ and his Spirit’s presence in us. We balk at God’s invitation to “be imitators of God” (Eph. 5:1) and to be transformed by him throughout the rest of our lives. If that’s not a description of a significant vice, it’s hard to see what else might count.

The War Within

At the same time, this very explanation raises hard questions: How could we possibly feel put out by God’s presence in our heart? What could make us unhappy about the gift of love that holds the key to our own happiness? We should count God’s love as the greatest gift that we could ever possess! Why would anyone who received it want to keep it at arm’s length?

Aquinas answers with the apostle Paul’s words in Galatians 5:17: the opposition of the Spirit to the flesh causes acedia.23 Initially, this seems to confuse the definition that we just clarified. Does his answer mean that acedia makes us prefer to be a lump of flesh on the couch while neglecting our spiritual duties? It sounds like he has returned to the mistaken view that acedia strikes when spiritual growth takes a backseat to bodily comfort. No wonder sixteenth-century artists like Hieronymus Bosch depict this vice with a wife scolding her slumbering husband for missing church.24 Is acedia nothing but laziness after all?

Aquinas and Paul both say no. The ancient as well as the medieval Christian tradition consistently identifies acedia as a spiritual vice. That means those with this vice do not primarily long for bodily goods like comfort and ease and pleasure, as the lustful and gluttonous do.25 Instead, when Paul makes the “Spirit-flesh” distinction, Aquinas notes, he means to contrast the old sinful nature with our new, redeemed nature in Christ. The battle lies not between body and soul, or between the physical and the spiritual. Instead, the old sinful self resists transformation into the new self enlivened by the Spirit. Letting go of our former identity and its familiar ways26 feels like dying—a project we might understandably feel like avoiding, even if a truly cruciform life requires it.

Spiritual battles take place on many fronts. Sometimes bodily pleasures or bodily weariness or even physical emotions like grief do make us more susceptible to sin. In the case of this vice, however, the resistance lodges first and foremost within our hearts. Acedia literally divides us against ourselves. We were made for a loving relationship with God. Acedia marks our character when we have chosen to reject that relationship as the way to find fulfillment and have instead tried to make something (anything!) else do its work.27 We attempt to settle for being less than what we truly are.

Think back to the marriage example with which we began—the way loving another person requires a thousand little deaths of our old individual selfish nature. This is the work that someone with acedia resists. On some occasions, the work may include bodily effort (so our resistance to love’s demands may be exacerbated by the physical effort required), but the two should not simply be equated. As we saw above, acedia cannot be defined as laziness, since people with this vice often pour tremendous effort and energy into the difficult task of distracting themselves from the unhappiness of their real condition. As we will see in the next section, people with acedia can be tireless, overworked, even restless. On the other hand, we should also not automatically equate overwhelming physical weariness with acedia: think of parents caring for a newborn infant. Their weariness results from their love and devotedness. The need or desire for real rest is not a vice.

Lazy about Love

In the film Groundhog Day, weatherman Phil Connors (Bill Murray) illustrates—at least by analogy—sloth’s resistance to the daily transformation required by real love.28 Through a mysterious turn of events, Phil finds himself waking up each morning, day after day, on February 2—Groundhog Day—reliving the same day over and over again in Punxsutawney, Pennsylvania. A shallow and self-centered Phil first amuses himself with various hedonistic pleasures, since what he does each day apparently has no consequences. “Don’t you worry about cholesterol?” his coworker asks as he scarfs down a platter of doughnuts. “I don’t worry about anything anymore,” replies Phil with a smug chuckle. Soon, however, he embarks on the more serious project of getting his producer, Rita (Andie MacDowell), into bed with him. He spends a long succession of days making diligent and careful efforts to seduce her. While he finds her attractive, he does not really love her—at least, not yet. Rather than really changing himself, he painstakingly figures out by trial and error what she likes and finds appealing. Then he puts up a false front designed to get her to fall for him—he memorizes lines of French poetry, pretends to share her interests in world peace, and caters to her taste in ice cream. His elaborate schemes aim to manipulate her into giving him what he wants. Although she is initially taken in, Rita eventually sees through his strategy and rejects his advances, despite his persistence. “I can’t believe I fell for this!” she cries at him in anger. “You don’t love me! I could never love someone like you, Phil, because you could never love anyone but yourself!”

Phil responds to her repeated rejections with despair. He gives up on any chance at happiness. He sits on the couch all day, eating popcorn and drinking whiskey, apathetically watching the same episode of Jeopardy! for the hundredth time. He can’t get what he wants the way he is, but he also refuses to change. And so he remains at an impasse.

After a few unsuccessful suicide attempts (which the film depicts as simply more despairing escapist strategies), Phil finally tries a new tactic. He begins, little by little, to try to change into the sort of person Rita could love. Like his earlier seduction project, he must invest regular and consistent effort—day after day, he studies for a medical degree, he takes piano lessons, he reads French poetry, he extends a helping hand to the young and old. But these efforts, unlike his previous stratagems, genuinely change his heart and his character. Unlike the old Phil, he finds himself no longer bored and restless, filling time with self-centered diversions and empty pleasures. For he no longer pretends to be but really becomes—through consistent habit and daily discipline—not just a poet, pianist, and philanthropist, but a person capable of unselfish love. Phil finds himself no longer solely motivated by the desire to produce sexual results in his relationship with Rita. Instead, he has learned to care about others and to genuinely care about what is right and good—for its own sake. In the end, his transformed character attracts not only the affection of all the townspeople but also the love of Rita herself.

From escapism, restlessness, and self-indulgence, to boredom, apathy, and despair, Phil’s adventures in Punxsutawney show us the full range of acedia’s symptoms. The tradition names evagatio mentis (a restless, wandering mind) as a typical offspring vice of acedia—something Phil demonstrates in his incessant use of diversions and distracting entertainment to avoid the demands he cannot face. (Hence sloth’s lingering association with procrastination in the modern mind.) Pusillanimity, the vice with which this book began, also counts as a signature mark of acedia. We fail to heed the call to love God and follow him wholeheartedly when this would draw us out of our comfort zone. Instead, we shrink back, electing to stagnate rather than mature. Paul Wadell explains: “Pusillanimity is a dangerous habit to acquire because through it we not only lose our taste for what is truly good, but also grow comfortable with mediocrity. [Such a person] never has to grow, never has to change or be challenged because he avoids any goals or commitments that would call him beyond himself in sacrifice, goodness, or love.”29

Paradoxically, someone with acedia can be either a reluctant couch potato or a preoccupied person who will never sit down. Whether through laziness or busyness, when we have acedia we avoid changing, stretching, or giving ourselves. Love takes effort, and it transforms us. The real work Phil resists, then, is not the physical effort itself (of seducing Rita or of helping the townspeople) but the effort to cultivate and sustain a real commitment to love.

In a sense, then, it’s true that those with acedia are slothful—they want an easy spiritual life. They find detachment from their old selfish nature too difficult, painful, and burdensome, so they neglect to perform the actions that would maintain and deepen relationships of love. They harden their hearts toward any change that requires sacrifice or surrender on their part. Wanting love to come easily and comfortably means, for example, preferring the shallow sentiment of pop songs and picture-perfect Hollywood romances to loving the family you actually have to live with. While the euphoria feels wonderful for a little while, such feelings and momentary highs cannot serve as the substance of a real relationship, nor are they sufficient to maintain it over time. They come cheap but don’t last. All the talk about “forever” has to be anchored in commitments marked by daily decisions to keep on loving, even when it’s hard or unexciting or doesn’t yield a big emotional payoff right away. In a similar way, acedia lures us when we want the security of having God’s love without an ongoing willingness to be made new. A person with acedia essentially says, “I want God to love me, but being changed by his love is too hard. I want the comfort and security of being loved by God, without having to give back, sacrifice anything, take responsibility, or invest myself in the relationship over the long haul.” In the end, any thought of spiritual commitment makes someone with acedia feel grieved, oppressed, and resentful.30

Acedia has turned out to be a spiritual vice after all—a vice marked by resistance to the transforming demands of God’s love.31 Why does someone with acedia resist love? Because a love relationship marks an identity change and a corresponding commitment to daily transformation. Novelist Anne Lamott recounts the words of a wise old woman at her church who once told her that “the secret is that God loves us exactly the way we are and that he loves us too much to let us stay like this.”32 Those with acedia object to not being able to stay the way they are. Something must die for the new self to be born, and—as in Phil Connors’s case, or ours—it might be an old self to which we are very attached.

Here we can finally sort out our initial thoughts on acedia’s connection to sloth. We can rightly define the vice of acedia as resistance to effort, but not only, or even primarily, in the sense of being physically lazy or lazy about our work. Rather, rightly understood, acedia is resistance to the disciplined transformation demanded by our new identity in Christ and brought to life by the Spirit. The slothful like the comforting thought of being saved by love, of being God’s own. At the same time, they balk at the prospect of change—the (often slow) putting to death of the old sinful nature, which feels too much like suffering—and the disciplined commitment it takes to sustain that relationship of love over a lifetime.

People with acedia, like the couple in our earlier analogy, dream of being unconditionally loved without having to condition their own selfish desires in return. They prefer a perpetual honeymoon to fifty years of married faithfulness. Acedia makes us feel like Evagrius’s and Theodora’s ascetics, wanting to live for God in desert contemplation, but finding their zeal flagging in the heat of the day and pining instead for the comfort and worldly happiness of the life they left behind. Traditionally, Lot’s wife represented acedia, because even amid a lifesaving rescue, she was reluctant to turn her back on her old familiar home and the only life and friends she had ever known (Gen. 19). How many of us have felt like we need two angels to drag us out of Sodom, while we look back over our shoulders, wistfully pining for what we must leave behind? Are we like the people of Israel, poised to enter their homeland and promised rest in Canaan after years of restless wandering, who prefer to retreat to the drearily familiar desert than have to fight giants, even with God’s help (Num. 13–14; Deut. 1)? Pieper writes that the person with acedia “will not [fully] accept supernatural goods because they are, by their very nature, linked to a claim on [the one] who receives them.”33 As a U2 song puts it, “Love is not an easy thing; the only baggage you can bring . . . is all that you can’t leave behind.” The song title’s advice echoes what the desert fathers and mothers recommended (as well as what Lot’s wife and the nation of Israel should have done): leave behind the reassuringly familiar lives and old loves you are clinging to, and “walk on.”34

Because it’s ultimately about love—accepting God’s love for us along with the cost of loving him back—acedia earns its place among the capital vices. Human beings are made for love. If we resist it, we must deny who we are. In her reluctance to die to her old self, the person with acedia chooses to stunt her own spiritual growth. She prefers slow spiritual suffocation to the birth pangs of new life. Eventually, her habitual neglect bears fruit: she finds that she can’t fully accept the only thing that would ultimately bring her joy. She refuses the thing she most desires, and she turns away in revulsion, resignation, or bored distaste from the only thing that will fulfill her. In the perversity of her sin, she pursues self-made sorrow over joy, emptiness over fullness, restlessness over rest.

When describing acedia, it can be difficult to find the right balance between grace and discipline. Christian living should not become another long list of dull duties to discharge on pain of not really counting as committed children of God. Therefore, neither the desert ascetics nor Aquinas offers a litmus test of required work; in fact, they warn against measuring our devotion this way. Commitment is part of the package, but there is also joy in the journey (see Ps. 119). On the other hand, we shouldn’t carelessly assume that God will happily leave us where we are and never make any demands on our time or effort, never cost us any inconvenience or upset our priorities, never need to wean us painfully away from old attachments and desires. Love will require us to learn how to die to our old selves. Moreover, all love relationships have their fallow seasons, their drier and more difficult times. Sometimes love is work—difficult, daily work. We need not confuse or conflate sheer meaningless drudgery with perseverance through times when our energy and fervor ebb, though. In the latter case we are still fueling a relationship of love and investing ourselves for the long haul in something that ultimately brings fulfillment.

Restlessness and False Rest

Acedia sabotages sanctification—the transforming power of God’s love in us. By sapping our willingness to lay down our old loves for the sake of love of God, it saps our energy for good altogether, since God is the source of that strength. As a result, this vice has a twofold effect. First, it makes us want to avoid people and activities that bring us face-to-face with our identity in Christ—most obviously, things like prayer, worship, Scripture, and the sacraments. Second, however, it builds a cold wall between us and the demands of love for others. This explains sloth’s association with indifference and apathy—what Aquinas calls “sluggishness” when it comes to heeding the demands of justice. Love for neighbor flows from accepting God’s love.35 The commandment tells us to love our neighbors as ourselves. In refusing to accept God’s love for himself, with the commitments that brings, someone with acedia also fails to love his neighbors and stay committed to them. When Rita calls him out on his selfishness, saying, “You could never love anyone but yourself!” Phil replies honestly, “That’s not true: I don’t even like myself.”

Rooted in resistance to love’s demands, acedia typically manifests itself in two apparently opposite ways: despairing resignation (apathy or “false rest”) and desperate escapism (avoidance or “restlessness”). Cassian mentions both effects: “And so the true athlete of Christ, who wishes to engage lawfully in the struggle for perfection, must . . . contend on both sides against this most wicked spirit of acedia in such a way as neither to be cut down by the sword of sleep and collapse nor to be driven out from the bulwark of the monastery and depart in flight, even for a seemingly pious reason.”36 Like Phil Connors, most of us try first to escape. Cassian comments:

For the adversary [the devil] will the more frequently and harshly try a person who he knows, once the battle is joined, will immediately offer him his back and who he sees hopes for safety not in victory or in struggle but in flight, until he is gradually drawn out of his cell and begins to forget the reason for his profession, which is nothing other than the vision and contemplation of that divine purity which is more excellent than anything else and which can be acquired only by silence, by remaining constantly in one’s cell, and by meditation.

Thus it is that the soldier of Christ, having become a fugitive and a deserter from his army, “entangles himself in worldly affairs” and displeases “him to whom he engaged himself” [quoting 2 Tim. 2:4].37

If we can’t escape successfully, either in reality or in fantasy, acedia will then usually take the form of despairing resignation. When our attempts to flee our self-made predicament fail, we tend to sink under oppressive hopelessness and despair. Just before the film’s turning point, a pajama-clad Phil languishes in a recliner, immobile and unaffected, drinking himself into oblivion after Rita’s rejection. So the film outwardly depicts the inertia or false rest of his inner condition. Phil realizes that his old selfish nature cannot give him what he really needs, but he can’t bear the thought of facing new life either. He has no way out of the position his own choices and character have put him in. The tension and sense of being trapped that Phil experiences are feelings that often characterize this vice. We can’t escape the truth about who we are called to be, and yet we refuse to face it or live up to it. Reality feels unbearable. The slothful person tends to cope by mentally and emotionally checking out. This sort of resignation, however, should “not to be confused with laziness,” according to Buechner.

Lazy people, people who sit around and watch the grass grow, may be people at peace. Their sun-drenched, bumblebee dreaming may be the prelude to action or itself an act well worth the acting. . . . Like somebody with a bad head cold, [slothful people] have mostly lost their sense of taste and smell. They know something’s wrong with them, but not wrong enough to want to do something about it. Other people come and go, but through glazed eyes they hardly notice them. They are letting things run their course. They are getting through their lives.38

Inwardly, the slothful will not be moved, so they stay stuck between a self they cannot bear and a self they can’t bear to become. Their visible behavior—lethargy and unresponsiveness—reflects the deadened state of their heart.39

Given how intolerable such a condition is, if we think we can escape from sorrow, we will pour all our energy into any form of flight that shows promise, no matter how desperate. Phil’s life of shallow pleasure-seeking and seduction early in the film portrays just such an attempt to escape love’s demands. Life becomes a long project of distracting ourselves from the truth about our true identity and vocation to love. Augustine famously wrote that we will be “restless” until we find our “rest in [God].”40 Blaise Pascal agreed; he predicted that the best way to make people truly miserable would be to take away all their diversions, whether at work or through recreation: “Without [diversions] we should be in a state of weariness, and this weariness would spur us to seek a more solid means of escaping from it. But diversion amuses us, and leads us unconsciously to death.”41 Victor Frankl paints a similar portrait of the workaholic’s “Sunday neurosis”—the vacuum of meaning she feels on the day when her work does not fulfill its distracting function every waking hour.42 Sadly, this escapist strategy can take even ostensibly pious forms: we can spend our whole lives avoiding the demands of true discipleship, love, commitment, and change as we dutifully engage in a flurry of religious activities, volunteerism, and ministry opportunities. We find any excuse to not pay attention to God and remain in his presence. Like the aptly named Sebastian “Flyte” in Evelyn Waugh’s novel Brideshead Revisited, the restlessness that characterizes our escapist strategies betrays a heart not at peace. Deep down, we are fleeing what God’s loving call would require of us. Aquinas follows the tradition in noting acedia’s opposition to the commandment to rest on the Sabbath (Exod. 20:8–11),43 which requires that “the soul take rest in God alone.”44 Acedia’s listlessness and languor—its restlessness and false rest—are both resistant to true rest. Instead of leading us to trust that God’s call to love leads to life and freedom, acedia tells us to resist this call as oppressive. In resisting, we refuse Jesus’s invitation to rest in him: “Come to me, all you that are weary and carrying heavy burdens, and I will give you rest. Take my yoke upon you, and learn from me; for I am gentle and humble in heart, and you will find rest for your souls. For my yoke is easy, and my burden is light” (Matt. 11:28–30).

The person with acedia ultimately insists on his own view of what is burdensome, following his own will in his own way, and seeking his own self-made pseudo-rest. His lack of love and commitment speaks of an unwillingness to surrender himself to the Spirit’s work and whatever transformation God is calling him to. In this resistance, the vice of acedia is nourished by its prideful roots. Unlike other forms of inertia, grief, or even depression, all of which can be mistaken for acedia, this capital vice results from a choice not to commit, a refusal to give ourselves wholly to God and then stay the course. Acedia’s response of “no thanks,” therefore, stands as the antithesis of Mary’s vulnerable “yes” at the annunciation, a “yes” that finds her faithful from the feedbox at Christ’s birth to the foot of the cross where she watches him die. Love is not an easy thing.

When our character is malformed by acedia, we try to find happiness for ourselves while evading the daily demands of self-giving love. We prefer our own diligent (or dilettantish) efforts to make ourselves happy with shortcuts and quick fixes. We avoid the onerousness of love’s demands by putting them off, trying to find fulfillment some easier way. By our prideful tactics, however, we cut ourselves off from the possibility of fulfillment and happiness. And so, says Gregory, acedia eventually brings us nowhere but the dead end of despair.

Although acedia can show itself in the total inertia of the couch potato or the restless distractions of endless activity, somewhere in between these two symptoms of vice lies a holy Sabbath for the heart that has given itself utterly to God. With such a heart, we will be overjoyed, not oppressed, by the thought that “love so amazing, so divine, demands my soul, my life, my all.”45

Stabilitas Loci

It’s hard to imagine what remedy to suggest for the vice of acedia. Like envy, it traps us in a self-perpetuating dynamic, prompting our refusal of the very thing required to cure it. The ancient strategy against acedia thus seems counterintuitive: rather than seeking some novel way to infuse life and breath into our relationship with God, the desert fathers recommended stabilitas loci—stability of place. To the ones who feel stuck and want to escape at all costs, they counsel, “Stay.” Evagrius writes, “You must not abandon the cell in the time of temptations, fashioning excuses seemingly reasonable. Rather, you must remain seated inside, exercise perseverance. . . . Fleeing and circumventing such struggles teaches the mind to be unskilled, cowardly, and evasive.”46 Elsewhere he writes, “Perseverance is the cure of acedia, along with the execution of all tasks with great attention and the fear of God.”47

In this discipline, the soul should mirror the body: “The spirit of acedia drives the monk out of his cell, but the monk who possesses perseverance will ever cultivate stillness.”48 David Brakke writes, “Listlessness [acedia] tempts the monk to give up the fight against the demons, and thus the primary way to resist it is to remain in one’s cell and hold one’s ground.”49 The psalmists also sound this refrain: “Be strong and take heart and wait for the LORD” (Ps. 27:14 NIV). In a nutshell, this discipline counters our desire to avoid or run away from what we’re called to be and do—whether through busy activities or through imaginative diversions.50 Instead, like the early desert Christians, we can push back against this vice by accepting and staying committed to our true spiritual vocation and identity, whatever it requires. Nuns and monks lived out their commitment to the religious life by willingly staying in their desert cell, rather than escaping to the city and the more entertaining world they had left behind. Leaving the cell was equivalent to abandoning their spiritual purpose and vocation. The decision to stay kept them engaged in the process of weaning themselves off their old self and worldly loves, while learning to enjoy the fellowship of God. Amma Syncletica said, “Just as a bird who abandons the eggs she was sitting on prevents them from hatching, so the monk or nun grows cold and their faith dies, when they go from one place to another.”51 To stay put physically implies staying put spiritually. In her book on the desert mothers, Laura Swan writes, “The desert journey is one inch long and many miles deep; inward is . . . the direction of travel.”52

Why did the fathers and mothers of desert communities describe acedia’s opposing virtues as courageous endurance, long-suffering, and perseverance? How is staying the course supposed to help? They knew that in the spiritual life, enthusiasm and energy will wax and wane, and periods of felt alienation from God or spiritual burnout will threaten. Given the human condition and our sinful nature—both our physical frailty and our fickleness of will—what we most need in countering the daily weariness of acedia is steady commitment and daily discipline, even when we don’t feel like it. A friend once compared worship to a military drill.53 Even if we are not personally uplifted in each and every instance, regular worship nevertheless cultivates and equips us with habitual right responses, forming our hearts and minds for future action and preparing us to react well even under pressure. Such rhythms of life shape what becomes natural to us.54 In our moral and spiritual practices, as in athletic training or musical rehearsals, we are apprenticed into a more virtuous life by enacting and embodying virtuous character in our present actions, for this is how we train our feelings to follow. Counselors, too, often recommend continuing to act “as if” in times of low motivation, because persevering in action creates dispositions to respond and feel appropriately as well. Praying the daily office or following the liturgy or keeping traditions alive works the same way—the point is to stay with something until it forms you. Similarly, St. Benedict’s Rule commends stability of place and loyalty to a particular religious community as the condition for learning to love.55

Perhaps in our age we are more prone than ever to expect too much of love as a feeling and too little of love as an ongoing choice and commitment. In our worship services and our family life, we expect emotional highs to carry us through life’s difficult times, when we would better expect engagement in daily practices to keep us faithful. Acedia tempts us to escapism and despair during times when we don’t feel like being godly or loving anymore. We want to abandon ship and give up, or to check out or drift away inwardly or outwardly toward something more comfortable or immediately comforting. “A light breeze bends a feeble plant; a fantasy about a trip away drags off a person overcome with acedia,” writes Evagrius.56 The remedy is to stick with stabilitas, because “the force of the wind does not shake a well rooted tree; [and] acedia does not bend the soul that is firmly established.”57 By remaining rooted, staying the course, and sticking to our commitments, we can resist acedia’s urge to get out or give up.58 Aquinas notes, and the writer of Psalm 119 concurs, that dwelling in the presence of God and seeking his face help us renew our taste for the sweetness of spiritual goods.59 With the Spirit’s help, we can move from half-heartedness to wholehearted love again.

Applying the wisdom of the desert today, we can see why our culture of busy escapism poses a serious spiritual danger: it too quickly provides an easy way out of this daily, disciplined effort of learning to love. Overcoming tendencies to acedia requires us to own up to the inner sources of our resistance to the demands of loving God. Temptation inclines us to grasp at a ready diversion any time we feel stretched or uncomfortable or just plain sick and tired of it all. We need a school of stability. Love flourishes in the context of daily deeds and constancy in our commitments, while acedia arises in a context of conveniently easy escape. As one prayerful petitioner put it, “Forgive me for letting love die when it demands action in order to live.”60 As the desert fathers knew, when acedia oppresses, we must stay the course, resisting the temptation to flee—in mind or in body. In the face of love’s demands, acedia wants the easy way out.

FURTHER REFLECTION

What surprised you most about the history of this vice? Why is this vice on the list of capital vices? Should it be?

In what areas of your life, or in what situations, are you most tempted to escape from the demands of love? What acts of love do you find oppressive or a daily burden? What form of sloth shows itself most in your life—“restlessness” or “false rest”? What forms of restlessness or busyness are you culturally rewarded for?

How will you intentionally nurture your relationships of love in healthy rhythms of work, worship, and rest? How can contemporary people like us practice stability?

FURTHER READING

cPV

6 
Avarice

Possession and Mastery

For what good would their prosperity do them if it did not provide them with the opportunity for good works?

—Aristotle, Nichomachean Ethics

Life is easier since I abdicated the throne. What a relief, to relinquish ownership of unownable things.

—Barbara Kingsolver, High Tide in Tucson

Now I had discovered the great pearl. To buy it I had to sell all that I had; and I hesitated.

—Augustine, Confessions

In the Harper’s magazine spoof of the seven deadly sins, the ad for avarice featured a picture of Santa Claus with the headline, “The world’s foremost authority speaks out on the subject of greed.” The letters piled in front of him all begin, “Dear Santa, I want . . .” Santa looks up over his glasses and comments, “Do you remember all of the things you told me you wanted as a child? Well, your list may have changed, but I bet it hasn’t gotten any shorter.” As Gordon Gecko’s (Michael Douglas) iconic “Greed Is Good” speech in the film Wall Street makes clear, greed is now such an American way of life that it’s hardly recognizable as a sin anymore: “The point is, ladies and gentleman, that greed, for lack of a better word, is good. Greed is right, greed works. Greed clarifies, cuts through, and captures the essence of the evolutionary spirit. Greed, in all of its forms—greed for life, for money, for love, [for] knowledge—has marked the upward surge of mankind. And greed, you mark my words, will not only save Teldar Paper, but that other malfunctioning corporation called the USA. Thank you very much.”1 The idea that greed fuels a capitalist and consumer economy counts merely as a recent way to justify it. Two millennia ago, in his Republic, Plato argued against opponents who claimed that the self-aggrandizing desire for more and more without limit worked as the principal motivation driving all human action. Only lack of power restrains our desire to take more than our share. Whatever era we live in, then, we might be so formed by greed that we consider our current condition normal. We can’t see why we would need more than minor lifestyle adjustments, scaling back on our most egregious excesses, to address this vice. Our greed has become both mundane and comfortable.

Scripture takes a hard line against greed, and it addresses this vice with striking frequency. Its warnings about money far outnumber those about sex. Prophet after prophet warns of the role greed plays in systemic injustice and the corresponding oppression of others, while the people of Israel (who are more like us than we prefer to admit) respond with remarkable callousness and with deaf ears. The most famous word about greed belongs to Jesus: “It is easier for a camel to go through the eye of a needle than for someone who is rich to enter the kingdom of God” (Matt. 19:24; Mark 10:25; Luke 18:25). Affluent Christians might wonder, Why can’t we be rich in material wealth and rich in love for God? Abraham, that great hero of the faith, possessed great wealth by God’s own blessing, after all. Yet the apostle Paul labels the love of money as “a root of all kinds of evil” (1 Tim. 6:10)—a text that raised questions about whether avarice, instead of pride (“the beginning of all sin,” according to early translations of Eccles. 10:13),2 deserved the distinction of being the root of the tree of vices. In addition to these puzzles, the Christian tradition encountered its own share of internal controversies over whether owning possessions is good or bad, whether the desire to possess is natural or unnatural to human beings, and whether the Christian life demands moderate acquisition of wealth along with stewardship and charitable giving or the more stringent standard of voluntary poverty and total abandonment of possessions.

Avarice’s Inner Attachment

Before we tackle these larger issues, we need a definition of the vice itself. Common parlance identifies avarice or greed in a variety of ways—by disordered intentions and inner attachments, and by behavioral patterns of possession, for example, excessive acquiring, saving, and hoarding, as well as lavish spending. In this chapter, we will define avarice primarily by what it looks like on the “inside,” since the capital vices designate habits of the heart. When Jesus instructed the rich young ruler to leave all that he had to follow him (Mark 10:17–31), he was commanding a reordering of the man’s deepest loves. Clement of Alexandria writes, “The young man’s refusal to part with his possessions points to deeper character flaws that Christ desired to heal. Christ’s command is not, as some hastily interpret it, a command that he should throw away what he possesses and renounce his wealth. What he is told to banish from his soul are his notions about his wealth, his attachment to it, his excessive desire for it, and his anxieties [over it]—those thorns of existence which choke the seed of true life.”3

The inner condition of the heart gives rise to greed’s outer manifestations, which typically include excessive acquisition and excessive retaining of money or possessions. Aquinas’s definition bears this out: we are greedy when we excessively love or desire money or any possession money can buy.4 The greedy person’s overattachment to things wears many faces—an overflowing shopping cart or a single cherished purchase, a stock portfolio that is aggressive or conservative, a wallet full of credit cards or a safety deposit box with a few carefully guarded treasures, a garage full of expensive cars or a closet full of great deals. Greed can captivate the young, the old, and everyone in between. In all its varied expressions of gain and grasping, however, greed is a perverted love. Its profile has disordered desire written all over it.

Despite greed’s internal attachment problem, we shouldn’t underestimate the connection between external actions and the formation of inner thoughts and desires—between the “outside” and the “inside” of greed. As with any habit, this vice depends on actions wearing a groove or pattern in the longings of our heart. We can’t monitor greed simply by asking what we can rightly or wrongly take (or keep) in a given instance, therefore; we need to think incrementally and cumulatively. What if we instead asked this question: “If I keep handling possessions like this for the next ten or twenty years, what sort of character will I develop and what kind of person will I become?” In both our giving and our getting, greed corrodes the virtue of generosity and leads us to ignore the claims of justice. Contrasting avarice with these two virtues shows us both greed’s internal character and its outward expression, and the way they feed off each other. Moreover, in our exploration of this vice, we will note how our greedy tendency to trust in wealth for happiness and security undercuts our trust in God. Like the other vices rooted in pride, greed expresses the do-it-yourself method of finding happiness, instead of the contentedness of receiving the good things that God gives us and gratefully depending on his provision.

Aquinas opposes the vice of greed to the virtue of generosity,5 except that he uses this virtue’s Latin name: “liberality,” which comes from the same Latin root as the English word “liberty.” The linguistic connection gives us a hint: this virtue concerns freedom—in this case, freedom from attachment to money and the promise of what money can buy. The liberal person’s free and open attitude contrasts with the greedy person’s preoccupation with possessions and tightfisted grip on money as “mine.”

In Genesis 13:1–13, Abram and Lot illustrate the opposition between this virtue and vice. Abram gives Lot first choice of the land for his flocks. Lot selfishly chooses the best for himself without a moment’s hesitation, leaving Abram the desert (which Abram takes without complaint). Lot’s choice of the fertile valley, however, lands him in the city of Sodom. There he grows so attached to the good life that angels later have to drag him and his family away, even though Lot knows they face mortal danger if they stay. Lot’s greed holds him so fast in its grip that he even resists God’s drastic plan to rescue him, because it means leaving behind all his treasure—and the life he had built upon it. Abram, on the other hand, trusts in God’s provision, even when the present reality (no property except a tomb) doesn’t seem to match the promises.

When we have the vice of avarice, we are too attached to money and possessions—we care too much about them, as its own Latin root reveals (aveo, avere: to crave). The virtue of liberality also stands opposed, however, to carelessness about the real value of things and our responsibility for them. Aquinas calls this the vice of prodigality. The most dramatic example of the latter vice is found in Luke 15, the parable of the prodigal son. The prodigal’s use of money shows him too eager to acquire wealth (he wants to collect his inheritance before his father has died) and then too eager to spend it unwisely on things of ephemeral value. The prodigal treats money wastefully, for he does not care enough about it or value it enough. (Is it merely coincidental that the money he is careless with was not earned by him?) In contrast to these two vices, then, the virtue of liberality stands on the fine line between excess (too much attachment to money) and deficiency (too little attachment).6

As a depressing side note, Aquinas thinks that a single person can have both vices opposed to liberality—that is, both avarice and prodigality. If we are entrenched in a lifestyle of excessive and careless spending, we are likely to be regularly in dire need of getting more. It is too easy, once we are in debt or desperate for cash, to rationalize unjust acquisition and spend too much time longing to have more. So, in our dealings with money a literally vicious cycle is possible. (Perhaps there is a consumerist version of this cycle too: first we overacquire, and then we overtrash.) Nevertheless, the inner attitude behind prodigality leans much closer to the virtue of liberality, for the signature practices of both prodigality and liberality are parting with money and giving things away. Those with avarice stand apart, holding on to their possessions with an unyielding grip.

To put it differently, we can define liberal or generous people primarily by their inner detachment from material wealth and goods. They may care about their possessions, but they do not have an “immoderate love of possessing” them. The generous, according to Aquinas, thus show “read[iness] to give with pleasure . . . when and where they ought.”7 Generosity’s loose grip makes it second nature to give things away when the occasion arises. The morally mature, for whom virtue feels natural, take delight in giving, rather than regarding it as an onerous duty or a dull chore. The test of liberality is whether you find giving things away easy and enjoyable.8

The Heart of the Giver

We often confuse greed with simply possessing excessive amounts of stuff, just as we mistakenly reduce gluttony to eating an excessive amount of food. First and foremost, however, virtues and vices concern the condition of our hearts. Generosity’s measure is not how much is given away, in terms of the flat amount, but rather the way it is given and why. The manner of giving reveals the inner desires and attachments of the giver. In Luke’s Gospel the widow gives away her last two copper coins out of devotion to the Lord, and Jesus commends her (Luke 21:1–4). Her contribution couldn’t cover the cost of a single church bulletin, but even in her poverty, her love and devotion made her willing to give. Likewise, the Gospels recount the costly gift of a newly cut tomb for Jesus’s body, given by a rich man, Joseph of Arimathea (Matt. 27:57–60). His generosity shows not (or not merely) in the size of the gift or the wealth of the giver but in the readiness to give what he does have to God, even at possible risk to himself. Aquinas quotes both Aristotle and Ambrose, the bishop of Milan, to reinforce this point: “The Philosopher says (NE iv.1): ‘Liberality is proportionate to a person’s . . . means, for it consists, not in the quantity given, but in the habit of the giver’; and Ambrose says (De Offic. I) that ‘it is the heart that makes the gift rich or poor, and gives things their value.’”9

Unfortunately, we can readily use this point to make excuses justifying our refusal to give enough or to give up the excessive things we own. If we are used to having too much, even giving up luxuries feels like a sacrifice. Like a Lenten fast in which we deem it adequate to give up dessert (a luxurious surfeit) instead of the main meal (basic sustenance), does what we give away consist merely of the leftovers we would have garage-saled anyway? Compare the counsel of Aquinas: “It is enough for people to have only a few things, so liberal people are commendable because in general they give away more than they keep.” As if it were obvious, he explains that God gives some people excess riches “so that they might have the merit of good stewardship.”10 Aquinas assumes that the point of owning stuff is to use it for the sake of serving. Do you look at your paycheck that way? How much of the income we use to upgrade our own lifestyle could and should be used to meet others’ needs? According to the early church fathers, our stewardship of all material goods imitates God when it serves the common good. To give is to offer the world a picture of God’s heart: “Whatever belongs to God, is for the common use of all, nor is anyone excluded from his benefits and gifts, nor is the human race prevented from equally enjoying God’s goodness and generosity. Whoever owns property and follows this example of equity, sharing his returns and fruits to his brothers [and sisters] and showing himself fair and just with his gratuitous bounties, is an imitator of God.”11

Henri Nouwen once said, “Perhaps nothing helps us make the movement from our little selves to a larger world than remembering God in gratitude.” Even Aristotle, quoted at the beginning of this chapter, begins his discussion of how friends and fellow citizens love each other with the comment that our prosperity is for others. Strikingly, he assumes without argument that our possessions are meant to be shared. To contemporary Christians, this isn’t obvious at all. Our cultural formation makes cultivating such a mind-set a daunting challenge.

What makes it so difficult to give things away? What drives acquisition of and attachment to material goods? Aquinas names two hindrances to generosity that make it hard for us to detach. The first, he says, is having sweated for it. If we have earned something ourselves, we will find it harder to give away. Children might find it enjoyable to put Mom’s and Dad’s money in the offering plate at church or shop with a parent’s credit card for back-to-school clothes. When, as teens, they begin to dispense their own meager, hard-earned income, suddenly they feel much less eager to give. We justify our actions accordingly: the little we give, from the little we earn, will make no difference anyway; or, we reason, prudence demands that we first make sure we have enough to save or to cover expenses, and then we give from what remains. Of course, these rationalizations miss the point of the offering as a remedy for the inner attachment of avarice. The point of the practice goes beyond succoring the needy and extends to reshaping our desires and correcting our myopic view of where money is from and what money is for. God doesn’t require imprudence in our giving; rather, he asks for our trust. Our regular practice of giving cultivates freedom and inscribes dependence on God in our hearts.

In the case of the first hindrance, the more something feels like our own, the more painful giving it away will be. In Aquinas’s words, “The avaricious person takes pleasure in the consideration of him or herself as a possessor of riches.”12 The greedy person values the view of herself as the master of her stuff, sufficient to satisfy all her wants and needs. “I earned this, so it’s mine. I am the owner.” Earning and owning make us feel important and in control of our lives. Giving stuff away relinquishes those claims. It’s much easier to be generous with other people’s money, since it’s always easier to judge in their case that they don’t need it or that it won’t cost them much to give it up. Similarly, we can swallow our anger more easily when things that get wrecked or misused or neglected or lost belong to someone else (“It’s just a rental”), because we invest our egos and identities in the things we earn and buy and count as our own.13 When we let avarice take root, we don’t merely want to have more; each of us wants things to count as mine. But in the end, do we possess our possessions, or are we possessed by them?

Along with the excessive attachments that come with having earned something ourselves, Aquinas mentions a second hindrance—namely, the experience of poverty. The vulnerability and anxiety of living with scarcity can deeply shape the habits of our heart and deform our patterns of saving and spending. Often we are not so much attached to the particular things themselves as we are to what they mean or represent to us: “If I have this, then I will have enough; I will have security from want; I will have protection against fear, need, and worry.” Many members of the generation that grew up during the hard years of the Great Depression lived under the shadow of the experience of need for the rest of their lives. We say we believe in a bountiful God, but we do not live like we believe it. The fear of want dies hard.

Developing generosity in the present requires adequately trusting God for the future. My grandfather taught me this—not by words, but by example. His family was too poor to pay for his tuition at the local Christian high school. In a moment of boldness, he went to the principal and told him he wanted to attend school but couldn’t afford it. The principal promptly admitted him, on one condition: “Remember us someday.” The principal’s act of generosity—as an investment in an uncertain future, likely stretching an already tight budget—expressed his trust in the Lord’s provision. His trust was rewarded. My grandfather was still donating generously back to that school until his death many decades later.

It’s true that in our giving we always need to allow adequate provision for ourselves and those under our care. Aquinas and others insist upon this point, with respect to both spiritual and temporal goods.14 Taking due care of our own needs shows practical wisdom and rightly avoids prodigality. The difficulty comes when we try to discern what counts as adequate provision and what counts as excess. The Christian ascetics of the fourth century maintained a solitary existence in the deserts of Egypt, broken only by trips to the market to sell the baskets they made and in turn to use the money to buy bread and oil. The elders of that community counseled selling below the market price and giving alms with any money left over. Bringing a profit home would only tempt them to regard their store of coins as security for the future, instead of learning daily reliance on God. Evagrius knew how vulnerable this left them; in this passage, he exposes greed’s mind games: “Avarice suggests a lengthy old age, inability to perform manual labor, famines that will come along, diseases that will arise, the bitter realities of poverty, and the shame there is in accepting goods from others to meet one’s needs.”15 This word from the desert challenges our own tendencies to stockpile against an uncertain future and our prudent justifications of that behavior. How then can we translate desert asceticism into our own daily practices? How do we loosen our greedy grip on what we think we need and want and deserve? Most importantly, what practices of outer detachment will be required to transform this view of things and the inner attachments it drives? Cassian teaches that total renunciation of possessions must include renunciation of the heart: “Just as the words of the Gospel declare that even those who are not soiled in body have committed adultery in their heart, it is also possible for those who are not weighed down by money to be condemned along with the avaricious for their disposition and attitude. For it was the opportunity to possess that they lacked, not the desire, and it is the latter, rather than [poverty itself], that God is always wont to crown.”16

Avarice in Action

Greed’s internal attachment reveals itself in both our getting and our giving. We probably picture avarice in the familiar form of misers such as Ebenezer Scrooge, whose possessiveness blinded him to others’ need. But our own excessive acquisition and consumption (also in the face of others’ real need)17 likewise reveals greediness, even if our culture socially sanitizes this form. “The one who is greedy is always in want,” writes Horace.18 Greed never says “enough.” The desires of those with liberality, by contrast, observe a rule or limit, regarding both what they want to acquire and what they retain for themselves.

James Twitchell calls luxury an ever-escalating category of things that are “totally unnecessary.”19 How much stuff is enough? When my husband and I married, we lived in a small duplex. Given the cheap price of rent, we weren’t surprised by the old, run-down building. Still, we found ourselves shocked and dismayed by the size of the closets. One cubicle per bedroom, they measured barely deep enough for a single pair of shoes and about an eighth of the size of any closet we had ever seen. Our workout clothes alone filled one of them. “What did the people who built that house ever do with all their clothes?” we wondered. Then I recalled my mother telling me that when she grew up, she had two dresses to her name—one for school and one for church on Sunday. That would have fit in our closet with room to spare! While our current home doesn’t have a walk-in closet, it does have double closets in every room and a cedar closet in the hallway. They are all full.

A professor once devoted a course to the topic of Christian discipleship. He challenged his students to consider Jesus’s example of voluntary poverty, a lifestyle the professor himself had adopted and lived out for many years. The course so inspired two of his students that they gave away all their clothes except two shirts and two pairs of pants, resolving to live according to their needs instead of according to the standards of endless acquisition and consumerist excess. Their commitment was notable and praiseworthy, to be sure—it even made the campus newspaper. But it bears mentioning that what they defined as voluntary poverty entailed living at or above the level of many people for most of human history. Not to mention that their parents were still footing the six-figure bill for their college tuition. Yet when I ask my students how hard it would be for them to give up all but two outfits, they regularly reply, “Nearly impossible.”20

When Aquinas and Augustine discuss “the needs of this life,” whether in the context of food or money, they emphasize not just what bare subsistence requires but also what is necessary for living a life befitting human beings. Pope Francis, for example, radically scaled back on opulence and expenditure when he assumed the papacy, but he still wears clothing appropriate to the dignity of his office. And your home might be large enough to welcome others around the table in hospitality, even if this exceeds the demands of mere shelter. (Alternately, you might find common spaces—at church or a local park—that serve hospitality equally well without the need for a larger home.) Artistic decoration and landscaping make places like cities, homes, and churches lovely and enjoyable, beyond mere functionality. So practices that respect the needs of a fully human life are not one-size-fits-all, calling us all to live on crusts of bread with bare walls and threadbare clothes. Rather, they point us to a meaningful and beautiful existence lived in freedom from being dominated by our desires for stuff. The most important question is, Are we moving closer to open-handedness and lightness of heart, or closer to enslavement? Our possessions should serve our needs and our humanness, instead of our lives revolving around service to our possessions and being driven by our desires for them. What is “needful,” even in this more expansive sense, guides us as a limiting rule, so that our acquisitiveness does not expand without any bounds.

Even if we do not hear a call to a life of voluntary poverty, however, we also needn’t be either misers or shopaholics in order to think twice about our buying and spending habits and what they reveal about the desires and attachments of our hearts. Consider the following thought experiment: Imagine that others had access to all your financial records and spending habits (investment portfolios, savings, checkbook registers, credit card bills, tax returns, receipts, cash flow, etc.) but knew nothing else about you. What sorts of judgments could they make about your character, your loves, your values, your excesses and deficiencies, your ideals and identity? Greed begins in the heart and mind, but it does not necessarily stay hidden inside. Patterns of getting and giving can reveal our hearts’ deepest loves and priorities. As another challenging exercise in self-examination, calculate how many hours a week you spend thinking about buying or acquiring things—by going to the mall, browsing the internet, working longer hours to finance a purchase, or imagining ways to upgrade your current lifestyle by watching home improvement shows or even just observing friends and neighbors. Are your feelings of frustration or depression temporarily alleviated by buying something for yourself? Does earning more money or acquiring more things create a renewed sense of power over your life? In Counterfeit Gods, Tim Keller recommends diagnosing our idols by noting what we spend our free time fantasizing about and imagining ourselves having—or what makes us intensely angry or despairing when we can’t have it.21 In other words, we can recognize our excessive attachments in both our dreams and our broken dreams. These exercises in self-examination may take us beyond the stereotypical pictures of avarice we clearly avoid and closer to confession of our own avaricious tendencies to seek more things because it feels so good to call them “mine.”

Seeking Justice, Loving Mercy

Greed’s inner clutch on the heart can corrode the virtue of generosity and dampen our eagerness to give freely. At its worst, however, greed also incites in us the obstinate refusal to meet even the demands of basic justice, as we opt instead to keep more than our share. The greedy acquire and hold on to possessions even to the point of depriving others of what they deserve or need. Greed causes indifference toward those in want. In this case, avarice undermines the virtue of justice—giving others their due.

Sometimes the greedy hoard more than their share out of malice; other times, out of sheer thoughtlessness. The Greek philosopher Plato called justice (dikaiosunē) the supreme virtue. Opposing it stands the vice of pleonexia—always grasping for more, and more than our share, with no regard for anyone but ourselves. Despite the divide of two millennia between Plato and the present day, contemporary people (like my students) seem instantly to understand what he means. Avarice can lead us to trample justice when we want what rightfully belongs to another, so that we will do anything to get it, even when we don’t need it. Some cases—like elbowing out a sibling for the last piece of pizza—may seem run-of-the-mill, but avarice can also turn murderous, as the story of Naboth’s vineyard illustrates (1 Kings 21). The biblical prophets have plenty to say about how the lifestyles of the wealthy and “religious” cause oppression of and heartlessness toward the poor, a critique that could just as easily be applied to twenty-first-century first-world nations as to ancient Israel. “In this way avarice is a sin directly against one’s neighbor, since one person cannot overabound in external riches without another person lacking them, for temporal goods cannot be possessed by many at the same time.”22 When our mundane buying habits entangle us in systems of exploitation that turn a blind eye to the suffering of our sisters and brothers, we grow more like the rich man who doesn’t even notice Lazarus groaning and begging at his doorstep: “his prosperity serve[d] as a physical, emotional, and spiritual anaesthetic, numbing him to the horrors of his environment.”23 Our overgrasping undercuts others’ real needs, while also hardening our hearts.

We have already seen that avarice has many symptoms and offspring vices, revealing itself in the time we spend planning what to buy and how to acquire things, and the belief that we will be happy and content if only we can purchase some new thing. It is expressed in the anxious restlessness we feel until we can acquire something and in the consumerist conviction that acquisition will quiet that feeling. In its characteristic callousness, however, avarice does more than ramp up our own wants; it also blithely tramples down others’ needs. It lurks behind the fantasies and desires that move us to rationalize lifestyles in which we regularly and programmatically take more than we need or take more than our share, regardless of the collateral damage to those already living without enough. Justice has faded from our view. How can we find our way back to contrition if we can’t see ourselves clearly or, even if we do see ourselves clearly, if we find that we don’t really care?

In response, the church fathers push a persistent line on the common good that undercuts our assumptions about what we really own. Basil, in a sermon on the parable of the rich man who builds bigger barns, tries to shake the avaricious from complacency over their injustice. When your acquisition or keeping deprives another person of what they need, he says, “It is the hungry one’s bread that you hoard, the naked one’s cloak that you retain, the needy one’s money that you withhold. Wherefore as many as you have wronged, you might have succored.”24 Or, as a contemporary paraphrase might put the point: “The money for your expensive coffee this morning belonged to the child who came to school with no breakfast. The new winter coat hanging in your closet next to four other coats (now out of style) belongs to the homeless person you passed on your way downtown last weekend. And the money you have saved for retirement would make the difference between subsistence and starvation for the sweatshop workers who made your favorite hiking boots (worn only twice). Wherefore as many as you have wronged, you might have aided.”

Let’s reframe this thought beyond the usual guilt trip; Basil is trying to teach us a different way of seeing the world. The generous are looking for opportunities to give because they recognize what they have themselves received as a gift. For example, the more generous your heart becomes, the more expansive your vision of your education (and the earnings that come with it) also grows. Perhaps your education, like mine, would not have been possible without your parents’ provision and the stability of your family (modeled through generations), which relied in turn on the safety of your neighborhood and the peace provided by those who protect your nation. Likewise, your successes may be partly due to your spouse’s sacrifice and support, not to mention the encouragement, expectations, and examples you found at your church, school, and elsewhere. The list of circumstances that made possible your education and subsequent job opportunities could easily go on. But here is a sobering point to consider: the same talent that earned you a satisfying career and higher salary lies wasted in less auspicious circumstances. Those who are generous have a larger perspective that also enlarges their hearts.

Perhaps we don’t care if our spending and acquiring deprive someone else, especially if that someone else is safely out of sight. (This might be a good reason to seek more personal encounters with those in real need.) Our not caring comes as no surprise, given that the tradition identifies a characteristic offspring vice of greed as “insensibility to mercy”—the callous hard-heartedness we mentioned above.25 In a way, apathy greases the slide toward injustice: not caring makes it easier to injure outright. From Cassian to Gregory to Aquinas, Christian thinkers taught that avarice’s bitter fruits included being willing to deceive, use, harm, and even betray others for the sake of money. Think of the way otherwise perfectly ordinary families can tear each other apart in court, or refuse to speak to each other for years, over their entitlements to a lucrative inheritance. Cassian likewise analyzes Judas’s betrayal of Christ as motivated by avarice: “He no longer secretly stole from the purse but sought to sell the Lord himself.”26 In other words, when avarice gets its grip on us, the excessive desire to possess trumps even the most fundamental demands of justice and love of neighbor.

What’s the Use?

As with all the vices, greed reveals our hearts’ longing for a good thing gone wrong. We need not label money and possessions as evil; in fact, they count as useful goods. Abundance has its place in human life: fasting does not preclude feasting. Neither should simplicity and stewardship imply that living on bare necessity is the only way to avoid avarice. The fact that a single Steinway concert grand piano costs enough to feed everyone below the poverty line in a small town for months does not give us reason enough, all by itself, to stop buying Steinway pianos (though it may give us pause). However, this view of luxuries sees, in a way that greed does not, that the value of possessions can reach well beyond the monetary realm.

People with well-entrenched avarice are willing to use people to serve their love for money, rather than using money to serve their love for people. Even King Midas stopped short when his greed for gold cost him his daughter—for she, unlike his gold, held a value for him that was beyond all measure. Because he loved her, he could not stand to see her used to satisfy his greedy desires. It is a chilling sign of the depths of his avarice that he needed the threat of a human sacrifice to shake him out of his craving for more.

In the film Ocean’s Eleven, Danny Ocean (professional thief, played by George Clooney) and Terry Benedict (casino owner, played by Andy Garcia) both pursue wealth, greedy for gain. But in a telling scene near the end of the story, Danny’s former wife, Tess (Julia Roberts), now Terry’s partner, secretly watches an exchange between the two men via security cameras. Danny asks Terry a pointed question: if Danny could tell him who had just robbed his casino and could get his money back, would Terry give Tess up in exchange? Coldly and without hesitation, Terry says, “Yes.” The difference between the two men suddenly becomes crystal clear, for Danny would—just as unhesitatingly—leave all his illicit gains to have Tess back. What do you love most, and what means will you use to get it? How we handle money regularly reveals the answers in our hearts.

Aquinas describes greed’s object as money or as whatever things money can buy that are considered useful or profitable.27 Lust and gluttony, on the other hand, desire things insofar as they give us pleasure. It is worth pondering how much of our avarice drives our default tendency to value everything, and indeed everyone, in terms of instrumental value. When the older generation laments that they feel like only a burden to society because they can no longer perform productive labor, and because their medical care constantly drains others’ material resources, have they internalized a system of value that measures people’s true worth in terms of a bottom-line, cost-benefit analysis? Time spent with children also counts as unprofitable in more than one sense, for childcare is usually underpaid or unpaid, and we have precious little in the way of tangible payoff to show for such investments. The use of financial transaction metaphors to describe personal care for other human beings points to a conceptual framework tilted toward avaricious calculations of value. The salary charts for service professions, especially compared to the salaries of those who work in financial fields managing investments, also point not so subtly to implicit assumptions of what we value most. Social systems and structures measure human beings (including ourselves) by their usefulness, and they measure usefulness by the production of things and the possession of money. Greed now invades human life and its value in this subtler way as well.

Pope John Paul II argued that avarice’s pernicious tendency to measure all else in terms of monetary value has successfully infiltrated contemporary society. It lies in the background, shaping the implicit assumptions behind our explicit actions and decisions. He criticized contemporary culture for valuing having things over being human persons—with all the moral and religious significance personhood includes. The darkest form of avarice, according to his view, has as its “only goal . . . the pursuit of one’s own material well-being. The so-called ‘quality of life’ [of human beings] is interpreted primarily or exclusively in terms of economic efficiency . . . [as well as] functionality and usefulness.” If society judges everything ultimately by its monetary or material value, then people who cannot contribute economically are too easily seen as not valuable, period. Even ending life becomes an attractive alternative, John Paul II argues, when “justified by the utilitarian motive of avoiding costs which bring no return and which weigh heavily on society.”28 In communities shaped by this more subtle form of greed, the weak, the very young, the disabled, and the elderly rank as the most vulnerable. But then, aren’t we all in one of those categories sooner or later? Making money the measure of all things, John Paul II continues, really functions as a cover for making human beings—the makers and controllers of money—the measure of all things, including the value of other human beings. This instrumentalization and commodification of human beings by others reveals a deeper connection between avarice and injustice than a particular individual’s impulse to steal or hoard. When avarice moves us to betray each other’s humanity, we betray our own humanity as well. A striking scriptural illustration of this phenomenon is Ahab and Jezebel’s murder of Naboth to acquire his vineyard. The story concludes with this telling line: “Indeed, there was no one like Ahab, who sold himself to do what was evil in the sight of the Lord, urged on by his wife Jezebel” (1 Kings 21:25, emphasis added).

Avarice, like all the vices, pervades our outlook, attitudes, reactions, and emotions even before it shows up in our actions. When we are greedy, we operate with a deeply self-centered perspective on our possessions: we think of them as our own, for us to use as we see fit. In his book Being Consumed, William T. Cavanaugh articulates the deeper picture that the avaricious are missing.

In the Christian tradition, detachment from material goods means using them as a means to a great end, and the greater end is attachment to God and to our fellow human beings. . . . In [this] tradition, the use of material things is meant to be a common use, for the sake of a larger body of people. We do not help each other as individuals but as members of one another. . . . The reason we do not cling to material things is precisely because of our attachment to others. We must constantly be ready to relinquish our claim of ownership, and to use our goods for the common good of the whole body.29

Similarly, Paul Griffiths traces the notion of commonness to Augustine, who argues that only God owns anything, properly speaking. Our yearning to privatize, possess, sequester, own, and master things reduces to both fantasy and idolatry.30 Everything comes to us as a gift from God; our job is to steward things well for him, in order to serve the good of all. In the mass shooting in Las Vegas in 2017, strangers and bystanders offered their cars to transport the wounded and dying to the hospital, and people used whatever fencing was on hand at the venue to create impromptu stretchers; similarly, the film Dunkirk depicts the British merchant marine using fishing boats of all shapes and sizes to rescue their stranded countrymen on the shores of France. In an emergency, we seem to find it natural to treat our things as common goods, meant to be used to serve whomever has the greatest need. In these moments, the things in our keeping truly become instruments of love.

A “Capital” Vice

“The love of money is a root of all kinds of evil,” says Paul (1 Tim. 6:10). Why? Because money is so attractive that we will commit many sins to acquire it? Or because money gives us the power to satisfy any sinful desire—and then get away with it by buying influence or immunity?

Even more, perhaps, loving money nourishes further evil when money itself becomes an overarching goal. Aquinas argues that material wealth allures us because it gives us the illusion that we are self-sufficient—and therefore serves as a powerful incentive to deny our need for God.31 Who among us would turn down the chance to make ourselves comfortable and secure in this world? The possession of money represents this self-sufficiency and the power to safeguard it. In this way, it is a convenient and much less demanding replacement for God. As Frederick Buechner once put it,

The trouble with being rich is that since you can solve with your checkbook virtually all practical problems that bedevil ordinary people, you are left in your leisure with nothing but the great human problems to contend with: how to be happy, how to love and be loved, how to find meaning and purpose in your life. In desperation the rich are continually tempted to believe that they can solve these problems too with their checkbooks, which is presumably what led Jesus to remark one day that for a rich man to get to Heaven is about as easy as for a Cadillac to get through a revolving door.32

Greed, the disordered attachment to possessions and possessing, is the root of all kinds of evil, because of its own roots in pride.

This last explanation cuts deeper than we might first think. Pride anchors and nourishes greed, just like all the other deadly sins. We find it much easier to acquire the means to provide for ourselves than to trust God to provide. In pride-fueled greed, we desire to take over God’s role and make sure we get enough of what we think we need. Augustine identifies the link between power and greed as libido dominandi, which designates the desire for mastery and domination over our lives and over others—the antithesis of dependence. We presume to provide fully for ourselves; when we are self-sufficient and powerful, we won’t have to rely on God. Aquinas explains avarice’s link to pride by pointing out why it counts as a spiritual vice. Although greed ostensibly focuses on money and possessions, it’s not the physical stuff we most desire. He argues for a more fundamental dimension of disorder. We love money and what money can buy, he says, because “avaricious people take pleasure in the consideration of themselves as the possessor of riches,”33 where “riches” are “possessions of which we are the absolute masters.”34 What we most cherish is the picture of ourselves as godlike.

From greed’s poisoned roots trouble spreads in all directions. We grow callous and restless, our desires for more escalate beyond all reason, and greed undercuts our ability to see the true value of things. Further, as Boethius pointed out centuries ago after losing all his earthly possessions, the more money and stuff we possess, the more money, time, and energy we need to protect and care for it all.35 A bigger house means more furniture, more time and money spent cleaning, and more repairs and upkeep, while bigger property and more cars and toys in the garage mean more of the same. Rather than providing freedom from anxiety and ample provision to satisfy our desires, wealth increases our vulnerability to loss and our preoccupation with our stuff, while also incentivizing further desire for upgrades and updates, additions and accessories. By then we’ve invested so much that we need a better security system to protect it all.

Mark Buchanan tells this story to illustrate the ways we rationalize an accumulative lifestyle:

There is an Indian parable in which a guru had a disciple and was so pleased with the man’s spiritual progress that he left him on his own. The man lived in a little mud hut. He lived simply, begging for his food. Each morning, after his devotions, the disciple washed his loincloth and hung it out to dry. One day, he came back to discover the loincloth torn and eaten by rats. He begged the villagers for another, and they gave it to him. But the rats ate that one, too. So he got himself a cat. That took care of the rats, but now when he begged for his food, he had to beg for milk for the cat as well. “This won’t do,” he thought, “I’ll get a cow.” So he got a cow and found he had to beg for fodder. So he decided to till and plant the ground around his hut. But soon he found he had no time for contemplation, so he hired servants to tend his farm. But overseeing the labors became a chore, so he married to have a wife to help him with the work. After time, the disciple became the wealthiest man in the village.

The guru was traveling by there and stopped in. He was shocked to see that where once stood a simple mud hut there now loomed a palace surrounded by a vast estate, worked by many servants.

“What is the meaning of this?” he asked his disciple.

“You won’t believe this, sir,” the man replied, “But there was no other way I could keep my loincloth.”36

Evagrius of Pontus, speaking for the desert fathers and mothers, concurs: “A monk with many possessions is like a heavily laden boat that easily sinks in a sea storm. Just as a very leaky ship is submerged by each wave, so the person with many possessions is awash with his concerns.”37

Augustine explains the insatiability of desire for temporal goods like this: we will never be able to satisfy our deep, human need for an eternal, perfect good with any amount of temporal, imperfect goods.38 So greedy people’s desires for more keep extending without limit, dooming them to a life of frustration and dissatisfaction, rather than contentment. Evagrius observes, “The sea is never filled up even though it takes in a multitude of rivers (cf. Eccles. 1:7); the desire of the avaricious person cannot get its fill of riches. He doubled his wealth and wants to double it again, and he does not stop doubling it until death puts a stop to his endless zeal.”39 In a more contemporary voice, Jen Hatmaker documents her family’s possession purge, only to question soberly whether they have truly shaken the habit of accumulation: “Will I just replace all this? Will I purge another one thousand items three years from now? Will I slowly refill the empty spaces?
