Or will my family disconnect from the machine, creating a more courageous legacy than simply consuming? I want to confront the big part [of me] that says ‘more’ with the smaller part that says ‘enough.’”40

Aquinas makes this point in terms of the distinction between natural wealth and artificial wealth. He defines natural wealth as whatever satisfies our natural needs for genuine human goods. Artificial wealth, on the other hand, is the object of artificially created and inflated desires. (It’s hard to imagine a more prophetic label for the effects of the advertising industry.) Possession of these desired things, he notes, just makes us “despise them and seek others,” because once we possess them, we “realize their insufficiency.”41 Jesus’s Sermon on the Mount makes the same point, linking contentment and freedom from anxiety with where we place the source of ultimate value and emphasizing the ephemeral nature of earthly goods, which can always be lost, spoiled, or taken away (Matt. 6:19–34). In the search for lasting satisfaction, nothing we grasp for ourselves can ever make us fully secure from want.

Getting Free from the Grip of Greed

Avarice has unquestionably got many of us in its grip. How can we struggle free? By knowing our own weakness, first of all. Try documenting all saving and spending for a month. What are we using money for when we spend, and why are we holding on to money when we save? The results reveal our hopes and fears. What do they tell us about where we have invested?

Our imaginations may also require a break from exposure to consumerist pictures of happiness via constant accumulation. For at least a month, refrain from going to the mall or looking at catalogs and magazines or browsing online, and limit exposure to advertising as much as possible, even if this means giving up the internet or television. (If you want to try something truly radical, try giving up spending during Advent, and learn to wait upon the Lord for his gifts, in his time, instead. I guarantee it will transform your celebration of Christmas!) We may well be shocked at how jaded we have become to the daily assault of marketing designed to inflame the desire to possess beyond all reasonable measure. In an article called “Why the Devil Takes VISA: A Christian Response to the Triumph of Consumerism,” Rodney Clapp quotes a retail analyst from the 1950s: “Our enormously productive economy . . . demands that we make consumption our way of life, that we convert the buying and use of goods into rituals, that we seek our spiritual satisfaction, our ego satisfaction, in consumption.”42 The term “ritual” is revealing: as acquisitive lifestyles of always-wanting-more form our character and shape our conception of the good, they, like patterns of worship, engrave loves and loyalties on our minds and hearts. Taking a break from constant exposure to this relentless assault on our desire to acquire and possess may feel like a welcome relief, rather than a burden. It may be the most countercultural thing we ever do.

John Cassian demands “total renunciation” as a condition of the monastic life: no personal possessions are allowed at all; in all things monks and nuns depend on the monastery. Ascetic practice, though extreme, seeks to embody the truth that nothing is truly our own. Mary Margaret Funk translates monastic life into family life this way: “A healthy marriage demands the same as a monastic practice of total renunciation. All is shared. The family pools assets. Priorities are established. Each person yields to the common good while personal needs are met as well as they can be. The household is the monastery. Goods are shared according to need. Things mediate love and compassion.”43 When learning to treat possessions as common goods, we can begin our training at home.

Another way forward reclaims perhaps the oldest advice about handling money on record: tithe every week. Total renunciation of possessions is an option for some, but (like food) material goods are something nobody can live entirely without.44 How can we reorder our love of things, calibrating our desire to match need rather than want, without completely forgoing possessions? By regularly giving our money and possessions back to the Giver in return for his gift. Tithing habitually reminds us that our possessions belong first and foremost to God. If we spend on ourselves first and then give away what remains, either to God or to others, we remain entrenched in the mind-set that it is our prerogative to use our possessions however we see fit. The practice of giving them away regularly (e.g., weekly), and on the first day of the week, loosens our attachment, an attachment that builds and tightens before we are even aware of it. Tithing works like a Sabbath from our natural tendencies to save and spend on ourselves first and foremost. Instead, we give and rely on God. Such use of money reminds us that there is nothing we have that we did not receive and that our job is to steward all our gifts (spiritual and material) in God-honoring ways. Like feasting after fasting, the habit of tithing and the generosity for which it frees us reorders our loves and teaches us a new, life-giving rhythm. Like Sabbath-keeping, tithing teaches us to prioritize giving God his due, and it lets that priority shape both the rest of our week and what remains in our wallets.

Such disciplines help us embody the truths that God is our provider and that we were made to worship him alone. When it comes to greed, can we put our money where our mouth is? Elijah asked the widow of Zarephath to give up her last bit of bread (1 Kings 17) and to trust God to provide for the future—including her life and the life of her child. Tithing may be just the sort of practice of releasing our grip that eventually prepares us for an act of trust and generosity like hers. What way of life would make regular, trustful giving feel more natural to us?

We can sum up such practices of resistance to greed in the spiritual discipline of simplicity. In his book Freedom of Simplicity, Richard Foster offers readers a countercultural vision of life, one grounded in seeking first the kingdom and trusting that all the rest will be added. Such a view of God and our possessions engenders the following practical habits:

Buy things for their usefulness rather than their status.

Reject anything that is producing an addiction in you.

Develop a habit of giving things away.

Refuse to be propagandized by the custodians of modern gadgetry.

Learn to enjoy things without owning them.

Develop a deeper appreciation for the creation.

Look with a healthy skepticism at all “buy now, pay later” schemes.

Obey Jesus’ instructions about plain, honest speech.

Reject anything that breeds the oppression of others.

Shun anything that distracts you from seeking first the kingdom of God.45

Of course, the point of practicing any form of liberation from the power of avarice is not rigid conformity to a code of conduct. Rather, such practices open us to the work of the Spirit, who re-forms our hearts and loosens our grip enough so that we can give, not because we have to, but because we want to. Then our giving flows from a deep well of trust, gratitude, joy, and love.

Richard Rohr says, “All great spirituality is about letting go.” Although many disciplines feel burdensome and difficult at the outset—Adele Ahlberg Calhoun even calls them “mini-death[s] of relinquishment”46—we realize over time that we are traveling lighter and breathing more easily, relieved from the anxiety of always having to manage our possessions. We can identify virtues like generosity and spiritual disciplines like simplicity not by percentages given but by their yield in freedom, lightness, and joy.

In God We Trust

Practices of trustful simplicity and open-handed generosity, arising from the wellspring of a grateful heart, are greed’s greatest antidotes. They prepare us for holy extravagance in our giving to God, as well as the final detachment from all worldly things at death. Recall the Gospel story of the woman who poured a bottle of expensive perfume on Jesus’s feet. Judas reacted by protesting that it could have been sold and the profits given to the poor (John 12:5). To our ears, doesn’t he sound stewardly? Prudential? Charitable? Fiscally responsible? Even if Judas was hoping to skim a little off the top, his basic reaction—“What a waste!”—still resonates. What would we rightly see as good stewardship rather than waste if only we could see the world without greed’s insistence on measuring the worth of each thing, act, and person by its material value or profitability? How do we acquire that vision?

Centuries ago, Aristotle ruled out money as the definition of what made human life happy. His reason? Money is only a means to happiness—an instrumental good valuable only for the sake of further goods we can acquire with it. The greedy mistakenly make money—or perhaps, more accurately, their possession of money and stuff—an end in itself. Why are they tempted to count this as their ultimate end? The wealth in their hands represents security and freedom from fear and want—deep human needs indeed. Thus, the tendency to substitute money’s promise of self-sufficiency for God’s promise to be our all in all easily takes hold of human hearts. Could we imagine a life free from worry over what we will eat or drink or wear (Matt. 6:25–34), a life in which we do not store up for ourselves treasures on earth (6:19)?

Robert Mulholland describes our grasping, greedy selves in spiritual formation language: “The false self is a possessive self,” he writes. “Part of the possessive nature of our false self is interwoven with our fear and protectiveness. Possessions are seen as a means of protection against threats that endanger our false self. Not only are possessions a hedge against the loss of material security, they can also be a valuable asset in manipulating the world in ways that protect our false self from threats to our status quo.”47

It is no coincidence that Scripture recurrently mentions fear in connection with avoiding avarice: “Do not be afraid, little flock, for it is your Father’s good pleasure to give you the kingdom. Sell your possessions, and give alms. Make purses for yourselves that do not wear out, an unfailing treasure in heaven, where no thief comes near and no moth destroys. For where your treasure is, there your heart will be also” (Luke 12:32–34). The writer of Hebrews likewise counsels: “Keep your lives free from the love of money, and be content with what you have; for [Jesus] has said, ‘I will never leave you or forsake you.’ So we can say with confidence, ‘The Lord is my helper; I will not be afraid. What can anyone do to me?’” (Heb. 13:5–6). We can try to assuage our fears by accumulating stuff that won’t last and isn’t secure, or we can invest our trust in God, who alone can promise and deliver real security. The refrain from the Taizé community reflects this biblical connection between lack of fear, security in God’s provision, and gratitude rather than grasping: “In the Lord I’ll be ever thankful / In the Lord I will rejoice / Look to God / Do not be afraid / Lift up your voices / The Lord is near / Lift up your voices / The Lord is near.”48

Our greed shows us trying to overcome fear of want by becoming all-powerful, shunning dependence, and providing for ourselves. Before the face of God, however, our attempts at security and sufficiency will always be exposed as a sham. As Augustine writes, “Avarice wishes to have large possessions; [but] you [God] possess everything.” Our avaricious grasping for more leaves us possessing only a “dim resemblance” of God’s omnipotence.49 When greed pulls at our hearts, we need to ask ourselves, “Why don’t I trust God to give me everything I need?”

Avarice sells us a spiritually dangerous lie. When we are in its grip we believe that the things we own are ours and that we have control over them and, therefore, control over our lives. As C. S. Lewis’s fictional devil Screwtape says,

The sense of ownership [in human beings] is always to be encouraged. The humans are always putting up claims to ownership that sound equally funny in Heaven and in Hell and we must keep them doing so. . . . [The human acts as if he were] a royal child whom his father has placed, for love’s sake, in titular command of some great province, under the real rule of wise counselors, [who comes] to fancy that he really owns the cities, the forests, and the corn, in the way he owns the [building blocks] on the nursery floor.

We [the demons] produce this sense of ownership not only by pride but by confusion. We teach them not to notice the different senses of the possessive pronoun—the finely graded differences that run from “my boots” through “my dog,” “my servant,” “my wife,” “my father,” “my master” and “my country,” to “my God.” They can be taught to reduce all these senses to that of “my boots,” the “my” of ownership. . . .

And all the time the joke is that the word “Mine” in its fully possessive sense cannot be uttered by a human being about anything. In the long run either Our Father [i.e., Satan] or the Enemy [i.e., God] will say “Mine” of each thing that exists, and specially of each [person]. They will find out in the end, never fear, to whom their time, their souls, and their bodies really belong—certainly not to them, whatever happens. At present the Enemy says “Mine” of everything on the pedantic, legalistic ground that He made it: Our Father hopes in the end to say “Mine” of all things on the more realistic and dynamic ground of conquest.50

The freedom to give generously and to live free from anxiety, by contrast, arises from the conviction that not only our things but our very selves belong to God. We must learn, says Stanley Hauerwas, that being “creatures of such a God . . . means we are a people who believe that we do not live in a world dominated by scarcity. Our Lord is a Lord of abundance.”51 Our truest picture of God is that of a lavish Giver. Correspondingly, we rightly see ourselves as grateful receivers of his gifts. When we embrace all things as “the gifts of God for the people of God,” we relearn and rediscover a creation to be celebrated gratefully and shared in common, rather than consumed by greed.

FURTHER REFLECTION

Is greed a problem mainly because of what it does to the greedy person (vice) or because of its harm to others (injustice)? What is so bad about greed’s internal damage?

What is your picture of a secure and comfortable life in which your needs and desires are met? What possessions and purchasing behavior fit that picture? What sorts of desires and fears drive your saving and spending?

What is the best picture of generosity and the freedom of detachment that you have encountered? How could you cultivate gratitude and generosity in your own life as a regular practice?

FURTHER READING

William T. Cavanaugh, Being Consumed: Economics and Christian Desire (Grand Rapids: Eerdmans, 2008).

Richard Foster, The Freedom of Simplicity: Finding Harmony in a Complex World, rev. ed. (San Francisco: HarperSanFrancisco, 2005).

———, “Simplicity,” chap. 6 in Celebration of Discipline: The Path to Spiritual Growth, anniversary ed. (San Francisco: HarperOne, 2018).

cUD

7 
Wrath

Holy Emotion or Hellish Passion?

[Wrath] is the love of justice perverted into the desire for revenge and for the injury of someone else; justice is the proclaimed motive for every manifestation of Wrath.

—Henry Fairlie, The Seven Deadly Sins Today

The power of anger is actually the power of resistance in the soul.

—Josef Pieper, The Four Cardinal Virtues

A Damascus Road Experience

One April, my family took a long-awaited spring break trip. Escape from Michigan’s icy, gray climate for a week of warm sunshine “down south” feels a bit like emerging into the daylight after being trapped in the suffocating darkness of a deep cave. So we hit the road early on Friday morning, alive with anticipation.

Just a few hours into our journey, the highway traffic jammed up. We inched through Indiana and crawled through Kentucky, watching in dismay as our precious vacation was eaten away, hour after hour. Apparently three of the four major cities on our travel route were NCAA March Madness tournament host sites. Combined with spring break traffic, this meant gridlock with no end in sight.

After almost ten exasperating hours in the car, desperate for any chance at forward movement, we maneuvered off the highway and took our chances on the twisting back roads of Tennessee. Forty-five miles per hour felt like flying as we rolled up and down the green hills, whizzing by a few cows and a tiny white-steepled Baptist church or two. Until . . . the car ahead of us slowed to a halt, behind a dozen others. “What on earth is it now?” the kids groaned.

My husband craned his neck out the window. “Looks like there’s some guy walking in the middle of the road,” he reported.

“What?!” I huffed. “What does that crazy fool think he’s doing? Can’t he see he’s holding up at least twenty cars?” As we inched closer, my blood was boiling. “Why can’t he get out of our way? Who does he think he is, anyway?”

“Wait a minute . . .” As we inched closer, my husband squinted at the figure again. Then he looked sheepishly at me. With a lowered voice he said, “Um, it looks like a guy carrying a cross. I think he’s supposed to be dressed up as Jesus.” We gaped at each other, suddenly remembering with chagrin: it was Good Friday. Everyone in the car watched in silence as the pastor, wearing a white robe and a crown of thorns, solemnly struggled down the middle of the road carrying a large wooden cross on his back, with a straggling bunch of followers from his congregation trailing behind.

When he was past, a child’s voice piped up from the back seat: “Wow, Mom, you just called Jesus a crazy fool and told him to get out of our way.”

Who was this Jesus to stand in the way of my expectations? Who was he to slow me down, disrupt my plans, and block my way? (And who was he to humiliate me in front of my children as they caught me red-handed yelling at him for it?)1

For many of us, anger is a problem, one that often reveals a spiritual problem. As the opening story illustrates, we express this emotion when something interferes with what we expect or feel we deserve. The emotion of anger, like fear and shame, haunts our hearts at the depths, where we have buried our hopes and hurts. Anger harnesses powerful energy—hence its volatility and dangerous potential. To confront our anger, we must enter territory fraught with difficulty, for we guard both ourselves and whatever our anger protects with great defensiveness.2

When we examine anger more closely, puzzles arise. Is anger always a spiritually toxic emotion, or can it sometimes be a fitting response to evil? Isn’t it morally good to feel righteous indignation, or are we just self-deceived and self-righteous rationalizers? More importantly, how should Christians—who seek to cultivate Christlike virtue—confront injury and offense, hurtful and hostile disagreement, and persistent injustice in the world? Should we make anger a habit?

A Divided Tradition?

The Christian tradition itself disagrees about whether anger is good or bad. We can hear both sides in the quotes that open this chapter. Maybe your experience leaves you conflicted too. When we picture anger, the emotion can be invigorating and alive with passion, or it can show itself equally ugly and violent. Martin Luther King Jr. inspires us by denouncing injustice in compelling and majestic rhetoric from the pulpit. If he had not felt deeply affronted by racial injustice as an intolerable injury to human dignity, his words would have lacked the right force. As Garret Keizer once said of Jesus, “I am unable to commit to any messiah who doesn’t knock over tables.”3 Anger can embolden the complacent to courageous action and spur us to sacrificial advocacy. But anger can destroy, too, as it does in the frenzied fury of an abusive parent lashing out at a child or the animosity of ordinary citizens turning on each other with road rage, racial epithets, or snarky denunciations that ripple across the Twittersphere. On a larger scale, the political climate seethes with contempt and incivility, protesters shout their outrage, entitled customers stew impatiently over incompetent service, and social media posts pontificate in condescending rhetoric.

Sorting good from bad anger in human cases is often perplexing. It becomes even more complicated when we think about God’s anger. The Bible tells us outright that God burns with anger, and the Minor Prophets make a convincing case for it. Every Catholic funeral mass includes a Dies irae—a call to fear the day of the Lord’s wrath. On the Protestant side, Jonathan Edwards’s famous sermon calls us “sinners in the hands of an angry God.”4 Nonetheless, the apostle Paul’s “sin lists” invariably include warnings against fits of anger, bitterness, and rage, along with the slander and malice they produce (e.g., Gal. 5:20; Col. 3:8). And most of what Scripture has to say about anger is negative—see Proverbs, James, and Jesus’s Sermon on the Mount, for starters.

Christian thinkers also disagree about anger. The tradition divides into two main camps. One side—including Aquinas—argues that anger is a natural expression of human emotion, like sadness or fear. As pain signals injury to the body, so anger signals injury to the soul. On this view, anger does not count as inherently bad, although it can go wrong by attacking the wrong target or burning out of control. However, identifying a disordered strain of anger presumes that we already have a picture of well-ordered anger that operates as a standard for judging deviant cases. Aquinas contrasts righteous, rightly ordered anger with two vices: complacent or apathetic deficiency on one side and wrathful excess on the other. So this line of thinking carefully distinguishes anger, the passion, one of many normal human emotional responses, from wrath, the vice, which names anger’s sinful, excessive, misdirected form.5

Aquinas claims that anger’s rightful target is injustice—that is, anger registers that someone has been wronged and responds with a desire that the wrong be redressed. Anger rightly expresses the thought, “This is not the way it’s supposed to be! Something ought to be done to set things straight!” In this way, as Pieper says, “The power of anger is actually the power of resistance in the soul.”6 Notice that anger requires the language of “ought” and “owe.” Like a flare, anger goes off when we detect not mere hurt, but offense. We can identify anger as a holy passion, then, when it expresses a desire for the “righting of wrongs,” to use Nicholas Wolterstorff’s apt phrase.7 Aquinas even argues that anger can more wholeheartedly express our will to do justice, while a completely dispassionate response may signal a lackluster commitment.8 Emotions rightly echo and enliven choices that reflect our moral values. Good anger, felt deeply, fuels the will’s mandate to do justice—so long as (quoting Gregory the Great) we never let it “withdraw from following reason like a handmaid ready to render service.”9

Christ gives us a model of rightly expressed and targeted anger. Aquinas argues that the Gospels describe Jesus as a human being with the full complement of sinless emotions—from sorrow to anger to delight. Jesus’s emotions lead him to righteous action and advocacy for justice. As Keizer puts it, “Smite him on the cheek and he turns you the other; slap the dignity of the house of prayer, however, and he turns over a table.”10 Notably, none of his anger is self-serving, and the virtue of gentleness nonetheless defines his overall character.

Given the grave injustice in the world, Aquinas’s picture of good anger seems exactly right. But other voices in the Christian tradition fall into a second camp, which counsels caution. Dallas Willard tells us, “There is nothing that can be done with anger that cannot be done better without it,” and King checks our anger with this thought: “[We] must evolve for all human conflict a method that rejects revenge, aggression, and retaliation. The foundation of such a method is love.”11 Can this second strain of Christian voices, which offers an unapologetically strong stance against anger, also have something to teach us?

Evagrius of Pontus warns that anger always disturbs “pure prayer”—the communion with God that is the goal of desert life and discipline.

Everything you do to avenge yourself against a brother who has wronged you will become a stumbling block at the time of prayer. . . .

When you are praying as you should, such things will come over you that you may think it utterly just to resort to anger, but there is absolutely no such thing as just anger against your neighbor. If you search you will find (cf. Matt. 7:7) that it is possible even without anger for the matter to be settled properly. Therefore, make use of every means to avoid an outburst of anger.12

Prayer, by contrast, “is the offshoot of gentleness and freedom from anger.” Seeking to imitate Christ in all things, we must aim above all to be “gentle and humble of heart” (Matt. 11:29). The desert tradition therefore makes mastering anger a key step in spiritual formation.

Cassian, following suit, counsels his monastic community that “in our fourth spiritual combat the deadly poison of anger has to be completely rooted out from the utmost corners of our soul.”13 Heeding the apostle James, he argues that “[our] anger does not produce God’s righteousness” (1:20) and likely does not produce anything righteous at all. Anger can be properly directed only at our own sin, and perhaps the evil demons that incite us to it, never at a human being. Love of neighbor precludes anger targeted directly at another person. This is why Jesus teaches that “anyone who is angry with a brother or sister will be subject to judgment” (Matt. 5:22 NIV) and why Paul instructs the Ephesians to “get rid of all bitterness, rage and anger” (4:31 NIV), noting that the apostle brooks no exceptions to this rule, even righteous causes.14

Why such dire warnings? Cassian makes the case that all anger causes blindness, obscuring our ability to see Christ: “The emotion of wrath blinds the eyes of the soul and prevents us from seeing the Sun of Righteousness [i.e., Christ].” In fact, “For any reason whatsoever the movement of wrath may boil over and blind the eyes of the heart. It is irrelevant whether a layer of gold or one of lead or of some other metal is placed over the eyes; the preciousness of the metal [i.e., the righteousness of the motive] does not change the fact of blindness.”15 Such effects make anger a “fundamental issue in the spiritual life.”16 Whatever our ostensibly good intentions or righteous causes, how can we work for justice if we cannot see Christ—in the situation or in others?

These are strong claims! I have to confess that the first time I read these thinkers I thought they must be exaggerating for rhetorical effect. The spiritual question behind their apparently radical counsel, however, is why we are angry. Evagrius tells the monk not to “give an evil sword to the [demons] . . . by getting inflamed by anger. . . . Over what,” he asks, “‘do you fall to fighting so quickly’ (Prov. 25:8), if indeed you have scorned food, riches, and esteem? And why do you feed this dog, if you claim to own nothing? If it barks and attacks people, obviously it has possessions inside and wants to guard them.”17 Anger rightly guards and defends. But its focus should be guarding the right things against their being wronged. The desert fathers and mothers observe that anger so disturbs reason that it twists any real concern about sin or injustice into service of the self. Look at what angers you. Wrath arises when we defend the false self (at all costs). The false self is our cherished picture of ourselves, propped up by pride. The term, from spiritual formation literature, refers to what the apostle Paul calls “the old self” or sinful nature. We jealously guard this vision of our success and self-importance, which rests on any number of goods that may sound eerily familiar to readers of this book: our reputation, wealth, independence, success, likability, superiority, or control. Evagrius asks each of us, “What is your anger guarding?” What do these possessions, and the fierceness with which we guard them, say about who we think we are and what we believe we deserve?

Desert wisdom took the passions (like anger) as cues or warning signs for disciples to read and heed on the road to humility, as we follow in Christ’s footsteps. According to Cassian’s pastoral report from the desert, most of our anger looks like road rage—yelling at Jesus to get out of our way, rather than walking with him to the cross. Recall from the previous chapter Keller’s diagnostic exercise: note when you feel the most intense negative emotions (e.g., terror, rage, frustration, despair). More often than not, these occasions and emotions indicate places where we guard our deepest attachments to worldly desires and the most significant sources of our identity, holding them safe against surrender to God.18 Because wrath reveals our idolatrous attachments and our prideful sense of self, even if we agree with Aquinas that theoretically anger can be good, Cassian’s spiritual counsel to get rid of all anger is, for most of us most of the time, sobering and sound practical advice.

Target Practice

Let’s return to Aquinas’s more moderate line on anger for a moment. If we grant that it could be a healthy emotion as well as a hellish habit, something we could feel and express either well or badly, then what would mark the difference between good anger and the vice of wrath?

Aquinas analyzes anger as an emotional force of resistance and a complex emotion. Its open antagonism toward whatever injures or threatens is driven by a passionate devotion to the good. Appropriate anger expresses the desire to enact “what is required for justice to be done.”19 When things go well, correcting injustice is our worthy goal. Any punishment of an offense (an evil) may be sought only for the sake of this good. (By contrast, willing evil as such to another person counts as hatred, the opposite of love.) Note that wrath is a peculiarly human sin, since its disorder requires that we have a sense of justice to be twisted. In an ironic testimony to the power of this good, the wrongly angered and wrathful person also perceives his or her end as just or works hard to rationalize it as such. In both cases, the desire for justice—whether real or apparent—strongly motivates us; our anger marches under its banner. Recall that the capital vices begin with great goods, which, when pursued wrongly or excessively, tend to produce much other sin. Thus, the excellence and desirability of its self-proclaimed good end make wrath a capital vice, as does its “impetuosity,” since wrath easily incites us to many other sinful actions by its force.

We also experience the passion of anger physically. Its effect on our bodies makes us alert and ready for action. Anger covers the “fight” portion of our fight-or-flight response to danger, evil, and difficulty. As such, it can be our ally in action, by helping us deal with both inner and outer difficulties that could discourage us from pursuing what’s right. Someone who would otherwise be too shy may need the push of anger to stand up and speak out when he needs to defend the downtrodden. Someone who would otherwise feel too weak may fight beyond the limit of her power if anger fires her spirit. A complacent congregation may need anger to lift it out of indifference and mobilize it into just advocacy.

But if anger makes us ready to fight, then we must “fight the good fight” in order to avoid anger’s turn toward vice. This means fighting for a good cause and fighting well. Our anger must serve the cause, not the other way around. Venting the emotion is not itself the point, even if we succumb to the common mistake of trying to dissipate anger by letting off some steam. With all sins against temperance—including wrath, lust, and gluttony—Aquinas observes (and contemporary psychological studies confirm)20 that, like two-year-olds, the passions get more unruly and harder to control the more we indulge them.21 But merely suppressing anger isn’t the point. The goal of restraining or “tempering” anger is to keep reason’s judgment clear.

“Feelings are, with a few exceptions, good servants,” says Willard. “But they are disastrous masters.”22 We measure the fittingness of anger’s expression by whether it effectively furthers the goal of justice or whether it lays waste to whatever lies in its path. The Greek virtue sophrosunē, which is hard to translate but means something close to “self-mastery” or “self-possession,” describes a person whose emotions serve and support her overall picture of the good. In the wrathful or unselfcontrolled person, by contrast, anger overwhelms, narrowing her view of the good and turning the world she sees red. It seizes control as a self-proclaimed commander-in-chief.

When anger is good, it expresses our passion for justice and love for others. Witness the ways we encounter especially fierce anger in families or intimate relationships, where we find strong ties of attachment. Novelist Alan Paton once described a mother as “like a tigress for [her] child.”23 The deeper the love and the greater the good at stake, the more intense our capacity for anger. You don’t get angry unless you are deeply invested, unless you care. Apathy stands apart as a tepid alternative to both love and anger. At its best, and rightly expressed, anger passionately protects and defends a loved good.

Anger turns wrathful when it fights for its own selfish cause, not for justice, and when it fights dirty. That is, anger turns into the vice of wrath because of problems with its target—whatever it is that makes us angry—or with the way we try to hit that target—how we express our anger. Let’s look at each of these problems in turn.

First, good anger must fight for a good cause—its rightful target. Ask, “What are you angry about?” The answer will be either some selfish end—if wrath is what motivates your anger—or the restoration of justice and shalom—if righteous anger is your incentive. “[Wrath’s] purpose and desire is to eliminate any obstacle to our self-seeking, to retaliate against any threat to our security, to avenge any insult or injury to our person.”24 Wrath’s fighting power tends to protect my ego and entitlements, to the exclusion of the claims of others. “The underlying message of highly angry people,” according to the American Psychological Association website, is that “‘things oughta go my way!’ Angry people tend to feel that . . . any blocking or changing of their plans is an unbearable indignity and that they should not have to suffer this way. Maybe other people do, but NOT them!”25 When anger becomes wrath, the goods it defends are my agenda (thwarted by your foolishness, thoughtlessness, or alternative plans), my control (undermined by your interference, or God’s), my dignity (cut by your offensive comment, neglect, or contemptuous tone), my cherished reputation (besmirched by your derisive slander or truthful exposé), or my high expectations (disappointed by your mistakes, my own failures, or inconvenient realities). If you have high standards and ideals, work as a leader with great responsibility, or devote great effort to planning, wrathful anger may be a persistent temptation.

We commonly respond more to the apparent insolence of an offender’s action against us than to the gravity or damage of the actual offense. Disrespect rankles. Wrathful people’s overprotectiveness about what they are owed signals wrath’s roots in pride. Anger might well serve to protect healthy boundaries, but we excessively inflate these demands. Good anger depends on a rightful sense of what is “due,” either to us or to another, and a sense of what justice requires, but we struggle to differentiate justice from self-justification. (Not to mention that expecting perfect justice in a broken world exacerbates anger further.) As Henry Fairlie notes, we must discern justice’s genuine claims from its counterfeits, lest our “love of justice [be] perverted into the desire for revenge and for the injury of someone,” since “justice is the proclaimed motive for every manifestation of Wrath.”26 Wrath brings out the rationalizer in all of us. In the tradition, “swelling of the mind” names the offspring vice of wrath that mentally magnifies the offense, our woundedness, and our worthiness compared to that of the offender. In retaliation for such “offenses,” wrath ostensibly returns measure for measure. When anger lacks justice’s good judgment and genuine impartiality, good reasoning becomes its puppet.

When anger has the wrong object, what we are angry about is inappropriate. Perhaps no real injury occurred—only a misunderstanding or an imagined offense. Or our anger aims at the wrong person, or the person who happens to be closest, or most available, or most readily wounded—the easiest target within range. Have you ever found yourself lashing out at an intermediate because you could not find or fight the true offender? Perhaps we even lash out at others around us because we feel impotent shaking our fists at God.

The second way wrathful anger can go wrong is in how we express it. Anger should not only aim at a truly just cause but should also seek that end with necessary, effective, and proportionate means. Wrath often falls afoul of this second requirement too. Managing to express anger well tends to be a tall order, as Keizer confesses: “My anger has often seemed out of proportion—that is, too great or too little, more often too great—for the occasion that gave rise to it. My anger has more often distressed those I love than it has afflicted those at whom I was angry. [And] my anger has not carried me far enough toward changing what legitimately enrages me.”27 Even if we admit that anger can be a natural and healthy God-given emotion, we have to come to grips with the fact that more often than not, it burns out of control. We feel enraged, furious, in the grip of something stronger than we are—even something demonic. Some medieval writings used the Latin ira (anger) to name righteous anger, the kind that rightly expresses a just judgment, while they used furor (fury) to identify malformed anger, whether it was irrationally expressed or wildly disproportionate.28 Even a legitimate grievance (the right target) can be dealt with in destructive and disastrous ways. A quick scan of a biblical concordance yields a dozen passages, most of them from Proverbs, giving counsel about excessive anger. Interestingly, none of them mentions a word about the object of our anger. The passages on anger’s rightful expression can be briefly summarized in the advice, “Cool down, you hotheads.”

Aquinas breaks down wrath’s disordered expression into three main categories. We can get angry too easily (e.g., when we are quick-tempered); we can get angrier than we should (e.g., when our anger is too vehement or otherwise disproportionate to the offense); and we can stay angry too long (i.e., when anger smolders into resentment and grudge holding).

The first type—getting angry too easily—often takes the form of irritability. When wrathful anger poisons our mood, every little thing sets us off. We feel quarrelsome and contrary. We see others as uncooperative, inconvenient hindrances and treat them with eye-rolling impatience. We respond to the slightest provocation with bickering, rudeness, complaint, annoyance, cutting remarks, and even profanity. If you are a busy person living without rest or margins, or if you have a foul mouth or a tendency to complain, watch out for this form of wrath in your life.

A prototypical case of the second type—getting angrier than we should—is the familiar blow-up scene, complete with shouting and door slamming. Our emotions explode and even turn violent over some insignificant incident, which in anger’s eye counts as the last straw in a long history of hurt. Or the excessiveness of our emotion betrays a perceived assault on a deeply invested ego. Our anger is calibrated to a swollen sense of what we are owed or exacerbated by too small a view of things. Drivers cut us off in traffic, referees make bad calls, shoppers cut ahead of us in line. How big of a deal will we make of their rudeness, mistakes, and selfishness? Why doesn’t it seem more reasonable to take a deep breath and extend them grace?

Holding on to our anger too long is a third way our expression of anger becomes vicious. Resentment often shows itself as sullenness or spite, the refusal to forgive, and dreaming about vengeance. More mundanely, we can employ passive-aggressive tactics like spoiling another’s pleasure by being uncooperative or disdainful, or letting our anger out in relentless pinpricks rather than mighty blows and great shouts. We spend our time plotting revenge and taking consolation in fantasies where we show them what they really deserve. But as musician Don Henley once put it, “If you keep carrying that anger, it’ll eat you up inside.”29 Moreover, holding grudges and nurturing resentment, even for genuine past offenses, makes us more prone to the first two vicious modes of wrathful expression. When resentment smolders beneath the surface, we go through the day like a snake poised to strike at the first sign of movement (we become quick-tempered), and our anger at the present moment swells to the size of the whole history of harm done (our anger is disproportionate to the offense). All anger is in danger of rationalization, but resentment perhaps more than anything can distort the truthfulness of our memory. As the saying goes, “The older I get, the more vividly I remember things that never happened.”

Worst of all, wrath can go wrong in several of these ways all at once, as when we get excessively angry and stay angry much too long at someone who does not deserve it. Expressions of wrath move us beyond being upset about an injustice to a desire to hurt someone, make them pay, and inflict punishment on them, not as a good but as an evil. Often this goes hand in hand with “totalizing” the offender—that is, reducing the offender’s whole person to his act of offending while omitting the larger context of his full humanity and life story. In such cases, the solution is to “tell more of the truth [about the offender], not less.”30 Cassian warns about anger-induced blindness for good reason.

Our cultural formation trains us in wrath and is known by its fruit. In the Christian tradition of the capital vices, wrath’s “offspring” vices catalog the manifestations of wrath from the inside out. Wrath leads us to demean our offender by magnifying our own importance and the gravity of the offense as we replay the offense over and over in our minds (selectively editing as we go). Today, we might call “swelling of the mind” the first mental step in rationalizing an excessive response, as we enter an imaginary courtroom to justify and avenge ourselves, even if the whole scene is only savored in our thoughts. Our speech and words also express wrathful patterns: these sinful habits include angry outbursts, insulting others, even blaspheming God. “What you say flows from what is in your heart” (Luke 6:45 NLT). If we regularly use insulting words for others, post social media rants, or mutter a steady stream of derogatory commentary while driving, we need to look to the inner source of those habits. Aquinas catalogs several forms of what he calls “injuries with words,” including “reviling, back-biting, gossiping, derision, and cursing.”31 Here we also find ourselves well-practiced in wrath: America’s new cultural trademark is contemptuous communication, often barely disguised as “humorous banter” or “political discourse.” Contempt comes in the form of middle school put-downs, memes and roasts, trash-talking, scoring social points by trading insults on Twitter, or monologuing on talk shows. How many hours of television or talk radio do we have to listen to before it seems like normal conversation to habitually demonize those who disagree with us? Is it really okay to trash people publicly on social media as a recreational pastime? We all know that it’s never a wise idea to read the comments section of anything posted online.

We also learn heightened expectations of the goods we are “due” from nearly incessant advertising. Our idealistic or overinflated presumptions are only further stoked by privileged economic status, a world that values efficiency and speed, the impersonality of our communication, and preferences for personal convenience that trump civility and the common good. No wonder we default to the deformities of wrath. We thrive on power plays and ego-boosting put downs, and humiliation is just another form of humor. Thus we not only drive angrily, but we also live angrily, a moment away from frustration. My Damascus road experience makes a perfect metaphor of modern life. We are all sitting behind the steering wheel, screaming, “Jesus, and everyone else, get the h— out of my way!”

Anger, the “Sword-Bearer of Desire”32

In my collection of cultural artifacts based on the seven deadly sins, I have a book containing “a lavish collection of wicked home interiors” depicting each of the seven vices. One photo displays a large crystal chandelier smashed on the floor, with a crystal-handled knife plunged into the middle of the heap of shattered, sparkling rubble. The authors call it an “unsettling still life.”33 Although wrath—along with lust and gluttony—counts among the “warm” sins, and the wrathful are often called “hot-blooded,” the effects of wrath are ultimately chilling. In other words, wrath may start with a head of steam and gain momentum with a string of epithets, but once we have dehumanized our opponent enough, this vice can lead us to further deeds that injure and even kill.

In The Mission, a film depicting the eighteenth-century European colonization of South America, we find a further set of portraits, this time contrasting wrath at its most murderous with righteous anger at its most loving.34 In the first scene, Rodrigo (Robert De Niro) is a slave trader returning from a recent foray into the jungle. Looking for the woman he loves, he discovers her in bed with his brother, Felipe. He storms out of the room in furious silence. His brother hastily dresses and rushes after him, begging for understanding. Words clash, and soon swords. The fight ends when Rodrigo stabs Felipe and then stands over him and watches him die. Rodrigo’s wrath expresses itself in vengeful and deadly harm to his own brother.

Overcome by remorse, Rodrigo confesses his crime to Father Gabriel (Jeremy Irons). Rodrigo eventually becomes a priest and follows Father Gabriel to a remote native village above the Iguazu waterfall, where the Guarani Indians have become Christians and the Jesuits have established a mission. A short time later, shifting political boundaries extend the slave trade above the falls, threatening the village with extinction. Rodrigo has renounced the sword and become a Catholic priest—forbidden by his own vows to take up arms and use violence against another, because he stands in persona Christi. How will he and Father Gabriel confront those who come with military might to destroy the village and enslave its people?

In a second scene, Rodrigo enters Father Gabriel’s room and declares his intention to renounce his priestly vows, take up the sword again, and fight in the villagers’ defense. Father Gabriel—usually a gentle, humble, and soft-spoken man—turns on him in anger. “You should never have become a priest!” he tells Rodrigo. The world will always try to solve its problems with violence, death, and the power of the sword, he argues passionately. If you want to help the villagers, “then help them as a priest!”

To take up the sword would undercut not only Rodrigo’s vows but also the priests’ witness with the Guarani. In a last appeal, Father Gabriel admonishes Rodrigo, “You have given your life to God, and God is love!” As we see in a later scene, he has not rejected Rodrigo, despite being grieved to the core at his actions. His vehement words are inspired by equally vehement love and concern for Rodrigo’s spiritual well-being and for right worship of God. He intends his words to correct and protect Rodrigo, not to wound or harm. In fact, he pleads not for his own way but for what he believes God is due. Even though he is Rodrigo’s superior in the Jesuit order, his words and manner clearly show that Rodrigo’s disobedience to God troubles him, not Rodrigo’s disobedience to himself. While his anger reveals a deep grief and frustration at Rodrigo’s choice, he does not express antipathy toward the man himself. He longs for his good as his brother in Christ.35

In the end, both Rodrigo and Gabriel die, Rodrigo while fighting valiantly but futilely to defend the villagers, and Gabriel while carrying the eucharistic host out of the village church, offering it to the attacking enemy. Rodrigo lived by the sword and he died by it. Even though he wielded it in the end not for his own selfish interests but for justice and the love of others, he fought from a desire to make things right in his own eyes and by his own might. Rodrigo’s choices revealed his ultimate loyalties. He preferred renouncing his vows to renouncing his sword.

What mission are you on? And what means will you use to pursue it? Dallas Willard once described wrath as our response to an “insult to our kingdom.”36 Sometimes we confuse defending our own kingdom with seeking first God’s kingdom. When we insist on our own way, and our own timing, have we really laid down the weapons of the false, prideful self? Christ teaches us to pray, “Your kingdom come” and “Your will be done” (Matt. 6:10). If, as some have argued, praying for God’s kingdom to come is the quintessential act of hope, then our impatience reveals a failure to trust God to make all things new, albeit in his sometimes unsearchable, upside-down ways.

What is the difference between Rodrigo’s anger at his brother and Gabriel’s anger at Rodrigo? In the first scene, Rodrigo’s wrath is motivated by injury to himself—a sexual betrayal. Moreover, his ill will toward his brother culminates in murder—violence that brings grief and harm to himself, his brother, and the woman they both desired. Gabriel’s anger, on the other hand, arises from a concern for Rodrigo’s spiritual good, the spiritual good of the villagers, and the obedience God is due. His anger does not have much if anything to do with what he claims for himself. He expresses his anger in a passionate admonition to be faithful to God’s way, rather than in harmful blows or even words designed to wound. Rodrigo’s wrath shows us anger’s disordered, disproportionate, and ultimately deadly form. Gabriel shows us anger in the service of justice and love, anger that seeks to win back the offender, rather than inflicting harm on him for harm’s sake or to assuage an offended ego. His anger—in both its object and its manner of expression—respects what was due to God and neighbor.

Even when we think we have a just cause in view, though, destructive expressions of anger serve as warning symptoms, alerting us to the temptations of wrath. We—like Rodrigo and unlike Father Gabriel—are impatient; we tend not to trust God to ultimately make things right, if not in this world, then in the next. Even when we believe there is a just cause at stake, and rightly so, how we get angry and how we express our anger can also show us the ways our own agendas and timetables intrude. Worries about avoiding wrath should certainly not temper our passion for justice now; rather, they should caution us to focus the passion and energy of our anger on God’s agenda, in submission to his will and his way.

Keeping our anger under control allows us to keep a clear view of the goal—namely, the need of the people for whom we seek justice, and the humanity of those who currently thwart it. Martin Luther King Jr., for example, called out racial injustice with passion, but without being dominated by anger or hating his racial oppressors. His love of justice stayed deeply rooted in his desire that all people learn to love each other and see others as God sees them. In his “Letter from a Birmingham Jail,” he writes, “If I have said anything in this letter that . . . is indicative of unreasonable impatience, I beg you to forgive me. If I have said anything in this letter that . . . is indicative of my . . . patience . . . with anything less than brotherhood, I beg God to forgive me. . . . I also hope that circumstances will soon make it possible for me to meet each of you, not as . . . a civil rights leader, but as a fellow clergyman and a Christian brother.”37

The Mission and King’s movement also raise questions about what a correct Christian response to injustice should be. The question “Should Christians be angry?” is ambiguous. It does not distinguish between a single episode of anger and a character trait or habit of getting angry. In giving the stronger views of the desert fathers and mothers their due, therefore, we do not need to claim that all anger is unnecessary or must be laid down. Instead, we can read their counsel as part of a conversation about vice and lifelong formation. If we address anger as a habit, rather than an isolated incident, and we focus on the lifelong project of cultivating a Christlike temperament, our questions shift. If being like Jesus is our calling, every moment of every day, what sort of character does this call us to? He, too, confronted a world in which injustice wreaked real and persistent damage. What virtues help us respond rightly in such a world, and what role should anger play in that response?

The movie Black Panther implicitly asks and answers the same question, though not from a Christian point of view.38 Confronted by systemic injustice toward people we care (and should care) for, what is the most virtuous response? Intervention seems called for, in contrast to withdrawal. But what sort of action should it be? Is violent aggression and arming the oppressed to level the playing field a realistic strategy, a rightly ordered response, or a recipe for more and more violence? Erik Killmonger (Michael B. Jordan) and King T’Challa (Chadwick Boseman) fundamentally disagree about means, even if they are both moved by similar good ends. Killmonger, like Rodrigo, lives violently, with sword in hand. But the king refuses his rival with the words, “That is not our way.” Even the way they compete for the throne shows the differences in their character—the use of raw power, wielded as a means to an end, versus the assertion of power that respects our own dignity, our opponent’s humanity, and the common good. T’Challa leads his council but also listens to the community’s concerns; Killmonger works alone. At the end of the film, T’Challa is rightly moved by Killmonger’s concerns for justice in a world of racism and poverty. He recognizes the “not-rightness” of the way things are and, like King, finds himself passionately denouncing them and prompting others to take action. In response, he mobilizes his resources, however, to build up, not tear down.

Our strategies reveal our hearts. In his Sermon on the Mount, Jesus exposes the root of our actions, linking wrath and wrathful insults to murder: “You have heard that it was said to those of ancient times, ‘You shall not murder’; and ‘whoever murders shall be liable to judgment.’ But I say to you that if you are angry with a brother or sister, you will be liable to judgment; and if you insult a brother or sister, you will be liable to the council; and if you say, ‘You fool,’ you will be liable to the hell of fire” (Matt. 5:21–22). We do not intend the words “You fool” to restore justice or to even the score—we use them to harm and destroy.39 As King once put it, “Returning violence for violence multiplies violence, adding deeper darkness to a night already devoid of stars. . . . Hate cannot drive out hate: only love can do that.”40

When we find ourselves caught in such a hurtful and hateful vicious cycle, wrath has shrunk our hearts and cut us off from life. Frederick Buechner says, “Of the Seven Deadly Sins, wrath is possibly the most fun. To lick your wounds, to smack your lips over grievances long past, to roll over your tongue the prospect of bitter confrontations still to come, to savor to the last toothsome morsel both the pain you are given and the pain you are giving back—in many ways it is a feast fit for a king. The chief drawback is that what you are wolfing down is yourself. The skeleton at the feast is you.”41

There must be another way.

God’s Anger and Ours

John Calvin famously begins his Institutes by saying that we will never know ourselves unless we first have knowledge of God, in whose image we are made. Let’s take that as a frame as we reflect on the place of anger in our lives. We first turn to the Old Testament to see what God’s character is like. Then we will turn to the incarnate Christ to fill out the picture.

The stereotypical view of God in the Old Testament puts wrath and judgment center stage, but a search for a more comprehensive view of God’s anger turns up both more context and convicting counterevidence. First, what does God get angry about?

In the Old Testament, we find God angered when his people break the commandments—that is, when they are unfaithful to him and when they break faith with each other. If we listen to Moses and the prophets, we hear God’s concerns loud and clear. His people fail to trust him above all and fail to show mercy to each other, especially the most vulnerable. We can see from its targets that God’s anger is roused when we disobey the first and second tables of the law: love God above all, and love your neighbor as yourself (Matt. 22:37–39; Mark 12:29–31). The commandments, according to Aquinas, summarize what we owe God and others in perfect justice, and they are perfectly fulfilled in love. Why is God angry? His anger (like ours) is rooted in what he cares most about. He stands against any violation of love.42 In his anger, then, we see that he will go to any length to defeat anything that thwarts the good of his creation, especially the good of his beloved children.

How does God get angry? Dozens of passages from Exodus through the Psalms and books of the prophets (even the ones preaching judgment on Israel) sound a single refrain, repeated over and over: the Lord is slow to anger and abounding in steadfast love (hesed; see Exod. 34:6; Neh. 9:17; Pss. 86:15; 103:8; Joel 2:13; and Jon. 4:2, among others). Anyone who knows the Scriptures will recognize this characterization of God resounding like a refrain in the narrative of Israel’s history: your God is slow to anger; he abounds in steadfast love. God’s love anchors his relationship to his people. And his love makes him slow to anger.

Proverbs’s portrait of the wise person echoes the same idea: almost all its warnings about anger tell us not to be irritable or easily angered. We have noted that Proverbs offers us abundant practical information about how we should be angry. In Proverbs 14:16–17; 17:27; 22:24–25; 29:1; and 29:22, to sample just a few passages, the writer warns against flying off the handle, becoming quickly and easily enraged, and being hotheaded. Its counsel about human anger repeats almost as many times as the refrain about God’s anger. James’s words in the New Testament—“Let everyone be quick to listen, slow to speak, slow to anger; for your anger does not produce God’s righteousness” (1:19–20)—echo the same theme. That this portrait of God and the perfect embodiment of wisdom fits Jesus’s character in the New Testament is, of course, no accident.

Jesus describes himself as “gentle and humble in heart” (Matt. 11:29). Matthew cites Isaiah to describe Christ in the triumphal entry as “gentle” or “lowly” and riding on a humble donkey, in direct contrast to the power and pride displayed by Roman conquerors on warhorses. The Christlike virtues Paul exhorts us to clothe ourselves with include patience and gentleness, qualities echoed in the list of the fruit of the Spirit of Christ (Gal. 5:22–23) and Paul’s description of Jesus in 2 Corinthians (“the humility and gentleness of Christ . . .” [2 Cor. 10:1 NIV]). Certainly, Jesus decisively takes action and exercises authority in many Gospel stories. Yet his overall character remains gentle and marked by humility. And he calls us to be like him (Eph. 5:1–2; Col. 3:9–14).

What about the temple-cleansing story—likely the most memorable episode of Jesus’s anger? It may be worth noting that John describes him not as angry but as consumed with “zeal for [the Lord’s] house” (John 2:7), and the other three Gospels describe his action, not his inner state. By contrast, a lesser-known story in Mark’s Gospel explicitly describes Jesus as angry—an unusual move for the most terse, action-packed Gospel writer. Jesus’s anger in this New Testament passage parallels the Old Testament portrait above. Despite its brevity, the story offers a poignant and moving account.

Again [Jesus] entered the synagogue, and a man was there who had a withered hand. [The religious leaders] watched him to see whether he would cure [the man] on the sabbath, so that they might accuse him. And he said to the man who had the withered hand, “Come forward.” Then he said to them, “Is it lawful to do good or to do harm on the sabbath, to save life or to kill?” But they were silent. He looked around at them with anger; he was grieved at their hardness of heart and said to the man, “Stretch out your hand.” He stretched it out, and his hand was restored. The Pharisees went out and immediately conspired with the Herodians against him, how to destroy him. (Mark 3:1–6)

Jesus feels angry because he loves the man with the shriveled hand and because he sees that the Pharisees refuse to do so. He is deeply frustrated and “grieved at their hardness of heart” toward a brother in need. Their prideful observance of the letter of the law and their sense of “due” has precious little to do with justice toward God or love of neighbor, despite their ostensible piety. They refuse to recognize the image of God in the man right in front of them. They want Jesus to get off their turf and out of their way.

Jesus’s anger clearly targets injustice—but does so with gentleness and love. How does he respond to the religious leaders’ stony silence and refusal to respond? How does Jesus’s anger move him to act? He does not hurt anyone. He does not lash out with words or whips. But neither does he back down. Instead, he heals the man. He authoritatively challenges the leaders, in violation of their supposedly righteous Sabbath laws, and counters their stubborn refusal to do justice and love mercy with a picture of God’s love and justice. He loves; he restores; he sets things right. In doing so, he gives us a character portrait of powerful advocacy carried out with self-possession. And as the end of the passage makes clear, not only does he heal this man, but ultimately, he also accepts the cross to heal the wounds of the world—even those who made themselves his enemies. “He was pierced for our transgressions, . . . and by his wounds we are healed” (Isa. 53:5 NIV). Jesus is a wounded healer.43 When anger rises in us, the desert community recommends that we try to imitate him.

Undoubtedly, God’s anger should be different from ours.44 God has all human history in view; our perspective of what is just may be too small to adequately honor God’s plans and purposes. Perhaps this is why Paul warns the Christians in Rome against prideful expressions of anger: “Beloved, never avenge yourselves, but leave room for the wrath of God; for it is written, ‘Vengeance is mine, I will repay, says the Lord’” (Rom. 12:19). Our anger must show us to be stewards under a God who remains in providential control. Still, in some ways, our anger should imitate God’s—slow to anger and abounding in steadfast love, yet so passionate for justice that we will lay down our lives to secure shalom.

Anger, when it is a holy emotion, has justice as its object and love as its root. Justice concerns giving to another what is due. Good anger fuels our passionate and persistent efforts to make sure others get the respect they deserve, to stand against oppression and tyranny, to hold accountable those who cause injury and damage, to honor our commitments and promises, to give equal treatment to the marginalized, and to uphold rightful authority. Motivated by good anger, we hunger and thirst for righteousness, an appetite that depends on justice as its object but on love as its right expression. Anger in these cases adds energy and passion to the execution of justice. The love that underlies it, however, keeps it in check, for love does not seek to destroy the offender but seeks to honor, protect, and restore all good things. In this way, too, our anger can reflect God’s.

In an interview about which side the Roman Catholic Church would take in the culture wars, Pope Francis responded that he hoped the church would not be on either side of the combat but rather would serve as “a field hospital after battle” in which “our first duty is to tend to the wounded.”45 It’s a beautiful image of what Christians can be if they follow Christ.

What Lurks beneath Wrathful Anger

Why does wrath, or vicious anger, pose such a temptation for us? Our wrath betrays our self-love and self-protective schemes. For all our investment in bolstering our fragile egos, the results are extremely tenuous and easily threatened. Recall that wrath is a vice. The problem with human wrath is not merely the damage it may inflict on others but the heart problem behind its excess.

Wrathful anger’s show of force often covers up or overcompensates for our perceived weakness and vulnerability. Like an animal backed into a corner, we’re on the defensive and lashing out because we feel threatened. Aren’t we overly eager to rise to our own angry defense especially when we feel afraid of what others might think? It seems that the roots of wrath may tap into fear as much as pride. Or, more accurately, the fear born of trusting ourselves in pride stands opposed to the freedom and peace of trusting God. If we can look only to ourselves as an ally and a source of security when we are threatened, then we would do well to be afraid—and angry.

Psychologists call anger a “secondary emotion” because it masks other sorts of distress. Most people feel more comfortable striking out at others than acknowledging what’s really eating them up inside. Marriage researchers John and Julie Gottman liken our emotional life to an iceberg: only anger shows above the surface, but fear, grief, and shame lurk hidden beneath.46 Our anger reveals the ways we avoid pain, protecting ourselves with our own power. As with all the vices, though, our power plays show us captive to just another prideful illusion of control. We rage and are outraged when we can’t control or keep at bay the things that hurt us.

Even in grief, it often feels more satisfying to shake our fists at God than to lament over our woundedness. Anger gives us a way to shout “no!” at a world not aligned with our will. Wrath presumes the power to fix and change things. That’s precisely why its energy aids us in addressing brokenness and injustice. In its desire for things to be made right and its forward-looking energy amid obstacles, anger also aligns with hope. Behind the scenes, then, both expressing lament and maintaining hope can serve as formative background practices that mitigate anger.

In the Christian tradition, the psalms of lament give full voice to our forsakenness, brokenheartedness, bewilderment, and sense of betrayal. Jesus himself spoke such words from Psalm 22 on the cross. How much of our anger at others would dissipate if we could face and share our pain, or if, Job-like, we felt allowed to cry out, “How long, O Lord?,” trusting that God hears our cries of distress? When we are willing to acknowledge pain and weep with those who weep, like Christ at Lazarus’s tomb, we make God’s love incarnate to each other. While wrath shuts the other out or lashes out, love enters into the pain and makes it easier to bear. What if we looked for the hurt and vulnerability behind each other’s anger and made lament a routine part of our lives and liturgies?

Practicing hope47 also reframes anger. In its yearning for shalom, hope shares anger’s energy and end. Christian hope finds its voice in prayer and song, both in worship and in daily life. Prayer is an act of hope that embraces both the power and the mercy of God. In hopeful prayer, we are reminded to put all things in God’s hands, trusting that he will make all things new. Hope leads us to pray, “Your kingdom come,” while releasing our images of what the kingdom ought to be like, and how and when it ought to come. Hope says, “Be strong, take heart, and wait for the Lord.”

Our hopes often take the form of song. Take Mary’s song, for example: the Magnificat (Luke 1:46–55) echoes the promises of God to those before her—to Miriam and Moses, to Hannah and David. A contemporary version of her song goes like this: “My heart shall sing of the day you bring; let the fires of your justice burn. Wipe away all tears, for the dawn draws near, and the world is about to turn!”48 Echoing anger’s principal concerns, Mary’s song asks for deliverance from oppression and counts on God to answer injustice with shalom. But her hopeful posture—like Christ’s humility and gentleness—says, “I am the Lord’s servant,” even as she fully expects to be his agent in bringing the kingdom to fruition. What songs do we, like Mary, have in our hearts? What stories and promises fire our vision of who we are and what we are called to become?

Much of my own anger makes me realize that, when push comes to shove, I really don’t trust God to manage the universe. I would much prefer to direct the traffic—shaking my fists and forcing my agenda—than to weep and to wait for him as he slowly walks up the middle of the road, bearing a cross. If practices of lament and hope gradually transform our hearts, whatever remains of our anger will be more of the right stuff, and less wrathful rage.49

Antidotes for Wrath

How do we know if we have a problem with wrath—the hellish habit rather than the holy emotion? And what can we do about it? Let’s return to the Christian tradition’s divided stance on anger to reconsider.

I once assigned my students a Cassian-like exercise in self-examination. For a week, we each kept a journal documenting every time we got angry. (You could just as easily make notes on your phone.) The instructions were to write down the object of your anger (i.e., what are you angry about?) and then rate the intensity of your anger on a scale of 1 to 5 (i.e., 1 if you are mildly irritated and 5 if you are irate—and if you feel compelled to write a paragraph about what made you angry, give yourself an automatic 5). A few weeks later, with cooler heads and more distance from the incidents that provoked us, we reviewed our journaled responses. How many episodes of anger could we label “righteous,” “justified,” “proportionate,” and “good”? And what patterns emerged? Were we angry too often? Or often too angry? Frequently, reactions that seemed perfectly justified and rational at the time ended up looking petty and self-serving in retrospect, and the situations that had occasioned our anger seemed, in hindsight, more trivial than genuinely offensive. Our records were also useful in helping us better see what sorts of things tended to set us off (our “anger triggers”).

No stranger to studying the vices, I fully expected to discover some bad anger, even a preponderance of it, in the results. But the verdict was even harsher than I anticipated: a vanishingly small amount, if any, of our anger counted as good. If this had been a test, we would have flunked outright. We were stunned. Suddenly Cassian’s unflinching advice to lay down all anger didn’t seem so radical anymore.

Remember, the desert fathers and mothers gave others spiritual direction, not a systematic theology. Likely we would concede to Aquinas that in a world full of injustice, we cannot imagine a right response that could never in principle include anger. But what if that is not the point? Evagrius and Cassian crafted manuals for practical discipleship. Their question as pastoral counselors and spiritual directors was this: Given the vast proportion of our anger that harms others and protects the false self, what sorts of spiritual disciplines do we need in order to become more Christlike? As it turns out, the regular purgation of anger would be a wise and worthwhile exercise for most of us. If we are honest in our self-examination, they tell us, getting rid of our bad anger would effectively mean getting rid of all our anger.

How could we translate desert practice into our lives today? If your blood pressure habitually rises when you’re behind the steering wheel, take your wrath for a ride, confronting temptations to rage while also training to become more Christlike “on the road.” As a spiritual discipline, invite Christ into your car. Commit to driving the speed limit for a month (hurry and impatience go together). Wait for each red light with a rhythmic breath prayer (practice waiting for the Lord, attentive to his presence). Pray for the safety of those who speed past or drive recklessly around you (they probably need your intercession more than your epithets).

As desert counsel further teaches, spiritual disciplines and virtue formation must heed our embodied condition, especially since the emotion of anger engages us on such a visceral level. Our physical busyness, tiredness, overstrained bodies, and hurried lifestyles take their toll on our emotional reactions, relationships, and spiritual well-being. Here, too, an anger journal can reveal destructive or dangerous patterns. Do stressful deadlines, fatigue, or physical pain trigger most of your angry outbursts? We will need to honor our bodies’ needs if we hope to remain “slow to anger.” Often enough, that means physically slowing down. If we slept enough, practiced silence and solitude each day, observed the Sabbath weekly, and took real retreats from work to breathe deeply and enjoy our natural surroundings, we might be less well-trained in wrath. If we paused before each activity to take a deep breath and took the time to listen before we spoke, wouldn’t gentleness come more easily to us?

As important as they are, though, such physical strategies for handling anger deal mainly with symptom control. They do not necessarily excavate our hearts to expose the source of the problem. And wrath, as a vice, starts in the heart and mind. Unlearning the habit of wrath requires attending better to our bodies, but it also requires a change of heart, a reordering of practices and priorities, a transformation of vision. Our wrath reveals problematic assumptions about who we think we really are, how much control over ourselves and our world we desire and assume we have, and what makes us feel threatened or insecure. Whereas wrath is rooted in pride, the strength of gentleness is rooted in deep trust.

G. K. Chesterton once quipped, “God commands us to love our neighbors, and also our enemies, because they are often the same people.”50 Sometimes, though, we are our own worst enemy. Like the religious leaders who stood against Jesus, we may stop at nothing to protect our idolized and idealized sense of self.51 The Christian desert tradition offers this story to illustrate the contrast between pride and humility:

The brothers surrounded Abba John the Short when he was sitting in front of the church, and each of them asked him about their thoughts. [He was providing spiritual direction as an elder to a number of younger disciples.]

Another old man flared up in envy at the sight, and said, “Abba John, your cup is full of poison.”

Abba John answered him, “Yes, Father, it is. But you have said this when you can only see the outside. What would you say if you saw the inside, too?”52

Abba John openly acknowledges his defects—the “inside of the cup,” which God already sees. Humility grounds his equanimity and gentleness. His utter dependence on God gives him freedom from the need to defend himself in the same way that a prideful person would. In the face of public insult, his gentle answer turns away wrath (Prov. 15:1).

Gentleness, a virtue that imitates Christ’s own character, does not imply that we never get angry. Nor does it imply that we should refrain from pressing claims of justice against others or from protecting ourselves against those who threaten. Likewise, gentleness does not lessen our wholehearted “no!” and our deep heartache at the world’s wounded, nor does it mitigate our agency or tireless advocacy against broken systems that beat others down. It does mean, however, that anger should not be the primary mark of our character. Jesus might well have been intensely angry when he cleared the temple of the money changers, and he of all human beings must have been acutely aware of the abundance of injustice in the world. Yet no one would dream of describing him as the wrathful type. Those with the virtue of gentleness have mastered their anger, rather than being mastered by it. When they act with anger, they channel its power rather than being swept away by its force.

Gentleness as a character trait—deeply embedded in humility—depends on much more than the power of self-restraint, however. With these virtues, we acknowledge and trust the mysterious combination of justice and mercy that is God’s way of setting things right. Confident that we do indeed have a role to play in addressing injustice, we can seek God’s kingdom and his righteousness with deep trust that “all manner of things shall be well.”53 Only then can anger serve as a holy “power of resistance” to injustice rather than a hellish habit of insisting on our own way.

FURTHER REFLECTION

Keep your own anger journal for a week. Review it later, asking: What are my anger triggers? What do they reveal about my expectations, my hurts, my hopes and plans, my sense of what I deserve, my sense of what I can control? How much of my anger is “good anger”?

When you consider the two strands in the Christian tradition—Aquinas (righteous anger is virtuous) and Cassian (put away all anger because it blinds us in prayer)—which side do you find more persuasive? Did you reconsider after reading the chapter and examining your own life?

What is a virtuous response to injustice? Reflect on how you could incorporate lament, hope, humility, and gentleness into your own training in Christlikeness.

FURTHER READING

Rebecca K. DeYoung, “What Are You Guarding? Virtuous Anger and Lifelong Practice,” in Becoming Good: New Philosophical Essays in Aid of Virtue Development, ed. Adam Pelser and Scott Cleveland (Oxford: Oxford University Press, forthcoming).

Garret Keizer, The Enigma of Anger: Essays on a Sometimes Deadly Sin (San Francisco: Jossey-Bass, 2002).

cY8

8 
Gluttony

Feeding Your Face and Starving Your Heart
