A glutton is one who raids the icebox for a cure for spiritual malnutrition.

—Frederick Buechner, Wishful Thinking

Pounds or Pleasure?

In the supermarket checkout line, the cover of Family Circle magazine vividly displays a chocolate cappuccino pie. The magazine features an article titled, “Trim Your Tummy: 10 Ways to Curb Your Cravings,” with another article just below it titled, “Irresistible Chocolate Desserts.” If combating gluttony requires curbing our cravings for the irresistible, it looks like we are doomed to lose the battle against our guilty pleasures. With food-obsessive mixed messages like this everywhere, should we be surprised that eating disorders are now something every middle-school kid knows about?

Fast-food diets have sent obesity rates soaring, while the diet industry makes billions of dollars a year. Restaurants advertise the caloric intake of each entrée and boast about serving only natural food, but Americans still out-consume the world in beef and burgers. It’s not even clear what counts as food (healthy or not): an average loaf of bread at the supermarket still contains eleven chemical ingredients we can hardly pronounce, and a dessert’s worth of sugar counts as breakfast cleverly disguised as cereal. America’s national obsession with cheese continues unabated, and now we can pair it with upscaled beverages like a wide array of new and expensive craft beers. Unhealthy foods also tend to be cheaper, so the physical consequences of regularly eating them further burdens those already in poverty. Cultural forces shape our desire for food early, and we feel their effects for a lifetime—on our health, our lifestyles, and our economy.

Would you be shocked to discover that the vice of gluttony does not necessarily have much to do with any of this, at least not directly? Our stereotypical picture of the glutton is captured in a cartoon that features an obese man who looks at his belt after dinner and comments, “One more notch. Room for dessert.” Similarly, a billboard ad for a fitness center features a little green alien issuing a warning: “When they come, they’ll eat the fat ones first.” (Their target audience retaliated with a sidewalk protest, bearing signs that said, “Eat me!”) And the spoof ad for gluttony in Harper’s magazine depicts an enormously overweight person leaping into a pool of water, while a SeaWorld-style audience looks on, mouths agape. The ad’s caption reads, “The Glutton Society: Helping People Make the Most of Themselves for Over 100 Years.” But what if the label of gluttony doesn’t first apply to overeating and being overweight, or forgoing the diet for the doughnuts? What if your extra pounds won’t be enough to tip the scales of judgment further toward sin? Can health-food eaters, the skim-milk latte crowd, and the gourmands be as gluttonous as the junk-food junkies? What about those of us who eat whatever food is fast, convenient, and easy, without giving it much thought at all? People who carry too much weight already bear regular ridicule and shaming. They likely don’t need to be burdened by shouldering the guilt of gluttony, too, while thinner people unfairly get free moral credit for their fast metabolism. Gluttony, like many of the other vices we are studying, has been both oversimplified and misunderstood.

The vice of gluttony names a habit of the heart. Its routine quest for gratification wears a groove in our character, accustoming us to disordered desires. Because we can’t see other people’s hearts and habits of thought, we tend to identify sinful tendencies solely by their behavioral symptoms. Gluttony’s behavioral cues are more complex than we might think, however. We know gluttony must have something to do with eating, and maybe drinking too. Our certainty, however, should end there. How does the mundane physical act of taking in food impact our spiritual life anyway?

Put simply, this vice gluts on pleasure, reducing human life to self-gratification. The glutton salivates at the thought of his or her own pleasure. Excessive pleasure. Immediate, tangible pleasure. Fundamentally, gluttony is primarily not about how much we’re eating but about how much pleasure we take in eating and why. As Aquinas puts it, “Gluttony primarily and intrinsically signifies the intemperate desire to consume food, not the intemperate consumption of food.” “It is a case of gluttony,” he says, “only when we knowingly exceed the measure in eating from a desire for the pleasures of the palate.”1

When we are hungry, we naturally enjoy eating and the satisfaction of feeling full that follows. These pleasures, the food itself, and the act of eating are all good, God-given gifts. God commanded human beings to eat good, pleasing, and delicious fruit in the garden of Eden, and the Gospels show Jesus feasting enough to draw criticism. In the early church, the Spirit swept away the former restrictions on unclean foods. Gluttony, like all the vices, begins with something created good. But sin creeps in and corrupts these pleasures when our desires for them run out of control. Paul talks about gluttony and lust in terms of a person being “mastered” by pleasure (1 Cor. 6:12); elsewhere he speaks of making a god of our stomachs (Phil. 3:19). Gluttony’s status as a vice depends on personal pleasure dominating everything else that’s important. This vice degrades us into being mere self-satisfaction seekers. How can we tell that we have turned pleasure into an idol? Our desire for pleasure can grow so obsessive and all-consuming that we may disregard the real needs of both body and soul to the point of eating ourselves to death.2

The main question we should ask ourselves about food and drink, therefore, is not, “How much is too much?” but, “How dominated by the desire for this pleasure am I?” Or, to put the discernment question in terms of detachment, “How difficult would it be to have to give it up or do without it?” Trouble starts when gluttony reduces eating to an exercise in rewarding ourselves with pleasure perks, consuming whatever we think will make us full and satisfied. The pursuit of pleasure can be obsessively and intentionally sought or so habitually ingrained that it becomes mindless. As gluttons, we use food as a drug, as a means of giving ourselves a needed “pleasure fix” rather than simply and genuinely enjoying it.

Gluttony’s excessive pursuit of the pleasures of the table eventually dulls our appreciation for the food we eat, the pleasure we take in eating it, those with whom we eat, and the God who created what we eat and gave us the ability to take pleasure in it. As Augustine put it, “Virtuous people avail themselves of the things of this life with the moderation of a user, not the attachment of a lover.”3 Or, as Jesus Christ put it, we were not meant to live “by bread alone” (Luke 4:4). Food and pleasure are goods, not gods. There is more to human happiness than gluttony’s quest for gratification.

We Like It “FRESH”

In the Middle Ages, Gregory the Great gave gluttony its own memorable ditty: “Too daintily, too sumptuously, too hastily, too greedily, too much.” The little verse probably lacked a catchy tune, but it did have the benefit of summarizing the forms of gluttony. A contemporary version catalogs the same five with the acronym FRESH: eating fastidiously, ravenously, excessively, sumptuously, and hastily.4 Each form of gluttony embodies a disordered way we can succumb to the pleasure of eating, regardless of whether that shows in our table manners or on our bathroom scales.

Eating “too daintily” or “fastidiously” is gluttony’s first form. Along with eating “too sumptuously,” this form of gluttony primarily has to do with seeking pleasure in what we eat. The other three show gluttonous pleasure-seeking expressed in how we eat. In The Screwtape Letters, C. S. Lewis offers an example of eating too fastidiously that may unseat our typical picture of gluttony. One devil boasts to the other that they have hoodwinked the human race into thinking that they have overcome gluttony because they do not overeat. But, the devils gloat, their demonic influence merely switched strategies—now cleverly tempting us into “the gluttony of delicacy” instead of “the gluttony of excess.” Lewis’s devil describes a woman plagued by the gluttony of delicacy:

She is a positive terror to hostesses and servants. She is always turning from what has been offered to her to say with a demure little sigh and a smile “Oh please, please . . . all I want is a cup of tea, weak but not too weak, and the teeniest weeniest bit of really crisp toast.” You see? Because what she wants is smaller and less costly than what has been set before her, she never recognizes as gluttony her determination to get what she wants, however troublesome it may be to others. . . . The real value of the quiet, unobtrusive work which [the devil] has been doing for years on this old woman can be gauged by the way in which her belly now dominates her whole life. The woman is in what may be called the “All-I-want” state of mind.5

Have you ever entertained a guest for whom nothing is ever quite right or who makes half a dozen special requests about their meal—“Oh, I don’t like that toasted.” “No, no, I never eat such and such.” Are you the predictably dissatisfied restaurant patron who regularly sends the plate back to the kitchen . . . two or three times? Do you insist on beverages (coffee, wine, beer, soda) of a certain brand or quality, declining to drink if you can’t have exactly that kind? Is your fussiness about food burdensome to others with whom you eat? Is your lack of contentment a perennial challenge to their attempts at hospitality and service? Whatever their outward symptoms, fastidious gluttons arrive at the table focused on their expectation of getting a certain pleasure, and they have an equally focused determination to do whatever it takes to get it. They may use good manners and eat moderate amounts, but the desire behind their experience is the drive to secure a certain pleasure for themselves, regardless of how others are put out or put off by their demands.

Remember that we are talking about the glutton as someone with a habit—the person who typically or regularly has this sort of reaction. All gluttons have an excessive and narrow preoccupation with their own satisfaction. The label “eating too daintily” pinpoints their concern for one and only one thing: making their own experience of eating or drinking pleasurable. To test yourself for this form of gluttony, ask yourself whether the experience is spoiled for you if what you eat or drink isn’t just what you expected or liked or wanted.

Gregory’s second form of gluttony—eating “too sumptuously”—resembles the picky eater’s problem in its preoccupation with what we eat. But this glutton excessively seeks the pleasures of satiety and fullness, so she chooses foods that promise this feeling. (Low-carb diets work on the same principle, prescribing protein and fat because they offer maximal satisfaction and keep you feeling full longer.) The typical American daily menu—heavy on the beef, with everything else buttered and battered and fried in fat and served with a cheese or cream sauce—follows the same recipe for satisfaction. And don’t forget chocolate for dessert. These foods taste rich and are filling. In many Christian traditions, the typical Lenten fast directly targeted excessive desire for just such satisfying and sumptuous pleasures by asking Christians to abstain from meat and dairy products and to restrict their consumption to bread and vegetables instead. By contrast, the regular expectation of such pleasures has formed an entire nation’s eating habits.

To understand this form of gluttony, it’s important to realize that we eat not just because food tastes good but also because we desire the satisfaction of feeling full. William Ian Miller points out that dieting would be easy if it were enough to taste the food and then spit it out.6 We want not only to taste what we eat but also to fill ourselves and be filled by it.

Illustrating these two forms of gluttony at once—that is, both eating fastidiously and eating too sumptuously—erstwhile religious dieting guru Gwen Shamblin, founder of the Weigh Down Workshop (a ’90s fad), offers the following “Christian” advice on how to enjoy a meal on her dieting plan:

Once you feel this hunger, select regular foods that you desire. The foods you want are not evil. This includes pizza, Fritos, sour cream, desserts, and ice cream. . . .

. . . Several selections are around [my] house, but the leftover barbecue looks the best. I served myself barbecue, coleslaw, beans, buttered roll, diet soda and a brownie. What I do is taste and rate each food item on the plate. . . .

. . . You will now want to eat your favorite foods first, except for dessert (unless that is what you want). Why? Because you cannot be certain as to when you are going to be full, and once you feel the full feeling, leaving the least-favorite foods on your plate will be easier than leaving the favorite.

I even rate bites inside each category. For example, I searched around in the pork for the juiciest pieces. Since I was approaching full by the time I got to the brownie, I ate the bites that had the most pecans in them. I left some of every food category on my plate, but the juiciest, best morsels were in my stomach. My plate looked as if it had been dissected! And what was left was not appealing.7

The other three forms of gluttony may strike us as more familiar: eating too hastily, too greedily (“ravenously”), and too much (“excessively”). As we might expect, they often go together. My children coined the term “shoveling” for someone who eats too quickly, too greedily, and too much all at once. The shoveler offers us a living picture of the verse, “All human toil is for the mouth, yet the appetite is not satisfied” (Eccles. 6:7). Hasty eating can include putting in another bite before the last is chewed and swallowed, but it also pinpoints the (modern or monastic) sneaky snacker—that is, the person dipping into the cupboard, sampling the stew, or nibbling chips before the appointed hour because they can’t resist or be without for long, especially regarding the food right under their noses.

The excessive overeater is willing to eat past the point of fullness for the sake of indulging her tastes. Even when she knows she will feel sick or bloated later, she takes another helping, savoring every mouthful. Like Augustus Gloop in Roald Dahl’s Charlie and the Chocolate Factory, she feels full, but can’t resist just one more bite. Or, she thinks, “I’m stuffed, but that cheesecake is too wonderful to turn down.” She takes every opportunity to “supersize it!” because her eyes are always bigger than her stomach, and her appetite for the pleasure of the moment exceeds whatever is sufficient to meet her real bodily needs. The brownie mix advertising that it has “More than two bars of chocolate in every box!” caters to the glutton who can never have enough of a good thing. Although this type of glutton does not intend harm to her body, she is willing to risk or ignore the consequences later if it means getting to enjoy more pleasure now.

The greedy eater often eats quickly to make sure he gets as much of the pleasurable dish as he wants before anyone else can elbow in and get to it before him. He fears the disappointment of going back for more and finding that the box of pizza is already empty or that someone else beat him to the last piece of chocolate dessert. Like an overgrasping hoarder, the greedy eater will tend to want to pile his plate high on the first trip to the buffet, taking two helpings right away to prevent the dissatisfaction of returning later and finding nothing left. Aquinas depicts intemperance as a childish sin because of the way such gluttons for pleasure, like children, live under the domination of their appetites, unabashedly behaving according to their wants (even disregarding social proprieties).

I once made the unfortunate parenting decision of allowing my child to have the most sugary confection off the doughnut rack at the bakery. The chocolate cake doughnut, itself already thickly frosted, was filled with a three-inch-high swirl of whipped chocolate mousse, and topped off with an artificially multicolored, sugar-coated gummy worm. According to my prudential calculations, a bite or two of this ridiculous pastry would quickly sicken the child, who would then more agreeably accept my reasonable judgment that it was too disgusting for human consumption. Unfortunately, Aquinas (and Screwtape) turned out to be right—my child delightedly inhaled the whole thing with gusto. Not only that, but the next time we visited the store, he begged for it again. Indulgence had only whetted and ramped up his appetite. Feeding a toddler’s desire for pleasure will only escalate it, not dampen it. As adults, how much have our tastes matured?

When we see these last three forms of gluttony in ourselves, we might regard our condition as a little pathetic. How undignified to find the image-bearers God made as the crown of creation—able to compose symphonies, invent spacecraft, and enjoy spiritual fellowship with God himself—sitting hunched over a plate of food, mouth overstuffed, shoving more in as if we can never get enough. But that’s the point of reflecting on what sort of creature we are. Our humanity means we are more than material beings, made for fulfillment well beyond anything that the pleasures of the table can offer us.

We have said that eating either too fastidiously or too sumptuously usually expresses a desire for pleasure that’s focused on what is being eaten, while eating too hastily, too greedily, and too much are forms of gluttony that express excessive desire for pleasure in how we eat. Note that we can go wrong in what we eat, or we can go wrong in how we eat, or both at once, just as we can be angry at the wrong target, or express anger with disproportionate vehemence, or both at once. It is just as possible to eat healthy and high-quality foods in a manner that still reveals desires gone awry as it is to overindulge on junk food. In all cases of gluttony, however, what we consume and how we consume it give us important clues about why we are eating. Examining our hearts for gluttony demands asking not whether we are fat or thin, polite or impolite, high- or low-brow in our tastes, but whether we are eating to satisfy our own wants in a way that elevates our own satisfaction above other good things.

A while ago I experimented with giving up sweets and snacks entirely for a few weeks. I found, to my chagrin, that I had subconsciously been scheduling my entire day around food. I was either eating something or anticipating the pleasure of eating most of the time. I looked forward to special events like parties and social gatherings as much for the food (or drink, like coffee or wine) that would be there as for the people. If the best food was gone before I arrived, my disappointment would tinge my assessment of the event. I saw the treats as the perk I deserved for showing up. At work, I enjoyed breaks not because they gave me a mental reprieve but because they gave me permission to eat something—and eating something was my mental reprieve. I couldn’t wait to get the kids in bed at night so that I could sit back and enjoy dessert, my reward for surviving the day. I found this discovery about myself rather horrifying. Several weeks into the practice of giving up sweets, however, I found that it felt good to go into a meal hungry and to stop eating before I was overly full. Vegetables tasted better if I hadn’t munched on sweets all afternoon. I appreciated a simple dinner after forgoing the afternoon snack, and I felt better about myself too. Like C. S. Lewis’s old woman, I had been relying on the pleasure of food to compensate for the lack of rest, relaxation, and joy in an overstressed life. I was behaving toward food like a single-minded, starving animal would, because I had starved my life of other things that kept me fully human (more on this story in chap. 10). Buechner sums up such a condition well, defining a glutton as “one who raids the icebox for a cure for spiritual malnutrition.”8

A Taste of the Good Life

Gluttons judge the world from the perspective of satisfying their own desire for pleasure. The pleasure they have in mind, however, is one that we have as material, embodied creatures—gluttons crave physical creature comforts. The effect of this focus is, inevitably, dissatisfaction. Why?

First, no matter how little or how much food we have, indulging our bodily desires offers only temporary satisfaction. Evagrius writes: “If you give yourself over to the desire for food, nothing will suffice to fulfil your pleasure, for the desire for food is a fire that ever takes in and is ever in flames.”9 No matter how delightfully delicious the pleasure we take in eating, we will always get hungry again. The pleasure doesn’t last. An entire book of the Bible—Ecclesiastes—makes this point. In its words, a life devoted to such pleasure-seeking has a predictable result: “the appetite is not satisfied.” This explains why our desires tend to escalate. We need “more and better” than the physical pleasure we already have to make us feel good and fill us up. That’s the trouble with instant gratification: it only gratifies for an instant. Gluttony’s insatiability thus stands opposed to godly contentment (1 Tim. 6:6) and gratitude.

Second, as human beings, we are made to find our flourishing in something more than temporary, physical pleasures. Fulfilling our desire for the pleasure of eating doesn’t fill up the whole person. It still leaves our desires for spiritual goods (e.g., love, friendship, identity, belonging, meaning) empty—hence our compensatory eating of “comfort foods.” If we neglect to fill those spiritual desires for long enough, they tend to become obscured from view. Instead, we find ourselves overly preoccupied with whatever is in immediate sights and under our noses. Our pursuit of such things draws us into an escalating and futile cycle of attempting to avoid spiritual starvation by indulging ourselves physically. When gluttony becomes a habit, we have effectively trained ourselves to appreciate the latter goods to the eclipse of the former. Traditional pictures portray the glutton as more animal-like than human.10 Animals go straight for food without thought for manners, conversation, health, or social commerce—that is, without attention to the symbolic or social aspects of eating that make it fully human or to the human goods that lie beyond the serving platter. When in the thrall of vice, if gluttons are exposed to spiritual pleasures, they fail to notice them or are left cold. When feelings of emptiness threaten, they reach for the quick fix of chocolate, soothing alcohol, a nice meal out, mac and cheese, or the bag of chips close at hand, not to resources that can satisfy them deep down. When we become entrenched in gluttony because we have not restrained or retrained our desires for physical enjoyment, eventually that is the only kind of pleasure we can appreciate, even though it leaves us emotionally depleted and spiritually starved.

Think about the ways we have already acquiesced to gluttony’s ramped-up demands for physical pleasure. Our society has invented things like chewing gum so that we can have the pleasant taste that comes from putting something in our mouths and chewing it and swallowing minty saliva without actually taking in any food or calories. Philosopher Slavoj Žižek once called Diet Coke the drink of nihilists because it has “nothing” (nihil) in every single nutrient category on the label. Yet it remains the beverage of choice for millions of Americans, who enjoy the taste but choose it precisely for its lack of nutritional content. We are hard-pressed to find foods in the supermarket without added sugars or sweeteners. Modern appetites also drive us to invent chemical substitutes for actual foods because they taste better and cost less to produce. Inventions like Olestra—a fat substitute in products like potato chips and microwave popcorn—help us enjoy these foods’ great fatty taste without itself being a digestible substance. Under the guise of manifold new products and “lighter” options of bewildering variety, we actually consume more artificial filler-ingredients and food substitutes, such as soy or corn thickeners, than the advertised product. Add all this up and our eating habits sound pretty perverse: we choose certain foods because they have no calories, chew things not meant to be swallowed, eat products that have been manufactured to look like food, and consume substances that cannot be digested, all so that we can have the unrestricted pleasures of eating while carefully bracketing the real nature and function of food itself. Without caloric consequences, our consumption is limited only by the amount of pleasure we crave, not by any bodily need, function, or capacity. Sometimes our food consumption looks like the moral equivalent of substance abuse, however legal.

Getting beyond Guilty Pleasures

If we take these critical observations too far, however, we might start to feel guilty about any pleasure we may take in eating, especially if it is intense. Can we really relish a meal or dessert or glass of wine without being vicious? Calling gluttony a vice should not slap a “sinful” label on food itself, or eating it, or even enjoying it. On the contrary, Aquinas remarks that the more natural and necessary the activity, the more pleasure God designed to accompany it.11 Aquinas counts eating, an activity necessary for self-preservation, as a case in point. God made food good for our bodies and good-tasting. He designed eating and drinking to give us pleasure. Our bodies rightly need food, and our taste buds enable us to enjoy it—whether sweet, sour, salty, bitter, or a savory combination. He’s also given us the creativity to concoct culinary delights of all kinds. That’s all right and good. Then how do we distinguish between the right sort of enjoyment and the wrong sort?

As we have already seen in previous chapters, we can often understand vices better when we contrast them with opposing virtues. In fact, for every moral virtue, Aristotle and Aquinas typically identify two vices: one takes a good thing too far in one direction, and the other takes it to the opposite extreme. For example, the virtue of courage has two opposing vices paired with it—rashness and cowardice. Courage names the virtue of handling fear well. Someone with courage fears the right things, in the right way, and at the right time, as years of formative emotional training have prepared her to do. The courageous person feels fear when and as she should but doesn’t let her fear keep her from doing the right thing.

The vice of rashness opposes the virtue of courage because the rash person has a habit of rushing headlong into danger or difficulty without due caution or apprehension. A rash person does not properly appreciate the value of her own life, so she will risk it unnecessarily or heedlessly. (Consider brash characters such as Han Solo from Star Wars and Sirius Black from Harry Potter.) It is one thing to risk your life running into a burning house to save a child; it is another thing to perform a life-threatening stunt or to recklessly show off. The rash person doesn’t feel fear in the right way, about the right things, or at the right time. As a rule, she isn’t afraid enough of things that could genuinely threaten the good of her own life, and she isn’t careful enough when risking things of genuine value. On the other hand, the person with the vice of cowardice is too afraid—more afraid than she should be, afraid when she shouldn’t be, and afraid of things that don’t warrant it. Fear holds the coward back from doing the right thing. Overcaution is her problem. She is so fearful of what threatens her that she can’t properly value and stand up for other things that are worth risking or sacrificing herself for. (Contrast Sirius Black with Peter Pettigrew, for example.)12

Both vices—rashness and cowardice—involve feeling the emotion of fear in the wrong way and therefore handling it (and other good things) badly. But they do so in opposite ways: with the first vice, we are deficient in fear, and with the second, our fear is excessive. Virtue follows a middle way between them, calibrating emotions to comport with a reasonable pursuit of human goodness overall.13 Human virtue is possible because the gradual moral formation of our fears and desires is also possible. We need practice to mature in virtue. (See chap. 10 for more on that process.)

The desire for the pleasure of eating and drinking, rightly ordered by the virtue of temperance (so called because we usually need to “temper” these desires), follows the same pattern. The desire for the pleasure of eating, like our capacity to feel fear, comes as part of the repertoire of our natural and God-given responses to the world. The emotional response of fear alerts us to real threats to our protection. Desires for pleasure motivate eating and drinking that sustains our bodies and therefore our lives.14 Both emotional reactions are good and useful for physical and physically vulnerable creatures like us.

How then does our desire for food and the pleasure we take in eating it get distorted? Left untutored, these desires can grow so powerful that they strain our control over them, swelling beyond the use for which God designed them and running roughshod over other good desires, sometimes even drowning them out altogether. When they do so, we have the vice of gluttony, a vice of excess. (To be clear, by excess and deficiency, we refer to the quality of the desire in us—i.e., how badly we want something or how strongly we are attached to it.)

Things easily go haywire in this area of human life, especially since we eat and drink as part of our everyday routine. That’s precisely why the desert fathers and mothers practiced scrutinizing their thoughts and prized discretion about their emotional reactions. Wary of the cumulative effect of indulged desire, they might first instruct us to exercise greater watchfulness and more frequent self-examination.15

Our account would be incomplete, however, if we did not also identify a vice of deficiency opposed to gluttony’s excess—namely, the lack of appreciation for the pleasure of food and drink, a vice Aristotle called “insensitivity.” If we condemn natural feelings as evil, or if we become unable to enjoy the things God meant us to enjoy, then we have become less than we are meant to be and our lives comparably poorer. If we reduce our eating to a bread-and-water subsistence regimen and make eating into a necessary evil that we must merely endure, we will have lost something good. As Christ’s incarnation and bodily resurrection show us, our bodies count as part of human nature. It’s important for virtue and human flourishing to honor and appreciate our physicality and bodily experience.

The classic film Babette’s Feast illustrates the impoverishment of a life stripped of enjoyment of the pleasures of the table. Babette, a French chef who arrives incognito in a pietistic Puritan community as a refugee, serves an austere household of two sisters. When she unexpectedly comes into a fortune, Babette serves them a banquet fit for royalty. Babette shows the little community for whom she cooks her feast that they have flattened and beaten down their ability to appreciate the real goodness of creation by insisting on a joyless asceticism about food.16 To savor a sip of fine wine or to celebrate a Thanksgiving feast rightly expresses proper enjoyment, not forbidden pleasure. Even in the extreme context of early Christian desert asceticism, with its strict regimen of just a little bread, oil, and water, the monk or nun fasting in the cell was expected not only to offer guests a meal but to join them in eating it, even if it meant temporarily breaking a fast. By fasting, they did not aim at restrained desire for its own sake. Instead, they practiced disciplining their own desires to better serve others and wholeheartedly enjoy communion with God. When these hermits regularly joined together on Sunday, they worshiped but also shared a meal. To have too little desire—the vice of insensitivity—and shun good things therefore counts as a disordered attitude along with having too much desire—the vice of gluttony.

Fasting excessively makes a similar mistake, as Aquinas explains: “Reason does not retrench so much from one’s food as to refuse nature its necessary support: thus Jerome says, ‘It matters not whether you are a long or a short time in destroying yourself, since to afflict the body immoderately, whether by excessive lack of nourishment, or by eating or sleeping too little, is to offer a sacrifice of stolen goods.’”17 Elsewhere he quotes Gregory the Great on the same point: we can fail to love and care for ourselves properly through deficiency as well as excess. “Gregory says in his work Moralia: ‘The flesh when restrained more than right is often weakened even for the performance of good deeds, so that while hastening to stifle the forces of sin within, it does not have enough strength to pray or preach. And so while pursuing the enemy, we slay the citizen we love.’”18 Because God designed our bodies with sensitive taste buds and strong natural desires to assuage our hunger and thirst, and because staying alive demands that we not give up food altogether, however, most of us struggle more with gluttony than with its opposite vice. Even back in the fifth century BC, Aristotle observes in his ethical reflections that human beings in general lean further toward excess than deficiency when it comes to pleasure (especially bodily pleasures). So he offers the following advice: counterbalance the desires drawing you toward the extreme of excess by aiming in the opposite direction, at what looks deficient. Following such commonsense counsel, you will more likely hit the golden mean of virtue between both extremes. And with repeated action comes well-formed desire.

Such advice might seem obvious (but still hard to follow). Importantly, however, Aristotle argues that training our desires counts as a crucial part of our character formation—training that “makes a very great difference, or rather, all the difference.” He says, “For moral excellence is concerned with pleasures and pains; it is on account of the pleasure that we do bad things, and on account of the pain that we abstain from noble ones. Hence we ought to have been brought up in a particular way from our very youth, as Plato says, so as both to delight in and to be pained by the things that we ought; for this is the right education.”19 The ancient Greeks thought it obvious that aligning our desires with what is right and good does not come automatically; our initial desires, like immature children, need to be tutored and trained as part of a normal developmental process. Rightly forming our desires requires intentionality and exertion (lest other social and cultural forces form them without our recognizing it). As with practicing a musical instrument or learning to read, we might need years of training before we can even recognize and appreciate certain goods at stake, including the full range of human goods open to us. But our desires—whether well or poorly trained—will shape our ability to see, long for, and enjoy what is good and appropriate, and they will move us to act accordingly.20 This is habit formation’s cumulative effect, as Aristotle notes above. Philosopher Iris Murdoch once described the goal of virtue formation this way: “It is a [moral] task to come to see the world as it is.”21

How do we discover the deeper human goods at stake here without the screen of self-indulgence blocking them from view? How do we find fulfillment beyond the tantalizing immediacy of a full plate? In the rest of this chapter, we’ll look at some ways to realign our view of the full range of goods at stake and to double-check our desires for the pleasure of eating and drinking for distorting effects.

Bread and Water, Bread and Wine

Augustine also offers some practical advice on avoiding gluttony. According to his counsel, what or how much food we eat makes no difference whatsoever to virtue, as long as we are eating in a way that is appropriate to our health, the people we live with, and our vocation.22 Human beings, he points out, are created for a life encompassing goods beyond mere physical subsistence. Therefore, our eating should be regulated not only by what is physically necessary for life and health but also by what is “becoming” or “befitting” all that God calls us to be and do. Further, in our pursuit of pleasure, we must also be attentive to those with whom we live out that calling. To test what really matters to us, he suggests a thought experiment in detachment: whatever and whenever we eat, are we so overly attached to the pleasure that we could not easily and uncomplainingly choose to give it up when duty or necessity requires this?23 Like scrutinizing every ingredient on a nutritional label, this might seem too complicated. Some further explanation of his advice may prove helpful.

First, Augustine tells us that our usual stereotypes about gluttons may not be on target—reminding us that we can’t necessarily pick them out by what they’re eating or how much or how often. Someone who is pregnant with twins may eat every two hours; a professional athlete may consume more at each meal than a nonathlete; a diabetic has to avoid more sugar than others; and babies need cholesterol in their diet, while adults should avoid it. No simple rule, therefore, can tell us what or how much to eat. (Aquinas accordingly ascribes judgments about what and how much we ought to eat in order to be healthy to the art of medicine—nutritionists, in today’s terminology—and not to moral theory.)24 The first of Augustine’s general guidelines for measuring our judgments about appropriate attachments to the pleasure of eating and drinking is this: Are we eating in a way that contributes to or at least maintains our overall health and well-being? If our pleasure from certain foods means that we consume them instead of getting the nutrients we really need, or if we eat pleasurable things even though they damage our body’s function, this signals a problem. Sensory desire is overriding judgments about our overall good. We need to eat well enough for our bodies to work well. Mistreatment of the body—by excess or deficiency—belies a flawed sort of self-love. Just as good parents know that their children’s appetites need a certain amount of discipline (e.g., limiting their access to chocolate doughnuts!), our love for our bodies precludes unguided self-indulgence.25

Augustine’s second guideline tells us to widen our concern beyond ourselves. Our personal physical health surely matters, but it isn’t the only good at stake in eating: we also need to have concern for those with whom we live. For human beings, eating is a social act that forms social bonds. How and what and why we eat should reflect what is appropriate given the needs of others in our family and our community. If we willingly flout justice, generosity, or even etiquette just to get our taste buds on some delicious morsel, we run afoul of this guideline. If we are willing to damage or deprive others to gratify our own desire for pleasure, we should recognize this as a symptom of gluttony. The examples vary widely, including eating the last or best portion ourselves without asking if others at the table needed or wanted some, serving or buying unhealthy foods for our children because we cannot restrain our own appetites for them, keeping foods that we like constantly available in the house when a family member or housemate is not allowed to eat them, or even being so absorbed in eating a delicious meal that we neglect to engage in conversation with others around the table. Is our desire to eat whatever gives us pleasure taking precedence over the good of others with whom we are eating? Is our own pleasure-seeking getting in the way of enjoying being together with others at the table?

A Nigerian student of mine described his family’s custom of serving the eldest child first and giving the eldest the largest helping. That child had to eat slowly enough, however, that if a younger child who finished first was still hungry, that younger child would be served seconds off the eldest child’s plate. The eldest child’s eating—for pleasure and for fullness—was thus always disciplined by and responsive to the needs of others eating with him or her. Likewise, most cultures serve their guests the first and finest portions. Such social customs require curbing our individual desires for pleasure.

Today, many ethical thinkers are stretching our imagination beyond our own dining room table to ask how others—other neighborhoods, nations, animals, and ecosystems—are affected by our eating? If we widen the frame further, we realize that there are more goods in the picture than merely our own tastes and needs.

The third guideline moves us a step further. While the first guideline tells us to double-check that our desire for pleasure isn’t interfering with our own physical good, the good of health,26 and the second reminds us to double-check that the good of others, and our relationship with them, isn’t being compromised by our cravings, the final guideline tells us to honor the spiritual purpose for which we were created. We have God-given gifts and assignments to carry out in the world as citizens of God’s kingdom. Such vocations bring extra factors into play in our decisions about how to handle our desires for food. For example, as Christians, regular fasting may appropriately play a larger and more necessary role in our choices about how often and what to eat, reaching beyond the all-things-in-moderation advice the Greek philosophers regularly offer. Greater abstinence from food may help us focus on spiritual goods and focus our prayers in a way that discipleship in general or a particular kingdom task requires. Our eating should not be ruled by our desire for pleasure; instead, it should be regulated by whatever befits our flourishing as people created for eternal communion with God.

Severe and regular restriction of food and drink—such as the asceticism of the desert fathers and mothers or the less severe rule of Benedictine monastics—might be inappropriate for, say, a head of state entertaining foreign dignitaries at a formal dinner or someone hosting a wedding celebration, since the dignity of office and the goods of hospitality and celebration make serving food of higher quality and quantity right and good in these social contexts. To take a different case, perhaps being a parent or a teacher of children means that we curb our own desires and consumption beyond what we might otherwise choose, in hopes of setting a good example and encouraging good habit formation in them. Or an athlete may choose foods not by how good they taste but by the extra nutrients and calories her body needs to perform at its best. Or perhaps we are Christians living in an overconsumptive culture, and we want to lean against the pull of assimilation by bearing witness to a different way of life. Aquinas claims that those like himself who dedicate their lives to bearing spiritual fruit rather than physical progeny have a vocation that limits the pleasure of eating beyond what is right for most people in families. A nursing mother’s life might revolve around feedings and food schedules; her calling to love and care for the most vulnerable demands an intimate relationship with nourishment. A pastor I know fasts every Wednesday, along with a regular regimen of prayer, solitude, and immersion in Scripture: in his calling, he needs these disciplines to maintain the integrity of his ministry and stay humbly centered on his need for Christ. The desert fathers and mothers broke their fasts when a guest arrived for the sake of hospitality. The point of all these examples is this: who we are called to be and what we are called to do as Christ-followers add further considerations of appropriateness to decisions about what, why, and how to eat. Good formation means pursuing our good as whole persons in our practices of eating as in all other areas of life—not just attending to our physical needs or desires for gratification. Gluttony, by contrast, would make our own pleasure the only, or the primary, consideration.

You are What You Eat

Eating is not merely a physical act for us. Like sexual activity, it also expresses our humanity and identity, with all the social and symbolic value that involves. As an analogy, take the work of a soldier. One of my students served as a US Marine. After a deployment abroad he once brought me an MRE—a “Meal Ready to Eat.” These meals come in a thick brown vinyl package and include an entrée; crackers with cheese, peanut butter, or jelly; a dessert or snack (the pound cake isn’t bad); a dry-mix packet of cider, coffee, or hot cocoa; tiny packets of salt, pepper, and Tabasco sauce; plastic silverware and a napkin; and a flameless ration heater that, when combined with water, produces a chemical reaction hot enough to cook the entrée. The military designs MREs to last for several years unopened, to survive temperatures from -60 to 120 degrees Fahrenheit, and to withstand a parachute drop from 1,250 feet (or 100 feet, from a helicopter, without a chute). The meals provide soldiers in the field with the sustenance they need to perform their mission until they can return to base camp. Some Marines I knew had eaten meals like this for weeks on end when they were deployed overseas on a mission.

Serving as a Marine, especially in the field of operations, involves personal discipline and teamwork dedicated to achieving a common mission. My student’s harrowing stories made clear that if a Marine did not subordinate his desire for pleasure to the demands of his mission, he could seriously endanger himself and his comrades. He would also jeopardize his effectiveness in carrying out his assignment. The military designed MREs for the purpose of providing maximal mission support. Note, however, that even in the remote desert, utensils and a napkin were provided, serving to humanize consumption. And the fare, which included spices and desserts, was intended to be appetizing (although I am told that some Marines put the Tabasco sauce on everything). Eating should still be pleasurable. But clearly, self-indulgence has no place in the character of a Marine on duty. Their eating habits and daily discipline have social consequences, and the way they eat expresses both their identity and their commitment to their corporate mission.

Extending this analogy to the Christian life, we can ask ourselves whether our eating habits serve our own pleasure more than they serve our spiritual mission. Given what God has asked each of us to do and be, does our pursuit of and attachment to pleasure equip us well to live out that identity and carry out that mission? Have we disciplined our bodies and appetites to be our “willing partners” in a life of discipleship?27 Scot McKnight notes that “bodily discipline prepares a Christian for the long haul,”28 and Dallas Willard recommends the “systematic practice” of self-denial and self-restraint because it teaches us to endure “deprivations of all kinds” while maintaining “a clear and constant sense of [our] resources in God.”29 If we learn through daily habit to be less dependent on having our desires met now, we will be free to turn our attention to goods beyond ourselves that require us to wait—primarily, eternal goods. The glutton, by contrast, eats now and for himself alone. His mission is to serve himself by gratifying his own appetites, the sooner the better. We could call his mission objectives “my pleasure first” and “my pleasure now,” since he orders the rest of his life around those two things. His god is his belly, and he honors its daily demands faithfully.

In the military, new recruits are not thrown straight into combat. Nobody would dream of immediately subjecting them to the rigors and stress of field operations on the front lines. They first go through an extensive training program together. What might training look like for Christians heading into a serious mission? Before Jesus began his ministry, he fasted in the desert for forty days. His spiritual mission eventually required the ultimate bodily sacrifice. Was his desert regimen of detachment necessary for his readiness? Is something comparable necessary for us? When we think about developing spiritual discipline regarding the physical pleasures of eating, we might return to Augustine’s advice again: Are we ready and willing to do without pleasure, if this is asked of us—and to do so uncomplainingly? Or does our longing for satisfaction and fullness turn us inward? How can we cultivate the interior freedom to seek God and serve others? How can we learn to see beyond what the world offers and keep eternal goods in view? What training would it take to get ourselves to the point of being prepared to carry out our calling?

We should note too that our mission and identity as Christians will include feasting as well as fasting. God created us for celebration and delight—including our enjoyment of food. A particular Marine I knew was deployed overseas during the official birthday of the Marine Corps. He sent back a picture of himself and his friends next to an enormous birthday cake that had “Happy Birthday, US Marine Corps” written across it in brightly colored frosting. They celebrated and feasted that day, despite 130-degree temperatures, grueling hours, the dangers of battle, and a spartan life in dusty canvas tents. Effective service in his mission required a regular reminder of who he was and what he stood for. That reminder came in the form of a celebration—the enjoyment of a specially prepared food.

As Christians, we have a similar identity marker in the form of the eucharistic feast, the celebration of the Lord’s Supper. Our eating and drinking at that meal give us a living picture of Jesus’s sacrifice and anticipate the wedding banquet of the Lamb. To take a seat at his table is to remember who we are and envision what we will be as members of his body. His miracles—producing the best wine at Cana’s wedding feast and abundant bread for multitudes on a mountainside—invite us to eat and drink with him as ways of learning who he is: the bridegroom and Bread of Life. We learn so much about God’s identity and ours through food. He invites us to celebrate and feast with alleluias and thanksgiving, and he feeds us accordingly. Perhaps the old saying is true then: food is love.

As the analogy of military discipline and identity illustrates, food and drink support far greater goods and serve far greater goals than mere bodily preservation (as good as that is). In their fully human form they have social and symbolic value as well. By keeping our desires for their pleasures well-ordered, we are also keeping our relationships to God and other people in order. Our eating creates social bonds, communicates love for each other, connects us to sources of provision and security, and calls for celebration. We mark life’s most important moments with foods—from a nursing mother’s bond with her child to a wedding or birthday cake, from family holiday traditions and shared cups of coffee with friends to church potlucks and warm suppers brought after a funeral. Eating nourishes us, not just physically but also emotionally and spiritually. For that reason, we can appreciate the value of virtue (and the destructive power of vice) in this area of our moral lives. Against gluttony’s temptations to self-centeredness and self-indulgence, virtue helps us affirm both the goodness of pleasure and the need to temper and train our desire for it. That point in turn helps us understand why both fasting and feasting are characteristic parts of the Christian life.

Gluttony’s Remedy: Fasting

If we suspect that eating for excessive pleasure is an area of our lives that needs moral scrutiny, we might try Augustine’s test as an initial exercise in self-examination. How hard would we find it to give up something for, say, a month, and to do so without complaint? Whether we practice them regularly or occasionally, disciplines of detachment reliably reveal where our excessive attachments, selfish preoccupations, idols of identity, and disordered loves lie. As Richard Foster remarks, “Fasting reveals the things that control us.”30

My students and I have tried giving up sugary foods, snacking between meals, caffeinated beverages, alcohol, and even lunch. Some of us found it exceedingly difficult to do without, even though—as centuries of monastic practice show us—none of these foods or meals were even necessary for health. The difficulty of detachment spoke volumes about how dependent we had become on certain pleasures associated with eating and drinking, without even realizing it. Our formation in pleasure-seeking had worn deep grooves of expectation about having our own cravings met, and immediately so.

Importantly, my students and I tried these disciplines together. As with military training or team sports, we find encouragement to persevere in rigorous exercises when we undertake them as a community of practice. Scot McKnight notes that the stationary (i.e., weekly appointed) fasts of the church took their name from the Latin statio, a term for a military cohort.31 As the body of Christ, the church also functions corporately: Paul calls it an organic unity of many members with a common mission. In our fasting and feasting, we enact practices of contrition and celebration as a common project, not simply as a matter of personal choice. Traditionally, the church appointed weekly and yearly fasts that were for everyone, not just for the super-saintly few. Moreover, everyone fasted on the same days (Wednesday and Friday) during the same seasons of the year (Advent and Lent) in preparation for the great feasts at Christmas and Easter. As a corporate body, we train together to be spiritually fit and ready.

Why do we need bodily practice, and bodily abstinence, for spiritual fitness and a spiritual mission? Dallas Willard answers this way:

Very little of our being lies under the direction of our conscious minds and very little of our action runs from our thoughts and consciously chosen intentions. Our mind on its own is an extremely feeble instrument, whose power over life we constantly tend to exaggerate. We are incarnate beings in our very nature, and we live from our bodies. If we are to be transformed, the body must be transformed, and this is not accomplished by talking to it.

The training that leads to doing what we hear from Jesus must therefore involve, first the purposeful disruption of our “automatic” thoughts, feelings, and actions by doing different things with our body. And then, through various intentional practices, we place the body before God . . . in such a way that our whole self is retrained away from the old kingdoms around and within us and into “the kingdom of the Son and his love” (Col. 1:13).32

Fasting as a spiritual discipline accomplishes at least two things. By giving up certain foods for a time, and by not eating to satiety, we learn to let go of pleasure as an idol we get to control and to receive it instead as a gift. Our preoccupying thoughts will shift, over time, to be freer from bodily needs,33 and we will accustom ourselves to appreciate and be content with simple foods. Who will appreciate a simple piece of cheese more—someone who eats several Big Macs every day or someone who has just undergone a Lenten fast, abstaining from meat and dairy for several weeks? We should not misunderstand fasting as a practice that devalues eating and food or regards it as evil. Nothing could be further from the truth. Only someone who has experienced gnawing hunger after a grueling hike up a mountain can say, as a friend of mine once did, “There’s nothing better than a peanut butter and jelly sandwich at ten thousand feet.” Fasting heightens our appreciation for material goods, while also keeping this appreciation in its place, with room for the enjoyment of both simple bodily pleasures and spiritual goods. Orthodox theologian Kallistos Ware wisely notes, “It would be misleading to speak only of this element of weariness and hunger. Abstinence leads, not merely to this, but also to a sense of lightness, wakefulness, freedom, and joy. . . . While involving self-denial, fasting does not seek to do violence to the body but rather to restore it to health and equilibrium. Most of us in the Western world habitually eat more than we need.”34 Cassian agrees. Whatever regimen of fasting we undertake—and he shows remarkable flexibility about this, depending on differences in bodily strength—the underlying principle is to eat without seeking satisfaction or satiety: “Integrity of mind,” he writes, “is closely connected with an empty stomach.”35

This brings us to fasting’s second effect, which is that it increases our appetite for spiritual goods and makes us keenly aware of our dependence on God. Ware explains:

The primary aim of fasting is to make us conscious of our dependence on God. If practiced seriously, the Lenten abstinence from food . . . involves a considerable measure of real hunger, and also a feeling of tiredness and physical exhaustion. The purpose of this is to lead us in turn to a sense of inward brokenness and contrition; to bring us, that is, to the point where we appreciate the full force of Christ’s statement, “Without Me you can do nothing” (John 15:5).

If we always take our fill of food and drink, we easily grow confident in our own abilities, acquiring a false sense of autonomy and self-sufficiency. The observance of a physical fast undermines this sinful complacency.36

Gluttony, like the other capital vices, grows out of pride. How do a heady sin like pride and a visceral vice like gluttony fit together? So far, we have described gluttony as the excessive desire for the pleasure of eating and drinking. Why do we find gluttony so appealing? When a gluttonous habit shapes our delights and longings, excessive desire marks our need to fill ourselves and to provide for our own happiness and pleasure. In gluttony, we not only center our lives on pleasure, but we also prefer to supply that pleasure by ourselves, for ourselves. Gluttons want to define happiness for themselves and keep its attainment under their own control.37 Rather than accepting food as a gift from God and looking to God to fill our spiritual hungers as well as our bodily ones, we take on God’s responsibility to meet our needs and wants. When the gluttonous feel need or emptiness, they do not want to have to depend on God or wait on God to fill it. We prefer vending-machine Christianity. The pleasure of food is not only readily available (multiple times a day, for many Americans), but it is something we can use to quell our own feelings of need and longing. With food, we can comfort ourselves, fill ourselves, provide pleasure for ourselves—if only physically, and if only for a short while. The glutton’s pursuit of fulfillment depends on what he can do, not on what God will give him.

When we equate hedonism with happiness, gluttony escalates to count as a capital vice. We easily mistake pleasure for happiness—we seek it for its own sake, and not for the sake of anything else, and it seems to deliver fulfillment-like satisfaction. Like Adam and Eve, however, we are not simply making an innocent mistake. The habitual and addictive quality of our pleasure-seeking reveals our prideful quest to ensure that we can make ourselves happy and full with whatever delightful and delicious morsel hangs on a tree or sits on our plates. With every meal, every day, we practice keeping happiness firmly in our own hands (and mouths and bellies). The fasting of the desert fathers, by contrast, was an attempt not only to pray but also to live the words “Give us this day our daily bread.” Dallas Willard goes even further: “Fasting confirms our utter dependence on God,” combating pride, embodying trust, and teaching us to “feast on him.”38

When we seek our own surfeit as gluttons, we make serving ourselves the focus of our own attention, because we make our own (nonshareable) pleasure the most important value. The way we use food to comfort ourselves, and its symbolic value as a cue for fullness and provision, however, further signals that more than simple self-indulgence is at work in gluttony. The gluttonous seem to want to defy the truth of Christ’s remark that human beings do not “live by bread alone” (Matt. 4:4). They do want to live by bread alone, and moreover, bread stripped of its deeper significance—as the eucharistic sacrifice, the body of Christ. The gluttonous approach food with gratification in mind, not gratitude. They treat food as a tool to sate themselves, rather than receiving it as a good gift to be enjoyed, celebrated, and shared. Think of our gluttonous approach as the antithesis of Christ’s example at the Last Supper—he gave thanks for the bread and blessed it, then passed it to all to eat in communion.

The regular practice of Christian fasting embodies our yearning not for satisfaction in the present life but for full communion with God that is yet to come. Those waiting in hope for the bridegroom to arrive practice fasting as a “necessary preparation” to receive him with unhindered joy.39 Our feasting, in a parallel way, gives us a foretaste of that delight. In each practice, our bodies enact a spiritual script.

Ironically, the pursuit of happiness in material goods and the pleasures they afford, as well as the attempt to provide them for ourselves in the form of pleasure, can never fill or fulfill us, even when we stuff ourselves full. Honoring our full nature as human beings challenges us to rethink “the role the body plays in our spirituality.”40 We need to affirm the goodness of bodily pleasure as a gift, even as we incarnate our conviction that no amount of that gift can adequately satisfy our heart’s desire for loving union with God. Our bodies are important to God. Our bodily needs and longings for bread are a living parable, pointing us—in dependence, humility, and joy—to our need for God, the Bread of Life. No wonder even the overstuffed glutton remains spiritually empty, always hungry for more.

FURTHER REFLECTION

There are many ways to be gluttonous. Which ones are most problematic for you? What would be the hardest food or drink (or meal) to give up and why? Why are you eating?

How is fasting different from dieting? How is feasting different from gluttony?

With pride in mind, reflect on these words, quoted above, from Ware: “While involving self-denial, fasting does not seek to do violence to the body but rather to restore it to health and equilibrium. Most of us in the Western world habitually eat more than we need. . . . The primary aim of fasting is to make us conscious of our dependence on God. . . . If we always take our fill of food and drink, we easily grow confident in our own abilities, acquiring a false sense of autonomy and self-sufficiency.”

FURTHER READING

Scot McKnight, Fasting, ed. Phyllis Tickle, Ancient Practices Series (Nashville: Nelson, 2009).

Robert C. Roberts, “Temperance,” in Virtues and Their Vices, ed. Kevin Timpe and Craig Boyd (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2014), 93–111.

c11D

9 
Lust

Sexuality Stripped Down

Sex is sinful to the degree that, instead of drawing you closer to other human beings in their humanness, it unites bodies but leaves the lives inside them hungrier and more alone than before.

—Frederick Buechner, Wishful Thinking

Sex: Is it our nemesis, the greatest and most shameful of all sins? Why are we then tempted to treat it as the key to happiness, the ultimate fulfillment of desire? Does it picture our relationship with God—holy, intimate, and beautiful—or does it merely offer a casual romp for recreation? If Scripture says sex and marriage are good, why does most Christian radio play only platonic love songs crooned to God, not to flesh-and-blood people whom we rightly sexually desire? On the other hand, if spring break sexual free-for-alls and hook-ups at parties provide all the excitement we need, why do secular rock songs regularly extol yearnings for a “hold me in your arms forever” view of sex linked to promises of lifelong love? Does sex mean everything or nothing? Is it sacred or sinful or something in between? Is it for procreation, personal intimacy, or physical pleasure? Why is it such a taboo topic to discuss? Why is it so ubiquitous that we see it on broadcasts and billboards everywhere? Needless to say, both the culture and the church send many mixed messages about sex—as if we needed confusion on top of the difficulty of handling sexual desire itself!

Like many cultural voices, William Gass calls lust merely “the sexual impulse dialed up.” What’s the harm in it? It merely makes one “alert and on the search, . . . encouragingly alive, paying attention to one’s friends and companions because they may relieve the itch.” As a further point in its favor, “satisfied lust may mean that two people are happy.” The real problem, he argues, lies with those who would squelch our natural desire for pleasure, like the religious prudes and the pleasure haters who taint it with guilt-ridden “thou shalt nots.”1 But is there no other way to order sexual desire than to restrict our unadulterated access to pleasure?2

Philosopher and spiritual writer Dallas Willard counters with a more complicated view: “Intimacy is a spiritual hunger of the human soul, and we cannot escape it. This has always been true and remains true today. We now keep hammering the sex button in the hope that a little intimacy might finally dribble out.”3 Gass and Willard represent two wildly opposing views about human sexuality. We rightly wonder if there is any black and white here, or only Fifty Shades of Grey.

Inside the church, no one can miss the havoc that unbounded sexual desire wreaks: pastors who admit to viewing porn, priests who sexually abuse children and cover it up for decades, and men exposed in the #MeToo movement who sexually harass female coworkers in ministry, to name just a few examples. Adultery and abortions happen in the Christian community at rates that are similar to those in the rest of North American culture. At the same time, the behavior and attitudes tolerated by corporations and sports teams or in entertainment industries and political circles show that disordered sexual desire and its damage is also a serious culture problem, not just a church problem. From prudes to politicians, and from parishioners to working professionals, it’s fair to admit that all of us find handling sexual desire fraught with difficulty. What can be said about the painfully toxic mess we’ve made of sex?

“Grant me chastity and continence, but not yet,” prays the young Augustine in his Confessions.4 Make me holy, he implores God, but please don’t make me give up my favorite sexual indulgences—at least not this weekend. Later in life, when he finally wanted to give up his lustful desires, however, he found he couldn’t. Imprisoned by the “overwhelming force of habit,”5 Augustine’s desire became a destructive force he had no success in exorcising through his own willpower. Following rules like requiring consent might be a useful legal strategy against the dangers of the unrestrained libido, but if we have learned anything from the tradition in this book, we know the transformation of our character must go deeper.

Back to the Beginning

First, as a preliminary note, I should clarify that this chapter will treat lust in its narrower meaning, as a vice concerned with the desire for sexual pleasure.6 “Lust” also often designates excessive desires or strong cravings (e.g., “a lust for power,” echoing Augustine’s libido dominandi). In this general sense, the term “lust” captures the tendency to excess that is shared by most of the vices. We also use “lust” in ways roughly synonymous with general uses of gluttony (“glutton for punishment”) or greed (“greedy for knowledge”) to indicate overindulgence or intensity of desire. Because I am trying to differentiate the vices from each other, I will focus only on lust’s specific form. In so doing, I’m following the earliest desert tradition, which called this vice porneia (Greek) or fornicatio (Latin) and thought of it as sexual temptation, rather than the later and broader term luxuria, which more generally refers to a surfeit or excess.

Before we can define disordered sexual desires, however, we need to backtrack further, to the beginning of the biblical account. Voices that condemn sexual desires and pleasures as evil in themselves, or that condone human sexual intercourse as a necessary evil tainted with shame, do not square with the account of creation in Scripture. God created us as sexual beings; we bear God’s image in our maleness and femaleness, as the poetic parallelism of Genesis 1:27 indicates. God designed human beings with sexual bodies capable of attraction and arousal. Our sexuality—including bodies, hormones, and the tactile desires and pleasures that go with them—counts as a good gift from God. For this reason, Aquinas, whose theology strongly affirms the goodness of creation, thought that sexual intercourse would have been more pleasurable in Eden than after the fall into sin.7 Scripture dedicates an entire book (Song of Songs) to celebrating sexual love between a man and a woman in its various erotic expressions. Marriage (a sexual relationship) between a bride and bridegroom is the most consistent picture God gives us of his relationship of loving communion with his people. Scripture testifies to the beauty and power of the gift of sexuality, the wondrous goodness of our bodies, and the blessings of life and love that flow to his people through human sexuality, not in spite of it. First and foremost, human sexuality and its attendant pleasures deserve our healthy respect and grateful affirmation, not our fearful condemnation or our casual brush-off.

Acknowledging the goodness of human sexuality and its pleasures is a necessary first step because, as with the other vices, we can’t define lust as damaging or disordered pleasure-seeking unless we can identify a well-ordered, delightful form to which it fails to measure up. The right view of this great good will avoid two traps. On the one hand, it will avoid degrading sexual pleasure into meaningless triviality or animal instinct, stripped of any relation to love and humanness. On the other hand, it will avoid elevating it into something so spiritual that any ecstasy involved counts as a quasimystical experience, out of fear, shame, or abhorrence for the actual flesh involved. We should treat our sexual desires and their expression as holistically human—both spiritually significant and earthily embodied.

As with many of the other vices we’ve studied so far, addressing sexuality holistically also means looking beyond behavioral symptoms. Some of my Christian college students operate under the misconception that well-ordered sexuality works like this: Before marriage, you can’t have any intercourse, and you have to squash your sexual urges. In marriage, all the rules fall away and life becomes a sexual free-for-all—as long as it’s with your spouse, of course. You can gratify your sexual desires whenever and however you want. In this view, chastity only matters for those who are actually abstaining in the premarital state or for those who are crazy enough to choose celibacy—although they usually fail to consider this latter option altogether. (It’s unclear whether their lack of consideration should be attributed simply to youthful hormones or to the fact that most Protestant churches, contrary to Scripture, no longer hold this up as a serious option for Christians.)

Like my students, many of us have a woefully narrow view of human sexuality. Few of us understand the virtue of chastity as a lifelong practice that applies to all. Without a wider view, behavioral rules, however correct, make little sense. John Cassian once distinguished two forms of virtuous sexuality, one a matter of bodily behavior and one a matter of the heart. The first could be curtailed in large part by some serious fasting and solitude in the desert—a wasteland known (in the monks’ case) for its lack of visual temptations and available women. However, waiting within lies the second battle. As Cassian observes, even when we renounce exterior things and temptations, we bring interior baggage with us everywhere, even into the desert. In the words of Evagrius, “The demons war with secular [people] more through objects, but with monks they do so especially through thoughts, for [monks] are deprived of objects because of [their] solitude. Further, to the extent that it is easier to sin in thought than in action, so is the warfare in thought more difficult than that which is conducted through objects. For the mind is a thing easily set in motion and difficult to check in its tendency toward unlawful fantasies.”8

We can find plenty of other places to debate the behavioral aspects of sex—that is, which activities should be allowed or forbidden. In this chapter, I will shift the conversation instead to questions of character, their deeper source. Which virtues are needed to direct our sexual desires toward flourishing and to perfect us as fully human sexual beings? How does the vice of lust dim our view of beauty and damage our ability to love? If we follow disordered sexual desires and the picture of happiness they give us over time, what sort of person will we become?

Considerations of sexual integrity and self-possession, as well as faithfulness to what is good, true, and beautiful, can and should move us beyond conversations about abstinence and intercourse toward asking more fruitful questions. If we reframe the conversation in terms of virtue-vice talk, perhaps we can better honor both this wider perspective and the goodness of sexuality. The author of a recent book on teen sexuality remarks with regret that “so much of how we see a young person’s sexuality is defined by all the things that can go wrong.”9 It’s true that when lust misshapes our hearts and minds, it obscures important things. Perhaps it’s time to reclaim the ways that both sexual pleasure and chastity attune us to love, beauty, and communion in ways we cannot now imagine.

“Sex Is Like Nitroglycerin”

Let’s admit it: People both inside and outside the church think Christians make too big a deal of sexual sin. The church obsesses with sex, they complain, and the word from the pulpit is always resoundingly negative. N. T. Wright recounts the joke about Moses carrying the commandments down from Mount Sinai. “‘Good news and bad news,’ he says. ‘The good news is—we’ve got them down from forty to ten. The bad news is—adultery is still in.’”10 From purity culture to porn, why does the church’s message about sex seem to center on shame? The sexual promiscuity of our culture supplies constant fodder for condemnation (though curiously, Christians tend to overlook systemic sexism within the church itself). But, my students wonder, what about judgmentalism itself, or abuses of power, or prejudice—sins that are equally damaging and culturally prevalent? The Bible itself gives more attention to other sins, like greed and idolatry. Why are Christians so focused on this particular hot-button issue?

I think it’s worth admitting at the outset that something of this charge sticks. Christians often treat sexual sin as if it were the culture’s main problem and other sinful desires as if they were less important. Churches also seem defensive about sexual ethics while largely ignoring the spiritual dangers of, say, affluence or power. That may well be a tragic mistake. It is certainly concerning and worth complaining about. Jesus came down hard on the pious posturers and power players of his day, while treating the prostitutes he encountered with compassion and mercy. We might better pursue the kind of character formation that makes us more like him.

Yet we can also respond to these charges against Christians by articulating good reasons to get upset about sexual sin. What is sex and what is it for? That’s not a question often addressed, in the church or in the culture. First, sexual intercourse—even at the physiological level—is one of the most intimately bonding activities two people can engage in, and other sexual desires and activities have this act as their ostensible goal. Scripture describes sexual union as becoming “one flesh” and consistently treats it as the signature act of marriage between bride and bridegroom, husband and wife, as well as a picture of Christ’s union with the church (Eph. 5).11 So the book of nature and the book of Scripture both point to human sexuality’s social and even spiritual significance. Second, human sexuality is bound up with reproduction. As with all animals, the design of human sexual organs seems geared toward procreating new human life, linking sexual activity to family and future generations. (We can surely work around and against the physiological grain here, but that’s just the point—there’s some default functionality here to contend with.) In short, our sexuality, including our sexual desire and pleasure, is implicated in human familial relationships and new human existence—that is, both love and life. With that much at stake, we are rightly wary of lust’s disordering potential. Frederick Buechner advises us to heed the power of our sexuality, good or bad: “Contrary to Mrs. Grundy, sex is not a sin. Contrary to Hugh Hefner, it’s not salvation either. Like nitroglycerin, it can be used either to blow up bridges or heal hearts.”12

As a word of grace, however, we should not forget that lust usually begins as a sin of weakness, not a sin of malice, to put it in Aquinas’s terms. People get carried away by curiosity or caught up in the moment by strong passions. They aren’t planning to hurt others or create scars that last a lifetime. Perhaps they feel desperately lonely and use sexual pleasure as a superficial panacea but quickly find themselves blindsided by the power of the desire they’ve awakened. Our concern here is how those choices accumulate into a current of habits that then sweep out of control. Since sexual desires figure powerfully in human life, it’s understandable that we underestimate their force and hold on us before we feel their full heat. Further, while lust does indeed have major potential for damage, people in its grip also tend to feel shame. They welcome offers of help to escape from it and often readily respond to mercy, while condemnation only further fuels a spiral of self-loathing. Of course, anyone can get hardened over time into casual or cruel lewdness—this explains why we should guard against making a habit out of lustful desire. But for many of us, it feels like an uninvited monster that has taken over our lives. Once we see it for what it is, we want to escape from it as much as anyone does.
