The cultural conversation about casual sex needs to more honestly acknowledge the power of human sexual desire (as the advertising industry’s exploitation of it implicitly attests). The life-altering forces in our bodies and hearts bespeak something with more than superficial or recreational significance. The church’s conversation, however, should start with the goodness of sex and virtuous sexual desire—its love- and life-giving power—not just its perversions or prohibitions. The right question for both is this: How should our sexual desires serve our full humanness? With proper use and enjoyment, our sexuality, sexual desires, and their fulfillment serve, strengthen, and signify our human capacity to give and receive love. An approach to lust as a vice will therefore ask, How do unruly sexual desires deform our character, our perception, and our emotions? How does our habitual attachment to pleasure enhance or erode our relationship with God? How does it build up or break down human relationships?

Thinking about lust as a vice turns our attention from symptoms to deeper sources. Lust need not be acted out to count as disordered desire. In the rest of the chapter, let’s look at the ways lust is a problem with the heart above your belt before it is a problem with the heat below it.

Defining the Disorder

Sexual desire and pleasure are an integral part of the gift of good sexual activity.13 The trouble comes with lust’s reductive impulses. Lust strips sexual pleasure-seeking down to individual gratification, apart from a love relationship to a person. Like gluttony, lust narrows the only end we have in view to our own personal sensory stimulation. Without a holistic view of sexuality and its purposes, lustful pleasure downgrades all the goodness of sex to the lowest denominator—its physical dimension.

The expression of fully human sexuality has a rich interpersonal and social dimension that brings us into connection and relationship with others. Lust deforms sexual desire by cutting us off from this potential, fed by the pretense that sexual pleasure is an individual good. The lustful seek self-gratification—their own pleasure—as a dominant and sometimes even exclusive end. This feature of lust more than any other puts it in opposition to well-ordered sexual enjoyment. Lust severs sexual pleasure-seeking from loving another person; lust involves my taking and getting, rather than mutual self-giving. The immediacy of its physical demands starkly contrasts with long-term personal bonds: once sated, lust turns and walks away. When we lust we want nothing to do with sharing love or giving life, if the thought even occurs to us. Lust says, Sexual pleasure is my pleasure.

This explains why lust characteristically reduces the fullness of sexuality to something less. Paul Ricoeur says, “Everything that makes the sexual encounter easy simultaneously speeds its collapse into insignificance.”14 Perhaps the hardest thing to appreciate until we have experienced it is the richly human, deeply personal character of sexual expression. If we dismiss our sexuality’s personal, emotional, and social meaning, as lust demands, nothing remains but the version of sex found in Cosmopolitan magazine, which offers tips and techniques on how to make things “hotter” in bed. Cosmo, Maxim, and the like have nothing to say about what sexual desire and its expression look like bathed in love’s familiarity, warmth, vulnerability, and intimacy. Teenage boys joke about the paper-thin plot of porn videos, implicitly noting that the sex depicted in them has no real story to it; for all the falsely scripted bodily motions, all human history has been stripped away. Such depictions of sex reduce it to a performance (bodies plus behavior) that predictably yields a product (pleasure), procurable on demand without respect for particular persons or their full personhood. Such “sex without humanness,” to borrow Buechner’s phrase, is cold, clinical, and superficial. Lustful sex makes the other person instrumental to my acquisition of pleasure. As Josef Pieper puts it, lust wants an impersonal “it,” while proper desire turns us, with delight, to a unique, beloved person.15

A pastor once illustrated the point this way:

Lust dehumanizes the other. In fact lust needs to dehumanize the other. Lust doesn’t work when the other person is fully human. That’s why exotic dancers always have fake stage names. You would never have a dancer use her real name. Why not? Because that gets in the way of objectification that lust needs. A man leering at an exotic dancer doesn’t want to know her real name. A great way to empty out these clubs would be to stand up before a dancer was about to come on and say, “This is Sultry Susan, but her real name is Mary Wallinski. She has four brothers and sisters. Her parents divorced when she was 5. Her mother is an alcoholic. She has been married twice; her last husband beat her. She has two kids and is struggling to get by. She likes dogs and would love to be a dental hygienist someday.” That would empty out the room. An introduction like that would short circuit the lust because it would put intimacy and humanity back into the picture. Lust doesn’t want the full humanity of the person with her needs and her vulnerabilities. Lust wants low lights, a haze of alcohol and lots of lies.16

We count lust a vice, then, because it does not honor the fullness of our sexuality as whole persons. It truncates the communion and the community for which sex was made. In fact, lustful pleasure-seeking alienates people from each other just when they are supposed to be drawn together. Lust can imitate acts of intimacy, but in truth, it is their antithesis.

Human beings find it difficult to be lustful without feeling that loss at some level. That emptiness, along with the strength of desire, drives lust toward compulsive excess. As with other addictions to pleasurable highs, if you are successful in numbing yourself to the goods involved, you will also have been successful in becoming less fully human. All entrenched patterns of vice lead us on paths of self-damage and self-destruction. Lust is no exception. No wonder it leaves us feeling alone and unfulfilled.

Filling and Fulfilling Myself

The dynamics of lust’s search for pleasure parallel those operating in its close cousin, gluttony. The excessiveness of these two vices reduces something designed for more than pleasure to mere pleasure. Buechner also notes the similarity: “At its roots the hunger to know someone sexually is the hunger to know and be known by that person humanly. Food without nourishment doesn’t fill the bill for long, and neither does sex without humanness.”17 Just as the glutton sees food only as a useful means for filling herself with comfort and satisfaction, so the lustful person sees her sexuality only in terms of its usefulness in providing pleasure for herself.18

We should not be surprised to find that lust imitates gluttony in this respect, since both are vices dealing with physical pleasures of touch. The glutton, given her disordered desires for pleasure in eating, grows less and less able to appreciate and be satisfied with the pleasure of a simple meal of ordinary quantity and quality. She needs more and better to stimulate and satisfy. And then more and better yet, for habitual indulgence only strengthens the desire and its demands for satisfaction.19 Her mistake? Trying to satisfy an infinite spiritual hunger by filling her stomach with food. Such self-fulfillment certainly seems easier at first. But her desire for eternal life and perfect fulfillment can never be quenched with the temporary pleasure of eating, even if indulgence soothes her physical cravings and seems to quell her emptiness for now. Lust’s escalation is no different.20 Despite their reputation to the contrary, virtues like temperance and chastity train and direct desire, teaching us how to celebrate and delight in the fullness of pleasure, rather than merely dampening it or forbidding it.21 As deceptive as the false reputations of these virtues might be, excess—not self-regulation—counts as the real pleasure-killer. Genuinely appreciating physical goods requires that we not use them to try to serve or satiate our spiritual needs.

Lust’s definition refers to physical pleasure that requires embodiment. The key to understanding lust as a vice, however, is to see that what we do physically is inextricably linked to spiritual needs and spiritual effects. As Buechner puts it, “Adultery, promiscuity either heterosexual or homosexual, masturbation—one appealing view is that anything goes as long as no one gets hurt. The trouble is that human beings are so hopelessly psychosomatic in composition that whatever happens to the soma [body] happens also to the psyche [soul] and vice versa.”22

Why does lust leave human beings both empty and driven to excess in their search for sensual satisfaction? Because it tries to substitute something less for something more. As beings with a spiritual as well as a physical dimension, we need something more than bodily satisfaction to fulfill us. No temporary physical fix or fleeting passion is enough. Lust—as a habitual vice—has an insatiable quality to it that testifies to this fact about us.

The Greek philosopher Plato once told a myth about eros (sexual desire) in the voice of a comic poet named Aristophanes.23 According to the whimsical story he spins, the gods originally created human beings as spherical creatures with four arms and four legs and two heads. As punishment for trying to usurp the gods’ power, the gods sliced them in half “like a flatfish.” In our current condition, then, each human being languishes as only half of his or her original whole. Eros, says Aristophanes, names our yearning to be reunited with our other half; our sexual urges express our desire for completeness.24 Nowadays, the longing this ancient story describes still drives us. Justin Timberlake offers us only one of many recent soundtracks for it, describing his desire for the woman who reflects him like a mirror: “I don’t want to lose you now / I’m looking right at the other half of me.”25

Even if sexual union holds our best hope for achieving this reunion or lost wholeness for now, however, Aristophanes continues, it is imperfect and transitory. “No one would think that [what lovers really want from each other] is the intimacy of sex—that mere sex is the reason each lover takes so great and deep a joy in being with the other. It’s obvious that the soul of every lover longs for something else; his soul cannot say what it is, but like an oracle it has a sense of what it wants, and like an oracle it lies hidden behind a riddle.”26 Such reunions express our yearning to reclaim our original “wholeness” or oneness, he argues, but no amount of physical intercourse or pleasure will fill that need. When we try to make “mere sex” and its sensual pleasure do all the work of human fulfillment, we are clinging to a strategy destined to fail. Trying to get sexual pleasure to satisfy our fundamental longing for human happiness holds out only a surefire recipe for disappointment. Then why do we keep trying it, deluding ourselves that it will be enough? Why are we perennially lured by lustful promises and stratagems?

Our pursuit of the vices takes our attachments to good things and turns them to excess, distorting both us and the objects of our desire. Wrath arises from ostensible concerns for justice and honor, greed regards sufficient possessions, gluttony looks for happiness in the food and drink that sustains life, vainglory seeks the affirmation and appreciation of others. Vice happens when we try to make these good things fill gaps and needs in our hearts that only God can fill, and when we define happiness in terms of them, rather than appreciating and receiving them as (finite) gifts from God.

Here again with lust, we uncover the familiar connection of the capital vices with pride. The temptation not to trust God’s will and way to our good was there in Eden, and it remains a great temptation now. Lust counts merely as another habitual version of trying to engineer happiness for myself, by myself. In lust, I decide to make my own pleasure the goal, and then I decide where to get it, and when, and how. My life revolves around my desires, wants, and “needs,” which I satisfy on demand and on my own terms. I make everything and everyone else an instrument serving that end. I disown my need for God’s love and the love of others, and I will not wait to receive it as a gift. I prefer to find my own delight, to meet my own desire for satisfaction, to fill myself. Unlike someone who risks depending on the love of another and vulnerably gives himself to another, the lustful person chooses to be autonomous and in control, providing his good for himself. As with avarice, taking for ourselves seems like a safer strategy than remaining open to accepting what we need and long for from another’s hand or heart. Surrender, vulnerability, intimacy, and dependence are the hard road we resist taking at all costs. Insisting on independence and creating a false form of happiness for ourselves signal lust’s prideful roots. Lust plays the same tired old game as the rest of the vices. As with all of them, winning the game means we lose.

Because human beings find true fulfillment in love for God and for each other, sexual expressions of love require real persons. They demand a fully human encounter, not simply a useful or pleasurable exchange. They require the freedom to give ourselves to each other, and the willingness to graciously welcome another person in. No one would deny the risks involved: we have witnessed manifold failures all around us, often in our own families. The choice to love is an opportunity to appreciate and value a flawed human person for his or her own sake, not just for what that person can do for us or the pleasure they promise to provide. Yet the hallmark of this real and raw love—the freedom of giving of ourselves to each other—stands excluded from lust’s instrumentalizing and manipulative view. To strip human sexuality of its link to love can make access to sexual pleasure safer and easier, and ostensibly and superficially under our control, perhaps, but the safety we seek in prideful self-provision also walls us off from what we really need.

The Pleasure Paradox

When we misuse something habitually, we tend to lose our ability to appreciate its true goodness. As with gluttony, the more single-mindedly we pursue something for the pleasure we can get from it, the less likely we are to find our desire for pleasure satisfied. Sexual self-gratification gradually loses its flavor. What once felt intoxicating eventually becomes dull and boring. Pornography use classically illustrates this dynamic, as the organization Fight the New Drug amply documents.27 They show, if we didn’t already know, that porn use (note the consumerist way we talk about this activity) has an incredible escalation rate. Habitual viewers quickly increase their frequency of use to the point where it disrupts and dominates their lives. Meanwhile, the level of perversity and novelty required to pique their interest also quickly spins beyond the range of what would shock even a more jaded adult. Like a drug dealer, the industry carefully designs the experience and exposure to maximize this progressive dynamic. Even ordinary viewers soon find themselves aroused only by ugly deviance of all stripes: sadomasochistic violence and rape, child porn, and more. In the words of one man, “As a struggling porn addict myself, I know what the producer [of pornographic films] said about porn getting more and more brutal on the women is true, and it will only get worse. [There’s] something about human nature that gets desensitized to the ordinary.”28 Why doesn’t indulging in lustful pleasure satisfy us? Why is truly fulfilling pleasure such an elusive goal for the lustful person?

As the ancient Greeks noted, we easily confuse pleasure with human fulfillment, because, like happiness, we seek pleasure for its own sake (or, as philosophers say, “as an end in itself”). The paradox of pleasure, however, as they also noted, is that you can’t get it by seeking it directly. Aristotle said that we experience pleasure as a sort of side effect of specific activities done in a particular way; certain activities give rise to corresponding pleasures as their characteristic fruit. We might mistake the pleasure for an independently graspable item, but in truth we cannot experience it without the correlative activity. Pleasures turn out to be as unique as the signature activities they accompany. The pleasure of sitting in the sunshine, or playing soccer, is not the same thing as the pleasure of reading a good book. The pleasure of reading a gripping biography like Unbroken is not the same as the pleasure of reading a comic book like Calvin and Hobbes. The pleasure of reading to myself is not the same as the pleasure of reading to my children.29

Sexual pleasure is no different. This explains why lust requires escalation and novelty, while a happily married person can still enjoy conventional sex decades into a marriage to the same spouse, often more so as time progresses. Sexual pleasure—both its quality and its ability to satisfy—depends on the type of activity from which you get it. The lustful person gets the shallow version, the physical rush that feels great for a moment but also cannot satisfy for more than a moment. Studies consistently show greater sexual satisfaction (in quality and quantity) among those in faithful, monogamous marriages than among those whose sex lives tend toward the promiscuous side of things. Why? Because in such marriages the sexual pleasure is the fruit of love that grows greater with time and experience. That sort of pleasure is simply unavailable to those who are in it only for the physical pleasure, and only for their own pleasure. In her poem “To a Long Loved Love,” Madeleine L’Engle writes,

We who have seen the new moon grow old together

Who have seen winter rime the fields and stones

As though it would claim earth and water forever

We who have known the touch of flesh and the shape of bones

Know the old moon stretching its shadows across a whitened field

More beautiful than spring with all its spate of blooms

What passions knowledge of tried flesh still yields,

What joy and comfort these familiar rooms.

In the moonless, lampless dark now of this bed

My body knows each line and curve of yours;

My fingers know the shape of limb and head:

As pure as mathematics ecstasy endures.30

In this case, with familiarity comes not contempt and boredom but a deepening of trust and intimacy—the very context in which sexual pleasure comes into full bloom. “Do not stir up or awaken love until it is ready,” counsels the author of Song of Songs (8:4). Such countercultural advice comes from those who want the heights of sexual pleasure to be possible for us—as the rest of this biblical book makes clear—not from those who seek to repress it. As these examples illustrate, fulfilling pleasure without full human intimacy is lust’s favorite false promise. Like the other vices, lust gives us an imitation of happiness, a substitute for the real thing. Satisfying sexual enjoyment finds its home in loving relationships with people who delight in us for who we really are. Whether you are single for a season, married, or celibate for life, having rightly ordered desire means honoring that truth in all you do with your mind, heart, and body.31

Lust’s Body Count

One of the most common assumptions about lust is that it doesn’t hurt anyone. Aquinas, in the thirteenth century, anticipates this “no-injury” objection. Fornication (the tradition’s term for sexual intercourse outside of marriage) involves two willing adults. Nobody is injured; therefore, fornication doesn’t count as sinful.32 In today’s culture, the reasoning goes something like “no harm, no foul.” As long as everyone involved gives their consent, why wouldn’t any expression of sexual desire be licit?33 Satisfying desire is pleasurable and fun, and what’s wrong with that?

In some cases more than others, we more readily recognize the injuries from lust. In the story of David and Bathsheba (2 Sam. 11–12), the king’s lust racks up quite a body count before it’s all over. We painfully witness as David’s selfish satisfaction of his sexual desires first costs Uriah his life, then costs Bathsheba the death of her husband and child, and finally costs David a painful rupture in his relationship with God (Ps. 51) and the loss of a son. But the damage does not just stop with those directly involved. Uriah is betrayed to the death by the king he has served faithfully, and David’s general, Joab, becomes complicit in that betrayal. So David’s army and, after a visit from the prophet Nathan, his palace servants and his people see their king willing to sacrifice his obedient subjects to indulge his own selfish desires. (This also includes Bathsheba, who presumably could have refused the king’s advances only on pain of death.) David’s lust breaks trust, undercuts loyalties, and damages relationships (personal and political) at all levels.

Adultery obviously causes harm, but even within an otherwise faithful marriage lust can inflict deep wounds. It’s notable that Aquinas (who overemphasizes the procreative purpose of sex almost to the point of forgetting about its potential for intimacy) explicitly warns husbands not to use their wives indecently for their own sexual pleasure. This is “counter to the good of marriage,” he says—even to the point of being a worse offense than adultery—because it “breaks faith” between husband and wife.34 She will know that he is using and disrespecting her, and that feels like betrayal too.

Even if lust does not directly burn us, its smoke damage can still wreak destruction far and wide. A neighborhood feels threatened when a sex offender moves in; advertising everywhere exposes young children to cringe-worthy explicit sexual content; teens having sex find themselves more depressed, while their choices create a culture of pressure for others; sexual leering and innuendo create hostile public spaces and uncomfortable workplaces; hooking up normalizes drunken encounters with strangers and increases the incidence of disease; we wield an ugly word for sex (the f-bomb) to express utter contempt; cohabiting young adults bring baggage into later relationships; and sexual abuse and affairs rip families, churches, and communities apart. Recent statistics show that at least 80 percent of teens age fifteen to seventeen have had multiple exposures to hard-core internet pornography. Even if we stop looking, everyone knows the power of these images to remain imprinted on the mind. (Naomi Wolf once described the male perspective of the college sex scene this way: “Real naked women are just bad porn.”)35 How do these experiences contaminate the atmosphere in a coed classroom, on a date, in an office setting, or in a marriage? As a community, we often live with lust’s collateral damage for the long term.

The damage may also be less sensational and more subtle. We underestimate the impact of our constant cultural formation to our own peril. Persistent lustful habits make our society more crass and crude, and the world less beautiful. Lust detracts from good relationships, in part because our imaginations and narratives about sexuality have been deformed. Lustful attitudes permeate the way we think and speak about the opposite sex. Check out the number of sexual terms in a slang dictionary and note the strikingly large percentage that are derogatory and violent. Is it reasonable to think that we can safely sequester or self-censor our deepest attitudes from our daily speech and behavior? The portrayal of masculine and feminine “ideal” bodies in video games and our regular experiences of misogyny and sexual violence as entertainment in music and movies—even if it does not rise to the level of the excesses of rap lyrics or characters in the Game of Thrones—implicitly shape our assumptions of what we can and should expect sexually from both men and women. When sexual malformation pollutes the air we breathe, lust is no longer a private, personal problem.

Robert Solomon pokes fun at the religious prudes who condemn the harmless fun of “one too many peeks at a naughty Playboy pictorial,”36 but his comments betray an embarrassing (or perhaps willful) ignorance. It’s almost impossible nowadays not to have read the statistics on pornography use and its compulsive power, or not to have recognized in the testimony of addicts their self-loathing and the brokenness they caused in their families,37 never mind the exploitative gender dynamics of the billion-dollar sex (and sex trafficking) industry and those whose lives are decimated by its abuses. Willard calls this out: such practices always involve “some element of contempt or even disgust. Those presented in it are obviously being used, hence are even regarded by the viewer as deserving disgust or even pain. There is no question of an appropriate human relation with them.”38

Even when lust doesn’t do that type or level of damage, it does not stay hidden. In his exposition on Jesus’s words about lust in the Sermon on the Mount, Willard argues that although lust begins in the heart and mind, it quickly pervades the whole person and his or her social relationships.

When one is inhabited by fantasizing visual lusting, it, like anger and contempt, makes its presence known. It is detectable in one’s “body language” and expressions [including the language one uses to talk about sex and members of the other sex]. As a result, it has pervasive effects on everyone in the situation, even though it is not “acted out.” Indeed, being what it is, a condition of the embodied social self [emphasis added], it is always acted out to some degree and simply cannot be kept a private reality. “The look” is a public act with public effects that restructure the entire framework of personal relations where it occurs.

The person subjected to the fantasy, as well as others alongside, is deeply affected by such lusting. . . . The person subjected to it and everyone else nearby must “deal with it,” often by constant planning and managing. Sexual harassment as we know it would simply disappear under Jesus’ ethic of sexuality.39

A society that has normalized the habitual indulgence of self-serving sexual pleasure in general hampers healthy relationships with friends, coworkers, and (future) spouses. It makes a celibate life or season of abstinence so hard that we now consider such commitments impossible to ask of people. And that’s before we consider lust’s spiritual effects on our ability to recognize and receive the love of God.

Alan Paton tells a story about the corrosive power of lust in his book Too Late the Phalarope. Pieter van Vlaanderen, a police officer in South Africa, reflects remorsefully on his sexual tryst with a young woman late at night in a vacant lot outside of town:

And how long he stayed there he could not remember, but at last he rose and came out of the vacant ground. And his body and clothes stank with the smell of the weeds, and the stinking was a symbol of his corruption. . . . And he thought again of his children with special agony, for what kind of man would destroy what he had created, and hurt what he had loved? . . . In those last twelve hours the whole world had changed, because of one insensate act. And what madness makes a man pursue something so unspeakable, deaf to the cries of wife and children and mother and friends and blind to their danger, to grasp one unspeakable pleasure that brought no joy, ten thousand of which pleasures were not worth one of the hairs on his children’s heads? Such desire could not surely be a desire of the flesh, but some mad desire of a sick and twisted soul. And why should I have this desire? he asked himself. Where did it come from? And how did one cure it? But he had no answers to these questions. . . .

And his terrible knowledge of himself lay in him darkly and heavily, and took away his laughter, and the laughter of his wife, so that the children were the only creatures that laughed in that house. He went to work darkly and heavily, and came back darkly and heavily, and played with the children because that was his habit, but his wife could hear and see that it was not the same.40

As Paton’s story shows, lust damages the person who lusts too. Such damage, ranging from jadedness to despair, includes depression, loneliness, shame, callousness, blindness to beauty, and self-loathing. Lust can certainly deliver on instant pleasure. Why else would anyone go back for more? But it also delivers on disrespect—of ourselves and others. Buechner comments: “Who is to say who gets hurt and who doesn’t get hurt, and how? Maybe the injuries are all internal. Maybe it will be years before the X-rays show up anything. Maybe the only person who gets hurt is you.”41

Body and Soul

Sometimes the early Christians’ asceticism and celibacy seem to imply a rejection of the body.42 Evagrius recommends that young monks trying to avoid lust flee from encounters with women.43 Likewise, he warns, “The sight of a woman is a poisoned arrow; it wounds the soul and injects the poison, and for as long a time as it stays there it causes an ever greater festering.”44 We might be tempted—against their own stated views elsewhere—to think that these early Christians label women as the cause of lust, rather than the human heart. In reply, the desert mother Amma Theodora says, “Give the body discipline, and you will see that the body is for the One who made it.”45 But even when the tradition does need tempering, too harsh a reading often oversimplifies and exaggerates earlier views. For one thing, these Christian thinkers were counseling those who had vowed perpetual sexual abstinence. Their audience consisted of Christians who had withdrawn from all worldly affairs to the desert or monastery to pursue single-minded devotion to God. You and I might also be called to hiddenness, humility, and radical obedience, but perhaps not to that form of radical withdrawal. Hence, we need to translate their insights and advice for our time and circumstances. Despite the differences between their ascetic context and our own, though, in many ways these early Christians took the unity of body and soul more seriously than we do. They insisted that what we do with our bodies affects our spiritual lives, as the apostle Paul affirms (1 Cor. 6:13–20). The famous modern philosopher Descartes once said, “I am really distinct from my body and can live without it.”46 By contrast, taking the habit of lust seriously means treating the body as an integral part of who we are as human beings.

Cassian therefore describes the virtue of chastity as something to be cultivated on the inside as well as the outside. His call for a “chastity of body and soul” means that, “first of all, the hidden places of our heart must be very carefully purified. For what those others wish to acquire in terms of purity of body, we must ourselves possess in the depths of our conscience.” When discussing celibacy for the monks, he remarks, “The incorruption of the flesh consists not so much in abstaining from woman as it does in integrity of heart.”47

Paul likewise insists on the truth that we can’t split ourselves in two—the “inner me” (soul) and its “outer case” (body). What we do bodily matters spiritually: “Do you not know that whoever is united to a prostitute becomes one body with her? For it is said, ‘The two shall be one flesh’” (1 Cor. 6:16). Paul says that sexual intercourse unites you with another person, whatever your intentions are. What our bodies do has human meaning and spiritual significance. You can’t take the soul out of bodily sex, dissociating yourself spiritually from the “oneness” of the “flesh” involved. In other words, nobody we lust after stays only in our head or only in our bed. Our minds and hearts influence our bodies—a fact that’s true of us in all areas, not just our sexuality. For example, when we are anxious or grieving, we may not be able to breathe calmly, or we may lose our appetite. So also, our bodies affect our hearts and minds, as when we don’t get enough sleep and become irritable or struggle to concentrate; on the positive side, after exercise, we feel better about ourselves, focus better, and have a brighter outlook. We were created as embodied beings, and that is how we will be resurrected. To become fully human like us, Christ took on flesh. We ought not make our spiritual lives too spiritual. What we do with our bodies helps form our character; our bodily desires and daily activities matter for spiritual discipline and discipleship.48 Why should it be any different with sexual desire? The tradition assumes that our body-soul unity figures in every human activity, integrating thought and desire with the outward pursuit of whatever we take to be good. In the case of the desire for sexual pleasure, being called to love your neighbor as yourself means that respecting persons and their bodies includes both your neighbor’s body and your own.

John Mayer sang a song back in 2001 called “Your Body Is a Wonderland.” Not surprisingly, the title and lyrics do not express wonder in the sense of awe. Instead, the song pictures a lover’s body as a personal amusement park, complete with cotton candy. It’s an exciting place to have fun, see the sights, and explore. Then, at the end of the day, when our amusement ends, we can freely leave it behind, along with our trash and our sweat on the vinyl seats. Do we treat our bodies as sacred spaces or a place for cheap thrills? Lustful leering is as irreverent about bodies and their sexuality as it is obsessed with them. Our bodies are more than pleasure-delivery devices, to be used (and discarded) at will. There is nothing wrong with being sexually attractive or thinking someone else is, but that can never be the whole story about us or our relationships to each other. The lustful person’s distorted desire for pleasure leads her to treat both herself and others with less value and dignity than she ought. And that makes lustful expressions of sexuality unfit to communicate the truth about sexuality or about us. Christians view their bodies as members of Christ’s own body, a place where a holy, eternal God dwells (1 Cor. 6:19). We are fearfully and wonderfully made (Ps. 139). God created us in his own image and calls us to represent his divinity in our humanity—so when we trivialize each other sexually, we willingly participate in the “degradation of a king [or queen].”49

Mind Games

Because lust denies what human beings are made for, the depth and value of personhood and our need for love, its siren song characteristically devolves into what Kanye West calls a “beautiful dark twisted fantasy.”50 The sexuality portrayed in music videos, romance novels, advertisements, video games, and films cultivates our imaginations, shapes our attitudes, and influences our inclinations. These carefully curated pictures aren’t real, but they are alluring enough to make us want to forsake reality to chase what they promise. Given our levels of lustful exposure, it would be surprising if our lives didn’t reflect their distorted picture of the good. The content need not be sexually explicit smut to carry forward lust’s work of character formation with alarming effectiveness. Taylor Swift songs make millions off such fantasies, from her contemporizing of Romeo and Juliet’s passionate “Love Story” (this time with a white-dress happy-ever-after ending) to her unapologetic request to be remembered in her lover’s “Wildest Dreams.” My intent is not to single out Swift—this formula for engaging our attention is ubiquitous—but simply to provide a clean enough illustration to include in this book. Male readers may feel a little picked on with all the discussion of porn,51 but female readers are also implicated: lust’s beginnings in our imaginations sell everything from romance novels (i.e., emotional fantasy) to tickets bought by middle-aged women to watch sadistic sex on the big screen (i.e., the Fifty Shades series). The profits from these products alone ought to convince us that lust works by captivating our minds, as the desert fathers and mothers pointed out centuries ago. Evagrius astutely observed that everything starts with “suggestions” (logismoi). If left to settle in, thoughts stir up passions and appetites, which eventually leak into outward expression and action. Married women smitten by the sensual heroes of romantic fiction gradually find their husbands more and more irritatingly inadequate and dissatisfying; men who regularly linger on images of models perfected by photoshopping and enhanced by plastic surgery find themselves longing for something that turns them on more than the imperfect women they are actually with.

Even real love struggles against the power of fantasy, as L’Engle recounts:

Because you’re not what I would have you be

I blind myself to who, in truth, you are

Seeking mirage where desert blooms, I mar

Your you. Aaah, I would like to see

Past all delusion to reality:

Then would I see God’s image in your face,

His hand in yours, and in your eyes his grace.

Because I’m not what I would have me be,

I idolize Two who are not any place,

Not you, not me, and so we never touch.

Reality would burn. I do not like it much.

And yet in you, in me, I find a trace

Of love which struggles to break through

The hidden lovely truth of me, of you.52

Lust colors the eye—and mind—of the beholder. What pictures of sexual satisfaction accumulate in our inner repertoire from soap operas, music videos, romance novels, magazines, catalogs, shop windows, R-rated films, late-night cable shows, or songs on the radio we unconsciously memorize? Do these sources convey truth about human sexuality and its goodness, presenting the real beauty of our bodies and the needs of our hearts? Or does their ubiquitous presence supply a steady stream of fodder to nourish our lustful fantasies?

The tradition describes the long-term effects of lust as a vicious cycle connecting our desires to the contents of our mind and back again. Aquinas and Gregory call lust’s most grave offspring vice “blindness of mind”—the inability to recognize and appreciate goods higher than the pleasure of the flesh or beyond the allure of the moment.53 When lust fills our minds and hearts, spiritual goods go dark. As a result, we pursue bodily pleasures with even greater energy, as if they were the sum total of available happiness. Lust effectively shrinks our world, so that our powers of deliberation and imagination serve its narrowly self-serving, flesh-aimed vision of the good. Pieper strikingly contrasts the distorting power of what he calls lust’s “self-centered will-to-pleasure” with love’s clear vision of the true good: “Only those who look at the world with pure eyes can experience its beauty.”54

Chastity and Other Impossible Virtues

Of course, the problem remains that we can be completely convinced that lust ruins love between human beings and short-circuits our relationship with God—and still not feel able to resist it. If what to do about lust is the question, we will need to offer something more than sermons and sex education as our answer. We should not expect a better book chapter to make temptation fade. Similarly, in matters of nutrition, knowledge of how many calories we need to be healthy does not equal the will to resist a second helping of dessert or forgo the french fries. On a beach full of tanned, glowing skin, the power of lust is still with us. To remedy lust, we will need to embody the truth of what we know in a daily regimen that pushes back against disordered desire.

What drives us to lust? Physiological urges, prodded toward temptation by cues that surround us everywhere and worn into habits by repeated choices and well-traveled neural pathways, are surely a factor. But more than that, what human needs and urges does lust promise to satisfy? As a diagnostic tool, consider when you tend to be most susceptible: late at night when you are tired and recovering from a stressful day, moments when you are alone and find yourself emotionally depleted from demanding encounters with others, times when you need or want an escape, days when lonely longings seem unbearable. Notably, Cassian and others paired gluttony and lust because they have similar triggers and soft spots in which temptation lurks. What do we see if we look beneath the surface? A compassionate view of these “warm” sins of the flesh reveals that they often first catch us feeling empty and afraid, hurt and vulnerable, defensive or frustrated, and promise us a ready reprieve from the ache within.55 Who among us isn’t looking for relief in a world where intimacy is risky, hard, high-investment, and sometimes terribly wounding? Lust feels easier and more comfortable—like an emotional pick-me-up, or a pleasant vacation without the planning or the price tag. Other times, our need to belong, to feel accepted, and to connect with others can lure us into the land of lust’s false promises. Or perhaps we are scraping our own way toward satiety because the relationships we have don’t feel like they will ever be enough, or we are nursing wounds from the past. When we feel rejected or don’t feel lovable, we are tempted to withdraw to more superficial forms of sexual encounter, because they feel safer somehow. Such distancing—even if it dehumanizes—leaves us feeling more in control.56 Lust looks best, therefore, when we are most starved for love. As human beings, we can’t live or thrive without love. We will go to desperate lengths to have it, or to escape from the pain of not having it. (It’s no accident, then, that lust is a typical escapist side effect of sloth.) In such a state, we need grace to drive out our shame and sense of unworthiness, and we need a warm embrace of genuine welcome to quell our sense of disconnection.

Deepening our view of the vice will also mean broadening the parameters of what counts as lustful so that we can see better the territory that chastity will also need to cover. Cultivating chastity includes countering all that we do individually and culturally to unthinkingly indulge hidden lustful patterns of thought, imagination, and attraction in ourselves and others. How can we cultivate our character, inwardly and outwardly, so that our own sexual gratification is not the lens through which we see the world and grasp at it?

Lust thrives in privacy and isolation. Lustful people often feel shame, which also motivates them to keep their struggles hidden from others. But when we hide our hearts, we cannot come to grips with the darkness within, or expose it to the light in confession. This means lust’s remedy requires community, openness, and accountability. Sheer individual willpower will not work. You will not beat this vice alone. Lust’s vicious cycle copycats the dynamics of addiction. We recognize lust as a problem to manage, but we keep our mental and emotional lives private, trying to tackle temptation on our own, making fervent new resolutions, but then failing to live up to them, despising ourselves afterward, and finally falling into despair (“It’s a hopeless fight”; “I’m a lost cause”). We compensate for despair by letting ourselves fall again, this time even deeper. To get out of this shame-and-failure loop, we will have to admit we need help and ask for it, opening ourselves up to someone else who can keep us honest and accountable. Countering lust’s alienating tendencies, chastity requires intentionally being willingly engaged in loving, life-giving community, including the communion of the saints whose stories of rescue and recovery encourage and inspire us. I wonder, too, if we really trust Jesus and his Spirit to give us the power to rebuild a new way forward, step by step. Do we ask for help daily, wanting him to respond, believing in hope that he can and will answer us in our need?

On the purely logistical side of things, a host of small, practical things will also help, of course. They might even be prerequisites for perseverance and progress. We can choose to use computers in public areas, get filters and firewalls, and install accountability apps on our phones. These already count as important ways of relying on community resources. We can know better what makes us vulnerable and schedule alternate activities or call for help during those times. We can resolve to use respectful language and keep our jokes clean. We—both males and females—can dress in ways that are attractive and professional but that are also deliberate in not putting ourselves on display for sexual consumption. We can more intentionally regulate which movies and television shows we watch, which music and video games we play regularly, and which internet sites we peruse. The Minute Maid slogan, “Put good in, get good out,” applies to our moral imagination most of all. Our minds will be full of images and ideas—but which ones? We can follow Paul’s advice to pursue what is true, honorable, just, pure, excellent, and praiseworthy (Phil. 4:8), rather than letting just anything that comes our way drift in.

Note, however, that choosing not to do things that spark or inflame temptation is an insufficient remedy. Cultivating chastity is not a rulebook of don’ts but a positive project of forming our hearts to love wholeheartedly what is beautiful and good. Too often we are half-hearted about it, filling our imaginations and senses with lustful stimuli and then despairing of our ability to keep our desires and behavior in check. We tend to try to walk as close to the edge of the cliff as we can, hoping to keep our balance and not fall off. Chastity commits us to staying away from the edge altogether, by investing ourselves in worthwhile alternatives. It chooses city building to cliff hanging, so to speak.

Of course, it’s needful to “flee from sexuality immorality” (1 Cor. 6:18 NIV)—Aquinas says that sensual temptation generally requires the physical presence of a particular trigger, so our response should be to run away from that object or stimulus or to remove ourselves. But it’s even better to fix our eyes on the good and run toward it instead. How do we do that? Long term, we can practice spiritual disciplines that counter our default worldly attachments and place us instead in the presence of God. When was the last time you pondered God’s name for you—“my beloved”—in contemplative prayer? Have you memorized the passage of Scripture that most reminds you of God’s love and faithfulness toward you? How often do you rehearse it? If someone observed your daily habits, would they see a life intentionally bathed in the love of God, a love that quiets our deep (and sometimes desperate) search for happiness and communion elsewhere? We need daily reminders of what is beautiful and best for us. We also need communities that show us how to rely on the Spirit’s power in faithfulness and hope. As the story of Joe and Jane in this book’s first chapter showed us, virtue yields more than persistent teeth-gritting restraint against temptation (although it often starts there). It frees us to love, so delightedly absorbed with true beauty and real goodness that temptation no longer plagues us. Good character bears fruit in integrity, ease, and joy.

Whether single or married, old or young, God wants us to learn to appreciate good and attractive things without our own ego in the way. Chastity as a full-bodied virtue keeps sexual goods, desires, and pleasures ordered to the good of the whole person and his or her vocation to love.57 It focuses on the fundamental question, “How can my whole life—my thoughts, my choices, my emotional responses, my conversation, and my behavior—make me a person who is best prepared to give and receive love in relationship with others?” Chastity preserves and protects and paves the way for wholeness in all our relationships, all the time, albeit through different disciplines for people with different vocations, in different relationships, and at different stages of life. All of us need good human community.58 Constructively channeling our sexual desires empowers us to love each other well.

If this kind of love seems unreachable, do not despair. To break the hold lust has on our hearts, we will need to claim the supernatural power and goodness of a loving God who reaches down into the depths and reclaims us. Recall the brash prayer of seventeen-year-old Augustine: “[Lord,] grant me chastity . . . but not yet.” Augustine later calls the cumulative effect of his choices “the chains of habit.” His story shows us that when we find ourselves helpless against the bondage of vice, God has the power to break any chain. Augustine’s hopeful and grace-filled testimony is that when he found himself failing over and over again, in despair because of his lack of willpower (and even his lack of wholehearted desire to be free of lust), he heard God asking him this question: “Why are you relying on yourself, only to find yourself unreliable?”59

Augustine’s story is timelessly human. His struggle with lust painfully and vividly illustrates the power of sin—because all sin is like this, for all of us. The hold of sin on our hearts can only be broken by divine grace and healed by love. There is simply no other cure. In the end, the relieved and newly freed Augustine prays, “My God . . . you have broken my chains.”60 Chastity must be our chosen habit, a new identity we intentionally claim and live into. But it is first and always God’s gracious and beautiful gift.

The best advice, then, for resisting lust is not to get a good internet filter (although you should do that too) but to build good friendships. If we have fully human relationships in which we learn to give and receive love in appropriate and satisfying ways, we will be less inclined to wander off looking for sham substitutes and quick fixes. Good friendships teach us how to respect each other, to offer appropriate physical affection, to appreciate and care for others without looking for something in return, and to trust one another. Someone who knows what real love looks like, whether in a sexual relationship or not, will be less swayed by lustful pleasures as a tempting alternative. C. S. Lewis said, “What we call ‘being in love’ is a glorious state, and, in several ways, good for us. It helps to make us generous and courageous, it opens our eyes not only to the beauty of the beloved but to all beauty, and it subordinates . . . our merely animal sexuality; in that sense, love is the great conqueror of lust.”61 If your relationships with others and with God adequately feed your need to love and be loved, you will both see through and despise what lust has to offer.

Aquinas strikingly describes our relationship to God as “the love of friendship,” following Jesus’s words in John 15.62 To overcome lust, we need to be fundamentally anchored in this love, for which no human substitute is ultimately possible—even in marriage (which we can easily idolize and expect too much from). Christ challenges us to live out of God’s love in his new commandment (John 15:12), a mandate he gives all disciples and members of his body. Chastity keeps our identity as members of Christ and those beloved by God at the fore of our self-understanding and the way we view others.

Sexual vice illustrates that the greater the power and beauty of the good at stake, the greater the destruction and the potential for ugliness and evil. A short film called Flame compares sex to fire. When we respect the power of fire and keep it in its proper place, we can appreciate its beauty and bask in its warmth.63 At the end of the film, a man douses a pile of timber as tall as a house with gasoline. Then he takes the tiny flame of his lighter and sets it ablaze. The whole night sky is illuminated by the conflagration, flames leaping up to the heavens in the middle of a snowy, barren landscape. Don’t settle for less than God’s good gifts, he says. Instead, by respecting and protecting the real power of love, you may “discover the big flame.”

L’Engle writes to her “long-loved love”:

Unclench your fists

Hold out your hands

Take mine.

Let us hold each other.

Thus is his Glory

Manifest.64

FURTHER REFLECTION

How has your disordered pursuit of sexual pleasure and self-gratification hurt you? What collateral damage has it had? Where are your struggles rooted? When are you most vulnerable? Has your community helped you, or has it made things harder? How?

How does lust spoil our ability to experience pleasure? To appreciate beauty? To rejoice in community?

What practices help us cultivate the virtues of temperance and chastity? How can we better celebrate human sexuality and healthy physical relationships as beautiful gifts? What picture of the human body would this require? What picture of our humanity and our relationships with each other would these practices express?

FURTHER READING

Colleen McCluskey, “Lust and Chastity,” in Virtues and Their Vices, ed. Kevin Timpe and Craig Boyd (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2014), 115–35.

Dallas Willard, The Divine Conspiracy (San Francisco: HarperSanFrancisco, 1998), 158–73.

XXXchurch.com

c15S

10 
The Rest of the Journey

Self-Examination, the Seven Capital Vices, and Spiritual Formation

The most important thing in your life is not what you do; it’s who you become.

—Dallas Willard, quoted in John Ortberg, Soul Keeping

Discipleship is the process of becoming who Jesus would be if he were you.

—Dallas Willard, Renovation of the Heart

One day I confessed my deep discouragement and spiritual struggle to a wise and beloved friend. In my bewilderment at what God was doing in my life, I could see only brokenness. I could taste the agonized tears of Gethsemane. I had prayed fervently for a bitter cup to pass, but it hadn’t. Now I felt like I was facing crucifixion. The pain was acute; my heart ached. I could see with terrible clarity what was dying and being stripped away. To be honest, I felt forsaken.

My friend listened to my struggle in somber silence. Then she gave me a picture. Construction workers had come to her house to replace a broken window. Expecting a simple substitution—new for old—after the day’s work, she was deeply dismayed to see her walls full of new cracks, her room full of construction debris, and everything coated with dust. Things had not been replaced and restored, only further torn apart.

Days passed.

Eventually, the workers returned, put up new drywall, and painted. When they left, the room looked better than it ever had. “You see, I had to wait for them to finish,” she said.

Then she turned to me, “Perhaps the word you need to hear from God today is this: ‘Wait for me to finish.’” In the darkness of my distress over what was broken, I had forgotten to wait in hope for God to make all things new.1

Growing in Godliness

I hope that the self-examination prompted by this study of the vices helps us recognize how serious a threat they are. They disorder our deepest loves and damage our relationships. However, that cannot be the whole reason we study them, nor can it be the whole story of our lives.

The vices tradition gives us a compelling picture of sin. We’ve learned that the word “capital” (caput, capitis in Latin) means “head” or “source.” The list of seven vices originally earned this label because they functioned as “first principles” or “sources” from which many other sins sprung. Before you’d read the previous chapters, could you have imagined how deeply pride was rooted in your heart or how much fruit the vices had borne in you?

It can be discouraging to see! But this book’s work would be incomplete if it left us in dismay and despair.

With Cassian, we have pictured sin as a tree—a tree with roots, trunk, branches, and fruit. Pride serves as the root and trunk of the tree. This depiction of the vices put pride at the foundation, as the principal source from which the seven other main branches grow. Each branch produces its own characteristic fruit. The tree’s rootedness in pride keeps it firmly planted and all its branches well nourished.

These images teach us that we can’t leave sin at the root of our lives. It won’t just sit there benignly, safely underground. Sin is organic and dynamic—it grows, it branches out, it bears fruit. We also can’t just pick off some fruit or trim a few twigs once in a while. A well-rooted and nourished tree will keep growing more.

Why single out pride for this ultimate position? The prideful person claims that God is not her ultimate good and that she does not need God to provide for her. That initial movement of refusal triggers a cascading reaction, coloring the rest of what we think, feel, and do.

If we follow our prideful desires for a moment, we see what it looks like to reject God as our ultimate good and to be lured away by other things. The past seven chapters give us plenty of options to choose from: pleasure, security, comfort, control, wealth, status, approval, success, reputation. These are all good, too, aren’t they? Yes, they are. So we take a good thing—let’s say wealth—and decide that it will make us happy.

How does money work as a God substitute? Pretty well, you might think: If we have enough money, we can provide whatever else we need for ourselves. And money is something we seem to be able to get on our own, without needing God (especially if we adopt the familiar advice to work hard, network well, invest shrewdly, etc.). So we substitute wealth for finding happiness in God. As we make money the center instead of God, though, the pursuit of this good becomes a pattern, a lifestyle. It’s not an occasional, “once in a while” sin. In the tradition’s terms, we have developed a vice. A habit has emerged, reflecting the shape of our hearts and minds, our dreams and imaginations, our expectations and emotions. Our habits say a lot about who we are.

There’s a problem with money, however—or with anything else we might choose on the list of created things promising us security and satisfaction: The more we have, the more we want. And more. And more. Our desires escalate, and with them, our commitments and devotion to pursue this good. Little by little, our lives become wrapped up in what our money can buy. Little by little, we become more indifferent to human need, more deserving of our economic status and success because we earned it, more willing to cut corners ethically to make sure we get a discount or turn a profit, more discontented with what we already have and anxious to upgrade, even severing relationships or turning a blind eye toward others’ harm in order to serve our lifestyle of having things and getting more.

Our greed bears fruit—fruits of character that the tree of vices predicts—fruit like hard-heartedness, deceit, and restlessness. The vice of greed applies not to a person who occasionally overvalues money or keeps too much for himself but to a greedy person. A greedy person’s thoughts are colored by greed; his conversation revolves around his earnings and possessions and his concern for them. He feels good about himself because of what he owns. In his eyes, what he has is well deserved, independent of others’ contributions; he cannot see the need to be grateful. Likewise, a greedy person’s feelings and desires reflect her greed-centered values. Her commitments and priorities and time are shaped by what she owns and by her desire for things that she is eager to buy. Her identity and conversation revolve around her purchases.

Maybe greed isn’t your particular weakness. Maybe the created good in which you would place your hopes for fulfillment looks more like comfort or pleasure, power or status, or getting recognition and approval from others. That’s the thing—the devil has a “sin package” specially designed for each of us. Nevertheless, whatever vice you find yourself susceptible to, the trouble is the same: habitual pursuit of any created thing in God’s place will lead to unhappiness, self-destruction, and enslavement. What starts out as apparently fulfilling turns out leaving us empty. Sometimes we don’t even realize what we’re doing or where we’ve ended up until it feels like it’s too late. Sometimes we’re so caught up in it that we can’t even see how far we’ve gone, how tightly we’re attached, how much we’ve changed.

Which is just to say that sin is a hard habit to break.

And if it were up to us, we’d all end up broken.

When we embark on a life of discipleship to Jesus Christ, these sinful habits need to die. We need to bring our broken selves to the foot of the cross in confession and repentance. We need to ask God to hack down the old tree, cut off the branches, and throw them into the fire.

But in the middle of that hard, messy process, we cannot forget the promise of new life, for we not only claim Jesus’s power in exposing and confessing our old bad habits and uprooting our old broken self; we also claim his power to be resurrected and re-created. To put it in terms of the tree image—there’s a tree for virtue too. When we tap into a new root system, we begin to grow into something new. Jesus promises, “I am the vine; you are the branches. If you remain in me and I in you, you will bear much fruit” (John 15:5 NIV). The Spirit nourishes growth in godliness. By rooting ourselves in God’s love, we begin to grow new branches and produce new fruit. These new habits build up a new lifestyle, and through them we become someone who is more and more Christlike in her character.

Spiritual practices immerse us intentionally in this growing process. The life of spiritual transformation follows a pattern: dying and rising, repentance and renewal. With enough self-examination, we can see a glaring gap between our old self, marked by attachment to vice, and the new self we can become, re-created with Christlike virtues. What bridges the gap? Practicing the spiritual, Spirit-empowered disciplines. The traditional disciplines push back against the old habits that we repent of and break us open to receive new ways of life. They call us to “not be conformed” to the ways we used to live, and they help us “be transformed” by the Spirit into a self whose life now imitates the character of Jesus Christ (Rom. 12:2).2 They teach us both resistance to sin and receptivity to the Spirit.

Recall that John Cassian puts the twofold strategy behind the disciplines this way: “As is the case with the most skilled physicians, who not only heal present ills but also confront future ones with shrewd expertise and forestall them with prescriptions . . . so also the true physicians of souls destroy as with some heavenly medicine these maladies of the heart just as they are about to emerge, not allowing them to grow in the minds of the young but disclosing to them both the causes of the passions that threaten them and the means of acquiring health.”3 Cassian’s metaphor shows that the process of self-examination exposes what’s wrong, destructive, and broken, but it also reveals the way forward, the way of life. After all, you go to the doctor not because you are hoping to receive a bad diagnosis. You seek a doctor because she is an expert who knows how to treat you, heal you, and restore you to wholeness.

Both looking within and finding a path forward will require our resolute attentiveness as well as our trust in God.

Self-knowledge is tied in with inner work, which is both demanding and painful. Change occurs amid birth pangs. It takes courage to walk such a path. Many avoid the path of self-knowledge because they are afraid of being swallowed up in their own abysses. But Christians have confidence that Christ has lived through all the abysses of human life and that he goes with us when we dare to engage in sincere confrontation with ourselves. Because God loves us unconditionally—along with our dark sides—we don’t need to dodge ourselves. In the light of this love, the pain of self-knowledge can be at the same time the beginning of our healing.4

We can think of the spiritual disciplines as a rehabilitation and wellness program through which we learn to live more and more fully the kind of life God made us for. Or, to return to our tree metaphor, the goal is not just to kill the weeds of vice but to cultivate a garden of Christlike virtues.

This suggests that the spiritual discipline of Sabbath-keeping, for example, may be a way of pushing back against or limiting our bad habits of being too confident in our own achievements or too controlled by our own work, plans, and performance. At the same time, however, it gives us a sweet taste of the rest and refreshment we need in order to do the work that God calls us to do, so that we can do it better and more wholeheartedly. It also provides a moment in which we hold still long enough to listen attentively to that call. “It is never a sign of health or godliness to be well adjusted to a sick society,” writes A. J. Swoboda. “Sabbath goes against the very structure and system of the world we have constructed. Sabbath, then, becomes a kind of resistance to that world.”5 That resistance targets internal rhythms along with external systems. Our hearts are overcommitted to the worlds we have made, internalized, and invested our identity in.

Initially, then, Sabbath may be “a gift we do not know how to receive” or value, or even how to desire.6 That is why spiritual formation includes discipline. With practice, Sabbath-keeping gradually awakens new desires, cultivates new habits, opens up new ways of seeing, and walks us into new rhythms of life. Then we can work from a deep well of love, in the peace of knowing that God is also working in and through us. Very likely, we will find that laying down the identity we have invested in our work and deliberately choosing instead to live against the world’s reward systems for that work requires (initially painful) detachment. But how many of us wouldn’t live fuller and more fruitful, gracious lives if we rested better and regularly attended quietly to the presence of the Lord? What would happen to our acquisitive consumption and our angry comportment toward the world if we practiced Sabbath regularly, as a habit? This book began with my own story of pusillanimity and my beginner’s attempts at Sabbath-keeping. That was over two decades ago now. God knows, almost all of us learn to lay things down the hard way, over the course of many, many years.

Consider another example of a spiritual discipline: Practicing stillness or silence may mean we have to curb the constant noisiness, music, and chatter that serve as convenient filler for the real emptiness at the center of our lives. It may mean we have to face our loneliness or lack of meaning. But it may also be a chance to relearn how to breathe deeply and rediscover how full of God’s presence and beauty that silence can be. It may teach us how to stop sleepwalking through life and how to be fully awake, fully alive, fully aware of the gifts present in each day. In silence, we may learn to listen, without intrusions from our own agenda. Josef Pieper may be right when he says, “Only the silent hear.”7

We have an opportunity every day to engage in all these spiritual disciplines and more—as practices of penitence and patterns of renewal.

All that’s to say, if you are rooted in Christ, you can expect to be changed. If you sink your roots deep into his love and power, you can expect to grow in godliness. If—with open hands—you give the Holy Spirit a grateful “yes” in your life, you will find yourself bearing new fruit.

A word of caution though: You should not only expect to grow; you should expect the unexpected. You see, these are graced disciplines, not valiant human efforts to be better. We should not reduce fasting to some kind of spiritual diet plan, fueled by human willpower and high-fiber, low-calorie snacks. Instead, by fasting we say to God, “Something as everyday as my eating is part of a life of discipleship too. I want to be attuned to the ways you are going to form me here. This discipline is my way of saying that my desires are deformed here in ways I probably don’t even understand, but I want you to transform them.”

Let me also say as a word of caution that the disciplines are just that: disciplines, daily practices, habit formation, doing the drill. They’re not always exciting. You might wonder what the big deal is and why these apparently little things are supposed to be magically transformative. But as days turn into weeks, and weeks turn into months, you may well find yourself turning too—turning into someone new. You might find that it takes years of faithfulness in a practice before you “get” what’s already happened to you and what’s still happening in you.

I’m using the language of something happening “to you” and “in you” on purpose. The spiritual disciplines often feel, at least initially, like something we’re doing ourselves—“I’m fasting,” “I’m keeping Sabbath,” “I’m practicing silence.” But this is only how things look on the surface. Remember Jesus’s words: “Just as the branch cannot bear fruit by itself unless it abides in the vine, neither can you unless you abide in me” (John 15:4).

Where are you rooted? How are you growing? What part of your life are you willing to open to God? What intentional, faithful practices will help you sink your roots deep into God’s love? Jesus’s command comes with a promise: “Abide in me as I abide in you. . . . Those who abide in me and I in them bear much fruit, because apart from me you can do nothing” (John 15:4–5). With him, we can bear much fruit, showing ourselves to be his disciples.

The Gift and Practice of Conforming (and Being Conformed)

Self-examination (of the sort likely prompted while you read this book, and while I wrote it) can feel painful. It might prompt discomfort, grief, agonized regret, or even hopelessness. We often don’t like what we discover when we use the lens of the capital vices to look within. Probably the most common reaction is to want to do something about the mess and darkness we find. That’s understandable. But it’s also a reason to be careful about next steps.

The end of a book on the vices would be a natural place to offer a self-help, fix-it, do-something-about-it solution, wouldn’t it? How handy it would be to offer a neat correspondence of vices and virtues. If you are greedy, be generous; if you are lustful, be chaste. Let’s get going, virtue team!

I think that’s a misleading way to put the matter, though. Here’s why: Aristotle was right about habituation, but only partly right. Habituation simply means forming a habit by practice—practicing your way into virtuous character and living. This approach is partly right because there are things we have to do. We rightly call Christianity a “way of life,” after all, and not merely a set of disembodied beliefs about something. “A disciple is a person who has decided that the most important thing in their life is to learn how to do what Jesus said to do,” writes Dallas Willard.8 So practice, or daily activity, stands at the heart of things in an undeniable way. As Aristotle agreed, practice aims not simply to produce behavior but to form character. Willard continues: Jesus “does not call us [only] to do what he did, but to be as he was, permeated with love. Then the doing of what he did and said becomes the natural expression of who we are in him.”9

You won’t practice the spiritual disciplines long, however, before you confront the sober truth: We can’t make ourselves Christlike, no matter how hard we try. Practice, discipline, and all the things we do can’t be the whole story, because human agency isn’t the whole story.

We learn this lesson from Augustine’s Confessions. Our own story—if it too is written as a confession of faith in God’s power and providential plans—must be more than simply recounting what we do and have done. It must be a prayer acknowledging God as the principal author of our lives. Is it any wonder that Augustine begins the narrative of his own life by praising God for his unsearchable wisdom? Augustine thereby admits that even though he’s narrating his own life story, he doesn’t have the whole story. Ultimately, his is a story of what God has done in and for and with him. So at the end of the book, after decades of wrestling unsuccessfully with his disordered and now habitual desires, Augustine directly confronts his need for God and for grace. God called him to Christlike humility, but instead Augustine tried to willpower his way to change. How like us! In the words of my favorite moment from the Confessions, Augustine hears the voice of God asking: “Why are you relying on yourself, only to find yourself unreliable?”10 If we recognize such self-reliance as one more prideful gasp of the old self, then we need to reframe our discussion. More is needed than willing and working our own way toward greater virtue, better selves, and happier lives.

The whole point of the desert self-examination program was, finally, to be more like Jesus Christ and to live in communion with God. Colossians 3 describes the Christlike virtues we need to put on as “compassion, kindness, humility, gentleness and patience” (v. 12 NIV). Over all of them, we put on love—because having those character traits fits us for a perfect relationship of love. If this book has shown us anything, it’s that the worst thing about the vices is that they thwart our ability to love and be loved.

If you look closer at the context of that passage in Colossians, however, you find more than a list of virtues to cultivate. You realize that these are the character traits of those who have been reborn in Christ and are now his new creations, full of God’s Spirit. To think that we can practice our way into this program is a terrible, and terribly frustrating, error. We can debate differences between “résumé virtues” and “eulogy virtues,” embark on “happiness projects,” and develop sixty-six-day programs to conscientiously cultivate new neural pathways of motivation for health and fitness, and yet still walk away blind to the way the project remains fundamentally humanistic, self-powered striving.11 If our final goal is to possess Christlike character—and all the virtues that are part of it—then human means will always fall short. The way to get to that kind of character takes a different kind of practice.

What’s the difference? Isn’t it practice just the same? The spiritual disciplines may seem like more things to do—that is, after all, why they are called disciplines. John Stott once said, “Holiness is not a condition into which we can drift.”12 One of my favorite cartoons is Coffee with Jesus. In a certain strip, the character Carl glibly says to Jesus:

“I like being a believer in you, Jesus. You’re a good friend.”

“And I like that you’re a believer, Carl,” Jesus responds. “But I’d prefer you were a disciple.”

“What’s the difference?” asks Carl.

Jesus replies: “Discipline.”13

Here’s the key: the spiritual disciplines do form us as disciples, but they do so by teaching us submission to the Spirit’s powerful but patient transformative work within. They create space in us to receive God’s love. This is no seven-step, ten-week program formulated to produce or achieve Spiritual Outcome X or Y. Spiritual disciplines gradually open us up to the Spirit’s work. Sharon Garlough Brown calls self-examination and subsequent transformational work “heart surgery.” Notice the language that fits such a metaphor: we undergo surgery. In this program Christ is the Great Physician. So you submit, while God operates. Spiritual disciplines put us in a position to receive what we need. Even when they prompt activity, what we do attunes us to ways Christ is already present and his Spirit is already working. Their outcomes and effects in us don’t ultimately lie under our control. “All great spirituality is about letting go.”14 All spiritual disciplines therefore embody humility, trust, and open-handed faith.

The tasks of conforming to the character of Christ and learning to be drawn into the life and heart of God take a lifetime. For this reason, some authors call the spiritual life a journey. The whole point is to move forward toward the goal, but sometimes it feels like slow going. Often we think we know where we’re headed, but then we face delays and detours. Occasionally we can’t see where we are going at all because it’s so dark or there are too many distractions.

To pick up Paul’s metaphor in Colossians 3, we can change our clothing relatively easily. With a single makeover, we can progress from fashion faux pas to phenomenal style. Putting on a new self, however—a new identity, with integrity—is much harder. Such transformation into virtue requires not our wardrobe, not just window dressing, but our whole person. As Paul puts it, “Clothe yourselves with compassion, kindness, humility, gentleness and patience” (Col. 3:12 NIV, emphasis added).

Think of it this way: we are always being formed by and conforming to something, so who and what we conform to makes all the difference. The best way to stop conforming to the old self and living according to the patterns of the vices is to be continually conforming to Christ instead. Ephesians 4 also uses the “putting off” and “putting on” picture of the life of discipleship—put off falsehood and put on truthfulness, put off anger and put on forgiveness, put off unwholesome talk and put on encouraging words, put off malice and put on kindness. Paul sums up all these commands using whole-self, whole-life language: “Be imitators of God, . . . and live in love, as Christ loved us” (Eph. 5:1).

Human lives get worn with all sorts of grooves and ruts from practice—in the form of daily routines, mental default patterns, imaginative habits, and emotional expectations. Perhaps you discovered some of these in yourself while reading this book. Sometimes we settle into habits that form us before we realize it.

To be always conforming, then, does not mean being a mindless conformist as we are when we are shaped by advertising or by our culture. If the desert fathers and mothers are right, our default patterns (the vices) are self-destructive. How can we get out of these ruts? When Paul tells us to conform instead to the character of Jesus Christ (Eph. 5), the very fact that he’s instructing us to do this means that he’s expecting us to make an intentional commitment. Likewise, he says, “Set your minds on things above” (Col. 3:2 NIV), “Be transformed by the renewing of your mind” (Rom. 12:2), “Be made new in the attitude of your minds” (Eph. 4:23 NIV), and “Whatever is excellent, and true, think about these things” (Phil. 4:8). In other words, don’t just drift along—be attentive to who you are becoming, be aware that your choices along the way make a difference, and watch that your words and actions mirror Christ’s. Hopefully this book’s prompts to self-examination have given you a head start down that road.

What does mindful conformity to Christ look like? No need to reinvent the wheel here. Christian disciples have been at this for ages. How do people conforming to the character of Jesus Christ live, work, and pray; eat, rest, save, spend, socialize, study, and play; celebrate, mourn, gather, and worship? Look back in history, look deep into Scripture, and look around you.
