Disciples of Jesus keep the Sabbath, they fast, they confess and lament, they pray through the psalms, they give alms and tithe, they recount God’s faithfulness, they sing spiritual songs and express gratitude and praise, they anoint the sick, they welcome the stranger, they feed the hungry, they meditate on Scripture, they rejoice and give thanks, they take time for quiet and rest, they weep but not as those who have no hope.

Some Christians have lists of these practices of conformity. We’ve called them spiritual disciplines. But whatever we call them, we need to understand them not as occasional acts of heroism or saintliness for the few but as a set of everyday practices for everyone. In the words of Tish Harrison Warren, they form a “liturgy of the ordinary.”15 How we eat, what we wear, how we work, how much we sleep, what we buy, how we speak, and what we speak about—all are mundane, daily things. Extraordinary conformity, it turns out, emerges from ordinary routines. The imitation of Christ counts as something to do today and each day. We can’t take up the project of living like Jesus on occasional retreats, or when the semester is over, or when we finally have a day off. We are to be always conforming.

Let me offer one more story of my own daily practice—already briefly alluded to in the chapter on gluttony—lest you come away with the impression that this is simply a matter of trying harder to conform yourself to the character of Jesus Christ. We have already heard Richard Foster say that “fasting reveals the things that control us.”16 When I first read his book, I was a bit taken aback. Wasn’t fasting just about eating less and praying more? I was curious, not having fasted much before. So that year during Lent, I decided to try it. Fasting can be done in many forms; the details aren’t so important here. For most of my day, I was either skipping eating when I was used to eating or I was eating much less than I was used to eating. The result? I was hungry most of the time. It probably won’t surprise you that my desires needed retraining and restraining even more than I initially expected. But even more, I discovered that all my breaks from work involved eating. First, I took a ten o’clock coffee break, then a short lunch break, then an after-school snack break with my kids, a break for dinner, and best of all, a break for dessert—which was my final break and a chance to relax when the day’s work was all done. I had built my entire day around this little help-yourself reward system. Without my usual eating at many of those times, I was at a loss as to what to do. So I began using that time to pray. Awkward as it felt at first, I would stop working, give thanks to God for whatever had come my way so far, and ask his blessing on the remainder of the day. It takes a while for a new habit to feel natural, but by week four or so, it began to dawn on me that my mindless eating had been just another busy activity keeping me away from inner quiet and prayer. I needed those prayer breaks more than I needed a cup of coffee or a cookie.

Beyond this noble spiritual lesson that I began (haltingly) to learn, however, other lessons emerged from the simple fact that fasting meant eating less. Being hungry most of the time made me downright irritable. I was irritable partly because I couldn’t have what I wanted. I couldn’t reward my work with the pleasures I felt I deserved. But I also found myself irritable because eating less made me tired. It turned out that I couldn’t keep up my usual agenda of activities while fasting—because that agenda was fueled by regular bursts of caffeine, sugar, and snacks (even healthy ones). I was a power-eater, grabbing whatever was within easy reach to fuel my performance-packed, on-the-run lifestyle. That’s how I was eating, because that’s how I was living. Day after day, I realized, the “old self” I had become was someone who measured her own worth in terms of efficiency, to-do lists, achievements, and productivity. While fasting, by contrast, I needed more breaks, more mental rest, and more sleep. That meant I had to give up some extra things I would otherwise have added to my already busy schedule. Not accidentally, it also meant that when I had to stop and rest, I ended up talking to people I would have otherwise ignored (including God) in my rush to get more things done.

Fasting as a regular practice didn’t just take food away. It began to eat away at a lifestyle enabled by eating in the wrong ways, for the wrong reasons, and with the wrong desires. To my surprise and chagrin, I found that I had been using food to conform to the wrong standards of success. About three weeks into Lent, I had a crabby conversation with God that went something like this: “Lord, I gave you my eating. I did not give you control of my schedule and all my plans for what needs to get done!” Fasting scratched through my surface-level Christianity and found pride alive, well, and running the real show.

As it turns out, God works transformation wherever we need it. Through the disciplines, God’s Spirit starts detaching us from our false pictures of happiness and our false views of the selves we want to be, and he starts drawing us closer to a Christlike self instead. Spiritual disciplines may be something we do intentionally, but at the same time they require submitting ourselves, being receptive, and opening ourselves to whatever ways we need to be changed.

It should not be surprising, then, that as hard as I found giving up food, what I found even harder about fasting was the self—a deeply valued picture of myself—that I had to relinquish along with the food I gave up. It was painful to let go of the always-busy, high-achiever, in-control version of me that I had invested my identity in and got so much social approval for. Those things confirmed my view of myself as successful and important. That way of doing things made me feel that my life counted as worthy and worthwhile. Behind my fasting, I came face-to-face with my false self. I realized for myself that “there is nothing people are so fixated on as their self-image. We are literally prepared to go through hell just so we don’t have to give it up. It determines most of what we do or don’t do, say or don’t say, what we occupy ourselves with and what we don’t. We are all affected by it. The question is: Do I have the freedom to be anything other than this?”17

Fasting counted as a discipline not only because it was a challenging exercise but also because it was God’s way of discipling me, challenging aspects of my old self and calling forth something new. In my practice of intentional conformity, God stripped away part of my old self and conformed me more to his image and likeness. God is in the new-creation business. The power of God’s Spirit surprised me along the way. After a while, I not only felt much better with less food but also delighted in the quiet and the time with others that a shorter to-do list made possible. Now when my schedule fills up, I find myself prompted to return to those good things by my own hunger for them. God calls us not to slavish conformity to rigid practices and pointless rules but rather to a grace-filled opportunity to taste true rest, love, and freedom.

But back up a minute—back to the “always” in “always conforming.” It’s not as though I could fast for a single Lenten season, learn a tidy and timeless lesson about grace and the new Christlike self, and then be done. Each year, I slip back a little and need a new Lenten season to relearn and be renewed as an imitator of Christ’s character and virtue. Lent comes around every year for a reason. The church year circles around and around again. There’s an “always” lesson here too. We must keep conforming to Christ Jesus—over and over and over again. We’ll find endless depths and eternal fruits as we do, and even a lifetime will not exhaust the ways we can become more like him.

The human-powered, self-help version of virtue cultivation will never be adequate because the project of “always conforming” ultimately describes the Holy Spirit’s work in us. We need the Spirit, because to become like Christ is a call to something immeasurably more than we can ask for or imagine. The process of putting off the old self and putting on the new self reveals nothing less than God’s power at work within us.

How can we parse the transformation that happened when I fasted? Was it God’s work or mine? It was both. Was it under my control? Yes and no. Yes, I had decided to practice and then had to follow through. But did I get to decide what parts of me needed rehabilitation? Not really (as my kicking and screaming attested). The exercise opened the door and let the Spirit come in to heal whatever needed healing. Stanley Hauerwas and Charles Pinches agree: “Our character is indeed an achievement, but one that comes as a gift. . . . We can grow in Christian virtue, yet it is best to describe this as growth in grace.”18 Living a life of graced discipline effects a gradual process—a rehabilitative walk in Christ’s footsteps that Eugene Peterson wisely called “a long obedience in the same direction.”19

Grace and God’s Spirit work the miracle of transformation beyond the reaches of our striving. The disciplines teach us to submit to that work and to open ourselves to the change that is needed.

Comprehensive, Concrete, and Communal

Fortunately, the world already contains a treasure trove of insight and instruction about the spiritual disciplines (see the end of the chapter for further reading). I won’t duplicate that material here, only point you to it. As you dig deeper and explore further, though, remember three things.

First, the disciplines are comprehensive. Spiritual disciplines mark off routine, quiet devotional time away from the rush and bustle of the daily grind—but they also redirect our eating, sleeping, working, listening, resting, speaking, paying attention, reading, celebrating, caregiving, giving, spending, and receiving. Practiced well, they leave no arena of your life unshaped and no corner of your heart untouched. In her introduction to the spiritual disciplines, Adele Ahlberg Calhoun lists all-embracing disciplines such as “slowing” and “detachment,” which inform the way we engage in all other activities. She also includes “creation care” and “hospitality,” which include large- and small-scale ways of comporting ourselves toward the earth and toward our neighbors.20 How we shop, what recreation we choose and enjoy, where we live, what we consume, how we spend our leisure time, the forms of transportation we use—everything that shapes and expresses our hearts’ deepest attachments falls under Christ’s lordship and the Spirit’s transforming mission. It’s not sufficient for Christians to tack prayer and worship onto their lifestyles as “extra activities” and then stick to business as usual everywhere else. Rather, the new narrative of our participation in the life and love of God colors everything we dream about, desire, and do—our entire way of life.21

Second, the disciplines are concrete. You may have already realized that by calling them “spiritual” I do not mean to suggest that they encompass only ethereal, mental, or soul-care type matters. Think of them instead as earthy, embodied, everyday practices. Kathleen Norris called them “quotidian mysteries.”22 They are things to do, activities carried out by embodied beings. Having the mind of Christ and being transformed inwardly should show in how we speak and act. But the reverse also holds. Repeated actions—the disciplines, which form a way of life—also form and inform our hearts and minds. How do you become virtuous? By performing acts of virtue—first, as a beginner or apprentice, copying what the mature virtuous person does (with imperfect motivation, unnaturalness, and even some resistance and discomfort).23 At this point, the learner cannot fully grasp why she must do the things she is instructed to do. But habituation itself includes or inculcates a kind of practical know-how. Through repetition, she learns to inhabit the world in a different way. Her practice shapes and colors different “passions and perceptions.”24 With time and the guidance of role models and the counsel of master practitioners, her practice produces greater and greater proficiency. Finally, the mature virtuous person can look back on her training and endorse the process as having made her genuinely better and led to her flourishing. At that moment, she not only sees the good more clearly; she can also intentionally endorse that way of life as best for her.25

In short, “being” and “doing” are not separate enterprises. Character formation is a way of life, and our way of life is character forming. Someone who practices gratitude does grateful things and expresses gratitude, but she also views the world through a different lens, experiencing its goodness as a gift. With enough practice, it becomes clear that her former cynical attitude led to a myopic vision of what was happening around her and caused her to miss the gift and beauty of what was really there. Her previous complaints no longer rise to the level of being noteworthy, or they are swamped by other features of the landscape that motivate rejoicing.

Finally, practicing the spiritual disciplines should be a communal endeavor. Protestants especially might be tempted to make the mistake of trying to engage in spiritual practices or formation on their own, as individuals. Each Christian, though loved by God uniquely as an individual, is essentially a member of the body of Christ and the communion of saints. Moreover, some disciplines are naturally social (e.g., worship and celebration).26 Our growth in godliness flourishes in the mutually encouraging community of the church, in both its local and its historical forms. This book opened with a story of how Thomas Aquinas helped me face my pusillanimity, and this chapter began with a word of wisdom from my spiritual director, speaking a bracing word like an “amma” or “abba” from the desert. We’ve already seen how fasting and feasting can be done corporately. When my students and I practiced three minutes of silence together at the beginning of each class, it changed the dynamics of our relationship to each other, and it changed our subsequent discussions. The discernment we need for self-examination and the guidance we need to practice the disciplines require a community of examples, role models, mentors, partners, encouragers, and loving friends. Never underestimate how much we need each other along the way.

A colleague once gave a chapel talk comparing the rhythms of the spiritual life to jumping on a trampoline.27 On the first few bounces, you sink and rise only a little. As you gain momentum, however, the force of your downward weight pushes you lower and lower, while the buoyancy of the springs propels you higher and higher. He compared this dynamic to facing the depths of our sin, which then serves as a springboard for rising to grasp the heights of God’s mercy. Likewise, the higher you drop from—that is, the more aware you are of the limitless reach of God’s gracious and loving embrace—the lower you can go as God unveils layers of sin further and further beneath the surface. This might help explain why some of the greatest saints have such a dark view of their own hearts, while at the same time appearing to be beautifully sanctified people full of trust and joy. Their heightened sense of their belovedness in Christ makes possible the depths of their contrition.

The introduction to self-examination encountered here marks only the beginning of a lifelong journey. As we continue to look within—through the eyes of love, not judgment—we will keep learning to see ourselves more truly, and we will learn to more truly long for greater holiness. As we accustom ourselves to hear God call us his beloved and secure our identity in that word about us, we will grow to trust him with our idols, our faults, our persistent weaknesses, our disordered attachments, and even the worst of our shame. Under the Great Physician’s care, we can become healed, whole, and well. Our spiritual health and abundant life are the good he seeks. Our spiritual wholeness enables us to give ourselves more fully to God and to his kingdom work with joy, gratitude, hope, and peace. With that vision and calling, and leaning on his grace and mercy, we can face our vices and find new life through the power of the Spirit.

In the deserts of Egypt, the practice of naming the vices and training for resistance was only the first stage of the project. Release from the distortions of the vices freed the Christians there to see creation (including other people) as a reflection of the Creator, transparent to his glory. That was the second stage. Finally, communing intimately and directly with God himself—unhindered and unmediated—was the goal, or last stage. They called it “peace” and “pure prayer” (hēsychia in Greek). We, like the early desert mothers and fathers, face the spiritual dangers of falling too much in love with the world and with ourselves and of failing to yearn enough for a life of delight in the Lord. Therefore, the practices of detachment from our disordered loves typically come first. Facing up to our vices, however, must always be embedded in this larger, life-giving program of spiritual formation, motivated by a desire for the God who calls us his own and draws us to himself in an embrace of love.

Know that your striving is not the solution. His grace will make a way for you. This book invites you to start that journey toward a closer relationship with him, confident that the God who began a good work in you will bring it to completion, for your good and for his glory.

FURTHER REFLECTION

Return to the tree image of the vices. Can you name how each one has its roots in pride? Which vice(s) did the previous chapters expose in your life? Can you identify its fruit?

Vices are habits of the heart and mind that shape our desires and our behavior. How would you define a spiritual discipline? Which disciplines are most familiar to you?

How do the disciplines forge a middle way between self-help (“works righteousness”) and religious complacency (“cheap grace”)? Which extreme is a greater danger for you?

FURTHER READING

John Ortberg, The Life You’ve Always Wanted: Spiritual Disciplines for Ordinary People, expanded ed. (Grand Rapids: Zondervan, 2002).

James Bryan Smith, The Good and Beautiful Community (Downers Grove, IL: InterVarsity, 2010).

———, The Good and Beautiful God (Downers Grove, IL: InterVarsity, 2009).

———, The Good and Beautiful Life (Downers Grove, IL: InterVarsity, 2009).

c18Y

Epilogue

Greed, gluttony, lust, envy, [and] pride are no more than sad efforts to fill the empty place where love belongs, and anger and sloth [are] just two things that may happen when you find that not even all seven of them at their deadliest ever can.

—Frederick Buechner, Whistling in the Dark

Imagine for a moment that you have died. Your friends and family gather to grieve their loss and to remember you. What conversations would they have about you? What sorts of memories of you would they share with each other? What sort of person would they describe if they gave your eulogy?

Funerals are one of the few places where we still pause to contemplate a human life taken as a whole and try to sum up a person’s character. At milestones and celebrations such as retirements and graduations, we typically celebrate someone’s specific accomplishments or achievements. But that is different from memorializing someone’s character. The real question at a funeral is not “Has this friend or family member been successful at some great task?” or “What recognizable milestones or markers of success has he or she reached?” Rather, we ask, “Who was this person?” and “How would we characterize his or her life?”

It might be a worthwhile exercise to spend ten minutes trying to write down the speech you think a loved one might write about you if you died today. What sorts of character traits would they mention? What traits would they know about but politely not mention? Would there be a dominant theme in discussions about what sort of person you were among colleagues, church members, and family? Would they mention qualities like integrity, a good sense of humor, generosity, kindness, or perseverance? Would your irritability or stubbornness or impulsiveness be part of their conversation? What stories from your life would best illustrate those telling moments that revealed who you were, at your best and at your worst?

I regularly assign this exercise to my philosophy students. (In fact, I find it helpful to do it myself, year after year.) After they jot down a sketch of the speech, I ask them to write a second speech—the speech they wish someone could have given at their funeral, a speech about the person they aspire to be but have not yet become. What ugly qualities of character do you wish you’d overcome or put behind you? What character traits would you want in the epitaph on your gravestone if you had more time or a chance to do it over again?

If you are anything like me, you will quickly discover a rather unhappy gap in the content of those two speeches—the one that would in fact be given and the one I wish someone could have given. This gap is simply an admission of the difference in character between the person I am now and the person I wish yet to become. It offers us a place to begin reflecting about ourselves and then living into real change.

The memento mori exercise of meditating on our death prompts us to look back on the past and honestly assess who we are and where we have been. However, such reflections are meant to serve not as an endpoint but as a starting point. The desert fathers and mothers used this exercise in two ways. First, it prompted confession of and contrition for vice—part of the process of relinquishing the old self and its habits. Second, as an exercise in self-examination, it prepared them to look ahead, with intentionality and hope, to what they could and would become—by prayerfully living into the habits of the new, regenerated self. Following their example, we need not fall into regretful rumination or narcissistic navel-gazing. Rather, we can use what we see to begin the project of living more fully into Christlike character, starting today.

This book presents the tree of capital vices—each vice rooted in pride and growing branches of further vice and sin—as a helpful tool for engaging in this type of reflection. These seven vices, canvassed in the previous chapters, name perennial areas of human weakness and typical displays of pride’s provide-your-own-happiness program. When we study the vices, we thus study ourselves. When you look closely in that mirror, what do you see? And what does seeing yourself more clearly make you long to become?

Like Christian thinkers long ago, I believe that examining the vices’ deformations of our character can and should move us forward, in a more clear-sighted way, toward being people of virtuous character, people whose lives are well lived, people who are Christlike. Nevertheless, you may find yourself first falling on your face at the foot of the cross. (Did you read each chapter with a sinking feeling that you had that vice too? Imagine what it felt like to write the book!) Take heart: God does not leave us on our own when we find our hearts and lives entrenched in vices. In the Christian tradition, the transformation we need is not powered by human aspirations but by the Holy Spirit. At the same time, neither is leaning on the Spirit a license to drift along or do nothing. We are encouraged to be intentional, reflective, strategic, specific, and energetic about moving with the Spirit’s formative work. Like Augustine, when we look back on the way the vices disorder our lives, we are moved to pray for the future: “Order love within me.”1 The vices, their offspring, and the remedies traditionally suggested for them give us the language to identify and track the disordering of our desires that we must, with God’s help, actively resist. Discipleship takes graced, Spirit-empowered discipline to straighten what is bent, retrain and strengthen our capacities, and loosen the bonds that constrain us. Graced discipline bears fruit in the beauty of Christlike virtue.

In this study of the vices, one theme kept rising to the surface, confirming the pastoral instincts and Augustinian theology of Gregory the Great: pride is the root of the seven capital vices. I did not devote a separate chapter in this book to the vice of pride, because every chapter in this book has to do with pride.2

When we had our backyard maple tree taken down, the tree-removal crew started with the uppermost outer branches, lopping off more and more until they got to the biggest limbs. Then they took down the main trunk piece by piece. As anyone who’s ever had a tree removed knows, however, you can chop off as many branches of the tree as you like, but eventually you have to dig it out roots and all, or the stump will relentlessly send out new shoots and grow new branches. The roots buried below the ground keep feeding the tree and giving it life. In the same way, pride feeds the other seven capital vices.

Pride can reveal itself in many guises—we have identified only seven common ones here. When we carefully examine each of the vices, we eventually unearth the same familiar, prideful pattern: a quest to provide happiness for ourselves through whatever God-substitute we choose—pleasure, approval, wealth, worth, power, or status. We are not willing to let God be in control, so we refuse to keep these goods in their place and accept them as gifts from his hand.

We readily recognize pride in its arrogant forms: the person who resists submitting to God because he thinks he can handle things just fine on his own, the one who snubs advice and counsel because she knows best what is good for herself, and those who shun dependence on anyone else as unnecessary weakness. This arrogant form of pride can show itself in vicious responses like an avaricious desire for self-provision and control, or a wrathful desire to avenge ourselves on our own terms.3

We should also be aware, however, of the way fear can drive us into vice. When we don’t trust God’s provision or control, we might be tempted to seek happiness in safer, more secure and self-sufficient ways, or we might anxiously protect ourselves when we feel our lack of power. Like my pusillanimity, each vice can therefore also show us compensating for our perceived vulnerabilities with an attempt to take control.4

When we are afraid we won’t get what we need, or when we worry that we won’t have enough, we clutch our things tightly and keep wanting more. This is the vice of avarice at work. When we are afraid that justice won’t be served or that we won’t get our just deserts unless we take charge of making things right, the vice of wrath seeths within. When we are afraid that we will not be accepted by others or be able to live up to their expectations, and hide behind a false reputation, the vice of vainglory has captivated us. When we are afraid we are not worth anything unless we are better than others, and our insecurities lead us to eye their downfall with relief, the vice of envy has marked us with its malice. When we are afraid we will always feel empty and so choose to fill ourselves with pleasures that satisfy only for a moment, our hungry hearts have swallowed the vice of gluttony’s deceptively sweet promises. When we are afraid we are unlovable and compensate by using people to gratify ourselves without the risk of giving ourselves in return, this is lust’s haunting handiwork. When we are afraid that commitments of love will cost us too much—and thus we hold everyone, even God, at arm’s length in indifference—the vice of acedia has closed off our hearts.

In the mirror of such reflections, we can see the sad, dark truth about ourselves: the tree of vices, rooted in pride, continues to live and grow and thrive in human nature in many malignant forms. The more we understand the dynamics of sin and the deep network of its combined forces in us, however, the more amazing we will find the grace and power God promises to help us change. The Christian tradition bears centuries-long witness to the hope and promise of our transformation from vice to virtue. It further proves a venerable guide to the ways and means of engaging in this difficult but fruitful process. Although we find ourselves separated by many years from the counsel of the desert hermits and the contemplation of medieval theologians, we can still find much of their witness and wisdom compelling today.

We have just looked backward—to imagine our death and the portrait of our character that might be offered at our funeral. Now we turn forward—to imagine our reformation and the road that might lead us into the future. It’s a journey for which we have a map, traveling companions, and well-seasoned guides. “To flee vice is the beginning of virtue,” said Horace.5 And virtue’s ending?

In flesh’s solitude I count it blest

That only you, my Lord, can see my heart

With passion’s darkness tearing it apart

With storms of self, and tempests of unrest.

But your love breaks through blackness, bursts with light;

We separate ourselves, but you rebind

In Dayspring all our fragments; body, mind,

And spirit join, unite against the night.

Healed by your love, corruption and decay

Are turned, and whole, we greet the light of day.6

FURTHER REFLECTION

Take some time to practice the memento mori (“remembrance of your death”) exercise described above. Use the language of the virtues and vices to articulate patterns in your life and aspects of your character. Journal your thoughts. Return to this exercise and the next one once a year (on New Year’s Eve, for example) to mark the ways God is at work reshaping your character.

Consider the spiritual disciplines discussed in this book. Commit to one new way of intentionally opening your life to the Spirit’s work. Or, to go deeper, investigate making a “rule of life.”

13. Psychological counseling is valuable and often necessary. In order to avoid falsely burdening people who are already struggling, I eschew the use of sin, vice, guilt, and blame language for conditions primarily related to mental illness rather than moral responsibility. However, it is equally unhelpful to reduce spiritual maladies to psychological problems, since this fails to diagnose the root of the problem or offer the right remedies. Using therapeutic models to excuse people from responsibility or deflate the significance of the problem can also serve (either implicitly or explicitly) to validate desires that need to be disciplined or reordered. Further, the early Christian tradition integrated theological, psychological, and spiritual matters (unlike current academic and professional divisions), which makes it difficult to draw these boundaries accurately as we study their work in our own context. See Dennis Okholm, Dangerous Passions, Deadly Sins: Learning from the Psychology of Ancient Monks (Grand Rapids: Brazos, 2014) for a better approach.

9. “For Evagrius the terms ‘thoughts’ (logismoi) and ‘fantasies’ (phantasiai) took on a strongly negative cast, with the latter term bearing much of its modern psychological connotation. Behind each thought there stood a demon at work, to the extent that Evagrius would frequently use the terms ‘thought’, ‘demon’, and ‘evil spirit’ interchangeably” (Sinkewicz, introduction to Evagrius of Pontus, xxv; see also xxv–vi). See also Columba Stewart, OSB, “Evagrius Ponticus and the Eight Generic Logismoi,” in Newhauser, In the Garden of Evil.
10. Evagrius, Praktikos 6, in Sinkewicz, Evagrius of Pontus. Evagrius is also a source for the enneagram (a list of nine, including fear and deceit as additional compulsions). In Greek, the vices are gastrimargia, porneia, orgē, lupē, akēdia, kenodoxia, hyperēphania (or hubris), and phthonos (envy, a later addition).
11. The acronym of Latin names was GLAIT(I)AVS—gula, luxuria, avaritia, ira, tristitia (invidia, a later addition to the list), acedia, vana gloria, and superbia. For more on this list’s history, see Carole Straw, “Gregory, Cassian, and the Cardinal Vices,” in Newhauser, In the Garden of Evil, 38; and Richard Newhauser, The Treatise on Vices and Virtues in Latin and in the Vernacular (Turnhout, Belgium: Brepols, 1993), chap. 6.
12. Cassian presents them as a pedagogical progression: novices find gluttony and lust—vices focused on bodily pleasures—comparatively easier to fight than vainglory and pride, which count as spiritual, easily hidden vices. In contrast, Gregory presents them as a degenerative series, beginning with pride: once we are separated from God through prideful disobedience, we fall from grace into a less and less human state, ending with gluttony. See Straw, “Gregory, Cassian, and the Cardinal Vices,” in Newhauser, In the Garden of Evil. Aquinas does not offer a serial account.

See William Harmless, SJ, Desert Christians: An Introduction to the Literature of Early Christian Monasticism (New York: Oxford University Press, 2004). Sinkewicz writes, “[Evagrius] is not rigid about his schema, because he realizes that there are variations in the ascetic experience of different individuals and personalities. . . . The Evagrian schematic order [of thoughts] is not intended to be rigidly applied to everyone’s ascetic experience, even though Evagrius does maintain that the schema does have a general validity that is useful in discerning the workings of the thoughts.” Sinkewicz, introduction to On the Eight Thoughts, in Evagrius of Pontus, 71.

Sadness or dejection (tristitia in Gregory’s Moralia) was a technical term for the sorrow that followed incomplete interior relinquishment of a monk’s or nun’s former worldly life and all its pleasures and treasures. It should not be confused with what is now called clinical depression, or with grief or sadness in general.

29. “All grace is from above, which shows even to sinners the schemes of the deceivers and which also offers assurance, saying, ‘For what do you have that you did not receive’ (1 Cor 4:7), so that in having received, on the one hand, we may give thanks to the one who granted the gift, and in having possession, on the other hand, we may not attribute to ourselves any boasting of honor, as though denying the gift” (Evagrius, prologue to On the Vices, in Sinkewicz, Evagrius of Pontus). Cassian concurs:

Wherefore it is well for us both to be certified by actual experience, and also to be instructed by countless passages of Scripture, that we cannot possibly overcome such mighty foes in our own strength, and unless supported by the aid of God alone; and that we ought always to refer the whole of our victory each day to God Himself . . . I ask what could be said clearer in opposition to that impious notion and impertinence of ours, in which we want to ascribe everything that we do to our own free will and our own exertions? (Conferences 5.15)

The British Library’s illuminated manuscripts include several vice trees (e.g., https://www.bl.uk/catalogues/illuminatedmanuscripts/ILLUMIN.ASP?Size=mid&IllID=7111) and virtue trees (e.g., https://www.bl.uk/catalogues/illuminatedmanuscripts/ILLUMIN.ASP?Size=mid&IllID=7112). Adam and Eve’s prideful “no” of disobedience in the garden of Eden roots the vice tree, while the virgin Mary’s humble “yes” of obedience at the annunciation roots the virtue tree. “Bearing good fruit” and being “rooted” in love are scriptural metaphors (see, e.g., Ps. 1; John 15:1–7; Gal. 5:22–23; Phil. 1:11; Col. 2:7). 48. Prudentius’s Psychomachia (likely written in the fourth century) is interesting for its blending of Christian virtues with Roman militaristic ideals. Alternately, spiritual counsel might commend resisting the vices not with virtues but with spiritual disciplines like fasting, psalmody, prayer, and vigils, as Evagrius suggests in his Praktikos. His Antirhetikkus (contemporary title: Talking Back) gives prompts from Scripture to use to “talk back” to the demons when they make a “suggestion,” following Christ’s example in his wilderness temptations.
49. The seven corporal works of mercy are these: to feed the hungry, to give drink to the thirsty, to clothe the naked, to give shelter to travelers, to visit the sick, to visit the imprisoned, and to bury the dead. The seven spiritual works of mercy are as follows: to instruct the ignorant, to counsel the doubtful, to admonish sinners, to bear patiently those who wrong us, to forgive offenses, to comfort the afflicted, and to pray for the living and the dead.

Luxury wants to be called abundance and satiety; but you are fullness and the inexhaustible treasure of incorruptible pleasure. . . . Avarice wishes to have large possessions; you possess everything. Envy contends about excellence; but what is more excellent than you? Anger seeks revenge; who avenges with greater justice than you?”

Chapter 5 Sloth (Acedia)

9. The Latin acedia (sometimes accidie) is derived from the Greek term akēdia (ἀκηδία), which means “lack of care.”
10. Morton W. Bloomfield, The Seven Deadly Sins (Lansing: Michigan State University Press, 1967), 75; Siegfried Wenzel, The Sin of Sloth: Acedia in Medieval Thought and Literature (Chapel Hill: University of North Carolina Press, 1967), 10; Evagrius of Pontus: The Greek Ascetic Corpus, trans. and ed. Robert E. Sinkewicz (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2003), 72.

14. Evagrius’s holistic approach to the human person and the desert fathers’ and mothers’ presentation of this and other vices blurs contemporary distinctions between emotional and spiritual life. See n. 17 below.
15. Cassian, Institutes 10.21; for a similar description, see Conferences 5.16, trans. Boniface Ramsey, OP, Ancient Christian Writers 57 (Mahwah, NJ: Newman, 1999).
16. Cassian, Institutes 10.2. This extended description of acedia’s torment of the monk is as colorful as Evagrius’s.
17. Laura M. Lysen, in “Vicious Sorrow: The Roots of a ‘Spiritual Sin’ in the Summa theologiae,” Studies in Christian Ethics 30, no. 3 (2017): 329–47, characterizes acedia primarily as an emotion, but in the tradition, consent of the will is crucial to the characterization of acedia as a vice. Lysen correctly notes that acedia manifests itself as sorrow, but it would be a mistake to attribute sinfulness to emotions not informed by “reason’s consent” (Aquinas’s term, although the mortal-venial distinction is relevant here; see ST II–II 35.3). Evagrius agrees: “Sin for a monk is consent of the thought to the forbidden pleasure [or passion]” (Praktikos 75; see also 71–90). While Lysen, as well as Norris (see n. 20), tends to fuse acedia and depression, I maintain that the two should remain distinguished. See also n. 18.
18. Originally, acedia and tristitia (sorrow) were distinct vices. Cassian and Evagrius describe sorrow’s cause as insufficient renunciation of worldly goods, as required by desert religious commitments. This habitual sorrow produces resentment of their religious vocation: this is acedia. Gregory combined sorrow and acedia under the title tristitia. Aquinas instead defined acedia as a distinct kind of sorrow. My account of sloth, while based on Aquinas’s definition, preserves the early sorrow-over-renunciation dynamic, with excessive attachment to the “old self” making commitment to and joy in the “new self” difficult and distasteful.
19. ST II-II 35; Aquinas, Disputed Questions on Evil 11, trans. Richard Regan, ed. Brian Davies (New York: Oxford University Press, 2003).
20. Kathleen Norris, The Quotidian Mysteries: On Laundry, Liturgy, and Women’s Work (Mahwah, NJ: Paulist Press, 1998), 53; Kathleen Norris, Acedia and Me: Marriage, Monks, and a Writer’s Life (New York: Riverhead, 2008).
21. ST II-II 24.3.
22. Granting acedia’s emotional character, we still need to explain why we are morally responsible for it as a vice. Aquinas notes that emotions like grief can make us more susceptible to acedia (ST II-II 35.1).
23. Aquinas, On Evil 11.2 and ST II-II 35.3.
24. Hieronymus Bosch, Table of the Seven Deadly Sins (early sixteenth century), https://www.museodelprado.es/en/the-collection/art-work/table-of-the-seven-deadly-sins/3fc0a84e-d77d-4217-b960-8a34b8873b70; see also Pieter Bruegel the Elder, Sloth (Desidia), from the Series the Seven Deadly Sins (mid-sixteenth century), https://www.metmuseum.org/toah/works-of-art/26.72.34/.
25. “It cannot be said that sloth [acedia] is a particular vice insofar as it shuns spiritual good as toilsome or troublesome to the body or as a hindrance to bodily repose, for this would not make sloth distinct from the carnal vices, whereby we seek bodily comfort and pleasure” (ST II-II 35.2).
26. Spiritual formation literature calls this the “false self.”
27. Søren Kierkegaard, in The Sickness unto Death, trans. Howard V. Hong and Edna Hong (Princeton, NJ: Princeton University Press, 1985), 67–74, describes this self as one who refuses, in defiance, to be itself because this would require acknowledging the self’s dependent relationship to God. If the refusal is less conscious, acedia is more akin to Kierkegaard’s despair of weakness (see esp. 55–56).

30. Amma Theodora describes acedia as a kind of grief: “There is grief that is useful, and there is grief that is destructive. The first sort consists in weeping over one’s faults and weeping over the weakness of one’s neighbors, in order not to destroy one’s purpose, and attach oneself to the perfect good. But there is also a grief that comes from the enemy, full of mockery, which some call accidie. This spirit must be cast out, mainly by prayer and psalmody” (quoted in Swan, Forgotten Desert Mothers, 62).

44. There is significant dispute over this claim in the Christian tradition. In monastic orders, individuals own nothing, although the monastery itself can own property, engage in trade for goods, and provide for its members’ needs. Is this possessionless state a counsel of perfection only for some? Different church fathers disagreed about whether private property or ownership was a necessary evil, a neutral matter, or a good, and about whether the desire for possessions was natural to human beings or a sinful desire resulting from the fall. My point is simply that human beings cannot live or live well without some material goods (e.g., food, clothing, and shelter). Thus, the remedies I suggest are directed toward the virtuous handling of those goods.

13. See Aristotle, Nichomachean Ethics 2.2, 6, 8–9, trans. T. Irwin (Indianapolis: Hackett, 1999). This feature of virtue is commonly thought to be limited primarily to the virtues of character that concern directing the passions. The notion that virtue lies in a mean should not be interpreted as indicating that you should moderately desire pleasure or be moderately afraid all the time (in life-threatening situations, for example, great fear may be an appropriate response). Neither is the mean exactly in the middle between the two extremes (in fact, it’s usually not). Rather, in our reactions of pleasure, desire, or fear to various situations, we go wrong by having these emotions excessively. While it is sometimes fitting to be very afraid, we fall into the vice of cowardice when we are so afraid that we abandon our duty instead of completing our mission. Thus having too much fear is a moral problem, even if we can also correctly say that it is right to feel very afraid. Not feeling much fear is also fitting sometimes (e.g., when driving), but again, if we feel too little fear, we have a vice of deficiency called rashness (e.g., carelessly speeding through red lights).

I am grateful to Lawrence Cunningham, whose review of the first edition prompted me to make this point more explicit; see his review in Commonweal, June 4, 2010, https://www.commonwealmagazine.org/religion-booknotes-11. Aquinas devotes most of his discussion to lust defined as a vice opposing temperance in its specific sense (i.e., moderating desires for the “natural” pleasures of touch—most specifically, taste and sexual touch) and not in its general sense (any virtue having the mode of moderating desires that tend to excess). In fact, this is true of all the cardinal virtues and their opposing vices, since Aquinas prefers to define cardinal virtues by their specific matter (ST I-II 61.4). For related comments on chastity and lust, see ST II-II 141.4–5 and 151.2. “Even as temperance chiefly and properly applies to pleasures of touch, yet consequently and by a kind of likeness is referred to other matters, so too, lust applies chiefly to venereal [or sexual] pleasures, which more than anything else work the greatest havoc in [the human] mind, yet secondarily it applies to any other matters pertaining to excess. Hence, a gloss on Galatians 5:19 says, ‘Lust is any kind of surfeit’” (ST II-II 153.3, emphasis added).

Insofar as sexual activity is ordered toward the continuation of the human race as a whole, Aquinas calls it a “very great good” (ST II-II 151.3). Contemporary treatments of sexuality rightly emphasize and equally affirm the unitive (and not just the procreative) aspect of sexuality, but Aquinas was already moving toward a much greater acceptance of human sexual activity and its goodness than many of his predecessors (e.g., the desert tradition).

That is sometimes the case when the conditions are chosen (e.g., by taking a religious vow) or unchosen (e.g., if you are unable to marry, or if your spouse dies or falls ill or is absent for a time). Epilogue

1. Augustine, City of God 15.22, trans. Marcus Dods (New York: Modern Library, 1950). See Peter Brown’s insightful analysis of this theme in Augustine of Hippo (Berkeley: University of California Press, 1967), chap. 27, esp. 325–26.
2. To clarify, this is only one of several senses of the term “pride.” Although I have been referring to the vice of pride, it’s also common for English speakers to use the term “pride” in perfectly benign ways. For example, we tell our children we are proud of them or we express pride in a hard-won accomplishment. It’s important to explain what sort of pride this might be, especially since I have tracked only the destructive footprints of pride throughout this book.

Aquinas distinguishes two kinds of pride—general pride and specific pride. General pride is the kind of pride I’ve been talking about in the book. It’s the basic pattern of all sin, turning away from God’s picture of the good and preferring our own—spurning the divine good for something less or lower instead. All the vices follow this prideful pattern of loving a created thing over the Creator.

Special pride, on the other hand, is not something I’ve discussed in the book other than briefly distinguishing it from vainglory. It denotes a particular vice, which we could include on any list of vices along with dishonesty, cowardice, and the like. Dishonesty is about truth, and cowardice is about threats that make us afraid; so pride is about the desire to have excellent goods. What makes them vices is that their desire or attitude toward their object is disordered. If we define the feeling of pride as a desire for or delight in some excellence (some superior good) that belongs to me (or is somehow considered “mine”), then there is nothing wrong with being proud of our children or our accomplishments. We can rightly desire or delight in excellent goods that belong to us (at least in some sense of “belong”). This desire goes wrong when it becomes excessive or attached to the wrong object—a familiar theme from the analysis of vice we’ve seen in this book. When the focus falls too hard on the “better than yours” aspect of excellence, rather than on “striving to meet the highest standards,” or when my delight over some excellence being mine is too possessive and not simply rejoicing over having something so wonderful, then being proud of something shades into disorder and counts as a particular vice. This kind of pride is not identical to the pattern behind all sin, so it remains distinguished from general pride. See, for example, Robert C. Roberts, “Pride” in Faith and Philosophy 26, no. 2 (2009): 119–33.

3. Traditional villains in fiction usually fit this pattern: J. K. Rowling’s Voldemort is a typical case. Many heroes typifying humanistic moral ideals, such as the self-sufficiency and self-reliance of the protagonists of American action-adventure films, also exhibit forms of pride.
4. For more on links between the two lists, see Richard Rohr and Andreas Ebert, The Enneagram: A Christian Perspective (New York: Crossroad, 2016), 6–14, esp. 10.
5. Horace, Epistles 1.1.41, ed. O. A. W. Dilke (London: Methuen, 1954).
6. Madeleine L’Engle, “Within This Quickened Dust,” in The Ordering of Love: New and Collected Poems of Madeleine L’Engle (Colorado Springs: Shaw Books/Waterbrook, 2005), 66.
7. For further sources, including scholarly resources, see the endnotes and further reading sections of each chapter.
